



































Rhapsodies of the Coming Regent









































































BOOK SIX: THE FULFILLMENT 



Lizzy 









































ONE





My holiday trip with Judith came as a surprise and a prayer answered. She wants my company in her big family house during the hols. She complained she’s mostly lonely because her siblings are out of the country. Her invitation was an answered prayer because I had been longing for some distance and distraction from all that is happening around me. 

The semester had ended with too much weight on my heart. Bitty’s departure, Sammy’s sudden disappearance from school, the rumours around Otunba’s missing election money, and then my journey to Ogbomosho, where I found Sammy living like a man whom life had stripped almost beyond recognition.

Now, I have just returned from Ogbomosho carrying more than luggage. The holiday will afford me the time and space to think through the secret Sammy shared with me about the Program hidden inside the fraternity’s Testament, a counterfeit system designed to harvest young people’s gifts and mortgage their future under the promise of fame, influence, and fulfilment.

I think of Rakiya. Her unsigned paintings leaning against a wall in that small room, each one crowded with symbols one cannot fully understand. 

When Judith’s invitation came to spend part of the Christmas break with her family, I accept it quickly. “I need rest,” I tell myself.

But the truth is simpler. I want one week without mysteries. One week without cult names, prophecies, police rumours, spiritual warfare, or young men whose destinies always seem to lead toward graves.

Judith takes the holiday more seriously than I do. She drags me into a boutique near the City Square, insisting I cannot spend Christmas looking like “a missionary on assignment.”

The boutique is small but stylish, with glass shelves displaying handbags, perfumes, shoes, costume jewellery, folded denim, and brightly coloured dresses arranged under soft white lights. A mannequin near the entrance wears a green jumpsuit with a gold belt and stands with the confidence of a woman who has never worried about school fees, cultists, or police stations.

Judith moves through the racks with practised excitement.

“This one will fit you.”

“No.”

“You haven’t even looked at it.”

“I can see the colour from here.”

“That is the problem with you, Lizzy. You dress like you are always preparing for a serious conversation.”

“I often am.”

She rolls her eyes. “Exactly. That is what I am trying to cure.”

I laugh, but my heart is not fully inside the laughter. Every now and then, my mind returns to Ogbomosho. To Rakiya’s left hand moving over paper and Sammy scanning the restaurant like a hunted man. I twitch at the thought that my own name, Lizzy, had become a possible trail to his hiding place. I dismissed the thought after I left.

Judith eventually convinces me to buy a modest dress, a scarf, and a pair of sandals I do not need. She buys twice as much and still complains that she has “shown restraint.”

When we step out of the boutique, the afternoon sun has softened, but the road remains busy. Cars move slowly. Hawkers pass with bottled drinks, biscuits, groundnuts and light food. A taxi stops ahead of us, then leaves before we can reach it.

Judith lifts her shopping bags with a sigh. We stand near the edge of the road waiting for another taxi. Then a car slows in front of us. I recognize the car.

David is always in Steel’s company, after Sammy left him. He sits behind the wheel. Steel occupies the front passenger seat.

He looks different in daylight. Cleaner. Better dressed. Less like the hardman of hostel corridors and more like a young man trying to resemble success. He’s brushed up with Otunba’s windfall. His eyes are the same, restless, proud, wounded, always searching for something to possess or accuse.

“Lizzy,” he calls.

I do not move.

Judith glances from him to me. “You know him?”

“Unfortunately.”

Steel smiles as if he has not heard. “Where are you going? Let me drop you.”

“No, thank you.”

“Come on. It’s Christmas period. Don’t stand under the sun forming independent woman.”

“We’re fine.”

Judith leans closer to me. “Lizzy, who is this?”

“Nobody we need.”

Steel’s smile fades slightly. David looks away, pretending sudden interest in the dashboard.

“Can I speak with you for a minute?” Steel asks.

“No.”

“It’s important.”

“It usually is when you want attention.”

His jaw tightens.

“It’s about Sammy.”

The world around me seems to pause. The road noise does not stop, but it moves farther away.

“What about Sammy?”

Steel’s eyes hold mine.

“He was arrested yesterday.”

For a moment, I do not understand the words. Or maybe I understand too quickly and my heart refuses them. “Arrested?”

Steel nods. “In Ogbomosho.”

My shopping bags suddenly feel heavy. 

Judith turns sharply to me. “Ogbomosho?”

I ignore her.

“Why?”

Steel opens the car door and steps out. The old arrogance remains, but beneath it I sense something else. Not guilt exactly. Fear, perhaps. Or the discomfort of someone who has delivered a message that may open a door he cannot control.

“You should go and see him,” he says.

“Answer me. Why was he arrested?”

He looks around, then lowers his voice. “There is a petition against him.”

“What petition?”

“Abduction. Exploitation of a minor. Maybe more.”

The words strike me like cold water. Rakiya.

My stomach tightens.

Steel watches my face carefully.

I sense he does not know everything. He knows enough to be dangerous, but not enough to understand what truly happened in Sammy’s room.

Judith grabs my arm. “Lizzy, what is going on?”

I cannot answer.

Steel continues, “He is at the station. If you care about him the way he thinks you do, you should go now.”

The sentence carries poison. But the information is real. I step closer to him. “Did you have anything to do with this?”

His face hardens. “Be careful.”

“No. You be careful.”

For once, my voice does not tremble. “You people have taken enough from him.”

His eyes flash. “You people?”

“Yes. You people.”

Judith tightens her grip on my arm.

Steel laughs once, without humour. “Lizzy, you don’t know the kind of things Sammy has done.”

“I know enough.”

“No. You know the church version.”

“And you know the version that excuses everything because it wears a black beret.”

His face darkens.

For a second, I think he will say something cruel. Instead, he steps back.

“Go and see him.”

He enters the car, shuts the door, and they drives off.

Judith remains beside me, staring as though I have become someone unfamiliar. “Lizzy,” she says slowly, “what deadly circle are you involved with?”

“I am not involved with them.”

“But you know them.”

“I knew them from school.”

“And Sammy?”

I do not answer quickly.

Judith stares at me.

“This is the same Sammy you travelled to see?”

“Yes.”

“The same one you said was trying to rebuild his life?”

“Yes.”

“And now he has been arrested for abduction and exploitation of a minor?”

“It is not what it sounds like.”

“How do you know?”

“Because I know him.”

Judith’s expression sharpens. “Do you?”

The question hurts. Not because it is unfair. Because it is the same question fear has begun asking me. How much do I truly know? I know Sammy’s repentance. I know his guilt. I know how desperately he wants to protect Rakiya. But I also know that parts of his past still stand behind him like dark trees.

Judith lowers her voice. “Lizzy, listen to me. You are not a police officer. You are not his lawyer. You are not his sister. You cannot keep throwing yourself into the fire because broken men tell you they are changing.”

“He did not ask me to throw myself anywhere.”

“But you are already preparing to go.”

I look at the road. A taxi approaches and passes without stopping.

Judith’s voice softens. “You were supposed to travel with me tomorrow.”

“I know.”

“My mother is expecting you.”

“I know.”

“You bought clothes.”

“I know.”

“Then call someone else. Call his family. Call whoever should handle this.”

“He has no one here.”

“That is not your fault.”

The words land heavily.

I look at her. “No. But it is my choice what I do with that knowledge.”

Judith closes her eyes briefly, frustrated.

“This friendship is dangerous.”

“Yes.”

“And you know it?”

“Yes.”

“Then why?”

I look down at the shopping bags in my hand. New sandals. A scarf. A dress for a holiday I suddenly cannot imagine taking. “Because if a person is drowning and you are the only one near the river, you don’t first ask whether helping will disturb your plans.”

Judith looks at me for a long time. Then she sighs.

“You Christians and your impossible conscience.”

A faint smile almost comes to my face. But it does not survive.

“Come,” she says. “Let’s get a taxi.”

“Judith…”

“I am still angry. But I am not leaving you on the road with cultists looking for you.”

That is Judith. She can scold you while saving you. 

By evening, my holiday bag remains unpacked. The dress stays folded on my bed. The sandals remain in their box.

I leave for Ogbomosho the next morning with a small bag, my Bible, some cash, and a fear I cannot discuss with anyone except God.

Wooden benches line the waiting area. A faded national emblem hangs crookedly on the wall. The police station is poorly ventilated, but I am not sweating from the heat circulated by the noisy standing fan in the lobby. Other visitors are seated in different corners, some angry, some resigned, some pretending not to be afraid. 

A woman argues with an officer near the counter. A boy no older than seventeen sits barefoot beside the wall, his eyes fixed on the floor.

When I give Sammy’s name, the officer behind the desk looks at me with a curiosity that tightens my skin.

“Relative?”

“Friend.”

His pen pauses.

“What kind of friend?”

“The kind that came to see him.”

He stares at me for another second, then points toward a corridor. A constable leads me to a small interview room.

Sammy is sitting on a wooden chair behind a metal table. For a moment, I stop at the door.

He looks thinner than when I left him in Ogbomosho. Not physically alone, but inwardly… as if some part of him has already begun withdrawing from the world. His shirt is rumpled. His eyes are tired but strangely calm. That calm frightens me more than panic would have.

He looks up. “Lizzy.”

My name in his mouth carries relief and sorrow together. I rush forward, but the constable’s presence restrains me.

“I’m sorry,” I say.

The words come before any greeting. “I am so sorry. I think I led them to you. Someone must have followed me that day I came.”

Sammy watches me quietly. There is no accusation in his face.

“I knew this hour would come,” he says.

“That does not make it my fault?”

“No.”

“It feels like my fault.”

“The Lord prepared me for this.”

His calmness annoys me.

“Sammy, don’t speak like someone already writing his last chapter.”

He looks away.

I sit opposite him.

“What are they charging you with?”

His mouth tightens.

“Abduction. Sexual exploitation of a minor. Possibly conspiracy.”

The words turn my stomach.

“Rakiya?”

He nods.

“Where is she?”

His eyes return to mine.

“Gone.”

For a moment, I do not understand.

“What do you mean gone?”

“When they took me, she was still in the house. By the time enquiries were made, she was no longer there.”

My breath catches.

“Her parents?”

“I don’t know.”

“Did they take her?”

“I don’t know.”

“The police?”

“I don’t know.”

His voice finally cracks.

“I don’t know, Lizzy. That is the worst part.”

The room seems to close in around us.

Rakiya’s smile flashes through my mind. Her shy correction when I told her not to call me aunty. Her left hand moving over paper. Her unsigned paintings. Her excitement when I asked her if she’d come and live with me, “Yes. Yes.”

I press my hand against my mouth.

Sammy leans forward.

“You have to find her.”

“Where do I look?”

“I don’t know.”

“Sammy, I barely know how to describe her properly.”

“You saw her.”

“Yes, but only once.”

“You saw the paintings.”

His eyes sharpen.

“Find the paintings. Find anyone who has them. Find Rachel if she exists. Find the hospital record. Anything.”

“Rachel?”

“The girl who led me to her.”

“The one you said disappeared?”

“Yes.”

I stare at him.

The case is already larger than an arrest. It is becoming a maze.

“This is a frame-up,” I whisper.

“Yes.”

“Otunba?”

“His petition opened the door. But Rakiya’s disappearance complicates it.”

He looks toward the door, then lowers his voice. “I don’t know how much time I have.”

“Stop saying that.”

“They will kill me.”

“No.”

“They will.”

“No, Sammy.”

He smiles faintly. Not with joy. With pity. As if he wishes my denial has power over the machinery closing around him.

“Lizzy, listen to me.”

“I am listening.”

“You remember what I told you in Ogbomosho? About the Program?”

I nod.

“The Program the GODs wanted you to execute with Otunba.”

“Yes. That politician is not merely a sponsor. He is an agent of the counterfeit system.”

The word moves strangely through the room.

Sammy’s voice drops further. “The world they are building is not just political. It is cultural. Spiritual. Intellectual. They want access to young people at the point where desire is strongest – fame, education, influence, escape, technology, art, entertainment, opportunity.”

I remember Rakiya’s paintings, and what Sammy told me about Tunde’s fake contract and the students at the Centre preparing for a concert as though performance can save them from obscurity.

“The Program promises fulfilment,” Sammy continues. “But it harvests identity.”

I swallow. “And Rakiya?”

“She is proof that God is doing a new thing.”

His eyes burn now, not with madness, but conviction. “The Lord is raising a remnant generation. Gifts that will not be owned by darkness. Talents that will not be sold to the merchants. Weak things of the world to confound the mighty.”

“Sammy…”

“I know how it sounds.”

“It sounds dangerous.”

“It is.”

The constable outside moves past the door. We both go quiet.

Then Sammy speaks again, softer. “I had a dream.”

I wait.

“My father was blessing me.” His face changes as he says it. 

The calm breaks.

For the first time since I entered, I see the frightened orphan beneath the prophet, beneath the fugitive, beneath the former cultist.

“He laid his hand on my head,” Sammy whispers. “He prayed for me. I could not hear all the words, but I knew he was blessing me.”

“Your foster father?”

He nods. “I want to see them, Lizzy.”

I feel the impact of his request more deeply than all the revelations before it. A son wanting to go home before death finds him.

“I ran away from them,” he says. “I thought they rejected me. But I have been remembering things. I think I was wrong. I want to reconcile. I want to see my father. I want his blessing before I die.”

“Before you live,” I correct him.

He looks at me.

“Lizzy…”

“No. Before you live.”

His eyes fill.

“I don’t know if they will want to see me.”

“Then we will find out.”

“You will help me?”

“Yes.”

“You promise?”

The word costs me. Because a promise, once given, becomes a road.

“I promise.”

He lowers his head.

For several seconds, he says nothing.

Then I reach across the table. The constable looks in through the door, but does not stop me. I place my hand over Sammy’s.

“But you must promise me something too.”

He looks up.

“What?”

“Stay alive.”

His gaze falls.

The silence that follows frightens me.

“Sammy.”

His fingers tremble beneath mine.

“I don’t know if I can control that.”

“I am not asking whether they will try to kill you. I am asking whether you will help me fight for your life.”

He closes his eyes. His breathing becomes uneven. For one terrible moment, I think he will refuse. Then he takes a short breath.

“I will stay alive,” he mutters.

“Say it like you mean it.”

He opens his eyes. They are wet.

“I will stay alive.”

I hold his hand tighter.

Outside the station, someone shouts. A phone rings. A typewriter clacks in another room. The world continues in its ordinary indifference. But inside that small interview room, my holiday ends completely. The vacation clothes folded on my bed no longer belong to the life I am living. 

Sammy has been arrested. Rakiya is missing. Otunba’s shadow stretches farther than I feared. And somewhere between a police station, a lost foster family, a vanished girl, and a secret Program designed to harvest a generation, I understand that my own calling has changed.

I came to help a friend. But I leave the station with an assignment.

















TWO





Following Sammy’s description, it’s easy for me to locate Destiny Education Institute. Although school was officially on vacation, it’s the proprietress, Mrs. Animashaun’s birthday and she had invited the students to come celebrate with her. 

Both the teachers and students will be expecting to see him. Not that Sammy commanded me to go, but as a burden handed over through trembling eyes in a police station.

“Tell them the truth what has happened, so they don’t hear a false version,” he had said.

Sammy was quietly rebuilding young people in Ogbomosho while the rest of us still thought of him only as a man hiding from the fraternity.

Destiny Institute stands at the edge of a busy road, a modest one-storey building with faded paint, old signboards, and classrooms that seem too small for the noise I imagine they contain. But this morning, the compound is unusually quiet.

No laughter. No shouting. They have heard the news, I suppose. It does not look like they came for a birthday party, but to mourn. 

A few students stand in clusters near the staircase. Some turn when I enter the gate. Others look away immediately, as though my arrival reminds them of the person who is missing. I recognize some faces from Sammy’s stories: Tunde, restless even in stillness; Genevieve, composed but troubled; Oluchi, visibly anxious; Patrick, standing with his hands in his pockets; Jumoke, unusually subdued; and Eno, seated near the veranda with a notebook pressed against her chest.

The security man directs me to the proprietress’ office.

Mrs. Animashaun receives me with the politeness of a woman trying to remain official while grief presses against her composure. She is in her late forties, perhaps early fifties, neatly dressed in a patterned blouse and skirt, her glasses resting low on her nose. Her desk is covered with files, examination timetables, a ledger, and photographs from what looks like a recent event.

“You must be Miss Lizzy,” she says.

“Yes, ma.” How did you know me? I ask myself. 

“Please sit.”

I sit.

For a brief moment, neither of us speaks.

“Happy Birthday ma. He told me, it’s…”

“Have you seen him?”

“Yes.”

Then she removes her glasses and sighs.

“How is he?”

The question is simple, but I do not know how to answer it. “He is alive,” I say finally.

Her face falls slightly, as though she understands everything I do not say.

“Alive is something,” she murmurs.

“Yes, ma.”

She looks toward the window, where students linger in the courtyard, pretending not to wait for information.

“They have not been themselves since the news came.”

“I can imagine.”

“No,” she says softly. “I don’t think you can.”

I look at her.

She turns back to me. “When Mr. Briggs first came here, I honestly did not know what to make of him. He had no strong certificate to present. No polished background. No confident professional history. But he had something I could not easily dismiss.”

“What was that?”

“Burden.”

The word surprises me.

“He looked at these children as if he could see beyond their noise. That was rare. Many teachers complain about students. He studied them.”

She picks up one photograph from the table and hands it to me. It is from the Christmas concert.

The students stand on a stage, dressed in coordinated outfits. Some are smiling broadly. Others look nervous. Tunde stands at one side holding what appears to be a painted backdrop. Eno stands near the centre, microphone in hand, her eyes lowered but her posture graceful.

I stare at the photograph.

“They performed?” I ask.

Mrs. Animashaun smiles faintly. “They did more than perform. They surprised us.”

She hands me another photograph. This one shows Sammy standing beside the students, not in front like a celebrity teacher, but slightly to the side, almost as if he wants the attention to remain on them.

My throat tightens.

“He never told me much about the concert,” I say.

“He would not. He was not a man who praised himself.”

She arranges the photographs slowly.

“These children came here disillusioned. Some had failed examinations. Some were rejected by schools. Some were waiting for admission that never came. Some had lost confidence completely. A few were simply careless. Others were angry. Many had already started believing that nothing meaningful would come out of their lives.”

She pauses.

“Mr. Briggs did not treat them like failures. He treated them like unfinished work.”

The sentence stays with me. Unfinished work. That is Sammy too.

The door opens gently and a man steps in. Mrs. Animashaun introduces him as Mr. Adewale, the principal. He is tall and lean, with a mild face and tired eyes. He greets me warmly, but sadness sits behind his smile.

“I was just telling her about Mr. Briggs,” Mrs. Animashaun says.

The principal nods.

“Then tell her the truth. He gave us trouble.”

Despite myself, I smile.

“What kind of trouble?”

“The kind good teachers give administrators,” he says. “He wanted things. Time with students. Permission for mentoring sessions. Space for rehearsals. Extra access to classrooms. Then he wanted us to look beyond exam preparation and help students identify talents.”

Mrs. Animashaun shakes her head.

“At first I thought it was too much.”

“It was too much,” the principal says. “But it worked.”

He turns to me.

“What amazed me most was how he handled misconduct. These students can be difficult. Very difficult. Tunde especially.”

As if summoned by his name, faint movement comes from outside the office window.

The principal notices and smiles sadly. “Normally, when students misbehave, teachers punish the behaviour. Sammy would ask what the behaviour was protecting.”

I frown slightly. “What do you mean?”

“He did not excuse nonsense. But he always looked deeper. A student mocked another student, and he would ask why that student needed laughter so badly. A girl stopped attending class, and he would ask what shame was holding her hostage. A boy became arrogant, and he would ask what insecurity the arrogance was covering.”

Mrs. Animashaun adds, “Sometimes I thought he was too patient.”

“And was he?” I ask.

The principal smiles. “No. He was patient in a way that made the rest of us feel shallow.”

The room becomes quiet. I look again at the concert photograph. Sammy had been hiding in Ogbomosho, yet he had not hidden from purpose. That realization humbles me.

“May I meet the students?” I ask.

Mrs. Animashaun nods. “I was hoping you would ask.”

They gather in Sammy’s classroom. Not because anyone instructs them to choose it.

They simply go there.

The room still carries his presence. The blackboard has faint chalk marks from a previous lesson. A pile of old assignment sheets sits near the teacher’s table. On one side of the board is written in fading chalk. Talent without discipline becomes public nuisance.

I almost laugh.

That sounds like him.

The students sit quietly, which makes them appear younger than they are. Without noise, their fear becomes visible.

Mrs. Animashaun introduces me briefly.

“This is Miss Lizzy. She is Mr. Briggs’ friend.”

Friend.

The word moves across the room with many meanings. Some students look at me with hope. Some with suspicion. Some with accusation. Tunde’s eyes are red, though he tries to hide it. Eno holds her notebook tighter.

I stand before them and suddenly understand why Sammy has asked me to come. This is not a courtesy visit. It is a handover of grief.

“Good morning,” I say.

A few respond weakly. “Good morning, ma.”

“Please don’t call me ma. Lizzy is fine.”

No one smiles.

So I continue. “I saw Sammy yesterday.”

The entire room sharpens. Tunde leans forward. Eno’s lips part slightly.

Genevieve asks the question first. “How is he?”

“He is alive.”

Tunde mutters, “That one is not answer.”

A few students turn to him, but I raise my hand gently. “It is not a full answer,” I say. “But it is the first truth.”

The room quiets again.

“He is in police custody. There are allegations against him. I believe those allegations are false.”

“False?” Patrick asks. “Then why did they arrest him?”

“Because false things can still wear uniforms and carry files.”

The principal glances at me, but he does not interrupt.

Tunde stands suddenly. “It is because of us.”

“No,” I say.

“It is because of Rakiya,” he insists. “Because he helped her. Because he always wants to help everybody. Now see.”

His voice cracks at the end. He sits down quickly, ashamed of his emotion.

Oluchi wipes her eyes. 

Jumoke speaks next, her proud voice unusually soft. “Will he come back?”

The question hurts.

“I don’t know.”

That answer wounds them more than a comforting lie would have, but I refuse to insult their grief with false certainty.

“I don’t know when. But I know he asked about you.”

At that, the room changes slightly.

“He asked about us?” Eno whispers.

“Yes.” I look at her.

“He asked me to come here. To check on you. To make sure you are not abandoned.”

Eno lowers her head and begins to cry quietly. Genevieve places an arm around her shoulder. For several seconds, I let the room breathe.

Then Tunde raises his hand, though he is already speaking. “Did he mention me?”

A few students look at him. 

His face reddens.  “I’m just asking.”

I smile faintly. “He did not mention names. But I know he would want you to stop acting like your talent is a joke.”

The class murmurs.

Someone whispers, “That one na for Tunde.”

Tunde sniffs and looks away. “He told you about my drawing?”

“He told me enough.”

Tunde swallows. “I was going to show him something after the holidays.”

“What?”

“A design. For the concert group.”

“Then keep it safe.”

His face tightens.

“What if he doesn’t come back?”

“Then you keep it safe until he does.”

“And if…”

“He will need to see that you did not waste what he believed in.”

That silences him.

Eno slowly raises her face. “Miss Lizzy?”

“Yes?”

“People are saying he ran away with money. That he was pretending to be good.”

A murmur moves through the class. I hear the fear beneath the question. Not fear that Sammy is guilty. Fear that the one adult who made them believe in themselves might become another disappointment.

I take a breath.

“People will say many things. Some from ignorance. Some from cruelty. Some because gossip makes them feel important. But you must learn something Sammy tried to teach you: do not judge a person only by rumours. Look at fruit.”

I point gently around the room. “You are part of his fruit.”

They become still.

“He met many of you discouraged. Now you are singing, drawing, studying, leading, apologizing, caring for one another. That is fruit.”

Eno wipes her face. “He saved my life,” she says.

The room goes quiet.

She stands slowly. “I don’t care what people say. He saved my life. When everybody would have laughed at me, he did not laugh. When I thought I was finished, he told me my story was not finished.” Her voice trembles, but she continues. “He called me miracle.”

She holds up her notebook.

On the back, in careful lettering, are the words, ENO, THE MIRACLE.

Several students lower their heads. Even Tunde. The principal turns away slightly, pretending to adjust his glasses.

Oluchi stands too. “He also told us not to gossip about her,” she says. “He said if the world was what she feared, we should be better than the world.”

Genevieve adds, “He made this class different.”

Patrick folds his arms. “He disturbed us too much.”

For the first time, faint laughter moves through the room.

Patrick shrugs. “But good disturbance.”

Jumoke smiles through tears. “He made us rehearse when we didn’t want to.”

Tunde mutters, “He punished me for drawing.”

“No,” Genevieve corrects. “He redirected you.”

The class laughs again, softer this time.

Tunde rolls his eyes. “I don’t like big grammar.”

“You need it,” Genevieve replies.

Watching them, I understand something. Sammy has not merely taught these students. He has entered their language. Their jokes. Their wounds. Their arrogance. Their shame. Their hunger to be seen. He has given them a small community where failure is not final and discipline does not mean rejection.

That is worth preserving. I walk to the blackboard and pick up a piece of chalk.

“What are you doing?” Tunde asks.

I write my phone number on the board. The students lean forward.

“This is my number,” I say. “Tunde, make sure everyone copies it.”

“Me?”

“Yes, you.”

He stands immediately, as if the responsibility itself has lifted him. “Yes, Miss Lizzy.”

“From today, if there is an emergency, if any of you is in trouble, if anyone threatens you because of Sammy, or if you hear anything about Rakiya, you call me.”

Genevieve’s eyes sharpen. “Rakiya is missing?”

The room freezes. I regret saying the name too quickly. But it is too late.

“Yes,” I say carefully. “And we need to find her.”

Tunde’s face changes. “The girl that paints?”

“You saw her?”

He nods. “Only once. She came to the Centre with Mr. Briggs. She stayed near the office and kept drawing. I remember because she held pencil like this.” He lifts his left hand and imitates a sketching motion.

My heart tightens. Left-handed.

“What did she draw?” I ask.

“I don’t know. I tried to look. She covered it.”

“Did she speak to anyone?”

Tunde looks around.	

“Eno?”

Eno shakes her head. “She smiled at me, but she didn’t talk.”

Oluchi says, “I saw her at the gate with a small book. She was drawing the signboard.”

“The institute signboard?” I ask.

“Yes.”

“Why?”

Oluchi shrugs.

“Listen carefully. No one goes looking for her alone. No one confronts strangers. No one follows anyone claiming to know where she is. You call me. Understand?”

“Yes,” they answer.

“Good.”

Tunde raises his hand. “Miss Lizzy?”

“Yes?”

“Are you going to be there for us?”

The question lands heavily. Not because it is childish. Because it is direct. Everyone looks at me. 

I think of Judith’s warning. This friendship is dangerous. My mind goes to my abandoned holiday plans. The dress still folded, the sandals still boxed. The journey I longed for, to rest. Then I think of Sammy’s face in the police station. “Find her. Find my father.”

I look at the students. “I will do my best.”

Tunde shakes his head. “That is adult answer.”

Some students murmur in agreement. I almost laugh.

“What answer do you want?”

“A real one,” he says.

Eno’s voice comes softly. “Will you leave us?”

The question is not about logistics. It is about abandonment. I feel it. I move closer to the first row.

“I cannot become Sammy,” I say.

Nobody speaks.

“And I cannot promise that everything will be easy. I have to search for someone very important to him. I also have to help find Rakiya. So I may not be here every day.”

Their faces fall slightly.

“But I promise this: I will not forget you. I will stay in contact. I will speak with Mrs. Animashaun and Mr. Adewale. We will make sure what Sammy started here does not die because he is absent.”

Tunde lowers his eyes. “That one is better.”

The class gives a faint laugh. I stretch out my hand toward him.

“Come.”

He looks confused. “Come where?”

“Here.”

He steps forward cautiously. I place my hand out, palm down. 

“Put your hand here.”

He stares at me. “Why?”

“Must everything have explanation?”

The class laughs. He places his hand over mine.

“Eno.”

She comes forward and places hers on top of Tunde’s.

“Genevieve.”

Then Oluchi. Patrick. Jumoke. Bisi. Chike. One by one, the students come forward, some shy, some emotional, some trying to hide tears behind jokes. Soon a cluster of hands forms at the front of the class. I look around at them.

“From now,” I say, “we protect what God started through Sammy.”

Tunde, unable to resist himself, says, “So we are now the Sammy Briggs gang?”

The class bursts into laughter. Even I can’t hold myself. The principal covers his mouth. Mrs. Animashaun shakes her head, smiling through tears.

I look at Tunde.

“No cult names.”

“Not cult, Miss Lizzy. Positive gang.”

“Still no.”

“Sammy Briggs Team?”

“That is better.”

“Remnant Squad?” Patrick suggests.

The room becomes noisy again, but this time the noise is alive with affection rather than disorder.

I raise my hand.

“Whatever you call yourselves, remember this: Sammy did not gather you to worship him. He helped you see that your lives matter to God. So, honour him by becoming serious with your lives.”

The room quiets.

“Study. Sing. Draw. Apologize when you are wrong. Protect one another. Don’t let shame destroy anyone here. And if any of you sees another student going down a dangerous path, do not laugh. Do not gossip. Stop them.”

Eno nods slowly. Genevieve wipes her eyes. Tunde looks at the board.

“I will copy the number,” he says.

“Good.”

As they return to their seats, the atmosphere has shifted.

They are still grieving. Still afraid. Still children in many ways. But something has been preserved. A small flame Sammy lit among them has not gone out.

Mrs. Animashaun walks me back to her office afterward.

“I did not expect that,” she says.

“Neither did I.”

“You have a hold on them.”

“No,” I reply. “Sammy does.”

She considers that.

“Then perhaps you are holding the rope until he returns.”

The sentence stays with me. Holding the rope.

When I leave the institute, the afternoon sun has begun to lean westward. Students linger near the gate, copying my number from Tunde’s notebook, arguing over whether “Sammy Briggs Team” sounds too ordinary.

I should smile. But my mind has already moved ahead. To Sammy’s request. His foster father. His dream of blessing. His fear of death.

The journey before me suddenly feels larger than finding an address. I am not merely searching for a man. I am searching for the missing root of Sammy’s life. The father whose blessing he believes he needs before darkness finishes its work.

As I walk away from Destiny Institute, I understand that Sammy’s assignment has placed assignments on all of us. The students have to keep hope alive. And somehow, somewhere, I have to find the father of a broken son who is trying desperately to come home before it is too late.





























THREE





The suitcase remains open on my bed long after I return from Destiny Institute. I stand before it without touching anything.

The clothes are neatly folded, arranged with the hopeful care of a woman who expected rest. A floral dress lies on top. Beneath it are two casual tops, a scarf Judith insisted I pack, a pair of sandals still wrapped in tissue paper, and a small bottle of perfume I bought because holidays, I told myself, deserve softness.

I stare at them until they begin to look like belongings from another woman’s life. That woman was supposed to be on her way to Abuja.

She was supposed to sit beside Judith in a bus, laughing at roadside drama, complaining about delayed departure, buying roasted plantain at a stopover, and arriving tired but light-hearted. She was supposed to spend Christmas in a house where nobody whispered about cultists, police stations, missing girls, secret Programs, or young men marked by forces they once served.

She was supposed to rest. I almost envy her. The woman I might have been if Sammy had never crossed my life. If Ikenna had not died. If Bitty had not carried guilt like a second shadow. If the name Jshap had remained only a testimony from fellowship, and not the doorway into a history of blood, prophecy, redemption, and unfinished assignments.

I sit on the edge of the bed. The suitcase stares back at me.

I think of Sammy behind that metal table at the police station, his eyes tired but strangely peaceful, as though he had already begun preparing himself for death while I was still arguing with life.

His final request echoes in my subconscious, “Find Rakiya. Find my father… Help me reconcile before death finds me.”

I reach for the floral dress and lift it out. For a moment, I press it against my chest. Then I hang it back in the wardrobe. One by one, I unpack. Each item returns to its place with the heaviness of surrender.

It is strange how sacrifice sometimes looks ordinary, without a drame. Just a woman taking clothes out of a suitcase because a friend is in trouble and she cannot pretend she does not know.

When the suitcase is empty, I close it and push it beneath the bed. I have a flash of illusion that I can remain uninvolved.

I leave the house before evening and go to Judith’s. She opens the door with excitement already forming on her face.

“Madam traveler!”

Then she sees me properly. Her smile fades.

“What happened?”

I step in without answering.

Her sitting room smells of air freshener. A Christmas garland hangs above the television. Two travel bags stand near the sofa, already packed. Judith has always been more organized than she admits.

“I’m not travelling again,” I say.

For several seconds, she stares at me. Then she laughs once. Not because she finds it funny. Because she thinks I cannot possibly be serious.

“Lizzy, please don’t start.”

“I’m serious.”

“You cannot be serious.”

“I am.”

She folds her arms. “Because of Sammy.”

I do not answer.

“That silence is your confession.”

I sit down.

Judith remains standing. “So you are cancelling your holiday because of a man who was arrested for abducting a girl?”

“It is a frame-up.”

“You keep saying that.”

“Because it is true.”

“How do you know?”

“Because I know what happened.”

“Do you?” she snaps.

The sharpness in her voice surprises me. “Lizzy, do you really know? Or do you know the part Sammy told you with tears in his eyes?”

I look away.

She comes closer. “I am not trying to be wicked. I am trying to keep you alive. Do you understand the kind of people you keep attaching yourself to?”

“I am not attached to them.”

“You are.”

“No.”

“Yes, you are. First it was Jehoshaphat.”

My chest tightens. “Do not bring him into this.”

“I will bring him into it because you never left that story. Jehoshaphat died, and somehow his death became a ministry in your life. Then Bitty came with his guilt and escape. Now Sammy. Three men connected to the same dark fraternity, and you keep standing near their fire as if smoke cannot stain you.”

Her words hurt because they are not careless. They are observations sharpened by concern.

“Ikenna was different,” I say quietly.

“Was he?”

“Yes.”

“And Bitty?”

“He needed help.”

“And Sammy?”

“He needs help too.”

Judith exhales sharply. “Everybody needs help, Lizzy. That does not mean God appointed you to carry every broken man with a cult history on your back.”

I feel tears pressing behind my eyes but refuse them. “This is not about broken men.”

“Then what is it about?”

I open my mouth. No answer comes quickly. Because the truth is not simple. It is not affection alone. IT’s not pity, stubbornness nor guilt. Something began with Jshap that I still do not fully understand.

His life, his death, his writings, his testimony, the strange way his choices continued moving people after he was gone. It had opened a door in me. I had tried to close it many times. I had told myself to return to ordinary concerns, ordinary friendships, ordinary ambitions. But something would not let go. Something in me would not give up on Jshap. Not the man alone. The meaning of him.

The proof that darkness could lose a soul it thought it owned. The proof that grace could enter a fraternity and overturn its symbols. The proof that a life surrendered to God could trouble systems built on fear even after death. And now Sammy stands at the edge of that same mystery.

Not as Jshap, not as a replacement. But as another witness that God is harvesting men from the very places the enemy thought he had secured.

“I don’t know how to explain it,” I say finally.

Judith softens slightly, but only slightly. “Try.”

I look at her.

“When Ikenna died, I thought it was the end of a tragic story. But it wasn’t. His death kept speaking. Bitty was changed by it. Sammy was changed by it. Even I was changed by it.”

Judith sits slowly.

“I don’t think this is just about helping Sammy. I think there is something God is doing through these people, through their redemption, their pain, their unfinished stories. I don’t understand it fully, but every time I try to step away, something pulls me back.”

“That sounds dangerous.”

“It is.”

“Then why are you calm?”

“I am not calm.” My voice breaks. “I am terrified.”

That silences her. The tears finally come.

“I am tired, Judith. I am tired of police stations. Tired of cult names. Tired of watching young men destroy themselves. Tired of hearing secrets that make sleep difficult. Tired of being the one people call when everything is falling apart.”

Judith’s face changes.

“I am tired of being brave,” I whisper.

She moves closer and sits beside me. For a while, neither of us speaks.

Then she says softly, “Then let someone else go.”

“I can’t.”

“Why?”

“Because Sammy has no one else.”

“He has God.”

“Yes. And sometimes God sends people.”

Judith wipes her face with one hand, frustrated and emotional at once.

“You always know how to make selfishness sound like wisdom if one disagrees with you.”

A faint laugh escapes me through tears. “I am not asking you to understand everything.”

“I don’t.”

“I know.”

“I don’t like this,” she confesses.

“I know.”

“I think it is dangerous.”

“It is.”

“I think you should inform someone older. A pastor. A lawyer. Someone with sense and gray hair.”

“I will.”

“You promise?”

“Yes.”

She studies me.

“And you are still not travelling.”

“No.”

She leans back and closes her eyes. “My mother will ask questions.”

“Tell her I’m sorry.”

“She will say you have started your drama again.”

“She may be right.”

Judith looks at me, and despite everything, we both smile faintly.

Then the smile fades. 

“What will you do now?” she asks.

I shake my head. “I don’t know.”

That is the most honest thing I have said all day. 

I do not know how to find Sammy’s foster father. I do not know where to begin looking for Rakiya. I do not know whether the fraternity is watching me. I do not know whether Steel is warning me, manipulating me, or both. I do not know whether Sammy will still be alive by the time I find the man whose blessing he wants.

I only know that I cannot travel to Abuja and pretend I have chosen peace.

Judith sees me off on my way home, and warns me again to be careful. I return home and kneel beside my bed. The room is quiet. 

I open my Bible but cannot read. Words blur. My mind keeps moving between faces. Jehoshaphat. His name still hurts.

I did not choose this road. Or maybe  I chose it each time I refused to walk away. My friends had warned me. Judith had warned me.

Others had hinted, scolded, questioned, and withdrawn. They wondered why my life kept circling the fraternity, why I remained emotionally tied to stories that should have frightened any sensible woman away.

Maybe they were right to worry. Maybe I am foolish. Maybe compassion can become recklessness when it refuses counsel. But there is something beneath my decisions that feels larger than pity. A thread I cannot cut. A burden I do not know how to drop.

I lower my head.

“Lord,” I whisper. The word comes out broken. “Please direct me.”

Silence.

I press my forehead to the edge of the bed. “I don’t know what to do.”

That confession opens the tears fully.

“I don’t know where to start. I don’t know who to trust. I don’t know how to find Rakiya. I don’t know how to find Sammy’s father. I don’t know how to stand against Otunba or the GODs or whatever Program they are building.”

My voice trembles. “But You know.”

The room remains still.

“Please save Sammy from their plot. If there is still mercy, let it speak for him. If there is still time, help me use it well. If this burden is from You, give me strength. If I am being foolish, correct me. But do not let fear make me abandon what You have placed in my hands.”

I weep quietly. The tired weeping of someone surrendering the illusion of control. After a while, my breathing steadies.

But a strange calm settles slowly over me. Not an answer. Not yet. More like a hand resting lightly on my shoulder. Enough for the night. I rise from the floor and sit on the bed. The next step is still unclear. 

But I know the first thing I have to do in the morning.

I will begin with Sammy’s past. His foster parents. The father whose blessing he wants before death finds him. Sometimes direction does not begin as a road. Sometimes it begins as a name you have not yet found.



















































FOUR





The thought of Reverend Obiajulu comes to me in the quiet hour before dawn. Not as a vivid vision. In the way some answers come after prayer, softly, almost ordinarily, as though the mind has been walking around a locked door all night and suddenly remembers where the key was kept.

Jshap’s father. Rev. Obiajulu. I sit up slowly.

The room is still dim. Morning has not fully broken through the curtains. For a while, I remain on the bed, holding the thought carefully, afraid it might dissolve if I move too quickly.

Sammy’s foster father had been an Anglican priest. That is all I have. A priest. A father. A man somewhere in the past who had loved a wounded boy Sammy is now desperate to find.

Where does one begin looking for an old Anglican priest whose name is not yet certain, whose parish history is unknown, and whose adopted son ran away years ago? The answer stands quietly before me.

Start with another priest.

Start with the father of the man whose life has somehow become the hinge upon which all our stories now turn.

“Rev. Joseph Obiajulu. Jshap’s father.” I whisper the name and feel a strange stirring in my chest.

It is not the first time I have thought of the Obiajulu family. Since Jshap’s death, their name has carried a weight in my life I cannot fully explain. I had known Ikenna as Jshap, the preacher whose testimony set fire to hearts and offended darkness. I had watched his death scatter grief like seed. I had seen Bitty bend under the weight of guilt. I had watched Sammy lay the Cross upright on his grave and become a hunted man.

But the Obiajulus are not merely Jshap’s family. They are the root from which part of this strange story has grown. And perhaps, without knowing it, I have been walking back toward them all along.

I pick up my phone and scroll to Nkeonyere’s number. For several seconds, I only stare at the screen.

Nkay and I have not spoken in depth for a while. We exchange messages occasionally, warm but careful, the way people do when grief connects them but distance prevents true closeness. She is Jshap’s sister, and though she has always been gracious to me, I still feel a nervousness whenever I have to cross into that family’s space.

Perhaps because I do not know what I am to them. A friend? A sister in Christ? A remnant of Ikenna’s ministry? A girl who keeps appearing in the lives of men connected to the fraternity that took their son?

I breathe in and dial.

She answers on the third ring.

“Lizzy?” Her voice carries surprise, then warmth.

“Nkay, good morning.”

“Good morning. This one you are calling so early, is everything all right?”

I almost laugh. In our world, early morning calls rarely come without a burden.

“I’m sorry. I hope I didn’t wake you.”

“No, not really. I was already awake. How are you?”

“I’m fine.”

There is a small pause.

Nkay does not believe me.

“Lizzy.”

I close my eyes briefly. “I’m trying to be fine.”

“That sounds more honest.”

Her gentleness nearly undoes me.

“How are Mummy and Daddy?” I ask.

“They are well. Daddy is still Daddy. Wake up, pray, counsel somebody, forget breakfast, and then wonder why everyone is worried about him.”

A smile touches my lips. “That sounds like him.”

“And Mummy is still managing all of us. She has already started planning Christmas as if the whole church is coming to the house.”

“That must be beautiful.”

“It is usually chaotic before it becomes beautiful.”

We both laugh softly. Then the silence returns, waiting.

“Nkay,” I say, “is it okay for me to visit the family house this holiday?”

Her response comes quickly. “Visit? Or spend Christmas with us?”

I hesitate.

“Something like that.”

“Oh, Lizzy, that would be amazing.”

The ease of her answer surprises me.

“You’re sure?”

“Why are you asking as if you are a stranger?”

I do not know what to say.

Nkay continues, softer now. “Lizzy, you should know this by now. In this family, anyone who loved Ikenna sincerely is not treated like an outsider.”

My throat tightens.

“I don’t know if I can claim that.”

“You can.”

Her voice becomes firmer. “You stood with the testimony he left behind. You were there when others ran from the discomfort of his story. You helped Sammy when his own people were ready to swallow him. Daddy knows these things. Mummy knows. Maybe they don’t say much, but they know.”

I look down at my hand. “I sometimes feel I keep bringing trouble close to your family.”

“No,” Nkay says. “Trouble came before you. You only keep refusing to pretend you don’t see it.”

The words settle deeply.

“Besides,” she adds, “Daddy has said more than once that God sometimes sends people into a family through grief, and they remain through purpose.”

I swallow.

“Did he say that about me?”

Nkay laughs gently. “Must everything be quoted with footnotes?”

“Nkay.”

“Yes. He was speaking about you.”

For a moment, I cannot answer.

The fear I have carried about entering the Obiajulu home loosens slightly. I had imagined myself as an intruder into their grief, but perhaps they had already made room for me in ways I did not understand.

“When do you plan to come?” she asks.

“Next tomorrow.”

“That’s fine. I’ll let Mummy know. My family will also be around after Christmas, so you will not be lonely.”

“I don’t want to inconvenience anyone.”

“You won’t. They will be happy to have you.”

“Are you sure?”

“Lizzy,” she says, mock stern now, “if you ask that question again, I’ll tell Mummy you are doubting her hospitality.”

I smile.

“Please don’t.”

“Good. Then it is settled. Come.”

“Thank you, Nkay.”

“Don’t thank me. Just come with appetite. Mummy will feed you whether you are hungry or not.”

The call ends with a warmth I did not know I needed. For several minutes, I remain seated, the phone still in my hand. I had called seeking access. I have received welcome. And beneath that welcome, something else stirs, an affinity I have not known how to name.

The Obiajulus are not only connected to Jshap by blood. They are connected to the work his life started. And somehow, through Sammy, Bitty, Rakiya, and the students, I am being drawn further into that work.

Later that morning, I call Bitty. 

His voice carries concern before I finish greeting him. “Lizzy, what happened?”

I tell him everything. Sammy’s arrest. The charges. Rakiya’s disappearance. The possibility that Otunba’s petition has opened the door for a larger frame-up.

Bitty listens without interrupting. When I finish, his voice is low.

“It is false.”

“I know.”

“No, Lizzy. I mean the charge itself is too convenient. They could not hold him easily with Otunba’s money alone, especially if they cannot prove he received it. But if they attach a minor to the story, they poison public sympathy before the truth even speaks.”

The thought chills me.

“So what do I do?”

“Have you reached anyone who can help? A lawyer? Someone in the church? Anyone with influence?”

“I don’t know where to begin.”

My voice weakens.

“I prayed. I have assurance that God will save him as He has done before, but I don’t know the steps.”

“What did Sammy ask you to do?”

“He wants me to help find his foster father.”

There is a brief silence.

“His foster father?”

“Yes. He said the man was an Anglican priest. He had a dream of him blessing him. Bitty, he thinks he is going to die.”

Bitty exhales. “Sammy and death.”

“He was calm when he said it.”

“That is what worries me.”

“I’m going to Jshap’s family house for the holiday,” I say. “I think Rev. Obiajulu can help me trace the church connection.”

“Yes,” Bitty replies quickly. “That makes sense. Anglican networks are usually traceable if you know where to ask. Parishes, dioceses, retired clergy, transfer history. Reverend Obiajulu may know how to start.”

“That is what I thought.”

“It is a good thought.”

The phone line goes quiet for a moment.

Then Bitty says, “I am sorry my friend dragged you into all this.”

“He didn’t drag me.”

“He did not plan to. That does not mean you are not entangled.”

I look away. “It’s okay, Bitty. The Lord is with me. He will guide me.”

“I’ll send you some money.”

“No. Don’t bother.”

“I’m sending it.”

“Bitty, please—”

“You can return it if you find somewhere to return it to.”

Despite everything, I almost smile.

“You are stubborn.”

“You are the stubborn one. You want to fight cultists, politicians, police petitions, missing persons, and spiritual mysteries with transport fare and faith alone.”

“Faith is enough.”

“Faith is not an excuse to reject help.”

I become quiet.

His voice softens. “We are in this together, okay? Don’t forget that.”

The words touch a place in me already tired of standing alone.

“Thank you.”

“And Lizzy?”

“Yes?”

“You sound troubled. More than what you have told me.”

“There is nothing.”

“Don’t do that.”

“Do what?”

“Lie badly.”

I close my eyes.

Bitty knows grief too well to be easily deceived by silence.

“Tell me.”

My lips tremble before the words come.

“It’s the children.”

“What children?”

“The students Sammy was caring for at the institute.”

The tears come suddenly, before I can prepare dignity for them.

“I saw them, Bitty. They are devastated. They were trying to be brave, but you could see it. Sammy became something to them. A teacher, mentor, father, pastor, I don’t even know. He saw them. He changed them.”

I wipe my face, but the tears keep coming.

“I don’t know how they will cope. I don’t know if anyone will care for them the way he did. I don’t know what happens to children when the one person helping them stand is taken away.”

Bitty is quiet for a while.

When he speaks, his voice is gentle. “Send me the proprietor’s contact.”

I sniff.

“Why?”

“I’ll send provisions to the institute. Food items, supplies, maybe some support for the students during the holiday. We will work out something more stable when you return from your trip.”

“Bitty…”

“Lizzy, let me do this.”

I press the phone to my ear and bow my head.

“Thank you.”

“No. Thank you for not walking away from them.”

I cannot answer.

He continues, “Sammy’s work there must not die because he is in custody. If the enemy cannot destroy the seed, he will try to scatter the soil around it. We won’t allow that.”

Those words steady me.

“I’ll send the contact.”

“Good. And Lizzy?”

“Yes?”

“Be careful at the Obiajulu house.”

I frown.

“Why?”

“Not because they are dangerous. Because that family carries more history than you know.”

The statement lingers.

“What does that mean?”

“It means listen more than you speak. There are things around Ikenna, his father, and the old networks that may help you. Don’t rush. Let the house speak.”

Let the house speak. I remember Nkay’s words. “Anyone who loved Ikenna sincerely is not treated like an outsider.”

Perhaps the journey to Onitsha is not only about finding Sammy’s foster father. Perhaps I am being taken to the family that has unknowingly been waiting with pieces of the map.

When the call ends, I sit quietly.

The day has only begun, but already the road ahead feels clearer. Not easy or safe, but clearer.

I will go to the Obiajulu house. I will meet Rev. Joseph Obiajulu not merely as Jshap’s grieving father, but as a priest, a witness, and perhaps the first living clue to Sammy’s lost home. I will carry Sammy’s request.

For the first time since Sammy’s arrest, I do not feel as though I am carrying them alone. The Obiajulu door is open. Bitty is standing with me.

And somewhere within that fragile network of welcome, grief, faith, and unfinished stories, I sense that God has already begun arranging the next step before I know enough to ask for it.

















































FIVE





I arrive in Onitsha just before sunset. The city announces itself long before the bus rolls into town. The traffic thickens gradually until the road becomes a slow-moving river of buses, tricycles, motorcycles, delivery vans, and impatient motorists competing for every available space. 

Street traders emerge from every direction, balancing impossible loads upon their heads while weaving effortlessly between vehicles locked in stubborn gridlock.

Above the chaos hang giant billboards advertising churches, pharmaceuticals, electronics, schools, and politicians whose smiling faces gaze down upon the restless city like silent spectators.

This is not the quiet university town Sammy described in his letters. This is Onitsha. A city that seems permanently awake. A city where commerce has become a language. A city where movement itself feels like an occupation.

The driver turns off the main road and enters older neighbourhoods where the noise softens and the streets narrow. Here, houses stand closer together, separated by low fences and familiar greetings exchanged between neighbours who have known one another for decades.

The closer we come to Okosi Street, the more the city seems to shed its commercial aggression and retreat into memory. Towering mango trees lean over the road, their branches meeting above the street like an ancient cathedral ceiling woven from leaves and fading sunlight.

Children play football in dusty compounds. Women sit beneath verandas, peeling vegetables and discussing family matters loud enough for the entire street to hear. Radio broadcasts drift from open windows. 

The taxi finally slows before a modest cream-coloured bungalow partially concealed behind a weathered concrete fence. I know immediately that this is Rev. Obiajulu’s house.

Not because of the address. But because the house looks exactly like the man. Simple, orderly, unpretentious.

A narrow pedestrian gate stands beside a wider iron gate wearing a green paint. Bougainvillea flowers spill over sections of the fence, softening its severity with bursts of crimson and violet.

Beyond the gate sits a four-bedroom bungalow built in a style popular decades ago. Its long front veranda stretches across the width of the building, supported by square concrete pillars painted white.

The roof is covered with aging corrugated sheets darkened by time. The walls bear faint stains left behind by countless rainy seasons. Nothing about the house suggests wealth. Everything about it suggests sacrifice.

A concrete pathway divides a modest front lawn where hibiscus shrubs, crotons, and flowering plants compete for space beneath the shade of two mature mango trees. Near one corner of the compound stands an old water tank mounted on a rusting steel tower.

The house stands before me not as a building, but as a testimony. A monument to faith. A witness to loss. And perhaps, hidden somewhere within its aging walls, the answers Sammy has sent me to find.

I find myself staring at the veranda. At the empty wooden chair near the entrance. At the Bible resting on a small side table. At the pair of worn leather sandals carefully positioned beneath it. 

Before I can knock, the front door opens.

Mrs. Obiajulu appears on the veranda, wiping her hands on the edge of her wrapper as though she has hurried out from the kitchen. She is not tall, but she carries herself with the quiet dignity of a woman who has spent years holding a household together through prayer, discipline, and grief. Her face is soft without being weak. Her eyes are bright, searching, and kind in a way that makes one feel seen before one is questioned.

For a moment, she only looks at me. Then her face opens into a smile.

“Elizabeth?”

“Yes, ma.”

“My daughter, you are welcome.” The words undo something in me.

Not because they are unusual. In Nigeria, elders welcome visitors generously.

But there is something about the way she says *my daughter* that enters the tired part of me and sits there.

I bend slightly and greet her properly.

“Good evening, ma.”

“Good evening, my child. How was the journey?”

“It was fine, ma. A little stressful because of the traffic.”

She shakes her head with the mild resignation of someone who has made peace with Onitsha’s stubbornness. “Onitsha traffic does not respect anybody. Come in, come in.”

Before I can lift my bag, she steps down from the veranda and reaches for it.

“No, ma, please,” I protest quickly. “I can carry it.”

She smiles.

“You are already in this house. Let us do our part.”

Another woman comes out from the side of the house, younger, probably a relative or neighbour helping in the kitchen. She takes the smaller bag from me and greets me warmly. From inside the sitting room, I hear voices. Children. A man’s low laughter. The clatter of plates.

The house is not waiting for me like a stranger’s house. It is alive. I suddenly remember the nylon bag in my hand.

“Ma, I brought these,” I say, holding it out.

Mrs. Obiajulu pauses.

Inside the bag are loaves of bread, a tin of beverage, and a small packet of biscuits I bought along the way. The bread is the important thing. One does not visit a Nigerian home for the first time, especially the home of elders, empty-handed. Bread may be simple, but it carries a language of respect. It says: I was raised to know that a visitor must not arrive with only a mouth to eat.

Mrs. Obiajulu looks into the bag and then looks back at me.

“Ah, Elizabeth.” Her voice softens. “You should not have bothered yourself.”

“It is nothing, ma.”

“It is not nothing.” She takes the bag with both hands. “It shows home training.”

I feel heat rise to my face.

“My mother would not forgive me if I came empty-handed.”

“Then your mother raised you well,” she says.

Something in her expression changes slightly, as though my small gift has crossed a quiet threshold I did not know existed.

She turns toward the sitting room. 

“Papa, Elizabeth is here!”

A chair shifts inside.

Rev. Obiajulu appears at the doorway a moment later, calm and spare in a simple white shirt and dark trousers. His hair is greying at the temples, and his face carries that peculiar mixture of sternness and gentleness common to old priests who have buried too many sorrows but still believe mercy is stronger.

He looks at me for a second too long. Not rudely. As though he is comparing the person before him with things he has heard, prayed over, or carried silently. Then he smiles.

“Elizabeth.”

“Good evening, sir.”

“You are welcome, my daughter.”

Again. My daughter.

I bow slightly. “Thank you, sir.”

“You had a safe journey?”

“Yes, sir.”

“Good. We thank God.”

Mrs. Obiajulu leads me into the sitting room.

The room is modest and orderly, bearing the kind of careful arrangement that belongs to women who clean not to impress visitors but because order is part of peace. A wooden centre table sits before a set of older but well-kept chairs. Family photographs line one wall. A framed Bible verse hangs above the doorway leading to the corridor. The air smells of stew, floor polish, and old books.

My eyes move before I can stop them. I find Ikenna almost immediately.

A framed photograph rests on the wall among the others. He looks younger there. Serious. Handsome in that intense, inward way that made Jshap seem older than his age whenever he spoke. My breath catches quietly.

Mrs. Obiajulu notices.

“That was taken before he left home,” she says softly.

I do not know whether to respond as Lizzy the guest or Lizzy the one still carrying pieces of his story.

So I only nod. “He looked peaceful,” I say.

Rev. Obiajulu’s face changes, but he says nothing.

A little girl peeps from behind a curtain and disappears when I smile at her. Someone laughs in the inner room. The younger woman who took my bag returns and tells Mrs. Obiajulu that the water is ready.

“You must bathe first,” Mrs. Obiajulu says. “Then you will eat.”

“Ma, I am fine.”

“You are not fine until you have bathed and eaten in this house.”

There is no room for argument.

I smile. “Yes, ma.”

She takes me through the corridor.

The house seems larger inside than it appears from outside. The corridor is narrow, cooled by old cement floors and the lingering scent of camphor from wardrobes. We pass a room with stacked hymn books and church files, another with children’s clothes folded on a bed, and then she stops before a door near the end of the passage.

Her hand rests on the handle for a brief moment. “This would have been Ikenna’s room,” she says.

The sentence stills me. Would have been. Not was. Not is. Would have been. As though the room itself had waited for a future that never returned.

She opens the door.

The room is simple. A modest bed stands near one wall, neatly made with a clean sheet and a folded wrapper placed at the foot of it. A wooden wardrobe leans against another wall, polished by years of careful use. An old reading table sits beside the window. On the wall, another framed photograph of Ikenna rests above the table.

This one is different. More personal and less formal. His smile is faint, almost reluctant, as if someone forced him to stand still for the camera.

Mrs. Obiajulu walks in and touches the edge of the table, almost absentmindedly.

“He liked to sit there,” she says. “Always reading. Always writing something. Sometimes arguing with books as if the authors were in the room.”

A small laugh escapes her, but it carries grief beneath it.

I stand at the doorway, unsure whether to enter fully.

“You can come in,” she says gently. “The room is ready for you.”

I step inside. I suddenly have a feeling of entering a story that has already known my name before I arrived.

“I hope you will be comfortable here,” she says.

“Yes, ma. Thank you.”

She studies me for a moment.

“I know it may feel strange.”

I look at her.

“To stay in his room?”

“Yes.”

“It does,” I admit.

“But not bad?”

“No, ma.” I look at the photograph again. “Not bad.”

She nods slowly.

“I prayed before preparing it. I did not want to put you here because of sentiment. But somehow, I felt it was right.”

Her words settle softly inside me.

“I am grateful.”

“You are not a stranger here, Elizabeth.”

I turn to her.

She smiles, but her eyes are wet.

“Anyone who loved what God did through my son is welcome in this house.”

For a moment, I cannot speak.

Since Jshap’s death, I have carried his memory like something borrowed, something I had no right to claim too closely. But here, in his father’s house, his mother speaks as though I have not trespassed into their grief. As though I have been expected. As though the road that brought me here had been prepared by hands I did not see.

“I don’t know what to say, ma.”

“You do not have to say anything.” She touches my shoulder lightly. “Bathe. Rest a little. Food will soon be ready.”

She turns to leave, then pauses at the door.

“Do you still eat garri?”

The question is so ordinary that it rescues me.

I laugh softly. “Yes, ma.”

“Good. Then you are truly at home.”

When she leaves, I stand alone in the room. The fading light outside leans against the window.

Somewhere in the compound, someone draws water into a bucket. A child laughs, then is quickly hushed. From the kitchen comes the sharp rhythm of a knife against a board.

I place my bag beside the bed and sit slowly. Jshap’s photograph watches from the wall. For a long moment, I do not move.

I thought I had come to the Obiajulu home as a visitor. A guest. A woman on an errand for Sammy. But sitting in this room, surrounded by the quiet memory of a man whose life and death changed mine, I feel something I cannot explain.

As if I have been folded gently into a family that grief had made larger. As if I am not merely being hosted. As if I belong here in some strange, undeserved way. That feeling frightens me a little.

Mrs. Obiajulu feeds me as if she has been waiting for the opportunity. Rice. Stew. Fried plantain. A small portion of vegetable soup she insists I must taste because, according to her, “travelling removes strength from the body.” Rev. Obiajulu asks about my journey, my family, and my studies. 

After the meal, we sit briefly in the parlour while Mrs. Obiajulu speaks about Christmas preparations and the relatives expected after the holiday. Rev. Obiajulu listens more than he speaks. Sometimes his eyes move to Ikenna’s photograph on the wall, and each time they do, I feel the room remember.

After dinner, the house settles slowly into sleep. Later, when I retire to the room, I do not sleep immediately. I sit at Ikenna’s old reading table and run my fingers lightly over the surface. There are faint scratch marks on the wood. I remember things Ikenna told me about his parents.

How morning devotion was not a casual habit in their house but a structure around which the day arranged itself. How his father prayed as though heaven were a courtroom and every petition required both reverence and argument.

How his mother sang hymns while cooking, sometimes repeating one line until the whole house seemed to breathe it.

He had told me these things lightly, almost humorously, but I now understand that he had been describing the atmosphere that formed him.































































SIX





For the first time in many nights, my dreams do not chase me. Dawn comes gently. Not with Lagos noise. Not with the irritation of horns, impatient buses, or people quarrelling before the sun has fully risen.

It comes with a rooster crowing somewhere behind the house, the rustle of leaves against the window, the distant sound of someone sweeping a compound. For a few seconds after waking, I do not remember where I am.

Then the room gathers itself around me: the modest bed, the folded wrapper at the foot of it, the wooden wardrobe, the old reading table beside the window, and Ikenna’s photograph on the wall.

The realization carries both comfort and ache. It is the most peaceful sleep I have had in a long while. No early morning lecture. No anxiety dragging me awake before dawn. Only stillness, softened by the sounds of a waking neighbourhood.

Then comes a gentle knock. Measured. Almost familiar.

“Elizabeth,” Mrs. Obiajulu calls softly from the other side of the door, “please come and join us for prayer.”

Her voice holds no pressure.

I sit up and smile despite myself. Of course. What else do I expect? Ikenna had warned me in one of our conversations. In our house, morning does not begin until my father has spoken to God about it. I had laughed when he said it. He had smiled too, but there was affection beneath the complaint.

Now, hearing Mrs. Obiajulu at the door, I understand him better.

When one chooses to spend Christmas in the home of a clergy family, especially one known for piety and genuine devotion, one must prepare for early morning prayers, long Bible conversations, hymns before breakfast, and perhaps a few spiritual questions that touch places one would rather leave untouched.

I had prepared myself before coming. For Sammy’s sake, I had told myself, I would be patient. And following Bitty’s advise, I would listen. I would endure. I would pretend not to be tired when the discussions became too theological.

But as I sit there in Ikenna’s room, wrapped in the quiet of a house that has already begun its day with God, I realize something. I am not reluctant. Not exactly. A part of me wants the prayers.

Perhaps because I have come into this house carrying burdens too heavy for ordinary conversation: 

“Yes, ma,” I answer softly. “I’m coming.”

The sitting room is already arranged. Rev. Obiajulu sits an armchair near the window, his Bible open on his lap. Mrs. Obiajulu sits beside him, a hymn book resting on her knees. The woman who helped in the kitchen last night sits on a wooden chair near the doorway, her wrapper tied firmly around her waist, her hair covered with a faded scarf.

A small centre table stands between us. On it are two Bibles, a notebook, Rev. Obiajulu’s reading glasses, and a Concordance whose edges have grown brown from use.

“Good morning, my daughter,” Rev. Obiajulu greets.

His voice is deep but gentle, the kind of voice that does not struggle to command attention.

“Good morning, sir,” I reply. “Good morning, ma.”

Mrs. Obiajulu smiles at me.

“You slept well?”

“Yes, ma. Very well.”

“Good,” she says. “Then Onitsha has not frightened you yet.”

The woman near the doorway chuckles softly. “Onitsha waits small before it frightens visitor.”

We all laugh. The laughter helps me settle.

Rev. Obiajulu closes his eyes briefly and begins with a short prayer.

“Father, we thank You for the gift of a new day. We thank You for mercy through the night, for the breath in our nostrils, for the roof over our heads, and for the grace that has brought us together around Your Word. Search us by Your truth. Correct what is crooked. Heal what is wounded. Align our hearts with the mind of Christ. Through Jesus Christ our Lord.”

“Amen,” we answer.

When he opens his eyes, he turns to me.

“Elizabeth, please read Mark chapter eight, from verse thirty-one to verse thirty-three.”

I open my Bible. My voice is still soft from sleep, but the room is quiet enough to carry it.

“And He began to teach them, that the Son of man must suffer many things, and be rejected of the elders, and of the chief priests, and scribes, and be killed, and after three days rise again.

“And He spake that saying openly. And Peter took Him, and began to rebuke Him.

“But when He had turned about and looked on His disciples, He rebuked Peter, saying, Get thee behind me, Satan: for thou savourest not the things that be of God, but the things that be of men.”

The room remains silent for a moment.

Rev. Obiajulu rests his hand on the open page of his Bible. “This passage is short,” he says, “but it can expose the very essence of a man.”

Mrs. Obiajulu nods.

“It is a hard passage.” “Yes,” he says. “Very hard. Especially because Peter does not look like an enemy here.”

He looks at me. “Elizabeth, when you read this passage, what do you first notice about Peter?”

I hesitate. “That he rebuked Jesus.”

“Yes. That is the obvious thing. But before he rebuked Jesus, what do you think was happening inside him?”

I think for a moment. “He did not want Jesus to suffer.”

“Good.”

He turns to the woman by the doorway. “What do you think, Ngozi?”

The woman shifts slightly, surprised to be included. “Sir, maybe he loved Him too much to accept what He was saying.”

Rev. Obiajulu smiles.

“That is a very honest answer. Peter loved Him. At least, Peter believed he loved Him. And because of that love, he could not accept a Messiah who must suffer and be killed.”

Mrs. Obiajulu adds quietly, “He wanted victory, but not the Cross.”

Rev. Obiajulu points gently toward her.

“That is it. Peter wanted glory, but not surrender. He wanted triumph, but not sacrifice. He wanted the Kingdom, but not the road by which the King must enter it.” He pauses.

I feel the words move quietly through me. The Kingdom. The Cross. Sacrifice. Sammy comes to my mind before I can stop him.

Rev. Obiajulu continues. “We must be careful not to judge Peter too quickly. If many of us were there, we might have done the same thing. If someone you love tells you, ‘I will suffer, I will be rejected, I will be killed,’ your first instinct will not be theology. Your first instinct will be fear.”

“That is true,” Mrs. Obiajulu murmurs.

“Peter’s mistake is not that he feels pain at the thought of Christ suffering. His mistake is that he allows his feeling to become counsel against the will of God.”

The sentence lands with quiet force.

Rev. Obiajulu picks up his glasses but does not wear them yet.

“That is why Jesus does not merely correct the words. He addresses the spirit behind the words. He says, ‘Get thee behind me, Satan.’ Now, we must understand this in agreement with the teaching of the Church. Jesus is not saying Peter has become satan in his nature. Peter remains Peter. A disciple. A man loved by Christ. But in that moment, Peter’s thought becomes useful to the adversary because it stands against the Father’s purpose.”

Ngozi lifts her head.

“So, sir, somebody can mean well and still speak against God?”

Rev. Obiajulu’s eyes brighten slightly. “Yes. That is one of the frightening lessons here.”

Mrs. Obiajulu sighs. “Good intention is not always obedience.”

“Exactly,” he says. “That is why Christians must not only ask, ‘Do I mean well?’ We must ask, ‘Is my mind aligned with the mind of God?’”

He opens the worn Concordance on the table and turns a few pages with deliberate care.

“In the King James Version, Jesus says, ‘thou savourest not the things that be of God.’ That word, savourest, is not a word we use every day now. But it is a rich word. It carries the idea of taste, affection, concern, inclination, appetite. What do you relish? What do you lean toward? What does your mind keep returning to?”

He taps the page lightly. “The Greek word is phroneō. It means to exercise the mind, to be mentally disposed in a direction, to set one’s affection or concern upon something, to regard, to be minded toward it.”

He looks at us over the top of his glasses.

“So Jesus is not simply saying, ‘Peter, you made a bad comment.’ He is saying, ‘Peter, your mind is facing the wrong direction.’”

My breath slows. 

Rev. Obiajulu leans back. “Now let us bring it home. It is possible to be around Jesus and still not savour what Jesus savours. It is possible to attend church and still have the mind governed by the things of men. It is possible to preach, sing, fast, give offerings, attend vigils, lead committees, organize programmes, help the poor, and still be more interested in self than in Christ.”

Mrs. Obiajulu looks down at her Bible. “That one is serious.”

“It is,” he says. “Because religious activity can hide a divided mind.”

I glance at him. The statement feels personal, though he does not aim it at anyone.

He continues. “Peter was active. Peter was zealous. Peter was bold. If you saw him among the disciples, you would say, ‘This one is serious for God.’ Yet, at that moment, Jesus exposes something beneath the zeal. Peter’s affection is divided. He loves the Master, but he does not yet love the Master’s mission enough to accept the suffering attached to it.”

That is too close. Too near to my own confusion.

Mrs. Obiajulu raises her eyes. “Papa, but Peter continued after that rebuke.”

“Yes,” Rev. Obiajulu says, with a faint smile. “That is one of the most beautiful things about him.”

He turns to me. “Elizabeth, imagine yourself in Peter’s place.”

I stiffen slightly. 

“Imagine that the Lord rebukes you openly. Not privately. In the presence of others. He says your thinking, your concern, your advice, your affection, is standing in the way of God. How would you feel?”

My eyes drop too my Bible. “Embarrassed.”

“Yes. 

“Wounded, maybe ashamed.”

“Good. And what else?”

I hesitate.
