“Misunderstood.”

Rev. Obiajulu nods slowly.

“That is honest.”

Ngozi speaks from the doorway. “I may not come back the next day.”

Mrs. Obiajulu turns to her with a small smile. “At least you are saying the truth.”

We laugh softly, but the laughter fades quickly.

Rev. Obiajulu points gently toward Ngozi.

“That is the human reaction. Many people do not leave God because they were never touched by truth. They leave because truth wounded their pride. Correction came, and instead of receiving it, they gathered their dignity and walked away.”

He pauses.

“But Peter stayed.”

The room becomes quiet.

“He continued because, beneath the shame, beneath the embarrassment, beneath the confusion, he knew Jesus was true. That is discipleship. Not that we never think wrongly. Not that we never speak wrongly. Not that we never allow fear to disguise itself as wisdom. But when truth rebukes us, we remain under truth.”

The words stay in the air. When truth rebukes us, we remain under truth.

Mrs. Obiajulu says softly, “That is why humility is not weakness.”

“No,” he agrees. “Humility is the strength to let God be right.”

Something in me loosens and tightens at the same time. I think of Judith. Her warning. Her fear for me. Her plea that I should let someone else go. Was she wrong to worry? No. But could fear use love as its voice? Could caution become disobedience if God had already placed a burden in my hands?

Rev. Obiajulu seems to follow the movement of my thoughts, though I know he cannot. “Let us be careful,” he says. “The things of men are not always evil-looking. Sometimes the things of men are comfort, reputation, personal safety, family approval, public sympathy, financial security, the desire not to suffer, the desire to be understood.”

He glances at his wife. “Sometimes the things of men sound like good advice.”

Mrs. Obiajulu nods. “Like Peter.”

“Like Peter,” he affirms.

Ngozi frowns slightly. “So how do we know when advice is from God or from fear?”

Rev. Obiajulu smiles with approval.

“That is a good question. We test it by Scripture, by prayer, by the character of Christ, and by whether it leads us toward obedience or away from it. God may preserve you from danger, yes. Wisdom is not carelessness. But any counsel that asks you to disobey God in order to preserve yourself must be questioned.”

He lets that settle.

“Jesus did not choose suffering because suffering is good in itself. He chose the will of the Father, and suffering was on that road.”

I lower my eyes. Sammy’s face rises in my mind again. Then Rakiya. Then the students at Destiny Institute. Then Ikenna… Jshap. The man who had carried the Cross until those who loved darkness could no longer tolerate his light.

Rev. Obiajulu continues, his voice quieter now. “Jesus goes on after this passage to say that anyone who will follow Him must deny himself, take up his cross, and follow. That is not a slogan. It is the foundation of Christian life. Self must be denied. Not decorated. Not baptized with religious language. Denied.”

Mrs. Obiajulu says, “Self can hide inside service.”

Rev. Obiajulu looks pleased.

“Yes. Self can preach. Self can sing. Self can give. Self can pray long prayers. Self can even suffer, if the suffering gives it importance.”

That startles me. He notices.

“Yes, my daughter. The flesh is not always outside the church. Sometimes it wears choir uniform.”

Ngozi laughs before she can stop herself, then covers her mouth.

Mrs. Obiajulu smiles.

Rev. Obiajulu smiles too, but his seriousness remains.

“We harbour malice because self wants to feel superior. We refuse forgiveness because self wants the offender to remain beneath us. We seek praise because self wants to be fed. We avoid obedience because self counts the cost and says God is asking too much. We pity ourselves because self wants to be enthroned even in pain.”

He touches the Concordance again.

“That is why Jesus speaks of the mind. Phroneō. What direction has your mind taken? What does it savour? What does it feed on? What does it protect?”

He turns to each of us slowly. “What do you think about when no one is watching?”

No one answers.

“What loss are you unwilling to accept?”

Silence.

“What correction makes you angry?”

Silence again.

“What obedience are you postponing because it may cost you comfort?”

The question finds me too easily.

“These are some of the attributes of the carnally minded, who savour the things of men.” Rev. Obiajulu’s voice softens. “Peter loved Jesus, but his mind still needed conversion.” 

I look up. “Sir,” I say before I can stop myself.

He turns to me. “Yes, Elizabeth.”

“Can a person love God and still resist what God is doing because it does not look like what they expected?”

His eyes hold mine.

“Yes. Many times.” The answer comes without hesitation.

“That is why the renewal of the mind is not a small matter. Love must be trained by truth. Zeal must be governed by revelation. Compassion must be submitted to obedience. Otherwise, we may try to protect what God intends to crucify, and we may oppose what God intends to redeem.”

The room becomes still again.

Mrs. Obiajulu looks at me gently, as if she hears something underneath my question but will not expose it.

Rev. Obiajulu asks, “Why do you ask?”

I take a breath.

“I am thinking about how easy it is to call something wisdom when it is actually fear.”

He nods. “That is very easy.”

“And how easy it is to call something compassion when it is really self-preservation.”

He studies me for a moment.

“That too.”

I stop there. I am not ready to speak about Sammy yet. Not in full. But something in his face tells me he knows a burden has travelled with me into the room.

Rev. Obiajulu closes the Concordance.

“The Christian life is not merely about avoiding sin. It is about learning to desire what God desires. To savour what He savours. To grieve over what grieves Him. To rejoice over what pleases Him. To be concerned with His purpose, even when that purpose crosses our preference.”

He pauses.

The sitting room is still holding the quiet weight of Reverend Obiajulu’s teaching when I lift my eyes from the open Bible on my lap.

Morning light filters through the lace curtains, falling in pale strips across the centre table where several Bibles lie open. Mrs. Obiajulu sits with her hands folded over her wrapper, her face thoughtful. 

Reverend Obiajulu has just finished explaining Peter’s rebuke of Jesus, and for a moment no one speaks.

I look down at the passage again. Then I slowly raise my hand.

“Daddy,” I say softly.

He turns to me with that patient fatherly gaze of his, the kind that does not merely permit a question but welcomes it.

“Yes, Elizabeth.”

I sit straighter, pressing my fingers lightly against the thin page of my Bible.

“I’ve often wondered what gave Peter the courage to literally drag Jesus away and rebuke Him.”

As I speak, I lift my hand and demonstrate with my fingers, almost unconsciously, as though I can see Peter pulling Jesus aside from the disciples, full of human confidence and spiritual ignorance.

“After he himself identified Him as the Christ, the Son of God,” I continue. My voice lowers a little. “And now that you mentioned this, it is clear…” I pause.

The room seems to lean closer.

“Pride.”

Reverend Obiajulu’s expression does not change, but his eyes sharpen with interest.

I look back at the scripture.

“And Jesus knew exactly the source of that pride. That was what He was addressing.” I draw in a quiet breath. “Unknown to Peter, he had been around Jesus, but he was careless with self, and satan crept in.”

The words settle heavily in the room.

Mrs. Obiajulu glances at me, then at her husband. Her fingers tighten slightly around the edge of her wrapper. Reverend Obiajulu remains still, but his silence encourages me to continue.

“And again,” I say, turning the Bible slightly toward myself, “I think there is a deeper lesson for us followers of Jesus that we have overlooked because it is recorded in just two verses.”

I look up now, no longer merely commenting on Peter, but feeling the warning pass through me personally.

“The knowledge of God and closeness to Jesus can make us proud. And that can be a dangerous spiritual pride, which tends to foolishly use the word of God to serve our self-centred cravings and desires, rather than the intents of God.” My voice trembles slightly at the end, not from fear, but from conviction.

The room is quiet.

I continue more slowly. “Peter’s seeming protection of Jesus echoes the second temptation of Satan in the wilderness, suggesting that God will command his angels and they will lift you up in their hands.” 

Reverend Obiajulu tilts his head slightly, his fingers resting against the arm of his chair.

“I think Jesus heard more than what Peter was speaking,” I say. “He heard the same tempter making a bargain contrary to God’s will.” I close the Bible gently, though my hand remains on it. “And He addressed the spirit promptly.”

For a moment, no one says anything.

Then Reverend Obiajulu exhales slowly, not as a man correcting a child, but as a teacher who has just watched a student touch the living nerve of a passage.

“That is very thoughtful, Elizabeth,” he says, his voice low and warm. “Very thoughtful.”

His approval does not make me proud. It makes me afraid of the very thing I have just described.

So, I lower my eyes again and whisper inwardly, Lord, keep me small enough to hear You.

Rev. Obiajulu nods gently and turns to his wife. She looks at me with astonishment.

“That is why Peter’s story gives us hope. He failed here. Later, he denied Jesus. Yet Christ restored him. The man who once resisted the Cross later preached the crucified and risen Christ with boldness. The same Peter who did not understand suffering became a shepherd of suffering believers.”

Mrs. Obiajulu says quietly, “Correction is not rejection.”

“No,” he says. “Correction is preparation.”

That sentence settles deeply inside me. Correction is preparation.

Rev. Obiajulu looks at me again, and this time his gaze is fatherly. “When God corrects the mind, He is often preparing the person for an assignment the old mind cannot carry.”

I feel the words press against the hidden places in me. An assignment the old mind cannot carry. For a moment, I cannot look at him anyone. I think of why I have come.

I had come to Onitsha looking for information. But this morning, in this small sitting room, God seems to begin somewhere else. With my mind. With what I savour. With the fears I call prudence. With the pity I call compassion.

Rev. Obiajulu bows his head.

“Let us pray.”

We all bow our heads.

When the prayer ends, nobody rises immediately.

Morning light has fully entered the room now. It rests on the open Bibles, on Mrs. Obiajulu’s folded hands, on the Concordance, on Rev. Obiajulu’s lined face, and on the quiet woman near the doorway who looks as though she has forgotten the pot she left in the kitchen.

















SEVEN





When Nkeonyere finally arrives, her voice entered the house before her feet cross the door. Full of laughter and movement.

I hear it from the passage while helping Mrs. Obiajulu arrange cups on a tray for brunch. The house has been quiet since morning devotion, wrapped in that calm domestic rhythm of a family preparing for visitors. Mrs. Obiajulu moves between the kitchen and dining area with practised grace, refusing to let me do too much, but accepting my help whenever I insist.

Then the front door opens.

“Mummy!”

The call rings through the house with the ease of a daughter who has never needed permission to belong.

Mrs. Obiajulu’s face lights up at once. “That is Nkay.”

I have met Nkeonyere before, but not like this. The first time was at Ikenna’s burial.

That day had not allowed anyone to be fully seen. Grief stood between us like smoke. Faces blurred beneath tears. Voices broke into greetings, condolences, hymns, and wails. The air around the grave had been heavy with dust, sorrow, and the terrible finality of sand falling over a man whose story had not finished speaking.

I remembered Nkeonyere from that day as Ikenna’s sister — composed for her mother’s sake, broken for her brother’s sake, moving between mourners with the sorrowful dignity of someone who had learned that grief must sometimes serve before it can collapse.

But the woman entering the house now is fuller than my memory of her. She is lively. Social and warm. 

She comes in wearing a tasteful native outfit, the fabric rich but not loud, her scarf tied with effortless confidence. She is tall like Ikenna, with the same family carriage in the shoulders and the same expressive eyes, eyes that seem capable of laughter and rebuke within the same breath. There is maturity in her figure now, the comfortable fullness of a woman settled into marriage and motherhood, but something youthful remains in the quickness of her smile.

Behind her comes her husband, Olusola Kayode. He carries the a calm courtesy without an air importance in an in-law’s house. He greets Rev. Obiajulu first with a slight bow, then Mrs. Obiajulu with respectful affection. His voice is measured, easy, and kind. The children flow in after him with bags, noise, and questions, filling the house with the cheerful disorder only children can carry without apology.

Nkeonyere sees me and opens her arms. “Elizabeth!”

Before I can decide whether to greet formally, she crosses the space between us and embraces me. “You came.”

“I did.”

She holds me a little longer than politeness requires. “I’m happy you came.”

Something in the way she says it touches me. Not merely because I am a visitor. Because I am expected, elcomed and recognized.

When she releases me, she turns toward her husband. “Honey, this is Elizabeth.”

Olusola smiles. “So this is Elizabeth.”

The words startle me slightly. This is Elizabeth. As if I have arrived after being described many times.

Nkeonyere continues, “Ikenna’s friend.” There is a brief pause after the name.

I notice that in this family, Ikenna’s name does not merely identify the dead. It opens a room.

Olusola extends his hand warmly. “It is good to finally meet you. Nkay has spoken about you.”

“I hope only good things,” I say.

He smiles. “Mostly.”

Nkeonyere slaps his arm lightly. “Don’t mind him.”

One of the children, a boy old enough to be bold and young enough to be unfiltered, stands before me with open curiosity. “Mummy, is this Uncle Ikenna’s friend?”

“Yes,” Nkeonyere replies.

He stares at me, then tilts his head as if studying and trying to place my face somewhere in his memory. The room softens suddenly.

“Did you know Uncle Ikenna when he was tall?”

I laugh despite myself. “I only knew him when he was tall.”

That answer satisfies him.

Another child, younger, holds onto Nkeonyere’s wrapper and looks at me shyly. I bend slightly and wave. “Hello.”

She hides her face.

Nkeonyere’s smile falters for half a second, then laughs. “She is forming shy. Give her ten minutes.”

The boy is still studying me. “Mummy said Uncle Ikenna was very kind.”

“He was,” I say softly.

“And stubborn.”

Everyone laughs. This time, the laughter carries no offence. Only memory.

“Yes,” I say. “He was also stubborn.”

Rev. Obiajulu looks up from where he sits and smiles faintly. “That part is well established.”

The house laughs again, and for a moment I see something I had missed at the burial. The Obiajulu family does not preserve Ikenna only through tears. They preserve him through stories, through teasing, through rooms where his name can still be spoken without everyone collapsing and through children who know him partly by memory, partly by inheritance, and partly by the way adults’ faces change when they say his name.

Nkeonyere moves easily from person to person, asking about food, luggage, water, who has eaten, who has not, whether Daddy has taken his tea, whether Mummy has overworked herself, whether the children have greeted properly. She is daughter, wife, mother, sister, and host all at once, never fully sitting, never fully still.

I watch her and think again of Ikenna. He had loved deeply, even when his life became entangled in darkness. That love had roots. It came from somewhere. From this house. From this mother who feeds people before asking why they came. From this father who corrects minds before condemning persons. From this sister who can laugh and grieve in the same hour.

Mrs. Obiajulu soon returns to the kitchen to complete brunch, refusing every protest from Nkeonyere that she should rest. Olusola joins Rev. Obiajulu in the sitting room, and within minutes the two men are deep in conversation about church matters, the country, and the rising cost of everything.

Nkeonyere remains near her husband for a while, responding to his remarks, adding her own humour, and occasionally calling one of the children to order without raising her voice too much.

I drift into the kitchen to continue my self-appointed chore. Tea for the man of God. It has become my small duty in the house. No one assigns it to me, but I have taken to serving Rev. Obiajulu his tea after devotion. Perhaps because I need something useful to do with my hands. 

I pour carefully. Hot water first. Tea bag. A little milk. No sugar, as Mrs. Obiajulu has instructed. When I carry the cup to Rev. Obiajulu, he accepts it with a grateful smile.

“Thank you, my daughter.”

There it is again. My daughter. I still have not grown used to it.

“You are welcome, sir.”

Nkeonyere watches the exchange from the side of the room. Her expression softens in a way I cannot immediately interpret. It is not surprise. More like confirmation.

As if she expected me to fit somewhere in the house and is pleased to see the place finding me.



































































EIGHT





By late afternoon, the Obiajulu house has become too full for secrets. Children run from room to room with the confidence of ownership. Mrs. Obiajulu moves between the kitchen and the dining area, calling instructions over her shoulder. Olusola sits with Rev. Obiajulu in the sitting room, their voices rising and falling over their private chat and laughter. 

Somewhere in the passage, one of Nkeonyere’s children hums a Christmas chorus with more enthusiasm than accuracy. It is a warm, living house.

Laughter spills from the kitchen, and the smell of food fills the air, the sound of children being corrected, and the easy movement of people who do not ask where cups are kept. But I have not come to Onitsha only to be welcomed. I have come with a burden. And burdens sometimes need quiet before they can be spoken properly.

Nkeonyere stands near the dining area, folding a small towel while listening to her husband say something from the sitting room. She laughs, answers him, then turns and catches me watching her.

“You look like someone rehearsing bad news,” she whispers.

I smile weakly. “Can you show me around the neighbourhood?”

Her expression changes immediately. Not that she’s alarmed, but a look of awareness. “Now?”

“Yes. Just a short walk.”

She studies my face for a moment. Then she nods. “Let me tell Mummy.”

A few minutes later, we step out of the compound.

Okosi Street carries the quiet dignity of an old Onitsha neighbourhood. The road is narrow and uneven in places, edged by fenced bungalows, modest storey buildings, small provision shops, and compounds where mango and almond trees lean over old walls. Some gates are freshly painted. Others bear the tired rust of many rainy seasons. 

The asphalt has long surrendered portions of itself to age and neglect. Patches of laterite earth interrupt the road in places, while shallow gutters trace both sides of the street.

This is one of those old Onitsha neighbourhoods where history lives quietly. Where retired teachers, civil servants, clergy, and traders spend decades building families and reputations.

Where everyone knows who built which house. Who married whom. Who prospered. Who fell. Who died.

Even in its quieter corners, one can still feel the commercial pulse of the city in the distance: horns, impatient drivers, market voices, and the restless energy of people who believe time is money and delay is an insult.

Nkeonyere walks beside me without rushing. She greets two elderly women in Igbo. One of them looks at me curiously, smiles, and asks something I do not understand.

Nkeonyere laughs. “She says you look like a good daughter-in-law.”

I nearly stumble. “What?”

“Don’t worry. In this street, every young woman walking with another woman is either a wife, a daughter-in-law, or someone they must investigate.”

Despite myself, I laugh.

The laughter helps. For a moment.

We walk past a small church building painted cream and blue, then a row of shops whose metal shutters are half-closed for the evening. At the end of a side street is a quiet spot beneath an old almond tree, beside a low concrete bench facing a fenced compound.

Nkeonyere stops there. “Will this do?”

I nod.

We sit.

For a few seconds, I watch a dry leaf drag itself across the road.

Then Nkeonyere turns to me. “So, what really brings you to Onitsha?”

I look at her.

She does not smile this time.

“I know you loved my brother,” she continues softly. “And I know you are welcome in our house. But you did not suddenly wake up and decide to spend Christmas with two boring old people.”

“Your parents are not boring.”

“They are holy. That is sometimes worse.”

I smile, but it fades quickly.

She leans closer. “Lizzy, talk to me.”

I take a slow breath.

“You are right. I came for a reason.”

Her eyes sharpen.

“Is this related to the Inverted Cross fraternity?” The question is too direct to dodge.

“Yes.”

Her face tightens.

“Lizzy…”

“But the person involved is repentant.”

She looks away briefly, then back at me.

“I am not sure my father will be comfortable getting involved with those people again.”

“I understand.”

“No, I don’t think you do.” Her voice remains gentle, but grief gives it weight. “That fraternity took my brother from us long before he died. It took years from his life. It took peace from this house. Even after he found Christ, the shadow of that world still followed him.”

“I know.”

“You know part of it.”

The correction is quiet but firm. 

I lower my eyes. “You’re right.”

Silence passes between us.

Then I say, “Do you know why Jshap willed that he should be buried in a cemetery and not in his father’s compound?”

Nkeonyere frowns slightly. “No. I always thought it was because he had chosen a life beyond family sentiment. He died in the faith. He is with Christ. What difference does the burial place make now?”

“It makes a difference.”

She watches me closely.

“There is more to his death, Nkay.”

Her expression hardens. “What do you mean?”

“I think your brother knew his death would do something to the satanic power at work in the fraternity he founded.”

Nkeonyere becomes very still.

For a moment, I wonder whether I have gone too far too quickly.

“How?” she asks.

I open my handbag and bring out the memoir.

The notebook looks ordinary in my hands, too ordinary for the weight it carries. Its edges are worn. Some pages have folded corners. I have kept it carefully, but every time I touch it, I feel as though I am handling something alive.

Nkeonyere stares at it. “What is that?”

“Jshap’s memoir.”

Her eyes move from the book to my face.

“Where did you get it?”

“From the police. After he died, they released some of his belongings to me. This was among the things recovered from his apartment.”

Her hand comes slowly toward it, then stops. “He wrote this?”

“Yes. Most of it during his months in prison. His darkest season.”

Nkeonyere receives the notebook reluctantly, almost reverently.

She does not open it immediately. For several seconds she only holds it. The noise of the street seems to recede.

“My brother’s handwriting is in here?”

“Yes.”

Her throat moves. She looks down at the cover again.

“I don’t know whether I am ready.”

“You don’t have to read everything now. But I need you to understand why I came.”

She holds the memoir tighter.

“Then tell me.”

I fold my hands together. “I was in the room the night Ikenna died.”

Nkeonyere closes her eyes briefly. When she opens them, grief has entered her face, but she does not interrupt.

“He did not sound confused,” I say. “He sounded like someone who knew something was about to happen. Like someone ready for an appointment others could not see.”

The words are hard to say. I had carried that night for so long that speaking of it still made my body remember the fear.

“There is a diagram in that memoir,” I continue. “A strange one. Symbols. A Seagull. An Archer. A Key. At first, I did not understand it. I still do not understand everything. But one of those symbols later became clear.”

I catch her fixed stare. “The Key?”

“Who is the Key?”

“Sammy.”

Nkeonyere stares.

“The same Sammy you mentioned?”

“Yes.”

“The one arrested?”

“Yes.”

Her face tightens. “What happened?”

I tell her carefully. I start with the burial. With Sammy laying the wreath on Ikenna’s grave, which was the reason he chose to be buried at the cemetery. In fulfillment of the requirement of the Testament, that his grave may receive the Hangman’s last respect, the cross.

“How was it supposed to be placed?” she asks.

“Inverted.”

Her eyes darken.

“But Sammy laid it upright?”

“Yes. The Cross of Jesus Christ.”

Nkeonyere looks down at the memoir, then back at me. “And the fraternity reacted?”

“They demanded that he reverse it. When he refused, everything changed. He became a marked man.”

“Because of a Cross?”

“Because of what it represented.”

I lean forward.

“Nkay, I believe Ikenna knew the antidote to checkmate the GODs. I think after he received Christ, he prayed for the one who would come after him. The one who would fulfill this rite at his grave may choose Christ over the inversion. Sammy became that breach.”

Nkeonyere’s lips part slightly.

“That is why they want him dead?”

“Not only dead. First, they wanted him broken, returned and used.”

“For what?”

I hesitate. “There is a deeper agenda Sammy was involved in, simply codenamed the Program.”

Her eyes narrow. “What Program?”

“That is part of what Sammy began to reveal before his arrest. The fraternity was not only built for campus violence. There is a deeper agenda — something designed to harvest the gifts and intellect of vulnerable young people through politics, influence, art, ambition, and false opportunity.”

Nkeonyere looks disturbed. “This sounds like something from a nightmare.”

“It feels like one.”

“And Sammy was involved?”

“He was chosen to execute part of it. But he defected.”

“Because of the Cross?”

“Because of Christ.”

Nkeonyere looks away, absorbing it.

The street has grown quieter. The children who were playing nearby have moved farther down. A motorcycle passes, then silence returns.

I continue.

“After he fled school, he ended up in Ogbomosho. God began rebuilding him there. He started teaching at a study centre. He began mentoring students. He rescued a girl named Rakiya, who is now missing.”

“Missing?”

“Yes. And the police are accusing him of abducting and exploiting her.”

Nkeonyere covers her mouth. “Oh God.”

“It is a frame-up. I know it is. But it is dangerous because the accusation is ugly enough to poison people against him before truth can speak.”

“And where does my father come in?”

“Sammy is an orphan. He was raised by foster parents. His foster father was an Anglican priest.”

Nkeonyere looks at me sharply. “An Anglican priest?”

“Yes. Sammy ran away years ago after a false allegation. He now believes he misjudged them. In custody, he told me he had a dream where his foster father laid hands on him and blessed him.”

Her face softens. “He wants to find him.”

“He wants to reconcile. He believes he may die, Nkay.”

“Lizzy…”

“He asked me to find his father before it is too late.”

For the first time since we sit down, my voice breaks.

“I don’t even know where to begin. I only know Rev. Obiajulu is the closest connection I have to the Anglican clergy. I thought if anyone could help me trace an old priest, he could.”

Nkeonyere is silent. She opens the memoir then. Only the first page. Her fingers touch the handwriting gently. I watch her face change. Grief first, then wonder, then pain.

“This is really his writing,” she whispers.

“Yes.”

She turns one more page and stops.

For a long moment, she says nothing. Then she closes the book carefully.

“You should have brought this to us earlier.”

“I know.”

“Why didn’t you?”

“I was afraid.”

“Of what?”

“That I had no right to keep carrying pieces of your brother’s life.”

Nkeonyere’s eyes soften. “Lizzy, maybe you carried them because God knew you would bring them back when the time was right.”

The words settle quietly inside me. I do not know whether to receive comfort or correction.

She holds the memoir against her lap. “Daddy must see this.”

“I know.”

“And Ifeanyi.”

At that name, I look up.

“Ifeanyi too?”

“Yes. Especially Ifeanyi.”

“Why?”

“Because he knows things about Ikenna that I do not. And because if Sammy’s problem has anything to do with old networks, hidden interests, or people using institutions to capture young people, Ifeanyi may understand the structure better than Daddy.”

I remember her earlier warning. Ifeanyi carries his own guilt about Ikenna.

“Will he listen?” I ask.

“He will listen to the memoir.”

“And to me?”

Nkeonyere looks at me steadily.

“Lizzy, you are not coming as a stranger making wild claims. You are coming with my brother’s handwriting, his burial mystery, the Cross, Sammy’s arrest, and a request from a man trying to find his father before death reaches him. That is serious enough.”

I breathe in.

“I was afraid you would think I was dragging your family back into darkness.”

She looks down at the memoir. “Maybe darkness never fully left us alone. Maybe we simply stopped knowing what name it was using.”

The sentence chills me. Nkeonyere stands slowly.

“We should go back.”

“Now?”

“Yes.”

“Should we tell Daddy immediately?”

“Not in the middle of brunch chaos. But tonight, after the house is quieter, we will speak to him. I will help you.”

Relief rises in me so quickly that it almost weakens my knees.

“Thank you.”

She touches my arm.

“Don’t thank me yet. This road may be harder than you think.”

“I know.”

“No,” she says gently. “You suspect. You do not know.”

I cannot argue.

We begin walking back toward the house.

The sky has softened into late-afternoon gold. Smoke rises from somewhere behind a compound. A church bell rings faintly in the distance. Nkeonyere holds the memoir against her chest as we walk, and I notice that her steps have changed.

She left the house as my hostess. She is returning as a witness. 

As we enter the compound, the noise of the Obiajulu home returns — children, plates, voices, laughter, life. But beneath that ordinary family sound, something has shifted. Jshap’s memoir has entered the house.

And I know that by nightfall, Rev. Obiajulu will no longer be merely a possible church contact. He will become part of the mission.

































































NINE





By evening, the Obiajulu house settles into the gentle disorder of family life. The children, now fully fed have become noisy again. Plates move from the dining table to the kitchen sink. Mrs. Obiajulu supervises everything with the quiet authority of a woman who has fed generations without ever needing to announce her importance. 

I stay beside her in the kitchen, rinsing cups, wiping plates, and passing things before she asks for them. I need the work. My hands need occupation because my mind has become restless.

Since Nkeonyere and I returned from our walk, I have felt the weight of the memoir sitting somewhere in the house like an unopened testimony. She took it from me gently, promised to speak to her father first, and asked me not to worry.

That is impossible. How can I not worry?

I am about to ask Rev. Joseph Obiajulu, father of Jshap, to step again toward the darkness that took his son. I am about to place Sammy’s burden before him – Sammy, a repentant disciple of the same fraternity that wounded his family. Sammy, hunted by the GODs. Sammy, desperate to find his foster father before death reaches him.

I scrub a plate longer than necessary. Mrs. Obiajulu notices.

“My daughter, that plate has repented already.”

I blink, then look down. The plate is spotless.

She smiles. “You are troubled.”

“I’m fine, ma.”

She gives me the look mothers give when they hear a lie they are too kind to expose immediately.

Before she can say more, Nkeonyere enters the kitchen.

“Lizzy.”

I turn.

Her face is calm, but serious. “Daddy wants to see you.”

My stomach tightens. “Now?”

“Yes.”

Mrs. Obiajulu takes the plate from my hand. “Go.”

I wipe my hands quickly on a kitchen towel.

“Should I bring anything?”

Nkeonyere shakes her head. “Just come.”

We walk out of the kitchen and down the passage toward the sitting room. My heartbeat grows too loud inside my chest. Nkeonyere seems to sense it because she touches my arm briefly before we enter.

“He is not angry,” she whispers.

That should comfort me. It does not.

Rev. Obiajulu sits in his usual armchair near the window, but the sitting room feels different now. Quieter. The television is off. The children have been sent farther inside. Olusola is no longer there. Mrs. Obiajulu remains in the kitchen, deliberately giving the conversation space.

On the centre table lies Jshap’s memoir. Closed, but present.

Rev. Obiajulu looks up as we enter. “Elizabeth,” he says gently, “sit.”

I sit on the edge of the sofa opposite him.

Nkeonyere sits beside me, close enough for support, silent enough to let her father lead.

For a few moments, Rev. Obiajulu says nothing. His eyes move from me to the memoir, then back to me. He looks older in that moment, not weak, but burdened by the return of a grief he has learned to carry without displaying.

Finally, he speaks. “Nkeonyere has told me why you came.”

I lower my eyes. “Yes, sir.”

“She has also shown me the notebook.”

My throat tightens. “I am sorry I did not bring it earlier.”

He lifts one hand gently. “We will speak of that later.”

The correction is mild, but it carries weight. He leans back slightly.

“Before we discuss your friend, I want to say something to you.”

I brace myself. “Thank you for honouring the sanctity of my home.”

The statement surprises me so much that I look up. He notices.

“You had the liberty to care less about it,” he continues. “Many young people do. And I have learned, with age, to tolerate many things I cannot always approve of.”

I do not understand immediately. Then memory returns. My first morning in the house.

I had worn jeans and a long T-shirt, ordinary clothes to me, modest enough by my own judgment. I had walked through the sitting room on my way to help Mrs. Obiajulu in the kitchen. Rev. Obiajulu had been seated in his chair with his Bible open. He had looked at me once, not harshly, but with a kind of restrained discomfort then returned his eyes to the page.

I had not understood until Mrs. Obiajulu gently drew me aside. “My husband is an old-school believer,” she had explained with a smile that tried to remove embarrassment from the moment. “He has reservations about some dressing and conduct, not because there is a doctrine that everyone who wears such things will not make heaven. But this is his house, and we respect his convictions here.”

I had apologized immediately. Not because I believed I had sinned, but because I understood honour. The next morning, I dressed differently. Not performatively, but respectfully. Now Rev. Obiajulu is referring to that small incident.

“It showed me something about you,” he says.

I cannot speak.

“It showed thoughtfulness. Care. Submission where submission was not demanded by force. Respect for an elder’s conscience, even where you may not share all his cultural instincts.”

His eyes soften. “These things matter.”

Nkeonyere remains quiet beside me, but I feel her approval.

Rev. Obiajulu continues, “A person who cannot honour a house may not be trusted with the burdens of that house. But you honoured mine.”

My eyes sting. “Thank you, sir.”

“No,” he says. “Thank you.”

For a moment, the room is silent. Then his gaze moves toward the memoir.

“Nkeonyere says your friend, Samuel, is in trouble.”

“Yes, sir.”

“And that he is a repentant member of the fraternity.”

“Yes.”

“The same fraternity connected to my son’s fall and death.”

The sentence makes the room heavy.

I swallow.

“Yes, sir.”

He looks at me for a long moment. “I will not pretend that this is easy.”

“I understand.”

“Do you?”

His voice is not accusing. Only searching.

I lower my gaze. “Maybe not fully.”

He nods slowly.

“Pain has history. People who come later often meet only the event. But those who lived through it remember the years before the event, the warnings, the prayers, the disappointments, the nights of waiting, the shame, the arguments, the silence.”

He places his hand on the memoir. “My son’s story did not begin on the day he died.”

“I know, sir.”

“I also know that God’s mercy did not end with his death.”

His voice softens.

“And if the Lord has shown mercy to Samuel, then I must be careful not to stand where God has already passed.”

The words humble me. I had expected hesitation. I had not expected such painful obedience.

Nkeonyere finally speaks. “Daddy, Sammy’s foster father was an Anglican priest.”

“So I heard.”

Rev. Obiajulu turns fully toward me.

“What exactly do you know?”

“Very little, sir. Sammy was raised by foster parents. His foster father was an Anglican priest. Sammy ran away after a false allegation. He now believes he misjudged them and wants to reconcile.”

“Do you know the man’s name?”

“Reverend Briggs,” I answer.

“Parish?”

“He has no recollection of it.”

“Country?”

“He is not certain where they served las, but the couple are citizens of the United States. He has not given me enough details though.”

Rev. Obiajulu’s brows gather slightly. “That makes it difficult.”

I allow him think through it.

“The Anglican Communion has structure, but it is not magic. If the priest served in Nigeria, especially in a known diocese, we may trace him through clergy records, parish transfers, retirement notices, old synod documents, or someone who remembers him.”

He pauses.

“But if he served outside the country, or moved through missionary channels, the local diocese may have little direct information.”

My hope falters slightly. He sees it.

“I am not saying it is impossible.”

I look up.

“I only want to be honest about the limitations.”

“Yes, sir.”

“Nkeonyere told me Samuel believes he may die.”

The sentence cuts through me. “He is afraid, sir. And not without reason.”

“He wants his father’s blessing.”

“Yes.”

Rev. Obiajulu closes his eyes briefly.

When he opens them, there is grief in his face. “A son who seeks his father’s blessing is not finished.”

The words settle inside me.

“He may be broken,” he continues, “but he is not finished.”

My lips tremble.

“I promised him I would help.”

“And you came here.”

“Yes, sir.”

He looks at the memoir again. “You came with my son’s handwriting.”

I become still.

“I did not bring it to manipulate you.”

“I know.”

“I only needed you to understand that Sammy’s case is connected to something larger than police charges.”

“I know.”

He touches the notebook gently. “This book has reopened things in me, Elizabeth.”

“I’m sorry.”

“Do not be sorry for truth.”

His voice is quiet.

“Truth may wound a house, but false peace kills it slowly.”

Nkeonyere takes my hand.

Rev. Obiajulu continues, “I will help you as far as I can.”

For a second, I stop breathing.

“You will?”

“Yes.”

Relief rises so quickly that my eyes fill.

“Thank you, sir.”

“But I must tell you something.”

“Yes, sir.”

“I may not be the most useful person for the practical search.”

I frown slightly.

He leans back.

“I can ask within church circles. I can reach retired priests I know. I can make inquiries discreetly. But this matter now involves police, political interests, a missing girl, possible records, movements, and people who may not want to be found.”

His eyes hold mine. “For that, you need someone with reach beyond the pulpit.”

Nkeonyere looks at him. “Ifeanyi.”

Rev. Obiajulu nods. “My son Ifeanyi.”

The name enters the room with a weight I do not yet understand.

I look from him to Nkeonyere. “You think he can help?”

“I think he is the only one who may know how to connect these pieces quickly,” Rev. Obiajulu says. “He moves in circles I do not move in. He knows lawyers, private investigators, businessmen, people in Abuja and Lagos, people in government offices. He has his own way of asking questions.”

Nkeonyere adds quietly, “And people answer him.”

Rev. Obiajulu’s face tightens slightly. “That is sometimes a gift, sometimes a danger.”

I notice the change in his tone. There is something unresolved around Ifeanyi. Something the family has learned to speak of carefully.

“Will he agree?” I ask.

Rev. Obiajulu looks at Nkeonyere.

“That depends.” Hie gaze fixed on Nkeonyere. “On whether his sister can convince him to stop running from old wounds long enough to help someone else survive them.”

Nkeonyere lowers her eyes. The room becomes still.

Rev. Obiajulu sighs. “Ifeanyi loved Ikenna, but love does not always behave well under guilt.”

I do not ask. Not yet.

He continues, “Nkeonyere will call him. If he agrees to meet you, tell him the truth. Do not decorate it. Do not protect him from discomfort. If this matter touches Ikenna’s memoir, he must know.”

I nod. “Yes, sir.”

“And Elizabeth.”

“Yes, sir?”

“Until we know more, be careful what you say outside this house. If your friend has been framed, then somebody is arranging narratives. People who arrange narratives do not like witnesses who ask better questions.”

A chill moves through me. “I understand.”

“Good.”

He places his palm flat over the memoir.

“As for Samuel’s father, I will begin with the clergy network. I will ask carefully. Not publicly. If we can get even a fragment – parish, diocese, country – then the search becomes easier.”

“I will try to get more details from Sammy.”

“If they allow you to see him again.”

The reality strikes me.

“Yes.”

Nkeonyere squeezes my hand.

Rev. Obiajulu’s voice softens. “Do not fear, my daughter.”

My daughter again. This time, the words nearly break me.

“The Lord does not always show us the whole road. Sometimes He gives us one person, one name, one notebook, one promise. We follow faithfully from there.”

I nod, unable to speak. He lifts his hand slightly.

“Let us pray.”

Nkeonyere and I bow our heads.

Rev. Obiajulu prays with the measured gravity of a father who has buried a son and still believes God is just. He prays for Sammy’s preservation, for truth to overtake false accusation, for Rakiya to be hidden from harm and brought into safety, for the church network to yield the right connection, and for Ifeanyi’s heart to be stirred toward obedience.

When he prays for Ifeanyi, his voice trembles almost imperceptibly. Only a father would hear it. Only a wounded house would understand it.

After the prayer, he opens his eyes. “Nkeonyere,” he says.

“Yes, Daddy.”

“Call your brother and let me know what he says.”

She nods. “I will.”

We both leave Rev. Obiajulu and return to the room. Nkeonyere stops in the passage and looks at me.

“I’ll call him tonight.”

I nod.

“Thank you.”

She smiles faintly. “Don’t thank me yet. Ifeanyi can be difficult.”

“How difficult?”

She looks toward the sitting room, then back at me. “The kind of difficult that still loves.”

That answer does not comfort me. But it puts me on notice.







TEN





It’s half past nine. The house has grown quieter. The children are inside, half asleep and still protesting bedtime. Mrs. Obiajulu has retired from the kitchen after arranging the plate, while Rev. Obiajulu sits in the sitting room with his Bible open but unread on his lap. 

The memoir remains on the centre table, closed now, but carrying the weight of everything it has reopened.

Nkeonyere stands near the window with her phone in her hand. For a moment, she does not dial. She looks at me instead.

“Are you ready?”

I do not know what readiness means in that moment. Ready to meet Ifeanyi, or ready to ask a stranger for help finding Sammy’s foster father while another girl remains missing and Sammy sits in police custody?

I only nod.

Nkeonyere dials. The phone rings long enough for my heartbeat to grow louder. Then he answers.

“Nkechi.”

His voice comes through the speaker, deep, calm, and distant with the confidence of a man used to being called from many directions.

Nkeonyere smiles despite herself.

“Only you still call me that.”

“Because it is your name, and It’s the one I like.”

There is affection in his voice, but also restraint. The kind of restraint families develop around wounds they have not fully healed.

“How is the Bishop?” he asks.

“He is fine.”

“And Mummy?”

“Still feeding everyone as if famine has been declared.”

Ifeanyi laughs softly. The sound is brief, but warm.

“And you?”

“I am fine. Olusola and the children are here.”

“Good.”

A pause follows. Then his tone changes slightly.

“You didn’t call just to give me family update.”

Nkeonyere looks at me. “No.”

I feel my palms tighten together.

“I need your help,” she says.

“With what?”

“With someone.”

“Who?”

“Elizabeth.”

The silence that follows is immediate.

I look at Nkeonyere. Her eyes remain on the phone. From the other end, Ifeanyi says nothing for several seconds.

Then, very quietly, he asks, “Elizabeth?”

He does not say the name like a question only. He says it like memory has stood up inside him.

“Yes,” Nkeonyere replies.

Another pause.

“Which Elizabeth?” His voice has changed.

The earlier ease has vanished. Something cautious has entered. Something almost reverent.

Nkeonyere glances at me again.

“Lizzy.”

My breath catches.

“Lizzy?” he repeats.

“Yes.”

Then Nkeonyere says the words that make my whole body go still. “Ikenna’s heartthrob.”

Heat rushes to my face.

“Nkay,” I whisper sharply.

She lifts a hand, begging me silently to let her finish. The phone remains quiet. So quiet I wonder whether Ifeanyi has ended the call.

Then his voice returns, lower than before. “Lizzy.” He sounds as though he has been handed something fragile from the dead. “She is there with you?”

“Yes.”

“In the Bishop’s house?”

“Yes.”

Another silence. This one carries grief. When he speaks again, the confidence in his voice has softened. “Ikenna spoke of her.”

My throat tightens.

Nkeonyere looks at me.

I turn my face away.

There are things one can suspect privately and still be unprepared to hear spoken aloud. I knew Ikenna had cared for me. I knew his eyes had sometimes betrayed what his mouth refused to confess. But hearing his sister offer me to another brother as “Ikenna’s heartthrob” makes the past suddenly too visible.

It is as if a curtain has been pulled open in a room I had kept dim for my own survival.

Nkeonyere continues carefully. “She needs help.”

“What kind of help?”

“It is connected to Ikenna’s memoir.”

That sentence changes the air again. Ifeanyi’s voice becomes guarded. “What memoir?”

“The notebook he wrote before he died.”

“How do you have it?”

“Lizzy has it.”

A long breath comes through the line. “Put her on the phone.”

Nkeonyere looks at me.

I shake my head immediately. No. Not yet. I am not ready to speak to him. Not with my heart still shaking from the word *heartthrob*.

Nkeonyere understands. “She is not ready yet,” she says.

“She will have to be.”

“I know.”

“What does she need?”

“She is looking for someone. A priest. Sammy’s foster father.”

“Sammy?”

“A repentant disciple of the Inverted Cross.”

The pause that follows is colder.

“Nkechi.”

“I know.”

“The Bishop agreed to this?”

“Yes.”

Another silence.

I imagine Ifeanyi wherever he is, perhaps in a hotel room, or an office, or his sitting room closing his eyes at the mention of the fraternity that had swallowed his brother and returned him only through death.

“Why me?” he asks.

“Because Daddy says you can help.”

“The Bishop said that?”

“Yes.”

A faint, humourless laugh comes through the phone.

“So now the Bishop remembers I am useful.”

Nkeonyere’s face tightens. “Ifeanyi.”

“I’m listening.”

“No, you are reacting.”

He says nothing.

She softens her voice. “This is not the time.”

A long silence passes. Then Ifeanyi exhales.

“Where is she now?”

“Onitsha.”

“I will be in Abuja for an art exhibition in three days.”

“Abuja?”

“Yes. A private exhibition. Collectors, galleries, foreign guests, ministry people. I can work faster from there. If she can come, I will meet her.”

Nkeonyere looks at me. My stomach tightens.

Abuja. A city far from the Obiajulu house. A city of power, politics, hotels, polished danger, and people who can disappear behind offices and influence.

Ifeanyi continues, “I will arrange her flight.”

“No, that’s too much,” I whisper.

Nkeonyere ignores me.

“And accommodation?” she asks.

“Of course. Which hotel would you prefer? Hilton? Sheraton?”

His tone has returned to efficiency now, as though emotion embarrasses him and logistics gives him something safer to hold.

“Do you want separate rooms?” he adds. “If you are coming with her.”

Nkeonyere hesitates.

“I’m not sure I can come. I’d have to get permission from Olusola, but it is too impromptu.”

“You should come.”

“I know.”

“If you don’t, I will still take care of her.”

My fingers tighten again.

The phrase should comfort me. Instead, it stirs an old discomfort. A hotel. A man I have not met. A room booked by someone with money and reach.

For one brief second, Bitty’s foolish act in the hotel flashes across my memory, not with detail, but with the sickening feeling of being cornered by a man’s confusion, guilt, and desire. My body reacts before my mind can stop it. I twitch slightly.

Nkeonyere notices. Her eyes shift to me.

“Are you okay?” she mouths.

I force a smile and nod. I am not okay.

But I have no better path.

Ifeanyi is still speaking. “I’ll have my assistant send the details once you confirm. If she is Ikenna’s Lizzy, she is family.”

My eyes sting. Family.

The word follows me everywhere in this house, disarming me.

Nkeonyere’s voice softens.

“Thank you.”

“Don’t thank me yet. I don’t know what she is bringing to my door.”

“She is bringing trouble.”

“I assumed.”

“And truth.”

“That is usually worse.”

Nkeonyere smiles faintly. “I’ll call you back.”

“Let me speak with her before she travels.”

“She will.”

“Promise me.”

Nkeonyere glances at me.

“I promise.”

The call ends.

For a few moments, neither of us speaks.

The sitting room feels unusually still. Even the muffled sounds of children from the inner rooms seem distant.

I look at Nkeonyere.

“He knows me?”

She sits slowly.

“Everybody knows you.”

I frown. “That is not comforting.”

She smiles gently.

“You know we once had a sister called Elizabeth.”

I nod.

“Ikenna wrote about her.”

“She died when she was seven,” Nkeonyere says. “She and Ikenna were almost inseparable. He carried her memory differently from the rest of us.”

Her voice softens further. “When he came home during one holiday, he mentioned you.”

I look down. “What did he say?”

“Not much at first. Ikenna was never careless with what mattered.”

That sounds painfully like him.

“But Mummy noticed,” she continues. “Daddy too. There was a lightness in him when he said your name. A kind of fear also. Like he had found something precious but did not trust himself to hold it.”

My throat tightens.

Nkeonyere watches me carefully. “He told us your name was Elizabeth.”

The room seems to contract around me.

“You must understand what that did in this house. It was not superstition. It was not that anyone thought you were replacing our sister. But names carry memory. And for Ikenna, Elizabeth was not just a name. It was love, loss, innocence, guilt, and longing all tied together.”

I cannot speak.

“I don’t know who showed Ifeanyi what our Elizabeth looked like,” she says. “Maybe Mummy. Maybe Daddy. Maybe an old photograph Ikenna had. But one day, Ifeanyi said you looked like her.”

I look up quickly. “Me?”

Nkeonyere nods.

“He said you were a replica.”

“That is impossible.”

“Maybe not exact. But enough to disturb him.”

I remember the way people in the family look at me sometimes, not rudely, but with a brief, searching tenderness.

“Did Ikenna know?”

“He must have. He promised he would invite you home one vacation.”

I laugh weakly, embarrassed and pained.

“As what?”

Nkeonyere tilts her head. “I don’t know.” Then she smiles, but the smile is sad. “Who knows what was on his mind?”

My face warms again. The word, heartthrob returns with fresh discomfort.

“Nkay, please don’t call me that again.”

She laughs softly. “I’m sorry.”

“You embarrassed me.”

“I know. But I needed Ifeanyi to understand quickly.”

“You could have said friend.”

“Friend would not have opened the same door.”

The truth of that silences me. She reaches for my hand.

“I did not mean to expose you carelessly. But Ifeanyi loved Ikenna. If he hears that Ikenna’s Lizzy needs help, he will listen with a different heart.”

I lower my eyes. “I don’t even know what I was to Ikenna.”

Nkeonyere’s voice becomes tender. “Maybe you were part of what he hoped for but never reached.”

That sentence hurts more than I expect.

For a moment, I see Ikenna again: grave-eyed, burdened, gentle in ways he tried to hide, carrying his past like a sentence even after Christ had forgiven him. I had loved him in a way I never fully named while he lived. Perhaps because naming it would have made his death more unbearable.

Nkeonyere becomes quiet. Then, as if she has stepped too close to a wound, she turns pale. “My brother was an amazing person,” she whispers.

“Yes.”

“May his soul rest in peace.”

“Amen.”

We sit in silence.

Then she takes a breath. “I need to tell you something before you meet Ifeanyi.”

I look at her.

“I may not be able to come with you.” My heart drops.

“What?”

“As much as I would love to, it is too sudden. I have my husband and children here. I need Olusola’s consent, and even if he agrees, the arrangement may be difficult.”

I try to hide my disappointment, but I fail.

“You will go and see Ifeanyi,” she says.

“Alone?”

“Yes.”

“I don’t know him.”

“I know.”

“I am not comfortable with this.”

“I know that too.”

She squeezes my hand.

“Ifeanyi is not perfect. He is not Ikenna. Do not expect him to be. But he is decent. He will take care of you, especially for Ikenna’s sake.”

The phrase again. For Ikenna’s sake.

So much of my life now seems to move beneath that shadow.

I glance away. The hotel memory stirs again, and I force it down.

Nkeonyere sees enough to regret something.

“I’m sorry if I made you uncomfortable with our family lore.”

I shake my head. “It’s not that alone.”

“What is it?”

I stare blankly at the floor. What can I say?

That I am afraid of entering yet another man’s world because too many male worlds have hidden danger under courtesy? That I trust Ikenna’s name but not my own judgment? That Sammy needs help badly enough for me to walk into unfamiliar cities, unfamiliar hotels, unfamiliar grief?

“I don’t know where else to look,” I say finally.

Nkeonyere’s face softens. “Then we will pray.”

I nod.

But even as she bows her head, my mind is already travelling ahead of me.































































ELEVEN





I sneaked out of the house yesterday evening to buy stalks of cinnamon. The previous afternoon, when Nkeonyere took me around the neighbourhood, I noticed a woman beside a small provision shop displaying them in a transparent nylon bag, tied neatly at the mouth. Their brown curls carried a dry, sweet fragrance that reminded me of Christmas before I remembered I had cancelled mine.

I had stood before the woman’s table longer than necessary, pretending to examine the cinnamon while my mind wandered back to how I emptied my suitcase after the news of Sammy’s arrest. I still have this confusion. I want safety, but I cannot walk away.

It was easier to carry a small nylon bag of fragrance back to the house than to carry the full weight of my thoughts. I thought adding it to Rev. Obiajulu’s tea would not only make it tastier, but also help his health.

This morning, I prepare his tea myself. This time, I boil the cinnamon in hot water first, watching the stalks darken as the water takes their colour. Then I place the tea bag in the cup and add a little milk. I stir slowly.

The spoon moves in small circles, touching the sides of the cup with a faint clink that sounds too loud in the quiet kitchen. The colour deepens. The fragrance rises gently – warm, woody, almost medicinal. For a moment, I close my eyes and breathe it in.

I smile to myself because I know Rev. Obiajulu will notice. He notices everything, especially small deviations from his household order. But beneath the smile, my stomach is tight. My hands are steadier than my heart.

I carry the cup to him in the sitting room.

He is sitting near the window, reading from the Bible open on his lap, his glasses resting low on his nose. Morning light comes through the curtains, touching his silver hair and the careful lines age has drawn across his face. He looks like a man who has spent many years listening to God, arguing with grief, and refusing to let sorrow make him bitter.

“Your tea, sir.”

He receives it with both hands. With a little hesitation, he’s perceived the fragrance.

“Thank you, my daughter.”

I wait.

He lifts the cup, takes the first sip, and stops. His eyes narrow slightly. Then he smacks his tongue softly, with the honest delight. 

“Hmmm.”

I fight back a smile.

“My wife did not make this.”

“No, sir. I did.”

He takes another sip. “This tastes good.”

“I added cinnamon. It is good for your health.”

“Please teach her to make it like this.”

“I will,” I reply, grinning.

For a brief moment, I feel light.

I turn to leave.

“Elizabeth.” His voice stops me at the door.

My fingers tighten around the empty saucer.

“Yes, sir?”

He studies me for a moment, as though the question has been waiting longer than the tea. “Are you expecting the return of Jesus?”

The question surprises me. “I am a believer, sir.”

He chuckles softly. “I know you are a believer. That is not what I asked.”

I turn fully toward him.

He points gently to the chair opposite him.

“Sit.”

I obey.

The saucer trembles slightly in my hand before I place it on the table. I hope he does not notice. But of course, he notices. Rev. Obiajulu’s eyes do not rush over things; they rest on them until they confess.

He takes a long look at me.

“You look troubled,” he says quietly.

I almost laugh. I should be. But the laughter remains lodged somewhere behind my ribs, dry and useless.

He places the cup on the table after another sip. “My daughter told me about your concerns over the proposed trip to meet Ifeanyi without her.”

I do not know if this is a question or a report. My head drops. The floor becomes safer than his eyes.

“You have every reason to be concerned,” he says. “And troubled. This can feel like exposure to danger – a young woman travelling to meet a stranger in a strange place, carrying a story too heavy for her age.”

My throat tightens. That is exactly how it feels.

He pauses and gives me a reassuring smile. “If I were not convinced about this, I would not have allowed it.”

Now I realize why Ifeanyi asked Nkeonyere, “Did the Bishop know about this?”  I lift my head and catch his gaze. “Sir, I do not mean to dishonour your trust or prejudge your son.” The words come slowly, weighty and ashamed. “I am scared.”

He waits.

I swallow. “I am scared that I will bring problems to Ifeanyi.”

Rev. Obiajulu lets out a short laugh. Not mocking. Almost tender.

I excuse him because perhaps I sound ridiculous. But I do not feel ridiculous. I feel like a woman standing at the entrance of another storm, asking whether her shadow itself has become dangerous.

“I don’t know what to make of my life,” I confess. My fingers fold into each other on my lap. “I try to keep myself pure and unspotted, but it seems I am destined to bring sorrow and death to people close to me.”

His face becomes still.

I look away before courage leaves me.

“I had planned to travel for the holiday with a friend. It was my visit to Sammy’s place that led to his arrest. They followed me.”

The words drag something out of me. Again, I am in Judith’s sitting room. I am crying into my palms while she sits beside me, angry on my behalf and frightened for me. I hear myself saying I am tired of police stations, cult names, secrets, missing girls, and men whose redemption seems to demand a witness from me.

I blink the memory away. “The night I took Bitty, Ikenna’s best friend, to—”

“Jshap,” Rev. Obiajulu interjects.

I pause.

“That is what you call him, isn’t it?”

“Yes.” I force a smile.

“Okay,” he says gently, “so the night you took his friend to his house…”

“That was the night he was shot.”

I pause and look at him.

“I was there. They asked to hurt me, and he chose rather to lay down his life for me.”

Rev. Obiajulu does not move. He does not interrupt. He waits like a man who understands that some confessions must walk slowly or they will collapse before reaching the end.

“I have been involved in the lives of three disciples,” I say. The number sounds terrible when spoken aloud. “Leaving a trail of suffering or death.”

My voice weakens. “I don’t know if I am going to bring trouble to Ifeanyi. I don’t know if there is going to be drama. I don’t know whether my presence opens doors or wounds.”

My eyes burn. I turn my face away from him. “I don’t know anymore.”

The silence that follows is not empty. It presses.

Rev. Obiajulu leans forward slightly.

“You will go to Abuja.” His voice is firm. “You will meet Ifeanyi. We will find your friend’s father.”

I stare at him.

The certainty in his voice steadies and frightens me at the same time.

After a moment, I whisper, “Thank you, sir.”

“What is happening is not your fault, Elizabeth.”

I want to believe him. But guilt is not a garment one drops simply because someone says it does not belong to you. It clings. It warms itself on the skin. It learns the shape of the person who wears it.

Rev. Obiajulu’s voice softens. “I have become convinced that your destiny is linked to my son’s — Ikenna.”

I lower my gaze. I have heard something like that several times since I arrived. Elizabeth. The name that returned to the Obiajulu house. A part of me resists it because it feels too heavy, too strange, too dangerous. Another part of me recognizes it with a fear I cannot explain.

“I thought deeply about this after our conversation yesterday,” he continues. “And I also presented the boy’s case before the Lord in prayer. Then a veil was torn open when the Lord took me to the Scriptures — the book of John, chapter twelve, verse twenty-four.”

“Unless a kernel of wheat falls into the ground and dies, it remains only a single grain. But if it dies, it produces many seeds,” I say quietly.

Rev. Obiajulu nods.

“And the Spirit said to me, it takes one to die to save the dead.”

For a brief moment, his face lightens. There is no trace of grief in it. Only revelation.

“It was for the salvation of many disciples that he died,” he says. “To point them to the Cross. The same Cross Samuel placed on his grave.”

The room seems to grow still around the word. A symbol I once wore as faith has become a road I keep being asked to walk.

Mrs. Obiajulu comes into the sitting room briefly. I cannot tell if she has been listening. She pauses near the doorway, looks from her father to me, then quietly picks up something from the side table and leaves.

Rev. Obiajulu takes another sip of tea.

“So you see, my daughter, you were chosen to be a voice for the Kingdom, a source of strength to the converted, to guide these disciples to fulfil their purpose.”

Me? A source of strength? A small laugh escapes me before I can stop it.

Rev. Obiajulu looks at me with grave tenderness. “Yes. And you do not need to tell me. I know Samuel is alive because of you.”

I am speechless. The room blurs for a moment. No one has said that to me before.

People have warned me. Questioned me. Pitied me. Some have implied that I am reckless. Others have treated my compassion like a weakness that dangerous men could exploit.

But Rev. Obiajulu looks at the same confused trail and calls it purpose. Something inside me trembles.

“We miss the work of God many times,” he says, “not because we do not recognize the need, but because our sense of how and who God is going to work with limits our understanding of His will. But God chooses what the world considers weak or foolish to demonstrate His power and to humble human pride. True wisdom comes from Him, not from worldly status or strength.”

I look at my hands.

They are the same hands that held Sammy’s fear. The same hands that wiped tears in Judith’s sitting room. Hands that have wanted to let go.

I have secretly borne this guilt since the night Ugo shot Ikenna and the whole spiral of events went out of control. Now this man of God, who has never met the old versions of me, unpacks my life and washes my heart clean like a launderer’s soap.

I do not know when the first tear falls. I only feel it warm against my cheek. I wipe it quickly, ashamed.

Rev. Obiajulu says nothing.

That mercy nearly breaks me. He allows me the dignity of silence. The fragrance of cinnamon still clings faintly to my fingers. I rub my thumb against them, as though I can return to the simplicity of making tea. But I cannot. Rev. Obiajulu’s words follow me as I leave.

When I return to Ikenna’s room, I sit on the edge of the bed for a long time, pondering over what he said about my destiny and Jshap’s. 

I take a look at my phone.

I have been avoiding Judith’s call since morning. Not because I do not want to speak with her, but because I know she will hear everything I have not said. Judith hears silence the way some people hear footsteps.

I intended to call her and say I am coming to Abuja eventually. I intended to ask if I could stay in her house, to avoid being trapped in whatever arrangement Ifeanyi has made. That was the plan. It was sensible. But not after my meeting with Nkay’s father. 

That will be a distraction from the assignment, I convince myself. 

I dial before I lose courage. She answers almost immediately.

“Lizzy?” Her voice comes sharp with concern. “How are you? Where are you? Are you back in Lagos? What happened to the holiday?”

I close my eyes.

Her questions arrive together, exactly like Judith.

“I’m fine,” I say.

“You are not fine. Your ‘fine’ has different sounds, and this one is lying.”

Despite myself, I smile.

“I’m in Onitsha.”

Silence.

Then, “You are where?”

“Onitsha.”

“What are you doing in Onitsha?”

I breathe in slowly. “I’m in Jshap’s house.”

The silence that follows is so sudden I check the screen to see whether the call has dropped.

“Judith?”

“Lizzy,” she says at last, her voice lower now, “are you beside yourself?”

The question should offend me. It does not. Maybe because I have asked myself worse.

“Maybe.”

“Maybe?”

“I don’t know how to explain it.”

“Start from the beginning.”

“There is no beginning anymore, Judith. Everything is connected to something before it.”

She exhales sharply. “You are scaring me.”

“I know.”

“Then stop speaking like a prophet in a bad Nollywood film and tell me what is going on.”

A weak laugh escapes me. Then it becomes a sigh.

“I came seeking a path to find Sammy’s foster father,” I try to explain. “He asked me to find him. His foster father was an Anglican priest, and I thought Rev. Obiajulu might help me trace him through the church.”

“I thought you were done going to police stations and carrying men’s broken histories.”

“So did I.”

“Lizzy.” Her voice softens now.

It is the voice she used that day in her sitting room when I broke down, the voice that did not scold until I had finished crying.

“Are you safe?”

I look around Ikenna’s room.

The old reading table. The photograph on the wall. The quiet that feels less like emptiness and more like waiting.

“Yes,” I say. “I am safe.”

“Do they know why you are there?”

“Yes.”

“And they are helping?”

“Yes.”

“Then why do you sound like this?”

I press my fingers against my forehead.

“Because I came here looking for a way to help Sammy, but I think I found something else.”

“What?”

I hesitate. The words feel too vulnerable.

“My lost self.”

Judith is silent.

I continue before fear stops me.

“I don’t know how to explain it. I entered this house afraid I was only a messenger of trouble. But they received me as if grief had made room for me before I arrived. Rev. Obiajulu spoke to me today, Judith. He said what is happening is not my fault. He said Ikenna’s death was like a seed. That it was meant to point many disciples to the Cross.”

My voice trembles.

“He said I was chosen to be a voice for the Kingdom, to strengthen the converted.”

Judith does not answer immediately. When she does, her voice is careful. “And what do you think?”

“I don’t know.”

“That is honest.”

“I am afraid.”

“That is also honest.”

“I am afraid because part of me believes him.” 

Judith exhales softly. “And the other part?”

“The other part remembers I cried in your sitting room because I was tired. Because I felt weak. Because I thought maybe every step I take toward these people brings danger.”

“You are allowed to be weak, Lizzy.”

“I know.”

“No, you don’t. You think weakness cancels calling. It doesn’t.”

Her words surprise me. “Judith.”

“I still don’t like this whole thing,” she says quickly, as if afraid I will mistake her. “Let me be clear. I don’t like cult stories and I don’t like police matters.”

My heart jerks. I go still. She catches it.

Then Judith says, “I may be afraid for you, Lizzy. But I am not your enemy.”

“I know.”

“Promise me you will not move alone into danger.”

“I promise I will be careful.”

“That is not what I said.”

“I know.”

“Promise me.”

I look at Ikenna’s photograph. At his faint, serious smile. At the face of a man whose death still opens doors among the living.

“I promise I will not be foolish,” I say.

Judith sighs. “That is still not what I said, but I know you.”

Tears gather again, but this time they do not feel as shameful.

“Thank you.”

“For what?”

“For not giving up on me.”

She makes a soft sound, almost irritation, almost affection. “Somebody has to remain sensible in your prophetic drama.”

I laugh. The laugh breaks at the edge, but it is still laughter.

“Call me tomorrow,” she says.

“I will.”

“And Lizzy?”

“Yes?”

“If you found your lost self in that house, make sure you don’t lose her again trying to save everyone else.”

Her words stay with me after the call ends and makes me remember what Rev. Obiajulu said, God chooses what the world considers weak. I am still the woman who wept in Judith’s room because bravery had worn her thin. But for the first time, I do not despise that weakness. 





































































TWELVE





By now, I have become used to the rhythm of the house. But this morning is different. I’m told it is fellowship day. Rev. Obiajulu has remained in his study, preparing the message he will teach in the evening.

The study is not large. Books stand in tall shelves against the wall, some arranged neatly, others leaning with the weariness of frequent use. A wooden desk sits near the window, on it is a small reading lamp. On the wall hangs framed certificates, faded photographs, and a simple wooden Cross.

Rev. Obiajulu sits behind the desk, his glasses low on his nose, writing slowly. Every now and then, he pauses, looks toward the window, and returns to the page with the seriousness of a man not merely composing words, but weighing them before God.

I stand at the doorway longer than I intend.

He looks up.

“Elizabeth.”

“I’m sorry, sir. I did not mean to disturb you.”

“You are not disturbing me.”

“I brought your tea.”

He smiles. “With cinnamon?”

“Yes, sir.”

I place the cup beside his notes and glance at the heading on the page.

“We are going to miss you,” he says in a low tone. 

“I… I don’t know how to say thank you for receiving me.”

“You don’t have to,” he gives me a reassuring smile. “You’re going to be okay.”

“Did God say that?” I tease him in a childish tone.

“Aha, now you sound like my daughter, Elizabeth,” he chuckles.

For a moment I wonder, what is the mystery of my connection to the Obiajulu family? Then the question rises before I can bury it.

“Am I…”

The words feel foolish even as they form. “Am I a reincarnation of your daughter Elizabeth?”

Rev. Obiajulu does not laugh. He does not frown either. He looks at me with fatherly seriousness, as though the question deserves more than a quick denial.

“No, my daughter.” His answer is gentle, but firm. “You are not.”

I lower my eyes, embarrassed.

He continues. “Every mortal has one earthly life. One pilgrimage under the sun. Scripture says it is appointed unto man once to die, and after that judgment. That does not leave room for the soul returning again and again as another person.”

He leans forward slightly. “But let us not answer only by quotation. Let us think.”

I look up.

“Human beings are not recycled identities. Each person is a unique conscious being before God. Your memories, your moral choices, your love, your wounds, your obedience, your sins, your repentance, your history – these are not costumes a soul drops in one generation and wears differently in another.”

He places his hand lightly on his Bible. “God does not treat persons as interchangeable containers. He calls us by name.”

The sentence moves through me quietly.

“You are Elizabeth,” he says. “But you are not our Elizabeth. You carry your own life before God. Your own accountability. Your own calling.”

I breathe slowly. “But why does it feel so familiar?”

He nods, as though he has expected that. “Because familiarity is not always reincarnation. Sometimes it is providence. Sometimes it is resemblance. Sometimes it is memory finding an echo. Sometimes it is God bringing a name, a face, or a person into a house not to repeat the dead, but to redeem something the living never healed from.”

My eyes sting.

He continues. “Death was not God’s original intention for man as we experience it now. Man was created for fellowship with God, for life, for immortality in communion with Him. At the Fall, sin entered, and death came as a rupture. But death did not erase the eternal nature of the human person. It discontinued earthly life, but it did not cancel existence.”

He speaks slowly, as if laying stones across deep water.

“When a person dies, that soul does not dissolve into the world to begin another earthly biography. The person moves beyond this present life toward judgment, mercy, resurrection, and eternity. That is why the Christian hope is not reincarnation. It is resurrection.”

I listen, strangely comforted by the distinction.

“Reincarnation says the soul returns to try again under another identity. Resurrection says God redeems the person, body and soul, without erasing who that person is. The hope of the Gospel is not that Elizabeth returns as you. It is that Elizabeth remains Elizabeth before God, and in Christ, death does not have the final word over her.”

A tear slips down my cheek. I wipe it quickly. He sees, but does not interrupt himself.

“God came in the form of fallen man,” he says, “clothed in our frailty, capable of suffering, capable of death. Christ entered death not to normalize it, but to conquer it. Through His death, the price of sin was paid. Through His resurrection, the inheritance of life was reopened.”

He lifts his cup and sets it gently on the table.

“So when you ask whether you are our daughter returned, I say no. Not because the connection you feel is meaningless, but because its meaning is greater than reincarnation.”

I frown slightly. “Greater?”

“Yes.”

His eyes soften. “If you were merely Elizabeth returned, then your presence would belong mostly to the past. But if God brought you here as Elizabeth, distinct and living, then your presence belongs to His present purpose.”

The words settle inside me with some weight.

“You are not a replacement child,” he says. “You are not a ghost. You are not a spiritual echo wearing flesh. You are a daughter of God whose life has crossed ours by providence.”

The room blurs.

“And perhaps,” he adds softly, “God is using you to touch wounds that began before you knew us.”

I blink and look away.  “I don’t know why I feel so tied to your family.”

“Because love and calling are often understood after obedience, not before.”

He lets that remain. Then he smiles faintly.

“Besides, if you were my daughter reincarnated, I would have to ask why she came back with such stubbornness.”

Despite my tears, I laugh. I know he said it to make me laugh.

He laughs too, softly. The heaviness lifts just enough for breath to return.

Then his face becomes serious again. “Elizabeth, do not chase mysteries beyond what God has revealed. The enemy often traps sincere people by making them interpret spiritual affinity wrongly. If he cannot deny that something is happening, he will misname it.”

I nod slowly.

“Do not call providence reincarnation. Do not call burden obsession. Do not call obedience foolishness merely because it costs you rest. And do not call fear wisdom.”

The words settle in me like instruction.

“Then what should I call this?” I ask.

He looks at me for a long moment. “Assignment.”

The room becomes quiet. Assignment.

The word does not frighten me as much as it should. Perhaps because I already know.

Rev. Obiajulu picks up his tea again.

“Test your love by Scripture, prayer, humility, and counsel. If it leads you to pride, secrecy, confusion, or disobedience, resist it. If it leads you to love, truth, sacrifice, courage, and Christ, then follow carefully.”

I bow my head. “Yes, sir.”

“And remember this: you are not responsible for saving everyone. Christ saves. You obey.”

That sentence rests on me as if an invisible hand is upon my head.

For days, I have carried Jshap’s memoir, Sammy, the students and now the search for the priest as though the whole world will collapse if I breathe wrong.

“Christ saves. You obey,” I whisper it inside myself.

Rev. Obiajulu leans back, satisfied. “Now,” he says, lifting the cup slightly, “about this cinnamon tea.”

I look up. “Yes, sir?”

“Have you taught my wife how to brew it.”

I smile through the last of my tears. “Yes, sir.”

As I turn to leave, the question that has troubled me no longer sits like confusion in my chest.



I return to Ikenna’s room and lying on the bed, I begin to put the pieces of the events of the past few days together to make sense of it all, as I prepare my mind for the trip tomorrow to finally meet Ifeanyi, Jshap’s brother. 

A stranger could easily sit in the wrong place without knowing it. But the Obiajulu family is different. Not perfect, and like most families, not without pain. But honest in its fragments. Each person seems to carry one piece of the family’s truth.

My namesake, their late sister. Did God send her ahead of me to make room for me… that I may receive her full measure of love for this assignment, as he called it?
