Rev. Obiajulu carries a depth of spiritual interpretation that has impacted my perception and worldview. He carries a father’s sorrow, the priest’s discipline, the regret of a man who had learned too late that authority without tenderness could drive a wounded son into another man’s hands.

Mrs. Obiajulu carries the warmth that words can’t describe – the meals, the welcome, the quiet acceptance that turns hospitality into healing. My mind goes back to the day she gently corrected my clothing choice in the house. I wonder at her unassuming submission and service to the man she loves and honours. I have imbibed it. 

Nkeonyere carries memory with laughter — she can speak of Ikenna and still smile, can tease the dead without dishonouring them, can open wounds without making them bleed unnecessarily. The way she called me Ikenna’s heartthrob, and the word settled in me like both wound and blessing.

And Ifeanyi…

I pause. My heart misses a beat.

Ifeanyi is the missing piece. The next keeper of the story. The one I have not yet seen. The one everyone speaks about carefully. The one whose breach allowed darkness to enter the family. The black sheep, yet the useful one.

Ifeanyi has arranged my visit with the ease of someone used to making things happen. Flight. Hotel. Transport. An invitation to the art exhibition. Everything is settled before I have finished worrying about whether I should accept.

I rise from the bed and pace to the small mirror near the wardrobe. Looking at my reflection I whisper to myself, “Who am I going to Abuja as?”

A friend seeking justice for Sammy? A messenger carrying Ikenna’s memoir? A woman searching for a priest? Or the Elizabeth whose name has reopened old tenderness in the Obiajulu family? I am going as someone already known before arrival. That makes me uneasy.

I see my reflection unconsciously touching the Cross pendant on the gold chain around my neck. The pendant is small, simple, and warm from resting against my skin. I rub my thumb slowly over its edges. The Cross. It keeps appearing… almost in every conversation.

It no longer merely symbolizes faith. It has become a line across the entire story, dividing surrender from self, truth from appearance, calling from convenience, and life from the kingdoms that feed on death. 

A strange sense that Ifeanyi will not merely assist the search; he will complicate it, deepen it, perhaps even redirect it. Maybe meeting him will finally close the chapter of the dark memories of Ikenna Obiajulu that I have borne since that fateful night. 

I had been afraid to meet him. Now I find myself looking forward to it. That realization startles me. Not because the fear has vanished. It has not. But something else has joined it. Curiosity… and expectation. 











































































THIRTEEN





The aircraft door opens, and warm dry air enters before the passengers begin to move. I step out with my handbag pressed close to my side, joining the slow line of travellers descending the stairs to the tarmac. Above us, the afternoon sky is wide and pale blue, almost too clean, as though the city has been polished for officials, diplomats, contractors, and people who know how to move through power without looking afraid.

I try not to look afraid. But fear has its own posture. It tightens the shoulders, sharpens the eyes and makes the hand close too firmly around a bag strap. Fear makes every stranger look like a possible message from danger.

Inside the arrival hall, passengers gather around the luggage belt. Men in suits answer calls with unnecessary volume. Women adjust scarves and lift children from tired arms. Porters move through the crowd with professional impatience. Somewhere behind me, someone laughs loudly, and I turn too quickly.

Calm down, Lizzy. I tell myself that twice… then a third time.

I have travelled alone to meet a man I have never met, in a city I do not know well, because Sammy is in custody, Rakiya is missing, and the only road to Sammy’s foster father now seems to pass through the Obiajulu family’s difficult son… Ifeanyi.

I remember how his voice softened when he heard my name, Elizabeth. That part still unsettles me.

I collect my small bag and move toward the public arrival area. Families wait behind the barrier. Drivers hold placards. Airport attendants call names. Men approach women too confidently, offering taxi service, hotel service, exchange service, help no one has requested.

I stand near a pillar and scan the hall. Not casually but carefully.

The old fear from Ogbomosho returns: the two boys at the restaurant, their lazy laughter, their sudden appearance after I left Sammy’s street, the police coming from the opposite side. I had become a trail once. I am determined not to become one again.

A man in a dark suit walks toward me. He is holding a small placard, not raised above his head, just angled discreetly in his hand. ELIZABETH.

My heart jumps at the sight of the name.

He is expressionless, neat, and professional. His shoes shine. His face betrays nothing. That makes me more cautious. He stops a respectful distance away.

“Elizabeth?”

I hold his gaze.

“Yes.”

“Mr. Obiajulu is waiting for you.” He gestures toward the exit.

I do not move immediately.

“Which Mr. Obiajulu?”

His expression does not change. “Mr. Ifeanyi Obiajulu.”

“Where is he?”

“At the pickup point.”

“Why didn’t he come in?”

“He asked me to receive you, ma. The vehicle is outside.”

I look past him toward the exit. Too many people. Too much movement. Too many chances to disappear into someone else’s arrangement.

“Please call him.”

For the first time, the man looks mildly surprised. “Ma?”

“Call him. Let me hear his voice.”

He hesitates, then brings out his phone. I watch his hands. Watch the number he dials. Watch his face. He passes the phone to me.

A male voice comes through, deep and controlled. “Elizabeth?”

The sound of my name in his mouth carries something I am not prepared for. Not possession or flirtation. But recognition. As if he has been expecting not merely a guest, but a memory.

“This is Lizzy,” I say.

“I know.”

The answer makes my throat tighten.

Then his tone softens. “You are right to be careful. The man with you is my assistant. His name is Madu. I am outside in a black Prado. The plate number ends with 48. Do not enter any other vehicle.”

The instruction steadies me. Not because it removes the danger, but because he acknowledges it.

“Thank you,” I say.

“I will be standing by the vehicle when you come out.”

I hand the phone back.

Madu receives it silently and steps aside, allowing me to lead the way rather than positioning himself behind me. I notice that too. Ifeanyi has either trained him well, or instructed him carefully.

Outside, Abuja Airport stretches bright and ordered beyond the glass doors. Cars move along the pickup lane. Drivers wave. Security men shout at vehicles that linger too long.  Then I see him.

Then I see him.

Ifeanyi Obiajulu stands beside a black Prado, one hand resting lightly on the open rear door, as though he has been waiting long enough to master even the posture of patience.

For a moment, my steps slow.

He is dressed with the quiet confidence of a man who understands the difference between wealth and display. A beige shacket rests over a black turtleneck, its clean lines giving him a controlled, almost military neatness without making him look severe. The black denim trousers fall straight over polished black boots, and a dark wristwatch catches a brief blade of sunlight when he shifts his hand. 

Nothing about him shouts, yet everything on him speaks – restraint, taste, authority, and the habit of moving through expensive rooms without needing anyone’s permission.

The family is there in the height, the carriage, the thoughtful gravity of the eyes, and the quiet intensity that seems to gather before he speaks. 

When he sees me, his composure shifts. Only slightly. His face opens, then closes again. The movement is brief, but I catch it before he recovers himself. Recognition. Nostalgia. Or maybe a memory rising too quickly.

For one unsettling second, I feel as if he is not looking only at me. He is looking through me, past me, into a room of the family’s history where another Elizabeth once stood, where Ikenna’s name still breathes, where grief has learned to wear silence like dignity.

I tighten my hand around my bag strap. This is the man I have come to meet.

And as he straightens beside the Prado, polished and unreadable under the Abuja sun, I know immediately that Ifeanyi Obiajulu is not merely going to help me find Sammy’s father. He is going to open doors I may not be ready to enter.

He has not merely seen me. He has recognized something in me that does not belong entirely to me. 

I stop a few steps away. “Mr. Obiajulu?”

He smiles faintly. “Ifeanyi, please.” His voice is gentler now than it sounded on the phone. “Welcome to Abuja, Elizabeth.”

The name makes me uncomfortable again.

“Lizzy is fine.”

He studies me for half a second. Then he nods. “Lizzy.”

The correction costs him something I cannot name. 

He turns slightly and opens the rear door wider. “Please.”

I look into the vehicle before entering.

Empty back seat. Driver in front. Madu moves to the front passenger side.

Ifeanyi notices my hesitation. “You may sit wherever you are comfortable,” he says.

The statement is simple, but important.

“Back seat,” I say.

“I’ll join you.”

My body tightens before I can hide it.

He sees that too.

A shadow crosses his face — not offense, but understanding.

“I can sit in front if you prefer.”

The offer disarms me more than insistence would have.

“No,” I say after a moment. “It’s fine.”

We enter the back seat from opposite sides.

The Prado pulls away from the airport.

For a few minutes, we say nothing.

Abuja moves past the tinted windows in broad, deliberate lines. The roads are wider than Lagos roads, cleaner in appearance, less frantic. Palm trees stand along the median in orderly rows. Government buildings appear at intervals, fenced and guarded. Hotels rise with polished confidence. The city looks planned, but beneath the planning I sense power moving in quieter ways.

I keep my bag on my lap. My phone remains in my hand.

Ifeanyi notices. “I have sent Nkeonyere my driver’s details and our route,” he says.

I look at him.

He glances at me, then out the window. “I thought that would help you feel safer.”

“It does.”

“Good.”

Another silence follows.

Then he says, “Hilton?”

“That is where you booked?”

“Yes. Unless you prefer Sheraton or somewhere else.”

“Hilton is fine.”

“I booked a separate room for you.”

I look at him again.

This time his face carries faint amusement. “I thought I should say that before your mind began another security meeting.”

His beard is neatly groomed, shaped close to his jaw, the sideburns trimmed with the precision of a man who pays attention to details others pretend not to notice. It gives his face a sharper frame, making his expression harder to read. He is not handsome in the careless, youthful way Ikenna had been. His appeal is older, darker, more deliberate, the kind that comes with confidence, pain, and secrets kept under discipline.

He has Ikenna’s stillness, but not his softness. Ikenna had carried sorrow like a wound he was trying to hide. Ifeanyi carries his like a tailored garment — fitted, controlled, almost elegant. 

Despite myself, I almost smile.

“Thank you.”

“You are welcome.”

He leans back slightly.

For a while, he looks out his window.

When he speaks again, his voice has changed. “You are important to the Bishop.”

He reads my reaction. Silence laced with a grimace.

“I mean my father.” 

“I don’t know about that.”

“You are.” He turns to me. “He called me himself.”

Something in that statement carries weight.

“He doesn’t call you often?”

Ifeanyi chuckles softly. “The Bishop and I have mastered the art of not speaking while remaining father and son.”

I do not know what to say.

“He called because of you,” he continues. “He told me you needed help locating a clergyman. An Anglican priest. He said it mattered.”

“It does.”

“He knows I have reservations about clergymen.”

The sentence is sharp but not bitter enough to be careless. It has history.

“He still called.”

“Yes.” He smiles faintly, but his eyes remain serious.

“That means you have reconciled me with him for the first useful conversation we have had in years.”

I am startled.

“I didn’t know that.”

“Of course you didn’t.”

He looks ahead.

“That is usually how providence works. People open doors they did not know were locked.”

The words sound like something Rev. Obiajulu might say, but with less theology and more weariness.

I study him discreetly.

He is not what I expected.

I had imagined arrogance. Perhaps danger. Perhaps a man too comfortable with influence to be trusted. And perhaps those things are still there, hidden behind polish. But there is also grief. Controlled, disciplined… expensive grief. The kind that does not weep because it has learned to curate itself.

He turns suddenly. “Ikenna spoke about you.”

My breath catches. I look away.

“So I’ve been told.”

“He did not speak about many people that way.”

I say nothing.

“He came home once and mentioned a girl named Elizabeth. He said the name carefully. Like he was afraid of what it meant.”

My fingers tighten around my bag.

“I did not know he spoke of me.”

“He did.”

His voice softens. “When Nkay said you were Ikenna’s…” He pauses, perhaps choosing not to repeat the word heartthrob. “I understood why my father sounded different.”

I look at him.

“And why did you agree to help?”

He meets my eyes. For the first time, I see the resemblance to Ikenna clearly, not in shape alone, but in the sadness that seems to live behind restraint.

“Because I did not help my brother enough when he was alive.”

The sentence enters the vehicle and sits between us. The driver keeps his eyes on the road. Madu remains still.

Ifeanyi looks away first. “And because if Ikenna’s Lizzy asks for help, I would have to be a worse man than I already am to refuse.”

My throat tightens. “Please don’t call me that.”

He turns back, surprised. “Ikenna’s Lizzy?” 

I lower my gaze. “I don’t know what I was to him.”

His expression changes. Less polished now. More human.

“None of us fully knew what we were to him,” he says quietly. “Not until after he was gone.”

That sentence loosens something in me. For a moment, the suspicion in the car softens into shared grief.

Then I remember why I am here.

“Sammy is running out of time.”

“I know.”

“Nkeonyere told me the outline. You will fill in the details when you rest.”

“I don’t need rest.”

“You do.”

“I need help.”

“You will get both.” His tone is calm, but not dismissive.

“I have already asked someone to begin a preliminary search around Anglican clergy records and missionary links. We will need Sammy’s foster surname, any parish memory, any school history, anything at all.”

“I don’t have much.”

“Then we start with little.”

I look at him carefully. “Why are you doing all this so quickly?”

“Because delay favours whoever arranged the accusation.”

I become still.

He continues. “If Sammy is framed, whoever framed him is depending on confusion, shame, fear, and time. The longer everyone panics, the colder the trail gets.”

His words are practical. Sharp, dangerous, yet reassuring. This is why Rev. Obiajulu pointed me to him. Ifeanyi understands systems.

Silence. I inhale slowly.

“Are you an artist?” I ask, partly to change the heaviness, partly because he mentioned he was in Abuja for an Art exhibition.

He laughs softly.

“No.”

“Then what are you?”

“A curator.”

“What is that? Like a middleman?”

He smiles. “In other industries, perhaps. But in art, it is more delicate. Artists, buyers, collectors, galleries.” He turns to me. “We are part of the machinery that decides what is seen, valued, preserved, sold, or forgotten.”

The word machinery catches my attention. He notices.

“A curator scouts talent, interprets work, organizes exhibitions, brokers relationships, protects artists sometimes, exploits them sometimes, depending on the curator’s conscience.”

“That sounds honest.”

“It is safer to start with honesty. Then disappointment can be gradual.”

This time, I smile fully. He sees it and looks away almost immediately, as if the smile has struck memory. 

I wonder then what he sees when he looks at me. Elizabeth? Ikenna’s unnamed longing? A sister he lost? A woman he is obliged to protect? Or merely a guest carrying trouble?

The Prado turns toward the hotel district.



In the distance, the Hilton rises with quiet authority, its height commanding the skyline around it. I stare at it through the window, conscious again of my vulnerability. A hotel arranged by a man I met only minutes ago. A city full of strangers. A mission wider than I know.

I touch the Cross pendant at my neck. Ifeanyi’s eyes follow the gesture.

“Are you afraid?” he asks.

I consider lying.

“Yes.”

“Of me?”

I look at him.

“Partly.”

He nods. No anger. No wounded pride. Only acceptance.

“Good.”

That surprises me.

“Good?”

“Fear keeps people careful. I would worry if you were not afraid.”

The vehicle slows at the hotel entrance. Security checks underneath with a mirror. The barrier lifts. Ifeanyi turns to me before the car moves forward.

“Lizzy, I cannot ask you to trust me because I am an Obiajulu. Families produce both saints and devils.”

The statement startles me.

“But I can promise you this: while you are here, I will not use your vulnerability against you.”

My eyes sting unexpectedly.

“I will help you find what can be found,” he continues. “I will ask questions. I will open doors. And if danger comes near you, I will not pretend not to see it.”

For a moment, I cannot speak.

“Thank you,” I say softly.

He looks ahead as the car stops.

“You are welcome.”

Then, almost under his breath, he adds, “For Ikenna.”

The driver opens the door. Hotel staff approach. I step out carefully.

But as Ifeanyi walks beside me into the hotel lobby, not too close, not too far, I understand that this meeting is not only an introduction. It is a collision. My fear meeting his guilt. My mission meeting his influence. My living face meeting his brother’s memory.

And somewhere between suspicion and nostalgia, I imagine what the next door that opens will bring.

























































FOURTEEN





Ifeanyi called and asked that I join him at the restaurant at nine-thirty. In order not to keep him waiting, and mostly to familiarize myself with the place, I’m already seated at a safe corner ten minutes earlier. 

I wonder again what kind of man I have come to meet. A curator, he had said. What in the world does that mean. 

This is my first proper visit to Abuja. I had passed through pictures, news clips, political conversations, and the imagination of people who spoke of the city as if it were a separate country within Nigeria. But sitting inside this restaurant, I begin to appreciate the Ifeanyi’s gesture and unusual kind of hospitality amidst the tension between us yesterday.  

The Hilton restaurant impresses me as a place where important people come to speak softly. Not because everyone there is important, but because the room carries that suggestion of importance: polished marble floors, tall glass panels, carefully arranged tables, silver cutlery resting beside folded napkins, waiters moving with trained discretion, and morning light pouring through wide windows as if Abuja itself has been invited to dine.

The restaurant smells of coffee, toasted bread, fresh fruit, grilled sausages, and expensive perfume. On one side, hotel guests move along the buffet line with plates in hand, choosing from trays of eggs, pastries, rice dishes, fruit slices, yoghurt, tea, and things I cannot immediately name. 

A group of men in suits sits near the far end, speaking in low tones over newspapers. Two women in elegant dresses laugh softly near the windows. A foreign couple studies the buffet as if breakfast requires strategy.

Now I understand why Abuja gives people a certain posture. It is not noisy like Lagos. It does not assault you with its hustle. It persuades you quietly that power does not need to shout. Everything looks arranged and calm. But I am not calm.

I check my watch when I sight him walking toward the restaurant entrance. It’s exactly nine-twenty-eight. As I watch him scan the restaurant once before he sights me and walks towards the table, I sense that art is only one part of him. Ifeanyi looks like a man trained by family, grief, and business to notice everything without appearing to notice anything. 

He takes a seat near the window. I take a quick glance at him, conscious of the distance between the world I have come from and the world he seems to occupy naturally. He does not look out of place here. 

“Good morning Ifeanyi,” 

Hi Lizzy, how are you?” 

“I am fine.”

He looks at his watch, as if to make sure he is on time.  “Did you sleep well?” Ifeanyi asks at last.

“Well enough.”

“That is not an answer.”

“It is the honest version.”

A faint smile touches his face. “I’ll accept it.”

Ifeanyi notices my stillness, but he does not challenge it. He unfolds his napkin, places it gently on his lap, and allows silence to sit between us like a third guest. That helps.

The environment gives me cover. In a small room, unease becomes obvious. In a luxury restaurant, silence can pass for etiquette. A person may be afraid and still appear composed if the cutlery is shiny enough and the waiter keeps refilling water.

A waiter comes to the table. Ifeanyi orders coffee. I ask for tea. He suggests I try something from the buffet later, and I agree mostly because refusing would make me appear more nervous than I want to look.

His shirt is crisp. His watch catches light when he lifts his glass. His manner with the waiter is courteous but familiar, as if service, travel, hotel lobbies, exhibitions, and discreet conversations are ordinary furniture in his life.

We return with plates neither of us seems eager to eat. For a while, we speak of neutral things. My flight. The hotel. Whether Abuja feels too quiet. Whether I prefer Lagos. I answer politely. He listens politely. Both of us know we are avoiding the real subject. Then he looks at me, and the politeness shifts.

“I learnt you were in the room the night my brother was shot.”

The fork in my hand pauses. There it is. Ikenna. The safest and most dangerous bridge between us.

“Yes,” I say quietly.

Ifeanyi leans back slightly, his face controlled. “I have imagined that room many times.”

I do not respond.

His tone is not accusatory. But the question behind it is old and wounded.

“So how come you were with him so late that night,” he asks, “if you were not dating?”

Heat rises to my face.

He notices immediately. “I’m sorry,” he says. “That is personal.”

“No.”

I lower my fork. “It’s fine.” But it is not fine. 

Because the question forces me to stand again inside a memory I had never fully explained even to myself.

“We were not dating,” I say.

The words sound too small for what Ikenna had been to me.

Ifeanyi waits.

“We had… an understanding, perhaps. Not spoken the way people expect. Not defined. But there was something.”

His face softens, just slightly.

“That sounds like Ikenna.”

I look at him.

“He was not careless with feelings,” Ifeanyi says. “He could carry a thing for years and still speak around it like he was afraid naming it would make it suffer.”

My throat tightens.

“That night, I was seeing him off,” I continue. “A friend from Metro University came around. Bitrus.”

“Bitrus Shak?”

I look up quickly. “You know him?”

“Yes I do. What was he doing in Lagos?”

“Well, he came to visit his fiancée, who happened to be in same hostel block a friend of mine was. He saw him when I saw him off and asked me to take him to see Jshap…” 

Ifeanyi’s eyes darken with thought.

“Bitrus was there?”

“Yes.”

I look down at my tea. “He sounded like he knew he was going to die.”

For the first time, Ifeanyi’s composure falters a little. His hand tightens around the glass of water.

“He said things that night,” I continue, “that I did not fully understand until later. He spoke as if someone would find him someday. As if his body, his burial, even his death had been prepared for something beyond us.”

Ifeanyi’s asks in a low tone. “What do you mean?”

I look around the restaurant.

The men in suits are still speaking over newspapers. The women by the window are laughing. A waiter arranges clean glasses at a service station. The world continues, unaware that a dead man has entered our breakfast.

“I believe he prepared his body to destroy the power that ruled the disciples of the Inverted Cross,” I say.

Ifeanyi stares at me.

“The spell of violence, rebellion, addiction, hatred… whatever held them.”

“How?”

“I don’t know everything.”

“That is not a small claim, Lizzy.”

“I know.”

His eyes search my face.

“Are you telling me this as doctrine, memory, or grief?”

The question is sharp. I respect it.

“All three.”

He looks away for a moment, then back at me.

“I need more than that.”

“I have something that belongs to him,” I say.

The sentence changes him. His gaze settles on me with sudden intensity.

“What?”

I open my handbag carefully and bring out the memoir.

The worn notebook looks strangely out of place in this restaurant, among white plates, silver cutlery, glass cups, and polished surfaces. Yet it carries more authority than everything around it.

I open my handbag carefully and bring out the memoir.

The worn notebook looks strangely out of place in this restaurant, among white plates, silver cutlery, glass cups, and polished surfaces. Yet it carries more authority than everything around it.

I stretch it toward him. “This.”

But Ifeanyi does not take it immediately. His eyes shift from the notebook to my chest.

For one suspended second, I think he is looking at my neckline. The old unease moves through me sharply. I drop the notebook on the table and lift my hand instinctively to cover the slit in my blouse.

Ifeanyi blinks, then looks away so quickly that I understand embarrassment before he speaks.

“I’m sorry.” His voice is low. “I wasn’t…”

He stops, swallows, and reaches for his glass of water. I remain still, my hand near my chest.

The brightness of the restaurant suddenly feels too exposed and too dangerous in its softness.

He drinks, sets the glass down, and forces himself to meet my eyes.

“I was looking at your chain.”

I do not answer. 

He points carefully, not touching. “The pendant.”

My fingers move to the small Cross on the gold chain. “This?”

“Yes.”

His face has changed. Not with desire. With recognition and seemingly, shock. “That chain is familiar.”

I look down at the pendant, then back at him. “Familiar how?”

He draws in a breath. “I gave it to Ikenna.”

The restaurant seems to fall away from me. The low conversations. The movement of waiters. The clinking of cutlery. The polished calm of Hilton breakfast. Everything fades behind that sentence.

“You gave it to him?”

“Yes.”

His eyes remain on the chain, but respectfully now, as though it has become a relic neither of us is ready to touch carelessly.

“It was before I travelled to the States,” he says. “Ikenna was preparing to enter the university. He was young, brilliant, stubborn, wounded, and too proud to admit how vulnerable he was.”

A faint smile touches his mouth, but it does not last.

“He had already begun to attract the wrong attention. Boys like him always do. Gifted boys. Angry boys. Boys who want to prove they cannot be controlled. The university has a way of smelling such boys before they arrive.”

I listen, not able to breathe properly.

“I was worried about him,” Ifeanyi continues. “More worried than I allowed myself to show. I knew Dike’s influence around him. I knew he was fascinated by strength. By secrecy. By the idea of belonging somewhere powerful enough to answer the humiliation he carried.”

His eyes darken. “So, on the eve of my trip, I made him swear.”

“Swear?”

“By the Almighty God.” He laughs once, bitterly.

“And the irony is that I did not even believe in God the way he still did then. But I knew he did. I knew if I made him swear before God, it would mean something to him.”

He looks at me. “I made him vow never to join any cult in the university.”

My hand tightens around the pendant.

“And the chain?”

“I gave it to him as the token of the vow.” His voice drops.

“A gold chain with the Cross. Not because I was spiritual. Not because I understood the Cross. I only wanted something he could wear close to his heart. Something he would touch when temptation came. Something that would remind him that he had promised me.”

He looks away. “For a while, it worked.”

There is a sorrow in that sentence that does not need explanation.

“Ikenna wore it?” he pauses and scans the restaurant.

“Yes. He wore it like a guard. Like a small stubborn witness against the darkness that was calling him.”

I look down at the pendant resting against my chest. I have worn it for so long without knowing the first promise it carried.

To me, it has been Ikenna’s last gift. His final nearness. A silent confession I never knew how to interpret fully. But before it reached me, it was a brother’s plea. Do not join them. Do not give yourself to darkness.

Ifeanyi’s fingers rest on the memoir now, but he still has not opened it.

“I failed him,” he says.

The words come quietly. Not as performance. As a wound accustomed to bleeding inwardly.

“You were not there,” I say softly.

“That is the point.”

He looks at me. “I gave him a chain and left. I gave him a symbol and trusted a boy’s vow to fight a system that was already circling him. I thought love could be wrapped around his neck and shipped into his future while I chased mine abroad.”

His voice tightens. “When I heard he joined them, I was angry. Not only at him. At myself. Because some part of me knew a pendant was not enough.”

I do not know what to say.

He looks again at the Cross. “And now you are wearing it.”

“It was given to me after he died.”

He nods.

“The doctor gave it to Bitrus. Ikenna asked him to tell me…”

My voice falters.

Ifeanyi’s eyes soften.

“What?”

I swallow.

“That he loved me.”

The words sit between us. Too tender for the restaurant. Too late for the living.

Ifeanyi closes his eyes briefly.

When he opens them, grief has made him older.

“So he gave you the token of the vow he broke,” he whispers, “after Christ had made it mean something greater than the vow.”

I look at him.

His gaze remains fixed on the Cross.

“At first, that chain was my warning to him,” he says. “A brother’s fear. A desperate attempt to keep him from the cult. But perhaps by the end, it became his testimony. The thing he once failed to honour became the sign of the One who still received him.”

My eyes fill.

I touch the pendant gently. I have carried the memory of a broken vow and the mercy that outlived it. And I had not known.

 finally reaches for the memoir. “May I?”

I nod.

He opens the notebook with unusual care. The first page holds him. He does not read aloud. His eyes move across the handwriting slowly, as though he is listening to a voice after years of silence.

For several minutes, he says nothing. The unease between us changes shape.

I watch him read his brother’s words in the middle of a lush hotel restaurant, surrounded by people who know nothing about the gravity of the conversation going on at this tiny table. When he finally closes the memoir, he keeps his palm on it.

“You know,” he says quietly, “I have often wondered how Ikenna became capable of such evil.”

His voice is low, almost ashamed of the question.

“Not because I thought he was perfect. He was wounded, yes. Proud, yes. But there was tenderness in him before all this. Conscience. Fear of God. A boy who could make a vow with tears in his eyes and mean it.”

He looks at the chain again.

“This explains something.”

“What?”

“That the cult did not merely recruit him. It dismantled him. It found the wound, entered through it, and slowly taught him to despise the very thing that once restrained him.”

The sentence chills me.

He taps the memoir gently.

“And if this book is what you say it is, then perhaps God did not only bring him back. Perhaps He made him remember what he had broken.”

I wipe my eyes quickly.

“I believe he died in the faith.”

Ifeanyi looks at me.

I hold his gaze. “I believe God forgave him.”

For a moment, something in Ifeanyi resists. I see it in the tension of his jaw, the shadow passing over his eyes, the old anger that does not know where to put itself.

Then he looks down at the memoir.

“If that is true,” he says, “then I have spent years accusing a man God had already received.”

I do not answer.

Some truths must not be forced. They must be allowed to sit beside grief until grief is ready to yield.

“I gave the chain to Ikenna to keep him from darkness. He gave it to you after darkness had lost its final claim on him.”

He pauses.

“And now it has brought you to me.”

After a while, Ifeanyi pushes his untouched breakfast aside.

“We will begin with Sammy’s father,” he says. “That is the immediate task. That is why you are here. 

I nod.

He glances once more at the pendant, then looks away respectfully.

Following his words, I do to not take the chain to be a jewellery any more, not even a memory, but as a thread.

And when Ifeanyi opens the memoir again, I know our breakfast has become something neither of us expected.































FIFTEEN





Our conversation is interrupted by Ifeanyi’s phone. At first, he ignores it. The device lies beside his glass of water, vibrating softly against the polished table. Once. Twice. Then again. He glances at the screen, and his expression changes so quickly that I almost miss it.

The controlled curator returns the phone face down. But it rings again.

This time, he exhales through his nose, picks it up, and looks at me with a small apologetic smile. 

“Excuse me.”

I nod.

He answers without leaving the table. “Yes.” His voice is low.

The kind of low voice people use when they do not want a private problem to become public evidence.

I look away immediately, pretending to study the restaurant. A waiter moves past with a tray of coffee. I fix my gaze on an aged grey-haired man in a grey reading a newspaper. The Hilton breakfast room continues its polished morning performance, shielding everyone’s private fractures behind cutlery, glass, and soft conversation.

But Ifeanyi’s silence has weight.

“No,” he says quietly. “It is not like that.”

A pause.

“I told you why I came to Abuja.”

Another pause. His jaw tightens.

“The event has not even started.”

My gaze returns to my tea. I lift the cup and take a sip. 

The voice on the other end is not audible, but its force is. My first thought is, a woman.

A wife, fiancée, lover, or someone positioned somewhere between entitlement and suspicion. It presses against him through the phone, and he receives it with a mixture of irritation, restraint, and a weariness that suggests this is not the first call of its kind.

“I am not having this conversation now,” he says.

Then softer. “No. I did not say you are disturbing me.”

The contradiction almost makes me smile, but there is nothing amusing in his face. He rubs his forehead with two fingers.

“Please. I will call you later.”

The pause that follows is long. Too long.

Then he says, “Fine.”

He ends the call and places the phone back on the table.

For a moment, he does not look at me.

His eyes remain on the glass of water, but I can see the anger moving beneath his composure. Not the loud anger of men who enjoy performing grievance. His is quieter, more disciplined, and therefore more dangerous to itself. It gathers inward.

He lifts his hand slightly, as if to dismiss the call from the air between us, then drops it. “I apologize.”

“It’s fine.”

“It is not.”

I say nothing.

He takes the glass, drinks, and sets it down with controlled care.

The silence that follows becomes uncomfortable.

I wonder if he will explain. I hope he doesn’t.

I have not come to Abuja to become audience to another man’s romantic complication. I have seen enough men turn emotional disorder into confession, confession into entitlement, and entitlement into danger.

Ifeanyi is not Bitty. I know that. Yet the old caution still moves inside me. Please don’t start a tale of the woman on the phone.

As if he hears the thought, he straightens slightly and reaches for the memoir. It is a deliberate movement.  A return to the table.

He flips slowly through the pages, no longer pretending the phone call has not unsettled him, but refusing to let it lead the morning. The pages move beneath his fingers until he stops at one of the diagrams.

The three symbols. The bird. The bow and arrow. The key.

He turns the notebook slightly toward me. “These,” he says. “Who are they?”

I look at the page.

Even now, the symbols unsettle me. They are crude in one sense, but not childish. They look like things received rather than invented. The kind of drawings that appear simple until life begins arranging itself around them.

“The bird is Jshap,” I say.

“Ikenna.”

“Yes.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes remain on the page. 

“The Seagull.” He says the name quietly, as though testing how much of his brother he did not know.

“The bow and arrow,” I continue, “is his best friend and co-founder of the fraternity. Bitrus Shak.”

His face sharpens. “Shak?”

“Yes.”

“Bitrus was involved in forming the cult?”

“He was not merely involved. According to the memoir, he was to take over from Ikenna as Hangman.”

Ifeanyi leans back.

For a few seconds, he only stares at the page.

“Bitrus Shak,” he repeats. His tone carries recognition now, but not affection. “I used to see Bitrus as a man who always keeps one door open behind him.”

That sounds like Bitty.

“He is different now,” I say.

Ifeanyi gives me a glance that is not disbelief, but caution.

“People can be different and still dangerous if their past has not finished following them.”

I have no answer.

He returns his eyes to the memoir. “You said the diagram was a revelation?”

“That is what Ikenna wrote. A revelation about the future of the fraternity.”

Ifeanyi’s fingers trace the edge of the page but do not touch the drawing itself. “And the third one?”

“The Key.”

He looks at me. “The reason you are here.”

“Yes.”

“Samuel Briggs.”

I nod.

“He is the disciple who laid the Cross upright on Ikenna’s grave.”

“The breach.”

The word comes from him, not me.

I look up.

Ifeanyi stares at the symbol of the key as if trying to unlock something from it by force of attention.

“Tell me about him.”

I do. Not all at once. Not everything.

I tell him Sammy is an orphan. That he was raised by foster parents. That his foster father was an Anglican priest. That he ran away after a false allegation and carried the wound of rejection into the fraternity. I tell him how the GODs marked him, how he became the point man for their political machine, how Otunba’s election money became a trap, how Legion died, how Sammy fled to Ogbomosho, and how he now sits in custody accused of what he did not do.

I tell him about the Cross. About Sammy’s restored sight. About his dream of his foster father blessing him. About his request…. Help me find my father.

Ifeanyi listens without interrupting.

That is one thing I notice quickly about him. When he listens, he does not offer small sounds to prove attention. He becomes still. His face closes, but his eyes sharpen. As if he is arranging information into structure.

When I finish, he looks down at the memoir again.

“So the boy who was meant to serve the Program becomes the one who disrupts it.”

“Something like that.”

“And now those who sent him want to recover the loss.”

“Or erase him.”

“Perhaps both.”

Ifeanyl’s bluntness gives me the chills.

He flips the pages back again, but this time he is no longer reading. He is delaying something. I can tell.

His fingers stop on a page where Ikenna had written in tight, urgent lines. Ifeanyi looks at the handwriting for a long while.

Then his voice changes. “I had great plans for IK.”

IK. The nickname leaves his mouth with the softness of an older brother returning to an old room.

He tries to smile. It does not hold.

“We used to discuss the future as if the future owed us cooperation. He would become a doctor. I would make money. One day, I would build him a specialist hospital.”

He laughs once, quietly.

“You know the kind of foolish confidence young men have before life proves it has other shareholders?”

I smile faintly, but my heart aches.

“It was going to be world-class,” he continues. “Latest equipment. Intensive care. Paediatrics. Diagnostics. A place where no child would die because a hospital did not have what it needed.”

He pauses. His gaze drifts beyond the restaurant.

“He was very passionate about medicine. Not in the ordinary way people say they want to be doctors. Ikenna wanted to fight death.”

The words move through me like a current. I feel goosebumps rise along my arms. Something inside me stirs. Not fear, not memory exactly, a presence.

I look around instinctively, as though someone has entered the restaurant and is standing behind me.

A waiter refills water at another table. The men in suits are leaving. A child whines for more bread near the buffet. Everything remains ordinary. Yet the air around the words has changed.

I touch the Cross pendant unconsciously. What are You trying to show me, Lord?

Ifeanyi notices. His face tightens, as if he has said more than he intended. Then, just as deliberately as he had reached for the memoir after the call, he closes it.

The sound is soft. Final.

He slides the notebook back toward me. The conversation is over, systematically.

“I will see who I can speak to about your friend’s foster father,” he says.

The shift is clear. Back to Sammy. Back to purpose.

I accept the memoir.

“Thank you.”

He picks up his glass, then sets it down without drinking.

“If you want to remain in Abuja while enquiries begin, I can extend your accommodation.”

I open my mouth to protest.

He raises a hand slightly.

“Do not argue yet. I am not offering charity. I am keeping a witness close while the trail is still warm.”

That makes it harder to refuse.

“Otherwise,” he continues, “you can return to Onitsha and I will update you through Nkay.”

I look toward the restaurant window.

Abuja moves beyond the glass, calm and powerful and unfamiliar. I think of Sammy in custody. Rakiya missing. The students waiting. Rev. Obiajulu’s enquiries. The Program. The chain against my chest. Ifeanyi’s unfinished sentence about Ikenna.

“I would like to stay,” I say.

He nods, as if he expected that.

“The pre-exhibition gallery opens tomorrow. Art enthusiasts, collectors, curators, and invited guests will preview the works before the final exhibition. It may look unrelated to your mission, but I want you there.”

“Why?”

He looks at me directly.

“Because the world Sammy described to you – talent, ambition, access, exploitation, visibility – does not exist only in cult rooms. Sometimes it wears perfume, speaks softly, and hangs framed work under expensive lights.”

A chill suddenly moves through my body.

“What are you saying?”

“I think gifted young people are never ignored by predators for long.”

He leans back.

The breakfast remains mostly untouched.

The meeting has moved through safety, suspicion, memory, grief, relationship tension, prophecy, and strategy. Yet Ifeanyi ends it as if all of that has been only the first layer of a professional briefing.

I notice something similar with the two meetings I have had with Ifeanyi. First in the car, and now. He allows emotion to rise only long enough to identify it, then converts it into motion. Perhaps that is how he survives.

As we leave the restaurant, his phone vibrates again. He looks at the screen. For a moment, the mask shifts. Then he silences it and slips it into his pocket.

“Come,” he says in a controlled tone. “We have work to do.”

Work? I follow him out, aware that Ifeanyi Obiajulu is not merely a helper in this story. He is a man carrying his own unresolved war.

And whatever waits for us in the art exhibition, I suspect that his conflict will eventually demand a name.





























SIXTEEN





Ifeanyi asks the driver for the car key, and gives him a message for Madu. He has company, me. And I think he also senses most of our conversations should be private. 

He didn’t mention where we are going. He simply asked if I was comfortable with what I am wearing. 

“That is not an explanation,” I protest.

“It is enough for our outing.”

I should know better than to argue.

An hour later, we arrive at a private gallery tucked away inside one of Abuja’s quieter districts.

Unlike the grand exhibition venues featured in magazines, this gallery feels intimate, almost exclusive. It has the aura of a place serious collectors visit when they want to discover artists before the rest of the world notices them.

A narrow stone pathway leads through a landscaped courtyard filled with sculptures, flowering shrubs, and carefully trimmed hedges. Floor-to-ceiling glass panels reflect the morning sunlight, while muted jazz drifts from hidden speakers near the entrance.

The atmosphere feels less like a commercial space and more like a sanctuary. As soon as we enter, the outside world disappears and the noise of traffic fades.

Conversations are soft in here. Visitors instinctively lower their voices. Art seems to demand reverence. A young attendant greets Ifeanyi warmly. They clearly know each other.

“You haven’t visited in months,” the attendant says.

“I’ve been busy.”

“You always say that.”

Ifeanyi smiles faintly, but I can already see his eyes moving across the room, taking inventory without seeming to.

I wander away while they exchange pleasantries.

The gallery consists of several interconnected rooms. Paintings occupy pristine white walls illuminated by carefully positioned spotlights. Sculptures stand like silent guardians throughout the space.

Every piece seems deliberate and every placement intentional. I move slowly from one artwork to another. Some are finished in landscapes, some portrait frames. Abstract compositions, political commentary, and traditional themes reimagined through contemporary styles. The diversity is astonishing.

A painting catches my attention. It occupies an entire wall. Unlike the others, it seems to command the room. Visitors approach it quietly. Study it, then linger. Something about it refuses to release viewers. I step closer.

The painting depicts a young girl standing in a field beneath a sky filled with migrating birds. At first glance, the scene appears beautiful. Then the details emerge. The birds cast shadows shaped like chains. The girl’s feet are rooted into the earth. The horizon glows with distant light she cannot quite reach.  Hope and captivity exist simultaneously on the canvas. I feel an involuntary chill.

The girl in the painting is not crying. She is not screaming. She is simply standing there, trapped in a field wide enough to look like freedom. That is what disturbs me most – the deception of space. The sky is open, the birds are flying, the light is calling, and yet everything in the painting insists that she cannot move.

I am not particularly an art enthusiast, but the expressions of the images are too vivid… too understandable to an ordinary viewer. The emotions are too specific. Too personal. Too real. This is not imagination, I tell myself. It feels like memory. 

The thought of Rakiya comes to my mind before I even know. The missing girl. The girl whose silence has become another room I keep entering in my mind. I step closer again.

The brushwork is urgent in some places, tender in others. The chains under the birds are not painted with obvious cruelty. They are almost hidden, softened into shadow, as though captivity has learned to disguise itself as nature. The girl’s dress is pale, but not clean. The hem is touched with earth. Her hands hang at her sides, fingers slightly curved, not helpless exactly, but waiting for instruction from a world that has denied her language.

The longer I look, the more I feel watched by the painting. Not by the girl. By the wound behind her. I search for the artist’s information. There is no name. Only a small brass plaque… R. Nothing else. No biography. No artist profile. Just a single letter.

I frown.

Who signs a masterpiece with a single letter? The mystery irritates me. And intrigues me. I take a step backward.  Then another. I try to absorb the entire composition.

The painting seems to change depending on where one stands. From the left, the birds look free. From the right, their shadows become heavier, almost predatory. Straight on, the girl appears trapped between both interpretations, as if the truth of her life depends on who is looking and what they are willing to see.

I find this intriguing. Instinctively, I reach for my camera. The investigator in me has already begun asking questions. The painting may contain clues. Symbolism. Patterns. Something worth examining later.

I lift the camera. Focus. Press the shutter. The flash illuminates the room.

The reaction is immediate.

“Ma’am!” The voice cuts across the gallery.

Every head turns.

Before I can lower the camera, a gallery attendant appears beside me.

His expression is polite. But firm. “Photography is prohibited.”

My face burns.

“I’m sorry.” The apology comes too late.

The attendant extends his hand. “The camera, please.”

I blink. “What?”

“The camera.”

For a moment, I think he is joking.

He is not.

Several visitors have stopped to watch.

The attention makes the situation infinitely worse. I feel heat rising from my neck to my face, and my first instinct is to apologize again, to shrink, to let the polished room swallow my mistake quietly.

But the attendant’s eyes are not on me. They are on the camera.

“I only took one photograph,” I say.

The attendant remains unmoved. “Gallery policy.”

Reluctantly, I hand it over.

He receives it with professional restraint and immediately checks the images.

Then his expression changes. Subtly. Yet unmistakably.

The moment he reaches the photograph of the painting. A flicker. His face betrays recognition and concern. The reaction lasts less than a second. But I notice.

And suddenly, I am interested for an entirely different reason. This is no longer about photography.

The attendant closes the camera. “We will keep this temporarily.”

My frustration evaporates. Curiosity takes its place. At that moment, Ifeanyi appears.

“What happened?” He asks.

The attendant explains. Patiently. Professionally.

I watch Ifeanyi’s face.

At first, he seems mildly embarrassed. Then he glances at the painting. Then at the attendant. Then at the camera.

He smiles. “I understand the policy,” he says.

The attendant relaxes slightly. “Good.”

“But surely we can resolve this without confiscating my guest’s camera.”

The conversation that follows remains remarkably civil. No arguments, threats or raised voices. Just polite negotiation.

Ifeanyi does not raise his volume because he does not need to. His courtesy has pressure inside it. He speaks as though he is merely asking, but the attendant responds like a man aware that the request has weight.

Eventually, the attendant relents. The camera is returned. The photograph, however, is deleted. Personally and under supervision. I should be annoyed. Instead, I am fascinated. I look again toward the painting.

The girl beneath the birds. The impossible sadness. The single letter… R.

As we leave the gallery, I glance back one final time.

The painting remains where it is, quiet and guarded, as if nothing has happened. Visitors still approach it with softened faces. The spotlights still fall gently on the girl’s rooted feet and the birds dragging chains across the sky.

But now I know something I did not know before. Somebody is watching who looks at that painting. Somebody cares who records it.

Outside, the sunlight feels strangely bright. I climb into the vehicle. For several minutes, neither of us speaks.

Ifeanyi looks ahead, one hand resting on the steering wheel, the other near his phone. His expression is controlled, but I can see his mind working behind it. He is not irritated by the incident. He is interested. So am I.

Finally, I turn toward him. “I’m sorry if I embarrassed you.”

He smiles. “It’s okay. It’s not your fault.”

I stare through the windshield. Back toward the gallery disappearing behind us from the side mirror. 



















SEVENTEEN





The vehicle rolls silently through Abuja’s afternoon traffic. For several minutes, neither of us speaks. The gallery recedes behind us, but my thoughts remain there. With the painting, the girl beneath the birds, the shadows shaped like chains. With the strange reaction of the attendant when he saw the photograph I had taken.

I sit with my handbag on my lap, my fingers resting on the clasp without opening it. Outside the window, Abuja continues its polished performance – wide roads, guarded buildings, tinted vehicles, roundabouts dressed with flowers, and people moving as though everyone has an appointment with power.

But I am not seeing Abuja. I am seeing the girl in the painting.

She stands in that field as if the whole sky has been offered to her and the earth has refused to release her feet. The migrating birds should have meant escape, but their shadows drag chains across the canvas. The light on the horizon should have meant hope, but it stays just far enough to become torment.

Ifeanyi navigates the traffic calmly, one hand resting on the steering wheel. He does not rush me into conversation. That is becoming one of the things I notice about him. He can ask questions sharply, but he can also leave silence alone when silence is still working.

Then he glances at me. “You’ve been quiet.”

I look out of the window. “I know.”

“What are you thinking about?”

I hesitate.

The answer sounds ridiculous even before I say it. “The painting.”

His eyes remain fixed on the road.

“What about it?”

I struggle to explain. How does one describe a feeling? An impression? A memory that is not quite a memory?

“It felt familiar.”

“Familiar how?”

I shake my head. “I don’t know.”

The frustration in my voice surprises even me.

Ifeanyi says nothing.

I stare through the windshield, but the road ahead begins to blur into colours and movement. The painting returns again, not as an image now, but as sensation — loneliness without noise, captivity without visible walls, hope that hurts because it remains unreachable.

Then the realization rises slowly. Unexpectedly.

“I think I know the artist.”

The SUV remains perfectly steady. Yet I feel the atmosphere change.

Ifeanyi does not turn immediately. “What makes you say that?”

“I don’t know.”

The answer is inadequate. But it is true. I do not know.

“I just…” I search for the words. “The emotions felt familiar.”

He slows slightly behind a line of cars.

“The emotions?”

“The way she painted loneliness. The way she painted hope. The way she painted captivity.”

I turn to him. “It felt personal.”

He considers this.

“Many artists paint emotions.”

“Not like that.” The response escapes me immediately because I know the difference.

The painting was not observing pain. It was remembering it. The distinction matters.

Ifeanyi glances briefly at me. “You think the artist experienced those things?”

“Yes.” The answer comes faster than I expect.

The city drifts past outside. A convoy of black SUVs glides ahead of us and turns into a guarded compound. A hawker moves between vehicles with bottled water and plantain chips. A traffic warden raises one hand like a priest blessing chaos into order.

For several moments, neither of us speaks.

Then Ifeanyi asks quietly, “Does the painting remind you of anyone?”

The question strikes a hidden place in me.

And suddenly, a face appears in my mind. A younger girl. Quiet. Gifted. Observant.

Someone who had stood at the edge of rooms as if she had learned early not to ask for too much space. Rakiya.

The name does not arrive loudly. It comes like a whisper from a corridor I have not entered yet.

I see her again as Sammy had described her. I see the fragile intensity in her eyes when she had been rescued. I see the strange seriousness of a girl who had looked at the world as if it had already shown her too many secrets. I remember the paintings connected to her – the left-handed strokes, the hidden signature, the single letter that had seemed almost accidental before today.

My fingers tighten around the bag clasp. The memory vanishes as quickly as it appears. Yet the feeling remains.

I stare ahead.

“I have a feeling.”

“What kind of feeling?”

I swallow.

The answer sounds impossible. Yet I cannot escape it. “A feeling that I’ve met the artist before.”

For the first time since we left the gallery, Ifeanyi smiles. Not because he agrees. Because he recognizes something. Instinct. And in investigations, instinct often arrives long before evidence.

He turns the steering wheel gently as we ease past the traffic light.

“You said the plaque had only one letter… R.”

“Yes.”

He repeats it almost to himself. “R.”

The sound of it sits between us. Small… ordinary.

“There are many names that begin with R,” he says.

“I know.”

“And many artists use initials.”

“I know that too.”

“And you have no proof.”

I look at him. “No.”

He nods. But he does not dismiss me. That steadies me.

A lesser man would have smiled, corrected me, and filed my intuition under female sentiment. Ifeanyi does not. He keeps his eyes on the road, but I can see his mind taking the thought apart carefully, testing it for weakness, weight, and usefulness.

“Tell me what you felt,” he says.

I look down at my hands.

“It felt like the person who painted that girl knew what it meant to be watched and hidden at the same time.”

Ifeanyi says nothing.

I continue.

“It felt like someone had given her space but not freedom. Like the field was wide only because the cage was invisible.”

The words surprise me. I had not known I understood the painting that clearly until I begin to speak.

“The birds bothered me,” I say. “They looked like movement, like migration, like escape. But the shadows betrayed them. They were carrying chains. Or maybe they were casting chains. I couldn’t tell which.”

“That ambiguity was intentional,” Ifeanyi says.

I look at him. “That is what made it powerful.”

He nods slowly.

“And threatening.”

“Yes.”

He exhales. “The attendant was not protecting an artwork. He was protecting access to its meaning.”

A chill moves through me. Access to its meaning. That is exactly it.

I turn in my seat, looking back through the rear windscreen as if the gallery might still be visible. It is not. Only traffic. 

I think of Sammy in custody, trapped inside a story arranged by people who needed him guilty before he could speak. I think of Rakiya missing, her name floating between police suspicion and frightened silence. I think of the painting, unsigned except for a letter, guarded as if the girl in it might escape if too many eyes understood her.

“What if she painted herself?” I ask.

Ifeanyi glances at me again. “That is where your mind has gone?”

“Yes.”

“Why?”

“Because the painting didn’t feel invented.” I pause, searching for the truest words. “It felt confessed.”

The word changes the air in the vehicle.

Confessed. Ifeanyi’s smile disappears.

For a while, he drives without speaking. Then he says, “There are artists who reveal more than they intend. Especially the untrained ones.”

I turn toward him. “Untrained?”

“That brushwork did not come from academic discipline.”

“You noticed?”

“Of course.” His voice is almost dry.

“The composition is instinctive. Powerful, but not conventionally trained. The emotional intelligence is stronger than the technical restraint. Whoever painted it has seen things she has not yet learned how to disguise professionally.”

She. He said she. I notice.

He notices that I notice.

“I said she because of the subject,” he adds.

But I do not think that is the only reason.

“And the signature?” I ask.

“R?”

“Yes.”

“It is not the signature of an established artist.”

“What is it then?”

“A trace.”

I frown. “A trace?”

“Someone trying to exist without being fully seen.”

The words enter me with quiet force. Someone trying to exist without being fully seen. Rakiya again. The thought returns more strongly this time.

I remember the way Sammy spoke of her gift. Not as entertainment. Not as a hobby. As burden. As something that had come with visions, fear, and an urgency that unsettled even him. I remember how he had wanted to protect her, and how quickly protection had been turned into accusation.

“Rakiya,” I whisper.

I do not mean to say it aloud. But I do.

Ifeanyi hears. His eyes remain on the road, but his posture changes.

“There it is,” he says quietly.

I look at him.

“The name you were avoiding.”

“I was not avoiding it.”

“You were waiting for courage.”

I want to deny it, but I cannot. Because he is right.

Saying her name beside that painting makes the suspicion more real. More dangerous. As long as it remained a feeling, I could excuse it. Now it has a face.

“Rakiya may not be the artist,” I say quickly.

“No.”

“But if she is…”

“Then she is not merely missing,” Ifeanyi says.

I finish the thought in my mind. She is hidden. Guarded. Displayed without being revealed. The idea makes my stomach tighten.

“How does a missing girl’s painting end up in a private Abuja gallery?” I ask.

“That is the question.”

“And why would staff panic over one photograph?”

“That is another.”

“And who gave them that instruction?”

“That,” Ifeanyi says, “is the question behind the questions.”

The vehicle turns into a quieter road lined with trees. The afternoon light falls in broken pieces across the dashboard. For the first time since leaving the gallery, I realize my heart is beating too fast.

I close my eyes briefly. Lord, let me not imagine what is not there. But another prayer rises beneath it. Lord, let me not miss what is there because I am afraid.

When I open my eyes, Ifeanyi is watching the road with unusual concentration.

“What happens now?” I ask.

“Now we do not rush.”

“That sounds like delay.”

“It is not. It is discipline.”

I say nothing.

He continues, “If the artist is protected, watched, or controlled, asking direct questions too early may shut the door. We need to know who owns the gallery, who submitted the painting, who priced it, who requested no photographs, and who benefits from keeping R anonymous.”

I listen carefully.

“And if it is Rakiya?” I ask.

He does not answer immediately.

Then he says, “Then the art exhibition may not be a detour from your mission.” He looks at me briefly. “It may be the mission.”

The words settle over me. Suddenly, the road ahead feels different.

As though Abuja itself has shifted, revealing another city beneath the one I entered – a quieter city of galleries, collectors, handlers, private rooms, and young gifted people whose pain can be framed, priced, and protected by people who do not want them free.

I look down at my hands. They are still trembling slightly. I press them together.

“Instinct often arrives before evidence,” Ifeanyi says, almost to himself.

I look at him.

He does not smile now. “But evidence is what keeps instinct alive long enough to become truth.”

I turn back to the window.

The city slides past, bright and controlled. But somewhere behind its polished walls, a girl may be painting captivity from memory. And for the first time since Rakiya disappeared, I feel she is no longer only absent.

She is speaking. Not with her mouth… with colour… with shadows… with birds that cannot fly away without dragging chains behind them.

































































EIGHTEEN





The knock comes shortly after eight o’clock. I have spent most of the evening reviewing notes from the gallery visit. Names. Questions. Possibilities. Fragments of conversation.

The painting continues to occupy my thoughts, even though I am no longer standing before it. It follows me back to the hotel room and settles somewhere behind my eyes, refusing to leave. Every time I close my notebook, I see the girl again — beneath the migrating birds, standing in that wide field that looks like freedom until the shadows turn into chains.

I have written the letter R several times on a blank page.

I close my notebook, slide it beneath one of the hotel magazines on the table, and rise slowly. My first instinct is not to open the door. Abuja has taught me too much in two days. Or perhaps life has. I walk quietly to the door and look through the peephole.

A hotel attendant stands outside. Behind him, a porter waits with a small trolley.

I open the door, but not widely.

“Good evening, ma.”

“Good evening.”

The attendant smiles politely. “A package was left for you.”

I frown. “For me?”

“Yes, ma.”

I look past him to the trolley. The object on it is large. Rectangular. Wrapped in protective brown paper and tied with an elegant ribbon. Immediately, I know it is not ordinary luggage.

“Who left it?”

The attendant’s smile remains professional. “I was only instructed to deliver it personally.”

“Instructed by whom?”

He hesitates, just enough for me to notice. “There was no sender’s name, ma.”

My fingers tighten on the edge of the door.

The porter looks from me to the attendant, then back to the package, as if he wants no part in whatever this is.

The attendant lifts a small envelope.

“This came with it.”

I take it before allowing them in.

The porter carefully wheels the object into the room and positions it near the centre, where the ceiling light falls across the brown wrapping. The package seems too large for the room, not physically, but in the way it changes the atmosphere.

A mystery has entered.

The attendant signs something on his tablet, nods, and steps back. “Good night, ma.”

I wait until they leave.

Then I lock the door. The room becomes very quiet. The package occupies the centre of the room like a question waiting for courage. I do not touch it immediately.

Instead, I stand there, listening. To the faint hum of the air-conditioner. To distant movement in the corridor. To my own heartbeat.

I tell myself not to be dramatic. It is only a package. A wrapped object. A hotel delivery. But caution has become a second skin, and it does not come off simply because the ribbon looks expensive.

I open the envelope first. Inside is a handwritten note. Four words. “Trust your instincts.” No signature. None is necessary.

I laugh softly. Only one person I know communicates like that.

Ifeanyi.

The laughter loosens the room a little, but not completely. Ifeanyi’s gesture is generous, yes, but generosity from a man like him is rarely simple. He does not send objects merely because they are beautiful. He sends them because he wants me to see something.

Curiosity overcomes caution. I untie the ribbon. Remove the wrapping. Then carefully lift away the protective covering. The moment I see it, I freeze.

The painting. 

The girl beneath the birds. The same impossible sadness. The same distant light beyond the horizon. The same rooted feet. The same shadows shaped like chains. The same signature… R.

For several seconds, I simply stare. Disbelief gives way to excitement. Excitement gives way to wonder. Then finally, to gratitude so sudden it unsettles me. Ifeanyi bought it… or borrowed it. Or did whatever people like him do when they decide that an object must move from one controlled room into another.

I walk closer.

The painting looks even more remarkable outside the gallery.

In the gallery, it had been surrounded by silence, glass, lighting, attendants, and hidden rules. Here, in my hotel room, stripped of that guarded atmosphere, it feels more intimate. More exposed. Almost vulnerable.

Every brushstroke seems intentional. Every colour carries emotion. The details I had missed earlier begin to reveal themselves. Tiny symbols hidden within the landscape.

A crooked line near the girl’s wrist that might be a bracelet or a restraint. A shape in the grass that could be a key, half-buried. A faint mark near the horizon, almost like a door drawn into light. Messages concealed inside imagery.

The work feels alive. Almost conversational.

As though the artist has left fragments of herself embedded within the canvas, trusting that someone, somewhere, will learn how to read them.

I find myself smiling. Not because of the gift. Though the gesture is generous. But because holding the painting feels strangely significant.  As though a missing piece of a puzzle has quietly found its way into my hands.

I move the painting beside the window.

The city lights reflect softly across its surface. Abuja glows beyond the glass — controlled, expensive, official. Streetlights trace the roads. Cars move like small beads of light beneath the night. Somewhere in this city, people are dining, negotiating, hiding, searching, lying, praying.

And perhaps somewhere in this same city, the person who painted this is waiting to be found. I sit on the edge of the bed and study the canvas. The birds. The chains. The horizon. The girl. And those eyes. The eyes trouble me most. Because the longer I look at them, the more certain I become. I know them. Somewhere. Somehow.

Not in the exact way one remembers a face from a photograph. Not even in the way one recognizes a person from a crowd. This is deeper and more uncertain. I know the sorrow behind them. The waiting. The silent appeal.

The discipline of someone who has learned not to ask directly for rescue because direct cries may have once been punished, ignored, or used against her.

A chill travels through me.

Rakiya.

The name rises again. This time, I do not push it away.

I remember Sammy speaking about her with a tenderness that had surprised me. He had not spoken of her as someone he pitied. He had spoken of her as someone gifted, strange, fragile, and frighteningly necessary. I remember the way he described her visions, her drawings, her silence, the left-handed strokes, the feeling that she was seeing what others refused to see.

Those memories had sounded scattered when he first told me. Now they begin to gather around the canvas. I stand and move closer again. I study the lower left corner. The single letter sits there quietly… R.

It is not decorative. It is not proud. It looks almost hidden. A trace. Someone trying to exist without being fully seen. Ifeanyi had said that.

And now the words return with weight. I touch the Cross pendant at my neck, then stop myself before my fingers can tremble. Lord, is this her?

The question enters the room and remains there. I do not expect an audible answer. But the silence that follows does not feel empty. It feels watchful.

I walk to the table, take my notebook, and open a fresh page.

I write:

Painting delivered — 8 p.m.

Note: Trust your instincts.

Signature: R.

Girl under birds.

Chains in shadows.

Rooted feet.

Hidden key?

Horizon-door?

Eyes familiar.

Then, after a pause, I write:

Could R be Rakiya?

I stare at the question for a long time. Writing it makes the suspicion real. Too real.

My phone lies beside the notebook. I consider calling Ifeanyi, but I stop myself. He sent the painting because he wants me to spend time with it. Because he understands that clues do not always reveal themselves under pressure. Some must be watched until they speak.

So I return to the painting.

I sit on the floor this time, directly in front of it, knees drawn close, chin resting lightly on them. The room around me fades into the low hum of electricity and distant corridor sounds. My fear does not leave, but it changes shape.

It becomes attention. I look at the girl’s eyes again. They are not pleading. That is what strikes me now. They are witnessing.

Whoever painted this did not merely paint suffering. She recorded it. She fixed it in colour so it could not be denied. That is why the gallery attendant reacted. That is why the photograph had to be deleted. A picture of this painting would have allowed someone like me to return to it, enlarge it, study it, ask questions beyond the gallery’s control.

And now the painting is here. With me. Ifeanyi has not only given me a gift. He has placed evidence in my room. A dangerous kindness. I release a slow breath.

Somewhere else in Abuja, perhaps not far from this hotel, perhaps in a place with locked doors, quiet handlers, and people who speak of talent as if it can be owned, a young woman may be wondering whether anyone will ever understand what she has been trying to say.

She may be wondering whether anyone will ever find her. I look again at the eyes. The longer I look, the more certain I become. I have not remembered from where.

Not yet.

But recognition has already begun its work. And if instinct is the first witness before evidence arrives, then tonight, in this hotel room, I know one thing. R is no longer only a letter. R is a voice. And she is speaking through colour.













































NINETEEN





Long after midnight, I am still studying the painting. The city beyond my window has gradually grown quieter, but sleep refuses to come. The longer I study the artwork, the stronger the feeling becomes. 

The artist is speaking. Not through words. Through details. The composition. The colours. The symbolism. The emotions hidden beneath the brushstrokes. The painting feels less like a product and more like evidence. 

When I check the time, it’s one o’clock. I finally reach for my phone.

I stare at Ifeanyi’s name for a moment before pressing the call button. A part of me expects him not to answer. Another part of me, the part that is beginning to understand him, knows he will.

The call connects on the second ring.

“Hello?” His voice sounds remarkably awake.

“Were you sleeping?”

“No.”

I smile. Of course not.

People like Ifeanyi probably negotiate business deals at midnight for recreation.

“I wanted to thank you.”

A brief silence follows.

“For the painting.”

“You’re welcome.”

The simplicity of his response almost annoys me.

Most people would at least acknowledge the significance of buying an expensive artwork and sending it to a woman’s hotel room after a gallery incident.

Not Ifeanyi.

For him, generosity seems to exist most comfortably when ignored.

“It’s beautiful,” I say.

“I know.”

I laugh.

The confidence in his voice is infuriating.

“That is not humility.”

“No.”

“It isn’t.”

For a moment, neither of us speaks.

I look at the painting again. The girl beneath the birds seems different under the hotel light, as if she is listening to my side of the conversation.

“I’d like to see you,” I say.

His response comes immediately. “Tomorrow?”

“Tomorrow.”

“Breakfast?”

“Breakfast.”

“Done.”

The call ends.

Yet as I lower the phone, another thought surfaces. Something I had discovered earlier while researching galleries and exhibitions online. Something Ifeanyi had neglected to mention. Deliberately.

The realization makes me smile. Tomorrow is going to be interesting.



The restaurant is quieter than usual this morning. Sunlight streams through enormous windows overlooking the gardens. Business travellers occupy a handful of tables. Waiters move discreetly between guests, carrying coffee and breakfast trays with the calm efficiency of people trained not to overhear anything important.

Ifeanyi is already seated when I arrive. Naturally.

He looks up when I approach, and for a brief second his eyes search my face before settling into their usual calm.

“Good morning.”

“Good morning.”

I settle into the chair opposite him.

The waiter appears.

Orders are placed.

Coffee arrives.

Only then do I decide to begin.

“You lied.”

The statement nearly causes him to choke on his coffee. Not completely. Just enough to make me feel victorious.

His expression remains calm, but one brow lifts. “That is a serious accusation.”

“It is.”

“What did I lie about?”

I fold my arms.

“The exhibition.”

His eyes narrow slightly.

Interesting. Not surprise. Recognition.

“You told me you were an art curator.”

“I am.”

“You neglected to mention that you organize exhibitions.”

Silence.

I smile. Victory. A small one. But still victory.

“I did some research.”

“I can see that.”

The corner of his mouth twitches.

It is the closest thing he ever comes to admitting defeat.

“You organized the exhibition,” I say.

“Partly.”

“Partly?”

“I was one of the organizers.”

I stare at him.

“Why didn’t you tell me?”

The answer comes without hesitation.

“Because it wasn’t necessary.”

I groan.

“There it is.”

“There what is?”

“That thing you do.”

“What thing?”

“Where you decide which information people need.”

His expression remains completely serious. “Most information people request is not necessary.”

I laugh despite myself.

Only Ifeanyi can say something so ridiculous with absolute conviction.

The waiter delivers breakfast.

Neither of us touches it immediately. My attention has already returned to the painting. The real reason I requested the meeting.

I reach into my handbag and remove several photographs I took of the artwork after it arrived in my room. Close-ups. Details. Brushwork. Symbols. Patterns. 

I show them to him. Immediately, his attention sharpens. The guarded host disappears. The investigator emerges.

“What are we looking at?” he asks.

“The artist.”

His eyes move across the photographs.

“The artist isn’t in the photographs.”

“I know.”

“Then what are we looking at?”

I lean forward.

“The painting.”

He nods. Patiently. Waiting.

I point at one section.  “Can a painting reveal information about its creator?”

The question seems to surprise him.

“What kind of information?”

“Anything.” I gesture toward the photographs. “Age. Gender. Handedness. Psychological state. Training. Background. Whether the painter was confident or afraid. Whether the work was planned or rushed. Whether the person knew what she was doing or was simply pouring something out.”

The list continues. The more I speak, the more interested he becomes.

Finally, he sits back and studies me.

“That is why you called this meeting.”

“Yes.”

The answer feels obvious.

“If the artist does not want to be found, perhaps the artwork can tell us who she is.”

For several seconds, he remains silent. Then he smiles. A genuine smile. Not because the idea is amusing. Because he likes it.

“You are serious.”

“Very.”

He looks again at the photographs. Then toward the painting visible on my phone. Then back at me.

Finally, he nods. “There are specialists who do exactly that.”

My pulse quickens.

“There are?”

“Art conservators. Forensic analysts. Authentication experts.” He taps one of the photographs. “Paintings reveal far more than most people realize.”

I feel excitement building. The first real lead. Not intuition. Not coincidence. Evidence.

“What can they reveal?”

He lifts one of the close-up photographs and studies it carefully.

The restaurant continues around us. Cups clink. A waiter laughs softly near the service station. A woman in a navy suit walks past with a folder under her arm. Abuja keeps wearing its morning polish while the canvas between us grows heavier.
