Ifeanyi gathers the photographs and arranges them carefully.

“What do we do?” I ask.

His eyes settle on the image signed with the single letter, R. Then he folds the photographs neatly.

“We ask the painting to introduce us to its artist.”

I lean back.

The phrase should sound theatrical. From him, it sounds like strategy.

“How?”

“First, I speak to a conservator I trust. Not someone inside the gallery’s circle. Someone discreet. Second, we identify the ownership path. Who submitted the work, who priced it, who requested the no-photography restriction, and who benefits from keeping the artist anonymous.”

“And third?”

“Third, you tell me everything Sammy ever told you about Rakiya’s art.”

I nod.

“Everything?”

“Everything. Even the things that sounded unimportant.”

I think back.

Sammy had spoken in fragments. He had been frightened, exhausted, full of guilt and urgency. At the time, I had not known which details mattered. I had listened like a friend, not like an investigator. Now I wish I had written down every word.

“I will try.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says.

I look at him.

“You will remember.”

His certainty unsettles me.

“Memory is not a file I can open because you commanded it.”

“No,” he says. “But memory often returns when the right object stands in front of it.”

His eyes move to my phone, where the painting glows on the screen.

“This is the right object.”

I follow his gaze.

The girl beneath the birds. The shadows shaped like chains. The rooted feet. The horizon that looks like a promise and a wound.

For the first time since entering the gallery, I feel as though we are no longer chasing a ghost. We are following a trail. And the trail is painted in colour.











































































TWENTY





When I enter the hotel lobby, he is already waiting near the entrance, scrolling through messages on his phone. He looks up before I reach him, as if he has sensed my approach before seeing me.

“You look excited.”

“I am.”

He smiles.

“Good. You’ll need that energy.”

“Where are we going?”

“You’ll see.”

I have learned by now that asking Ifeanyi for information before he is ready to provide it is usually a waste of effort.

Abuja is already awake by the time we leave the hotel.

The roads pulse with movement. Civil servants hurry toward government offices. Commercial vehicles dart between lanes. Street vendors arrange goods beneath colourful umbrellas. The city seems determined to accomplish everything before noon.

I sit beside him with my handbag on my lap and the memory of the painting still alive in my mind. The girl beneath the birds. The shadows shaped like chains. The single letter… R.

After nearly thirty minutes, the SUV turns into a quiet district lined with mature trees and detached buildings hidden behind high walls. The neighbourhood feels older, more established. The kind of place where institutions rather than businesses occupy the properties.

Eventually, we stop before a two-storey cream-coloured building partially concealed behind flowering bougainvillea.

There is no flashy sign. No advertising. Only a modest brass plaque mounted beside the gate. ADEBAYO INSTITUTE FOR ART CONSERVATION AND VISUAL RESEARCH.

The name sounds important. The building looks indifferent to whether anyone notices.

Ifeanyi glances at me. “Still excited?”

“More curious now.”

“That is better.”

A security man opens the gate after recognizing him, and we drive into a small compound shaded by old trees. The quiet here is different from the quiet of the hotel. The hotel’s quiet is polished, manufactured, arranged for comfort. This quiet has history in it.

Inside, the atmosphere reminds me of a library. Or a museum. Or perhaps both.

The air carries a faint scent of paper, wood polish and aged canvas. Shelves line the walls. Books occupy every available space. Paintings rest on easels. Framed artworks lean carefully against partitions awaiting restoration. Several young researchers work quietly at long tables covered with photographs and catalogues.

Nobody seems interested in impressing visitors. Everyone appears interested in preserving history. 

A woman greets Ifeanyi warmly. “Mr. Obiajulu.”

“Good morning.”

She smiles. “Professor Adebayo is expecting you.”

I follow him upstairs.

The professor’s office occupies an entire corner of the building. Sunlight streams through tall windows overlooking a garden. Bookshelves stretch from floor to ceiling. Paintings cover nearly every wall. The room belongs to someone who has spent decades studying art and has never once considered retirement.

Professor Adebayo rises to greet us.

He looks to be in his late sixties. His silver hair contrasts sharply against his dark complexion. Half-moon glasses rest low on his nose. His eyes possess the curious alertness of a man who genuinely enjoys solving puzzles.

“Mr. Obiajulu.”

“Professor.”

The two men shake hands.

“You brought the painting?”

Ifeanyi nods.

The wrapped canvas is carried in and carefully placed on a large examination table. I stand beside it, suddenly aware that I am no longer looking at the painting as a troubled admirer in a hotel room.

Here, it becomes something else. A subject. A specimen. A witness.

Professor Adebayo puts on cotton gloves. The overhead lighting changes. A magnifying lamp is positioned nearby. Several instruments appear from drawers. One of the assistants adjusts the blinds until the room holds the kind of controlled brightness that exposes rather than beautifies.

The atmosphere shifts. The painting is no longer decoration. It is evidence.

For nearly twenty minutes, Professor Adebayo says nothing. He studies, measures, observes, photographs and occasionally, he makes notes. The silence becomes almost unbearable.

I watch his face for clues, but he gives away very little. He moves around the canvas slowly, bending close, straightening, tilting his head, adjusting the lamp, then returning to a section as if the painting has whispered something he wants repeated.

Ifeanyi stands near the window, arms folded, expression unreadable. I remain closer to the table, unable to stop searching the girl’s painted eyes. 

Finally, the professor steps back. “What would you like to know?”

I speak before Ifeanyi can answer. “Can a painting tell us anything about its artist?”

The professor smiles. “Everything.”

I blink.

“Everything?”

“Almost.”

He gestures toward the canvas. “Art is one of the few things human beings create that inevitably carries traces of its creator.”

My pulse quickens. This is exactly what I have hoped.

The professor points toward a section of the sky. “The artist has a developed hand.”

I exchange a glance with Ifeanyi.

“What does that mean?”

“It means she is not merely throwing colour at emotion,” he says. “There is control here. Layering. Blending. Depth. Repeated practice. Someone has either guided her, or she has spent considerable time training herself through observation and repetition.”

He points again.

“But this is not the stiffness of a formally drilled academy painter. There is something informal in the training. Something private. Instinct is still leading the hand more than rules.”

I lean forward.

“So she may not have studied art formally?”

“Not necessarily,” he replies. “At least, not in the conventional way. But she has been developed. There is a difference between raw talent and a hand that has been watched, corrected, encouraged, or used.”

The last word settles strangely. Used.

Professor Adebayo adjusts his glasses. “Someone invested time in this person’s ability.”

He moves toward another section.

“The artist is relatively young.”

“How young?”

“Probably between late adolescence and early adulthood.”

I feel my breathing change.

The profile is already becoming familiar. Then he surprises us.

“The artist is likely female.”

I stare at him.

“How do you know that?”

The professor smiles patiently.

“I do not know.”

The distinction matters.

“I infer.”

He taps the air just above the painting, careful not to touch the surface.

“The emotional vocabulary. The recurring themes. The symbolic treatment of confinement. The portrayal of vulnerability from inside the experience rather than outside it. These are not proofs. Only indicators.”

Still, the indicators are aligning.

The professor becomes increasingly interested. He moves closer to the canvas and studies the brushwork with a concentration that makes the room feel smaller. Then he pauses.

A long pause.

Long enough to attract even Ifeanyi’s full attention.

“What is it?” I ask.

For several seconds, he does not answer.

Then he points toward several sweeping brushstrokes crossing the lower section of the painting.

“Interesting.”

My heartbeat quickens.

“What?”

The professor smiles.

“Your artist is left-handed.”

The room becomes completely silent.

I look at Ifeanyi. He looks at me. Neither of us speaks.

The professor continues.

“Notice the directional flow. The pressure pattern. The natural angle of application.”

He demonstrates the movement with his hand.

“This was not done by a right-handed painter imitating a left-handed technique. There are places where the movement is too natural, too repeated, too consistent.”

He looks up. “This person paints naturally with the left hand.”

A memory flashes through my mind. A quiet girl. Sketchbooks. Paint smudges. A pencil held in the left hand.

The flash is not complete enough to become certainty, but it is sharp enough to disturb me.

My pulse begins racing.

The professor is not finished.

“There is something else.”

His voice has changed. More serious.

He examines the painting again, then looks directly at us. “This artist paints from memory.”

I frown. “Meaning?”

He is silent for several moments, choosing his words carefully.

“Many artists paint ideas. Some paint imagination. Some paint observation.”

He gestures toward the girl standing beneath the birds. “This artist paints experience.”

The room feels suddenly smaller.

The professor’s finger traces several hidden elements embedded within the composition without touching the canvas. The shadows. The chains. The positioning of the horizon. The expression in the girl’s eyes. The almost buried key near the roots. The faint doorway suggested in the far light. 

“None of it is accidental, he concludes, “Whoever created this work understands captivity.”

The statement lands heavily. Not metaphorical captivity. Not literary captivity. Captivity.

I feel the word settle into my chest.

I think of the first time I saw the painting in the gallery. I had not known the artist, but I had felt the pain before I understood it. The girl had stood in a field wide enough to pretend it was freedom, yet every detail had insisted she could not move. I had felt watched by the wound behind her eyes.

Now Professor Adebayo is giving language to what my spirit had recognized before my mind could defend it.

He removes his glasses.

“I have spent forty years studying artists,” he says.

His gaze returns to the painting. “This one is telling a story.”

The room falls silent.

Outside, birds move through the garden beyond the windows. Inside, nobody speaks. Because for the first time, the painting has stopped being only a clue. It has become testimony.

I look again at the single letter in the corner… R. Small. Careful. Almost hidden. A trace, Ifeanyi had called it. Someone trying to exist without being fully seen.

“Professor,” I ask, my voice lower now, “if the artist is being controlled, can the painting show that?”

He looks at me carefully.

“It cannot prove control,” he says. “But it can reveal pressure. Repetition of confinement. Fear of exposure. Hidden symbols. A pattern of speaking indirectly. Artists who cannot speak plainly often learn to encode.”

Ifeanyi steps away from the window. “Encode?”

“Yes.” The professor gestures toward the lower portion of the canvas. “Some details are emotional. Some are symbolic. Some may be private language. This is why the painting should be studied alongside other works by the same artist.”

I swallow.

“And if we find other works?”

“Then we may begin to see whether she repeats certain images. Birds. Chains. Roots. Doors. Keys. Horizons she cannot reach. Figures who are watched.”

Ifeanyi’s gaze sharpens. “Watched?”

Professor Adebayo nods. “The girl is not alone in the painting.”

I look quickly at the canvas. My skin prickles.

“Where?”

He directs our attention to the far left of the field, where dark strokes gather near the edge of a tree line. I had thought it was shadow. Now, under the lamp, I see it differently.

Not a person exactly. Not fully. But the suggestion of presence. A shape almost concealed among the darkness. Someone watching.

The air leaves my lungs slowly.

“I didn’t see that.”

“You were not supposed to see it first,” the professor says. “You were supposed to feel it first.”

My eyes sting. Rakiya.

The name rises again, stronger than before. I do not say it immediately. But Ifeanyi hears it in the silence. Or perhaps he has begun to think it too.

He looks at Professor Adebayo.

“If we brought you another sample, could you compare it?”

“Yes.”

“What kind of sample?”

“Sketches. Handwritten notes. Studies. Marginal drawings. Anything made by the suspected artist.”

I think of Sammy. Of the things he had told me. Of Rakiya’s drawings and visions. Of the scattered pieces I had not known how to value when I first heard them.

“I may know where to get something,” I say.

Ifeanyi turns to me. “From Sammy?”

“Maybe. Or from the people who kept her before she disappeared.”

The thought chills me as soon as I say it.

The professor watches both of us now with a different expression. Less academic. More cautious. “What exactly are you investigating?”

Neither of us answers immediately.

How do we explain it? A missing girl. A framed boy. A dead student whose memoir contains symbols. A secret fraternity. A political machine. An artist known only as R. A painting that behaves like testimony.

Ifeanyi answers first. “A person hidden behind a painting.”

Professor Adebayo studies him. Then studies me. “And this person may be in danger?”

“Yes,” I say.

The answer comes before I can soften it.

The professor nods once. Slowly. “Then do not approach the gallery directly again.”

The warning lands with quiet force.

Ifeanyi’s face does not change, but I see the confirmation in his eyes. He has already been thinking the same thing.

Professor Adebayo removes his gloves. “If the artist is protected, there may be a legitimate reason. Privacy. Contract. Trauma. Commercial strategy. But if she is controlled, direct curiosity will frighten whoever controls access.”

He looks at the painting again. “And fear makes handlers careless.”

Handlers. The word is ugly.

I think of the gallery attendant’s reaction when he saw the photograph. The flicker of concern. The sudden insistence on keeping the camera. The photograph deleted under supervision. At the time, the whole incident had embarrassed me.

Now it looks like instruction. A boundary had been drawn. And I had crossed it.

Professor Adebayo steps back from the table.

“My preliminary opinion is this. The artist is young, likely female, left-handed, guided or informally trained, emotionally intense, and painting from lived experience. The work suggests confinement, surveillance, and a desire for release.”

He pauses.

“Whether that is symbolic or literal, you must discover.”

I stare at the girl beneath the birds.

The shadows shaped like chains no longer look merely dramatic. They look like evidence of a life.

Somewhere beyond the canvas, beyond the brushstrokes, beyond the signature marked only with the letter R, a young woman is waiting to be found.

I look at Ifeanyi. His expression tells me everything.

We are no longer searching for a possibility. We are searching for Rakiya.

















































TWENTY-ONE





Ifeanyi looks at me for a little too long after breakfast. Not rudely. Not openly. But long enough for me to notice that he is assessing something.

I have begun to recognize that look in him. Ifeanyi does not merely see people. He arranges them in contexts. He measures rooms, entrances, silences, clothes, names, gestures, and the invisible power of appearances. Perhaps it comes from his work as a curator. Perhaps from being an Obiajulu. Perhaps from moving too long among people who can tell a person’s usefulness by the cut of her dress and the confidence of her shoes.

I shift slightly in my seat.

“What?” I ask.

He blinks, then smiles faintly. “Nothing.”

“That is not true.”

“It is not nothing,” he admits. “But it is also not an insult.”

“That sounds like the beginning of an insult.”

He laughs softly.

“No. It is the beginning of preparation.”

“For what?”

“For tomorrow.”

“The exhibition?”

“Yes.”

I look down at myself.

I am wearing one of the dresses I packed from school, decent, clean, and modest enough for travel. There is nothing wrong with it. At least, not in my world. But Ifeanyi’s world, I am beginning to learn, does not merely ask whether something is wrong. It asks whether it is positioned.

“You think I am not properly dressed for the exhibition,” I say.

“I think you are dressed like a sincere person who has never attended this kind of exhibition.”

I frown.

“What does that mean?”

“It means you are dressed truthfully.”

“And that is a problem?”

“In ordinary life, no. In certain rooms, truth must be well-presented before people decide whether they will listen to it.”

I stare at him. “That sounds manipulative.”

“It is social reality.”

“I thought we were going there to see artworks.”

“We are,” he says. “But artworks are rarely the only things on display at exhibitions.”

His answer unsettles me.

He leans back and watches the movement of guests through the hotel lobby. We have moved from the restaurant after breakfast and now sit in a quiet lounge area near the glass wall. Outside, cars arrive and depart beneath the hotel entrance canopy. Inside, the lobby carries that polished hush of money and movement: wheels of suitcases gliding over marble, perfume drifting through cold air, people speaking softly into phones as if every call involves contracts.

“Tomorrow,” he continues, “you will meet collectors, gallery representatives, curators, investors, art enthusiasts, people from ministries, foreign guests, journalists, and opportunists dressed as patrons.”

“Opportunists?”

He looks at me.

“Every industry has them.”

I think of Sammy’s warning. Merchants. Predators who find talent at the gate of hunger.

“So what do clothes have to do with it?” I ask.

“Everything and nothing.”

“That is not an answer.”

“It is. Clothes will not give you substance. But poor presentation can make shallow people dismiss your substance before you speak.”

I say nothing.

He softens his tone.

“Lizzy, you are coming into the room as my guest. People will ask questions. They may not ask them aloud, but they will ask them with their eyes. Who is she? Why is she with him? What does she know? What does she want? Is she a collector? A client? A journalist? A friend? A liability?”

The word stings.

“I am not a liability.”

“I know.”

“Then why say it?”

“Because they may not know. And because I refuse to let people treat you carelessly because I did not prepare you properly.”

The sincerity in his voice quiets me.

I look away.

There are ways men can make provision feel like control. I had learned to be suspicious of them. But Ifeanyi’s tone does not carry possession. It carries responsibility. Still, responsibility from a man can warm the heart dangerously if one is not careful.

“What exactly are you suggesting?” I ask.

“Shopping.”

“No.”

“Yes.”

“No, Ifeanyi.”

He smiles. “I expected resistance.”

“I came to Abuja to help Sammy, not to become a mannequin.”

“You came to Abuja to help Sammy, and tomorrow the road to helping Sammy may pass through a room where how you appear will affect how people receive you.”

“I have clothes.”

“You have clothes for school, church, travel, and respectable visits to clergy homes.”

I fold my arms. “And what is wrong with that?”

“Nothing. Unless you intend to interrogate an art ecosystem wearing the emotional equivalent of a Sunday school certificate.”

I try not to laugh. I fail.

He stands. “Come.”

“Where?”

“To correct the emergency.”

“It is not an emergency.”

“It is. You just don’t know the category.”

Within thirty minutes, I am seated in the back of the Prado, driving through Abuja with Ifeanyi beside me, his assistant in front, and my mind arguing with itself.

This is unnecessary. This is thoughtful. Is this not too much.

The city unfolds through the window in broad roads, clean medians, imposing buildings, gated estates, and plazas that look as though they exist for people who have already solved the problems of ordinary survival. Abuja has a way of making poverty feel impolite. Even the silence of its wealthy spaces carries exclusion.

We stop at a fashion boutique inside a high-end shopping complex.

I pause at the entrance. Glass doors. Soft lighting. Mannequins in elegant dresses. Perfume in the air. Attendants with professional smiles. Clothes arranged not by need, but by mood.

I suddenly feel like turning back. Ifeanyi notices.

“You are safe.”

“You keep saying that.”

“Because you keep needing to hear it.”

I look at him.

He does not smile this time.

“Nothing will be forced on you. If you do not like anything, you say so. If you want to leave, we leave.”

That helps.

A stylist attends to us almost immediately. Ifeanyi steps back after a brief instruction, allowing the woman to speak to me directly.

“She needs something for an art exhibition,” he says. “Elegant. Intelligent. Not loud. She must look like she belongs in the room without looking like she is trying to prove it.”

The stylist looks at me with renewed interest. I wish he had not described me so accurately.

She brings out dresses, jumpsuits, and two-piece outfits in soft, rich colours. I reject the first three quickly. Too fitted. Too exposed. Too dramatic. The fourth makes me pause: a deep emerald wrap dress with clean lines, modest neckline, and a fall that moves gracefully without clinging. It is beautiful in a way that does not beg.

The stylist holds it up. “This will be lovely on you.”

I look at Ifeanyi.

He has been studying a display of scarves, giving me space. But when he turns and sees the dress, something flickers across his face. Approval. Then memory. Then restraint.

“It suits you,” he says simply.

That is all. But it is enough to make my pulse misbehave.

I try it on.

When I step out of the fitting room, the stylist smiles before speaking. That frightens me more than criticism.

Ifeanyi turns.

For one brief moment, he forgets to hide his reaction. Not desire. Not exactly. A softening. As if the room has shown him someone he expected and feared to see. 

Then he recovers. “That one,” he says.

I look in the mirror.

The woman staring back at me does not look like the tired Lizzy who cancelled a holiday, wept beside her bed, argued with Judith, and travelled to Abuja with fear folded inside her handbag.

She looks composed. Older. Almost luminous.

I touch the Cross pendant at my chest. It rests perfectly against the dress. The sight unsettles me.

“You don’t like it?” Ifeanyi asks.

“I do.”

“Then why do you look like you are about to apologize to it?”

I smile faintly.

“I’m not used to this.”

“To dresses?”

“To being taken care of like this.”

The words escape before I can stop them.

The air between us changes.

The stylist quietly moves away to adjust another outfit.

Ifeanyi’s face becomes serious. “Lizzy, this is not charity.”

“I didn’t say it was.”

“And it is not…”

He pauses.

The unfinished sentence says too much. Not romance. Not courtship. Not whatever your mind is trying to make it.

I look away quickly. “I understand.”

But do I?

After the dress come shoes. Then a small clutch. Then a simple pair of earrings.

I protest after each item until Ifeanyi finally says, “You are allowed to say thank you without fighting for your dignity.”

That silences me.

“I don’t want to owe you.”

“You don’t.”

“People always say that before they start collecting repayment in ways they did not name.”

His expression changes.

The remark has come from somewhere in me deeper than the boutique. He understands enough not to take offence.

“I am not people,” he says quietly.

“That is what everyone thinks.”

For a moment, we stand among expensive fabrics and polished mirrors, both of us carrying histories that make kindness complicated.

Then he nods. “Fair.”

He pays without drama. That almost makes it worse.

Men who perform generosity can be resisted easily. They announce the price of their kindness before asking for loyalty. Ifeanyi does not. He gives his card, signs, collects receipts, and moves on as if the expense is merely part of the day’s logistics.

But to me, it is not logistics. It is attention. And attention, when one has been tired and afraid for too long, can begin to resemble affection.

After the boutique, I think we are done. I am wrong.

“We have one more stop,” he says.

“No.”

“Yes.”

“Ifeanyi.”

“Salon.”

I stare at him.

“My hair is fine.”

“Your hair is school-fine.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means it is tidy enough for a lecture hall and insufficient for the warfare of elegant women at an art exhibition.”

I laugh despite myself.

“You are impossible.”

“I have been told.”

The salon is in a quieter part of the city, inside a building that smells of shampoo, hair products, nail polish, coffee, and something floral. Women sit beneath dryers. Stylists move with combs, brushes, pins, and professional confidence. Soft music plays from hidden speakers.

Ifeanyi speaks to the salon manager like someone who has been here before. That fact bothers me for reasons I do not want to examine.

A woman named Sandra takes over.

She washes my hair gently, conditions it, and begins working it into a soft, elegant style that lifts my face without making me feel disguised. Another artist offers light makeup.

“No heavy makeup,” I say quickly.

Sandra smiles. “Don’t worry. We will not change your face. We will only remind it to show up.”

I laugh.

Even Ifeanyi, seated at a distance with his phone, smiles.

For almost two hours, I sit while women transform me in stages. Hair. Brows. Light foundation. Soft colour. A subtle gloss. Nothing loud. Nothing artificial.

Yet with each touch, I feel a version of myself emerge that I have not made room for in years. Not vain. Not worldly. Not unserious. Just beautiful.

The word embarrasses me even inside my own mind. 

When Sandra finally turns the chair toward the mirror, I become still. I do not look like someone else. That is the strange thing. I look like myself relieved of exhaustion.

The grief is still there in my eyes. The burden too. But it has been framed differently, softened without being erased.

Ifeanyi comes closer, then stops at a respectful distance. For a moment, he says nothing.

My heart becomes stupid again.

Then he says, “Tomorrow, no one will dismiss you before you speak.”

Not beautiful. Not stunning. Not any of the careless compliments men sometimes throw like nets. Something better. Strategic. Respectful.

Yet the way he says it makes it feel dangerously intimate.

“Thank you,” I say softly.

“You are welcome.”

On the drive back to the hotel, I call Nkeonyere.

She answers with her usual warmth.

“How is Abuja treating you?”

“Your brother is spoiling me.”

“Oh?”

“He took me shopping. Salon. Makeup. Everything.”

Nkeonyere bursts into laughter. “That sounds like Ifeanyi.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means he does not know how to do small when he has decided something matters.”

“I don’t know what I am going to do when I return to school.”

“Return and face your lectures like a humble child of God.”

“Nkay.”

She laughs again. “Relax. He is preparing you for his world. Ifeanyi understands presentation. Don’t read too much into it.”

I become quiet. Too quiet.

Nkeonyere notices. “Lizzy.”

“Yes?”

“Don’t read too much into it.”

“I heard you.”

“But also don’t be afraid of kindness because other men abused confusion.”

Her words reach too accurately.

I swallow.

“I’m trying.”

“I know.”

After the call ends, I sit by the hotel window and look out over Abuja.

My shopping bags are arranged beside the wardrobe. My new dress hangs carefully, still covered in protective nylon. My hair smells faintly of the salon. My face, freshly washed now, still carries the memory of being handled gently.

I should rest. Instead, I call Bitty.

He answers almost immediately. “Lizzy. Are you okay?”

“I’m fine.”

“How is Abuja?”

“Different.”

“And Ifeanyi?”

I hesitate.

“Helpful.”

“What does helpful mean?”

“He has started making enquiries. We will attend the art exhibition tomorrow.”

“Art exhibition?”

“Yes. He thinks it may help me understand the kind of world Rakiya’s gift belongs to.”

Bitty is quiet.

“What else?”

I should avoid it. I do not.

“He took me shopping today. For the exhibition. And to the salon.”

Silence.

Then, “He did what?”

I lean back.

“There is nothing in it.”

“I didn’t say there was.”

“You went quiet.”

“I am allowed to process information.”

I almost smile. “Bitty.”

“I don’t know this man.”

“Nkeonyere trusts him.”

“Nkeonyere is his sister.”

“Rev. Obiajulu trusts him enough to send me to him.”

“That is better.”

“But?”

Another silence.

“But be careful.”

“I am.”

“I mean it, Lizzy.”

“I know.”

His voice softens.

“I’m not trying to control you.”

“I know.”

“I just…”

He stops.

“What?”

“I have already seen too much damage around you. I don’t want another complicated man adding confusion to your life.”

The words land gently but heavily. Another complicated man. I look at the dress hanging near the wardrobe.

“Ifeanyi is not like that.”

“You have known him for how many hours?”

“Long enough to know he is carrying something.”

“That is exactly what I mean.”

I close my eyes.

“I can’t do this now, Bitty.”

“I’m sorry.”

We are both silent.

Then he says, “How are you, really?”

The question undoes the irritation.

“I’m tired.”

“Then sleep.”

“I will.”

“Send me your hotel details.”

“I already sent them to Nkay.”

“Send them to me too.”

“Bitty—”

“Please.”

His voice is quiet. Not jealous now. Worried.

I send them after the call. Then I sit on the bed for a long time, staring at the shopping bags.

Nkeonyere says not to read too much into it. Bitty says to be careful. My own heart says nothing clearly.

It only replays small moments. Ifeanyi noticing my fear at the boutique entrance, stepping back so the stylist can speak to me directly, choosing words that give me dignity instead of flattery, paying without making me feel purchased, stopping at a respectful distance after the salon, telling me I will not be dismissed before I speak.

Is that romance? No. Maybe. No. It is care. It is strategy. It is Ikenna’s memory. It is Ifeanyi’s guilt. It is my vulnerability answering to attention too quickly.

I rise and walk to the mirror. The Cross pendant rests against my nightdress now, small and steady. “I came for Sammy,” I whisper to my reflection.

The woman in the mirror looks back at me with eyes that do not fully believe that is all anymore. I turn away.

Tomorrow is the exhibition. Ifeanyi’s world will open. And I need to remember why I entered it.











































TWENTY-TWO





The shopping bags remain in one corner of my hotel room like evidence of another life.The dress for the exhibition hangs from the wardrobe, still covered in protective nylon bag. The shoes are arranged neatly beneath it. The small clutch sits on the table beside the earrings Ifeanyi had insisted were “quiet enough to be intelligent.” Everything about the room suggests preparation for glamour.

But we are not talking about glamour. We are discussing Rakiya.

Ifeanyi has returned after making several calls downstairs. When he steps into the sitting area of the suite, he looks less like the polished curator who took me shopping and more like a man assembling a case inside his head.

He does not sit immediately.

He paces to the window and looks down at Abuja’s evening traffic. From the height of the hotel, the city seems deceptively calm – wide roads, lights gathering slowly, vehicles moving in disciplined streams, buildings rising with official confidence. Abuja has a way of hiding danger beneath order.

“Tell me her story again,” he says.

I look up.

“From where?”

“From the first time you suspected Rakiya’s artwork had entered a gallery system.”

I take a breath. The story feels stranger each time I tell it.

“Sammy said she was painting almost constantly after she recovered. He bought her materials. She had no formal training. She drew from visions, memory, impressions — things she said she saw.”

Ifeanyi turns slightly. “And she signed nothing?”

“Not originally. She did not want people to know.”

“Fear?”

“Yes. Or shame. Maybe both.”

“Then the letter R came from you.”

I hesitate. “I suggested it casually when I saw her works in Ogbomosho. I told her she did not need to put her full name, but that a mark would help identify the work.”

His eyes sharpen, but he does not accuse me. “Good.”

“Good?”

“It means if we find work signed R, and the technique matches what you saw, the timing matters.”

I lean forward. “You think the gallery already has her work?”

“I do not think yet. I am arranging possibilities.”

That is Ifeanyi’s way. He does not like assumptions wearing the clothes of conclusions.

He takes out his tablet and places it on the table between us. Several tabs are open: art directories, gallery registries, international exhibition listings, and a spreadsheet of names I do not recognize.

“I checked the global directory of art galleries,” he says. “At least the public-facing ones. Nothing obvious links Otunba Raymond Fasanu to any gallery.”

Hearing the name in that hotel room makes my stomach tighten. Otunba Raymond Fasanu. The politician whose lost election money had become the rope around Sammy’s neck. The man whose shadow had followed Sammy from campus to Ogbomosho.

“The name may not be there,” I say. “People like that do not always put their own names on things.”

Ifeanyi nods. “Exactly.”

He sits at last. “Tell me about him.”

I clasp my hands together. “I don’t know him personally. Everything I know came from Sammy. Otunba offered him money to run his election campaign through the fraternity’s machinery. Sammy declined. But the money had already entered the wrong hands. Legion took it, or part of it, and when Legion died, the disciples shifted the debt toward Sammy.”

“How much?”

“Two million naira.”

Ifeanyi’s expression does not change. “That is not a large amount to a serious politician.”

“I know.”

“So the money is not the real issue.”

“It is control.”

“And embarrassment,” he adds. “A politician can lose money quietly. He cannot easily tolerate disobedience becoming public within his own network.”

I watch him. He is no longer merely listening. He is mapping power.

“Sammy believed Otunba was connected to the Program,” I say.

“The Merchants of the Ivory Towers.”

“Yes.”

He taps one finger lightly on the table. “A politician with access to students, cult networks, patronage, public funding, youth mobilization, and possibly cultural fronts.” 

His voice is calm. Too calm. “That combination is plausible.”

The word frightens me because it sounds like professional agreement.

“But,” he continues, “plausible is not proof.”

I nod. “I know.”

“We must be careful not to make Otunba the answer simply because he is already the villain in Sammy’s story.”

That checks me. “Are you saying he may not be involved?”

“I am saying a powerful enemy can be guilty of one thing and innocent of another. If we assume too quickly, we may miss the real lead.”

I sit back slowly. That possibility does not comfort me. It makes everything wider. “Who else would want Rakiya?” I ask.

Ifeanyi’s eyes move to the tablet. “Many people, depending on what they saw.”

“She is a girl.”

“She is also a gifted, undocumented, vulnerable artist with no formal representation, no family protection in the city, no contract, no legal guardian properly established, no public identity, and possibly no understanding of the value of her own work.”

He pauses. “That combination attracts predators.”

The word chills me.

“Art predators?”

“Scouts. Dealers. Fake patrons. Religious exploiters. Talent brokers. Gallery middlemen. Collectors who prefer artists they can control. People who know how to turn vulnerability into ownership.”

I think of Sammy’s warning. They study the wound before offering the promise.

“If she ran away from Sammy,” I say slowly, “someone could have picked her up.”

“Yes.”

“But why would she run? Sammy was protecting her.”

“Protection can still feel like confinement to a frightened child.”

I want to resist that. But I know he is right. Rakiya had already fled once. From home. From a forced marriage. From people who called her gift a curse. If she felt panic, she could run again.

“There is another angle,” Ifeanyi says.

“What?”

“What if the person who took her did not abduct her from the beginning? What if she moved voluntarily toward the first person who seemed to understand her art?”

I look at him. “A scout?”

“Possibly.”

“Someone who saw her painting?”

“Or saw the work through someone else.”

He turns the tablet toward me.

“Art does not need the artist to travel first. A photograph can move. A sketch can move. A cheap canvas can pass from one hand to another. Someone may have seen enough to know there was value there.”

My mind returns to Ogbomosho. Rakiya sketching near the window. Rakiya at the institute gate. Rakiya hiding drawings.

“Could someone have followed me from Ogbomosho and seen her?”

“Possibly.”

“Could the same people who followed me to Sammy have taken her?”

“Also possible.”

The room seems to grow smaller.

I press my fingers to my forehead.

“So we have Otunba, unknown scouts, the gallery, possibly Rakiya’s parents, possibly the fraternity, and maybe someone else entirely.”

“Yes.”

“That is not encouraging.”

“It is honest.”

He leans forward.

“Now listen carefully. The worst thing we can do is walk into a gallery and begin asking questions.”

I look up.

“Because if the gallery is clean, we alert them unnecessarily and appear foolish. If the gallery is dirty, we alert them dangerously and lose the trail. If they are only a front, the person behind them disappears before we know his name. And if Rakiya is being kept somewhere, careless inquiries may make them move her.”

The logic settles coldly.

“So what do we do?”

“We gather quietly.”

“How?”

“First, identify the gallery without approaching it directly. Full registered name. Directors. Beneficial owners if possible. Address history. Exhibition records. Artists represented. Recent acquisitions. Who introduced new works. Who handled logistics. Who framed or transported the pieces.”

I stare at him.

“That sounds like an investigation.”

“It is.”

“I thought you were a curator.”

“A good curator investigates provenance.”

“Provenance?”

“The history of an artwork — who made it, who owned it, where it has been, how it entered the market, whether its story is clean or fabricated.”

He holds my gaze.

“With Rakiya, provenance may lead us to the person who touched her work before the gallery did.”

For the first time, the art world begins to look less beautiful and more forensic. A painting is not just a painting. It is evidence. A trail, a transaction and a witness.

“We can start with the exhibition tomorrow,” he says.

My heartbeat quickens.

“And if we find one?”

“We do not react dramatically.”

“Ifeanyi.”

“I mean it. You must control your face.”

“I don’t know if I can.”

“You must.”

His tone is firm now.

“At an exhibition, faces are read. Curators read faces. Dealers read faces. Buyers read faces. Predators read faces. If you see something that looks like Rakiya’s work and panic, you may tell the wrong person what the work is worth to you.”

I swallow.

“Okay.”

“If we find anything, I will ask ordinary industry questions. Not about Rakiya. About the work. Medium. Artist. Representation. Availability. Price. Previous collection. Whether the artist has other works.”

“And if they lie?”

“They will.”

I look at him.

He almost smiles. “People in art sales rarely tell the full truth at first contact. Even honest ones curate the truth.”

“Curate the truth?”

“That is a polite phrase for controlling information.”

I shake my head. “Your world is frightening.”

“So is yours. You just call it fellowship and cultism.”

Despite myself, I smile faintly.

Then his face becomes serious again. “There is one more thing.”

“What?”

“I think we need police involved.”

I stiffen. “The police?”

“Yes.”

“No.”

“Privately.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means not walking into the nearest station and filing a loud missing-person report that may land on the desk of someone already compromised.”

I say nothing.

“It means a trusted officer. Someone discreet. Someone who can check movement, custody records, hotel logs if necessary, transport routes, and possibly help with a controlled inquiry if Rakiya appears linked to a gallery.”

I think of Sammy behind the table at the station. Police custody does not feel like safety.

“It was the police who arrested Sammy.”

“It was police machinery that arrested Sammy. That does not mean every officer is part of the frame-up.”

“How do we know whom to trust?”

“We don’t at first. That is why we choose through relationship, not through complaint.”

“Do you know someone?”

He hesitates.

That hesitation tells me the answer is yes, but not simple.

“I know someone who can help.”

“You do?”

“Yes. The officer that led the team that rescued Sammy at the mountain.”

“Good. We keep that in mind. But we do not contact him now. We need to do our own work first.”

“It is safer than desperation,” he concludes.

I lean back.

“Rakiya may be frightened if police appear.”

“That is why they do not appear first.”

He softens his voice. “Lizzy, private involvement does not mean dragging her into a station. It means building a safety net before we touch the web. If we find her and the people holding her are powerful, what will you do? Beg? Quote Scripture? Call Nkay?”

I look away. The question hurts because it is true.

“You need lawful cover,” he says. “Sammy needs it too. If Rakiya is found alive and can testify that Sammy did not exploit her, it weakens the case against him. But if we mishandle her recovery, the same people can twist it again.”

My stomach tightens.

“So everything depends on finding her.”

“Not everything. But much.” He closes the tablet.

“Tomorrow, we attend the exhibition. We observe. We identify any R-related work. We do not confront. We do not accuse. We do not ask foolish questions.”

“Foolish questions like?”

“Who brought this stolen child’s painting here?”

I almost laugh, but the sound dies quickly.

“What if I see something and cannot control myself?”

“Then you hold your clutch with both hands and step away.”

“That is your strategy?”

“Yes. Elegant panic management.”

This time I laugh. A small laugh. Tired, but real.

He smiles faintly.

The heaviness in the room lifts for a moment, then returns. I look toward the wardrobe where the emerald dress hangs.

“So tomorrow I dress beautifully and pretend not to be looking for a missing girl.”

“Yes.”

“That sounds wrong.”

“It is often how rescue begins in rooms where danger wears perfume.”

Ifeanyi rises. “I will make a few calls tonight. You should rest.”

“And Otunba?”

“I will check him quietly.”

“Please be careful.”

He looks at me. “You are warning me?”

“Yes.”

Something like amusement enters his eyes. “Good. That means you are no longer only afraid of me.”

I do not answer.

Because he is right. My fear has shifted. It has not disappeared. But now, mixed with it, is a troubling concern.

He walks to the door, then stops. “Lizzy.”

“Yes?”

“Tomorrow, whatever you see, remember this: the gallery is not the battlefield. It is the showroom. The real transaction may be somewhere behind it.”

I nod.

He leaves.

After the door closes, I stand alone in the room, listening to the softened hum of the hotel air-conditioning.

My eyes move again to the dress. Then to the Cross pendant in the mirror. Then to my handbag where Ikenna’s memoir rests.

Tomorrow, I will enter Ifeanyi’s world dressed like I belong in it, looking for traces of a girl who painted from visions and vanished into a system that knows how to turn gifts into merchandise. I whisper her name. “Rakiya.”

And for the first time, I understand that finding her will require more than compassion. It will require patience. Strategy. Silence. And the discipline not to scream truth in a room where lies are waiting to hear who knows it.











































TWENTY-THREE





The much-expected art exhibition is underway, and already it is nothing like I imagined. I had expected a quiet gathering of eccentric painters, wealthy collectors, and a few curious spectators discussing beauty over glasses of wine. Instead, the entire place hums with movement, calculation, and controlled urgency. 

The exhibition hall occupies the ground floor of a contemporary glass-fronted building in Abuja’s arts district. From outside, the structure looks deceptively simple – a minimalist block of concrete, steel, and glass framed by carefully manicured lawns and sculptured hedges. But inside, an entirely different world is taking shape.

By seven o’clock in the morning, when I arrived with Ifeanyi for a final inspection of preparations, assistants move hurriedly between rooms, carrying framed paintings wrapped in protective sheets. Electricians adjust spotlights suspended from ceiling tracks. Gallery attendants polish glass display cases until they gleam beneath the white lights. Technicians test projectors and audiovisual installations.

The aroma of fresh paint, wood polish and newly unpacked canvases lingers in the air. Everything appears calm.

Ifeanyi moves through the gallery like a conductor leading an orchestra. 

“That painting goes in Hall B.”

“The spotlight is too harsh.”

“Shift the sculpture thirty centimetres to the left.”

“That label is crooked.”

“Has the collector from Lagos confirmed arrival?”

He seems to notice everything. Nothing escapes his attention.

For the first time, I begin to understand that exhibitions are not merely artistic events. They are military operations disguised as elegance.

Every painting has to be documented. Every artwork insured. He’s also checking through the logs to ensure that every piece is catalogued, collector identified, and every invitation is confirmed and security protocol reviewed.

Weeks of preparation culminate in a few carefully orchestrated hours, and Ifeanyi stands at the centre of it all as though he has memorized every moving part.

A gallery assistant carrying a clipboard rushes past us. “The catalogue books just arrived.”

Ifeanyi nods. “Have them arranged at registration.”

The young man disappears immediately. We continue through the main exhibition hall. The gallery itself is breathtaking.

White walls stretch endlessly beneath soaring ceilings designed to disappear into shadow. The architecture intentionally surrenders attention to the art. Nothing competes with the paintings. Every spotlight has been positioned to direct the eye exactly where the curator intends. Every wall possesses its own rhythm.

Large statement pieces occupy dominant positions. Smaller works create visual pauses between them. Sculptures interrupt the space like punctuation marks within a sentence. The arrangement feels effortless.

Which, Ifeanyi later explains, means months of planning have succeeded. 

“People think exhibitions are about paintings,” he says. 

“They’re not?”

“They’re about stories.”

He stops before a large contemporary canvas.

“The artist tells one story, the curator tells another, the gallery tells another, the collector tells another.”

“And the audience?”

“They make their own stories about the artwork.” He smiles faintly. “An exhibition is simply a room full of competing narratives.”

I look around again, and the hall changes before my eyes. It is no longer only a place of beauty. It is a place of interpretation.

A place where meanings are arranged, priced, contested, elevated, and sometimes buried. As midday approaches, catering vendors arrive.

Tables are dressed in white linen. Wine glasses are arranged in military precision. Name tags appear at registration desks. Media crews begin setting up cameras. Security personnel discreetly assume their positions. 

The transformation is remarkable.

The same hall that looked like a construction site only hours earlier now resembles a cultural cathedral.

I spend most of the afternoon observing.

Sometimes Ifeanyi sends me to check catalogue placements. Sometimes he asks me to confirm whether a label is straight, as if my untrained eyes can be trusted with such details. Sometimes he simply lets me walk through the rooms alone.

I am wearing the emerald dress. The Cross rests against my chest. My hair is pinned softly away from my face, and the earrings Ifeanyi chose catch the light when I turn. I try not to think about the boutique. I try not to remember Sandra turning the salon chair toward the mirror. I try not to replay Nkeonyere’s warning not to read too much into kindness, or Bitty’s quiet caution about complicated men.

I remind myself why I am here. Still, I cannot ignore the strange feeling that the dress has changed how people look at me. No one knows my story, yet fewer eyes dismiss me. 

Attendants speak to me with a kind of assumed relevance. Artists nod when I pause near their work. A journalist mistakes me for a gallery associate and asks where the media desk is. I understand then what Ifeanyi meant.

Presentation does not create substance. But it can prevent shallow people from refusing to see it. 

Guests begin arriving shortly after four o’clock. Diplomats, collectors, artists, academics, journalists, government officials, socialites, investors, students.

The wealthy arrive looking for acquisitions, the curious arrive looking for inspiration, artists arrive hoping for validation, everyone comes seeking something.

At first, the room feels festive. Laughter rises in polished bursts. Glasses clink. Camera flashes flicker. People lean toward paintings with thoughtful faces, as if each canvas has invited them into private conversation.

But as the evening progresses, I discover another side of the art world. Behind every smile is negotiation. Behind every compliment is evaluation. Behind every painting is a market.

Eavesdropping on conversations as I move around, I make out collectors discussing appreciation value, gallery owners discuss representation rights, curators discuss international opportunities and artists discuss visibility.

Journalists search for compelling stories.

The exhibition floor becomes a marketplace of ideas, influence, reputation, and money. Beauty is the attraction. Commerce is the engine.

I watch Ifeanyi navigate the room effortlessly. One moment, he is discussing symbolism with a professor. The next, he is negotiating acquisition terms with a collector. Then he is reassuring a nervous artist. Then he is coordinating press interviews, or greeting a government official. He seems perfectly at home.

Yet beneath his composure, I sense something else. He is not only observing the paintings. He is observing the people. Watching. Listening. Connecting fragments. Following invisible patterns.

And suddenly, I understand why he insisted I attend. The exhibition is not merely about art. It is about access. The art world attracts gifted people. Collectors, sponsors, institutions, patrons and young talents searching for opportunity.

The same ecosystem that nurtures genius can also exploit it. The same doors that open pathways to greatness can open pathways to trafficking. The same networks capable of elevating an artist can also conceal predators. That realization unsettles me.

The paintings hanging around us suddenly feel different. Less like decorations, more like witnesses.

As guests move from one masterpiece to another, sipping wine and discussing creativity, I find myself wondering how many stories remain hidden behind the polished walls of the gallery.

I think of Sammy’s warning. Merchants. Predators who find talent at the gate of hunger.

At the time he had spoken, the word had sounded like a moral category. Here, in this room, it begins to look like a system. A well-dressed system. A fragrant system. A system that knows how to shake hands, sponsor exhibitions, buy silence, praise genius, and own the hands that produce it.

A woman in a gold headwrap stands before an abstract painting and says, “This one will appreciate quickly.”

Beside her, a young artist smiles as though he has been complimented. But I hear the transaction beneath the sentence. This one, not this story. This one.

I move away.

The hall becomes warmer as more guests arrive. The air fills with perfume, wine, expensive fabrics, and the soft impatience of people waiting to be noticed. I see an older collector place his hand too low on a young female artist’s back as he praises her work. She smiles politely, but her shoulders stiffen.

No one else seems to notice. Or perhaps everyone notices and calls it normal.

Then I think of Rakiya. I think of the girl beneath the birds. I think of the hidden watcher Professor Adebayo had shown us inside the painting. I think of the photograph that had been deleted under supervision.

Suddenly, every painting in the room looks as if it may be hiding a cry beneath its colours.

I find Ifeanyi near the registration desk, speaking with a man in a navy suit. He sees me approaching and ends the conversation with practiced ease.

“You’re quiet,” he says.

“I’m learning.”

“That is better than talking.”

“Is that one of your rules?”

“One of my favourites.”

I almost smile.

Then my eyes drift beyond him.

Something catches at the edge of my vision.

A small crowd has gathered near the far end of the hall, where a narrower passage opens into a side gallery. People stand there longer than they do before most of the works. Not many. Just enough to form a quiet knot of attention.

I take one step. Then another.

My heartbeat changes before I understand why.

At the far end of the hall, under a controlled wash of light, hangs a painting signed only with a single letter, R.

The room seems to tilt. It is not the same painting Ifeanyi sent to my room. This one is darker. A narrow room. A girl seated on a chair. A window too high to reach. A bowl on the floor. A wall marked faintly with scratches that look like counting.

At first, the painting appears restrained, almost minimal. Then the details begin to emerge, one by one, each more troubling than the last.

The chair legs cast shadows like prison bars. The window light falls just short of the girl’s feet. The bowl is empty. The scratches on the wall are not decoration. They are days.

My breath catches.

R. Again. Not a letter now.

A voice.

Ifeanyi sees it too.

The colour drains from his face. Not all at once. Just enough for me to know that he understands what I understand.

Our search has brought us to the place where art, memory, and truth are about to collide.

I do not move closer immediately. I cannot.

The exhibition continues around us — glasses clinking, guests murmuring, cameras flashing, collectors valuing, journalists smiling, patrons pretending, predators perhaps watching.

But everything in me narrows to the painting.

Ifeanyi steps beside me. 

His voice is almost too low to hear. “Do not react too much.”

“I’m trying.”

“Someone may be watching who watches this painting.”

The warning slides coldly through me. I remember the gallery attendant. The deleted photograph. The hidden figure in the first painting. The professor’s word.

Handlers.

I force myself to breathe. I let my face settle into the expression of a curious guest admiring a troubling work of art.

But inside me, something has already broken open. This is no longer instinct. It is not coincidence. It is not one mysterious painting. It is a pattern.

And somewhere behind this polished room, behind the controlled light and curated silence, Rakiya is speaking again.

Not with her mouth. With colour. With captivity. With evidence disguised as beauty.

























































TWENTY-FOUR





Guests drift from one section of the gallery to another, their voices blending into a soft murmur beneath the ambient jazz floating from hidden speakers. Glasses clink. Camera flashes punctuate conversations. Curators guide collectors through selected works while journalists move from artist to artist searching for quotes.

The atmosphere is sophisticated. Predictable. Controlled. Until it is not.

I join a crowd that has gathered around a painting titled, The Silent Witness. A breathtaking work. The canvas depicts a field of children standing beneath a storm-dark sky. Their faces are turned toward a distant light beyond the horizon. The symbolism is unmistakable.

Hope. Waiting. Deliverance. I do not initially recognize it because of the style, but there, in the lower corner, is the now-familiar signature, R. The same letter. The same emotional intensity. I feel the familiar tightening in my chest.

Every road seems to lead back to this mysterious artist, R. The invisible painter. The ghost behind the canvases. The girl we suspect may be Rakiya.

“You’re staring too hard.” Ifeanyi appears beside me.

“I’m thinking.”

“No,” he replies quietly. “You’re hoping.”

Before I can respond, his attention shifts.

His expression changes. Not dramatically. Just enough for me to notice.

His eyes have locked onto something across the room.

I follow his gaze.

At first, I see nothing unusual.

Only guests moving in elegant clusters. A sculpture display near the far wall. A waiter passing with a tray of glasses. A woman in gold laughing beside a man whose smile looks more expensive than sincere.

Then I notice the young woman.

She stands near the sculpture display several metres away.

Her back is partly turned toward us.

She wears a simple cream-coloured dress that seems almost out of place among the fashionable guests. Her hair is covered with a dark headscarf. Nothing about her appearance invites attention.

In fact, she looks as though she wants to disappear. Yet something about her posture feels strangely familiar. As though she occupies the room physically while remaining emotionally elsewhere.

“What is it?” I whisper.

Ifeanyi does not answer immediately.

Instead, he says, “Look at her hands.”

I focus.

The young woman holds a programme booklet against her chest. Long fingers. Paint stains. Faint traces of colour embedded beneath her fingernails.

My pulse quickens.

Artists carry their profession in their hands. And those hands look familiar. Too familiar. I remember Professor Adebayo’s voice. Your artist is left-handed. I remember the sweeping strokes beneath the chained birds.

Then she turns.

Only slightly. Enough for me to see her face.

The world seems to stop.

Not because I recognize her completely. Because I recognize the eyes. I have seen those eyes before. Eyes that carry both innocence and grief.

My heart begins hammering. “Rakiya…”

The name escapes my lips before I realize I have spoken aloud. The young woman’s head snaps toward me.

Our eyes meet. And in that instant, I know. The recognition is mutual.

Shock flashes across her face. Followed immediately by fear. Raw fear. Her grip tightens around the programme booklet. For a fraction of a second, hope appears in her eyes. Then it vanishes.

A man steps into view beside her. I have not noticed him before. Average height. Dark suit. Expressionless face. He’s the kind of man people forget seconds after seeing him. Which is exactly what makes him dangerous.

He leans toward her. Not aggressively. Not enough for anyone else to notice. But I see her shoulders tense.

I see her eyes drop. I see her retreat inward. The transformation is immediate. The hopeful young woman disappears. The obedient captive returns.

“Don’t move,” Ifeanyi says quietly.

But he is already moving. The man has seen us.

And worse —

he has seen Rakiya recognize us.

His eyes narrow slightly. Calculating. Assessing. The way predators assess threats.

The gallery suddenly feels smaller. The music sounds distant. The crowd transforms from guests into obstacles. For several terrifying seconds, nobody moves.

Rakiya. The man. Ifeanyi. Me. Four people connected by a secret no one else in the room understands. Then Rakiya does something unexpected.

Her gaze returns to me. Only briefly. Almost imperceptibly.

She lowers the programme booklet.

On the back page, hidden from everyone else, a single word has been written in pencil. HELP.

The letters are small. Hurried. Almost swallowed by the cream surface of the paper. But they are there.

The man beside her turns. She immediately conceals it. But it is enough. I have seen it. Ifeanyi has seen it.

And from the expression on his face, he knows exactly what it means. Rakiya has not disappeared willingly. She is sending a message.

My body wants to move before wisdom can stop it. I want to cross the room. To call her name again. To reach for her hand.

To pull her out of that man’s shadow and tell everyone in the gallery to look, really look, at what is happening beneath their polished noses.

But Ifeanyi’s hand closes gently around my wrist. Not hard. A warning.

“Not here,” he murmurs.

I turn to him.

My chest is rising too quickly.

“She asked for help.”

“I know.”

“We can’t just stand here.”

“We won’t.”

His eyes remain fixed across the room.

“But if we rush, we lose her.”

The words strike through my panic.

I look back.

The man beside Rakiya is speaking to someone now, a smile placed on his face like a borrowed mask. His body remains angled toward her, blocking her without appearing to restrain her. To anyone else, he may look like an associate, a guardian, perhaps a manager.

To me, he looks like a door. Closed. Guarded. Dangerous.

Rakiya’s eyes meet mine once more. This time, she does not say anything with paper. She does not need to. The fear is enough. The plea is enough.

I think of the girl beneath the birds. The girl in the room with the unreachable window. All those paintings have been speaking one language. Captivity. Surveillance. Waiting. Deliverance.

And now the painter is standing inside the same room as her witnesses, unable to walk freely toward them.

A cold anger begins to settle beneath my fear.

Ifeanyi releases my wrist.

“Stay close,” he says.

“What are you going to do?”

“First, we make sure she does not leave through a door we are not watching.”

He speaks without looking at me, already measuring exits, security positions, sightlines, staff movement, and the man in the dark suit.

“And then?”

His jaw tightens.

“Then we find out who brought her here.”

Across the room, Rakiya lowers her gaze again.

The man places a hand lightly at her back.

Too light to accuse. Too familiar to ignore. 

My fingers curl around my clutch. Every instinct in me screams. But I stand still.

Because Ifeanyi is right. If we frighten the handler, he will remove her. If we make a scene, the system around him may close ranks.

If we act like guests, perhaps we can keep watching long enough to act properly.

The exhibition continues around us.

Glasses clink. Guests laugh. I observe a journalist asking an artist to repeat a quote for the camera. Somewhere, a collector praises a painting he has not understood. And beneath all that elegance, a very dangerous game begins.

Rakiya is alive. Rakiya is here and she has asked for help. And now, that request changes everything.

























TWENTY-FIVE





I have lost sight of Ifeanyi almost immediately. One moment, he is standing beside me, staring across the gallery at Rakiya and the man who shadows her. The next, he disappears into the crowd.

I resist the urge to follow him. My knowledge of Ifeanyi through stories, fragments, warnings, and the few intense days I have spent with him have taught me one thing: whenever he appears calm, he is usually several steps ahead of everyone else.

The exhibition continues around us as though nothing has happened. Collectors discuss acquisition prices. Journalists interview artists. Waiters float between guests carrying trays of drinks. And photographers capturing the paintings and the mood of people around them.

Nobody appears aware that a missing girl has just been found.

I watch from across the hall. The man remains close to Rakiya. Not close enough to attract suspicion. Just close enough to remind her she is not alone.

Every few minutes, he exchanges greetings with visitors or pretends to examine nearby paintings. Once, he accepts a glass from a waiter and does not drink it. Another time, he laughs at something a guest says, but his eyes do not laugh. They keep returning to Rakiya.

He is not guarding the artwork. He is guarding the artist. The realization makes my skin tighten.

Rakiya stands near the sculpture display with the programme booklet held against her chest again. Her face has recovered its careful emptiness, the one I had seen in the paintings before I saw it on her living face. She looks present, but not available. Visible, but not free.

I want to cross the room. Every part of me wants to cross the room. But Ifeanyi’s warning keeps me rooted. If we rush, we lose her.

So I stand still and learn the hardest kind of obedience – the kind that looks like inaction while your heart is begging you to move.

A distinguished-looking visitor enters the hall accompanied by two gallery staff.

The man appears to be a wealthy collector. His agbada is pale blue and embroidered with quiet extravagance, the kind of clothing that announces money without shouting. Several people greet him enthusiastically. A photographer turns quickly. A gallery assistant straightens as if a minister has entered.

Then I see Ifeanyi.

He approaches the collector with a smile spreading across his face, as though he has just encountered an old friend. The two men exchange handshakes. A brief embrace follows. They speak for a moment.

Ifeanyi’s expression is open, professional, easy. Too easy.

I understand at once that this is not casual. He is setting a table in the middle of a battlefield. He gestures toward one of the exhibition sections. The collector nods. Together they begin walking.

Toward Rakiya’s paintings.

I hold my breath. Ifeanyi is not approaching her. He is approaching the artwork. 

Nobody can object to a curator or consultant discussing paintings with a prospective buyer. Nobody can accuse him of interference for doing what everyone in the room already expects him to do.

The collector stops before The Silent Witness. Ifeanyi begins explaining something. The man listens attentively.

Several gallery guests gather around them almost immediately. That is another thing about influence: it attracts witnesses without requesting them. People do not always know why they gather around money and reputation. They simply feel the pull.

The handler relaxes slightly. This is normal.

Then the collector points toward another painting. One of Rakiya’s. Ifeanyi turns to a nearby gallery assistant.  “Could we have the artist join us?”

The request sounds innocent. Professional and expected.

The assistant nods.

My heart begins to beat harder. A few moments later, Rakiya is escorted toward the group.

I see her hesitation. I see the brief glance she exchanges with the handler. He gives the slightest nod. Permission. Nothing more.

Rakiya approaches.

The collector immediately begins asking questions about her inspiration, technique, and symbolism.

At first, she answers cautiously. Softly. Like someone afraid of speaking too much.

Her voice barely rises above the surrounding murmur. I cannot hear every word from where I stand, but I can read the restraint in the movement of her mouth, the careful lowering of her eyes, the way her fingers tighten around the programme booklet as if she needs something to hold so she does not vanish inside herself.

Then something changes.

Perhaps it is the familiarity of discussing her work, or it is the fact that art gives her a language her fear cannot fully silence. Perhaps it is relief. For the first time that evening, she comes alive. The artist emerges from beneath the captive. She points at details in the painting. Explains colours. Describes imagery. Speaks about memory, loss and hope. The very themes that have led us to her.

I watch her hands as she speaks. The left hand moves more naturally than the right. It rises when she explains the direction of light. It curves slightly when she describes the children’s faces.

It hovers over the canvas without touching it, as if she knows every inch of the painting by pulse and not by sight alone. Professor Adebayo had been right. The painting had carried traces of her. Now the woman carries traces of the painting.

 The collector appears fascinated. Several guests join the conversation. Soon, a small crowd forms around them. Exactly as Ifeanyi intends. A crowd creates distraction. Distraction creates opportunity.

I watch him carefully. He waits. Patiently.

The way a chess player waits several moves before revealing his strategy.

The handler watches too, but he no longer looks fully alarmed. The presence of the collector has reassured him. Wealth does that in rooms like this. It makes danger look like patronage. It makes scrutiny look like appreciation.

Then it happens. The collector turns toward another guest.

The crowd shifts. A photographer steps slightly to the side. A woman in a gold headwrap leans closer to see the signature.

For perhaps two seconds, nobody is looking directly at Ifeanyi and Rakiya. Two seconds. His hand moves naturally. Almost invisibly. A business card appears between his fingers.

He extends it while continuing his conversation as though nothing unusual has happened.

Rakiya accepts it. Not fully. Just enough for it to pass into her hand. Their eyes meet briefly. No words. No recognition. Nothing that can betray them. Yet something unmistakable passes between them. Hope.

For the first time all evening, I see it clearly on her face. Then the handler moves. Fast. Much faster than I expect. His hand appears almost instantly.

“Thank you,” he says pleasantly. Too pleasantly.

He plucks the card from Rakiya’s fingers with the ease of someone retrieving a misplaced document.

His smile never wavers. “Allow me to keep that with the exhibition materials.”

Nobody around them seems to notice. To everyone else, it looks harmless, professional and routine. But I see Rakiya’s face fall. The hope vanishes.

The handler slips the card into his jacket pocket. Control restored. Or so he thinks.

Because during that brief exchange, Rakiya has done something remarkable. As the card passed through her hands, her eyes had dropped for the briefest instant.

Less than a second. Long enough. I know she saw the number. Ifeanyi knows it too.

The handler may have confiscated the card, but he has been a fraction of a second too late. I press my fingers into my clutch to steady myself.

The room feels too bright. Too full.

How can people keep sipping wine while a girl learns a lifeline by memory? How can collectors keep discussing acquisition value while a handler pockets the only card she has been allowed to touch? How can beauty and captivity stand so near each other without the whole room tearing open?

Ifeanyi continues the conversation as though nothing has happened. The collector laughs at something he says. The crowd remains engaged.

The exhibition rolls forward. Yet beneath the polished surface of the evening, the balance has shifted. A line of communication now exists. Invisible and fragile.

And judging by the look in Rakiya’s eyes as she returns to her place beside the handler, she knows it. Someone has finally found her. And now she knows exactly where to look for help.

I do not move. Not yet. I only watch.

The handler stands beside her again, unaware that control has already leaked through his fingers.

Ifeanyi glances at me once from across the room. Only once. His face reveals nothing. But I understand. 

The first move has been made.

And in a room full of paintings, patrons, collectors, cameras, and polished lies, the rescue has begun without anyone hearing the opening shot.













TWENTY-SIX





The Pool bar itself is quieter than the bustling lobby and restaurant that has become our rendezvous for our morning strategy meeting. God, I’m beginning to sound like Ifeanyi.

Soft jazz drifts through hidden speakers. The scent of grilled seafood and roasted meat fills the air. A few of the tables are occupied. I scan for Ifeanyi as I walk by the swimming pool. 

A foreign family sits near the windows. Several guests linger over late dinners and quiet conversations, speaking in the low, careful tones of people who understand that money does not need to shout.

He is sitting alone near the far end of the open space. A folder rests on the table beside him. A cup of untouched coffee sits before him. He appears calm as always. But I know his mind is working furiously beneath the surface.

He rises as I approach.

“Good evening.” I smile.

“Good evening.”

A waiter appears.

Menus arrive.

Neither of us looks at them properly.

Our thoughts are elsewhere.

The moment the waiter leaves, the atmosphere changes.

The casual conversation disappears. The investigation returns.

“You found her,” I say. It is not a question.

Ifeanyi nods. “We found her.”

The distinction matters.

For months, Rakiya had existed only as a possibility. A theory. A collection of paintings and a signature. Now she is real. Alive. And trapped.

I watch him carefully.

“How bad is it?”

He leans back slightly.

“Worse than I hoped.”

The answer unsettles me. Ifeanyi is not easily intimidated. If he is worried, there is a reason.

“The man beside her?”

“He is not family.”

“I know.”

“He is not security either.”

I remain silent.

He opens the folder.

Photographs. Names. Business records. Gallery registrations. Transportation notes. Printed pages with dates circled. Handwritten comments scribbled in the margins.

The table suddenly looks less like a dinner meeting and more like an intelligence briefing.

“The exhibition gave us confirmation,” he says.

He slides one photograph across the table.

The man from the gallery. The handler. Expressionless. Forgettable and dangerous.

The photograph has captured the exact thing that made him frightening: nothing obvious. No theatrical menace. No visible weapon. No crude aggression. Just a face designed to disappear from memory the moment one stops looking.

“His name is not real,” Ifeanyi says.

I look up.

“What do you mean?”

“I mean he has used at least three identities in the last five years.”

My stomach tightens.

The restaurant suddenly feels colder.

“Who is he?”

Ifeanyi stares briefly through the restaurant windows.

“A gatekeeper.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means people like him rarely occupy the top.”

His voice drops.

“They manage access.”

“Access to what?”

“Talent.”

The answer lands heavily between us. I understand immediately. Artists, musicians, models, gifted children and young adults.

The very people Sammy believed were often targeted by corrupt systems. The very people whose hunger, innocence, ambition, or desperation could be turned into merchandise by men who knew how to dress exploitation as opportunity.

I remember Rakiya’s paintings. The hand that had written HELP on the back of a programme booklet. Perhaps she had not been painting symbolism at all. Perhaps she had been painting memory.

A waiter arrives with our meals. Neither of us touches the food.

The interruption lasts less than a minute, but it feels longer because the documents remain open on the table, accusing the silence. The waiter places the dishes before us with professional care, asks if we need anything else, and leaves after Ifeanyi gives a brief answer.

As soon as he is gone, Ifeanyi continues.

“The card worked.”

I look up quickly.

“The card?”

He nods. “She saw the number.”

I smile involuntarily. For the first time that evening, hope appears. “You’re sure?”

“Absolutely.”

“How can you be certain?”

His expression softens slightly. “Because when she looked at me after the handler took it, she repeated the last four digits with her eyes.”

I blink.

“What?”

He almost smiles. “You did not notice?”

“No.”

“She was confirming she remembered.”

I stare at him. “That is impossible.”

“It is not.”

His smile fades. “People in captivity learn to communicate differently.”

The statement lingers between us. Captivity.

The word feels heavier than missing. He is no longer speaking hypothetically. He believes Rakiya is being controlled. The realization sends a chill through me.

Outside the restaurant windows, the pool lights reflect against the water. The city glitters peacefully beyond the glass, pretending innocence. Inside, two people sit over untouched dinner, discussing a rescue that cannot yet look like a rescue. The contrast feels surreal.

I look down at the photograph again. The handler’s face stares back at me.

“What is his real name?”

“I don’t know yet.”

“That does not sound like you.”

“It does not,” he concedes.

The admission frightens me more than the uncertainty.

“But you found three identities.”

“Yes.”

“Connected to what?”

“Private art acquisitions. Youth talent programmes. Two gallery transport arrangements. One foundation that no longer exists.”

I sit back slowly.

“A foundation?”

“Yes.”

“What kind?”

“Creative development. Young artists. Mentorship. Scholarships. The usual language.”

The usual language. The words taste bitter.

I think of the way respectable words can cover ugly things. Programme. Foundation. Mentorship. Patronage. Protection. Representation. Words that should carry hope, turned into ropes.

“Was Rakiya part of it?”

“I don’t know.”

“But you think she may have been.”

“I think someone brought her into a network that knows how to hide people in plain sight.”

I glance around the restaurant. My gaze catches a man in grey suit signing a bill without looking at the amount. The world continues. Even when someone is asking for help.

“What happens next?” I ask.

For the first time all evening, Ifeanyi hesitates. He folds his hands. Studies the table.

Then looks directly at me. “We wait.”

I frown.

“We wait?”

“Yes.”

“That is the plan?”

“It is the only plan.”

I hear frustration creeping into my voice. “She asked for help.”

“And she knows where to find it now.”

His response is immediate. Measured. Certain.

“If she is as intelligent as her paintings suggest, she will contact us.”

“And if she doesn’t?”

The question hangs heavily.

For several seconds, he says nothing.

Then he glances toward the city lights. “Then we go looking for her.”

His voice is quiet. But there is something in it I have not heard before. Resolve. Dangerous resolve. The kind that comes from someone who has already decided how far he is willing to go.

I study him.
