The lights from the pool touch one side of his face, leaving the other in shadow. He looks tired now, though he would never admit it. Not physically tired. Something deeper. Something older. As if Rakiya’s frightened eyes have opened a door inside him he has spent years pretending was sealed.

“Why does this matter so much to you?” I ask.

He does not answer immediately. I think he may refuse. Then he looks at the folder.

“Because I know what it means for a gifted person to fall into the wrong hands.”

The words are simple. But they carry Ikenna’s shadow. I do not press.

Some grief does not need to be interrogated to be understood. I think of Ikenna too. Of his broken vow. Of the chain resting against my chest. Of what Sammy told me about the Program, and the vow I made to Sammy’s students at the Destiny Institute.

Perhaps Rakiya is not separate from that story. A girl with a gift against a system with insatiable appetite to devour. A room full of patrons who may admire her work without ever asking whether she is free.

My throat tightens. “We cannot lose her,” I say.

“We won’t.”

“You can’t promise that.”

“No,” he says. “But I can decide what I will do.”

The answer is not comforting. It is better than comfort. It is commitment.

For a while, neither of us speaks. The food grows colder. The jazz continues. The pool lights tremble against the water.

Then Ifeanyi closes the folder, but his palm remains on top of it.

“Tonight, you rest.”

I almost laugh. “Rest?”

“Yes.”

“After seeing Rakiya?”

“Especially after seeing Rakiya.”

“How does that make sense?”

“Because panic wastes strength. Tomorrow may need all of yours.”

The truth of that silences me.

I look up at the hotel’s glass curtain walls – perfectly finished and glittering, almost clean. But I know now that beneath its polished surfaces, dangerous rooms exist. Quiet rooms. Private rooms. Rooms where talent can be fed, dressed, praised, controlled, and displayed.

Rakiya is somewhere in this city. Perhaps waiting for darkness to give her courage. Perhaps repeating a phone number in her mind like a prayer. At that moment, I realize something. This is no longer about an exhibition. It’s no longer about paintings, or even about an investigation.

Somewhere in Abuja, a frightened young artist is waiting for an opportunity to escape. And whether Ifeanyi admits it or not, he has already made a decision. He is going to bring her home. Whatever it costs.

























































TWENTY-SEVEN





I am awake. Not because I cannot sleep. Because Abuja has become a city of unanswered questions.

The hotel room is dark except for the glow of city lights beyond the window. The shopping bags sit quietly in one corner. The emerald dress hangs from the wardrobe like something from another life. The Cross pendant rests against my chest, small and steady, but my thoughts refuse to settle.

Rakiya is somewhere in this city. Alive. Watched. Waiting. I keep seeing her eyes in the gallery. I keep seeing the word she had written in pencil. HELP.

Then my phone vibrates on the bedside table. I snatch it up. Ifeanyi. I answer immediately.

“Hello?”

“Get dressed.” His voice is calm.

I sit up. “What has happened?”

A brief silence follows.

Then he says, “She called.”

For a moment, I cannot speak.

“Rakiya?”

“Yes.”

The name alone erases every trace of fatigue.

“Where is she?”

“I don’t know.”

“What?”

“I don’t know where she is now.”

I hear movement in the background. Car doors. Voices. Traffic. He is already outside. Already moving.

“But she gave us something,” he says.

“What?”

“A route.”

The word enters me like a spark.

A route. Not a location. Not yet. But enough to become hope.

Twenty minutes later, I am seated beside him inside the SUV, speeding through the quiet Abuja streets. The city looks different at night. More secretive.

Ifeanyi keeps his eyes on the road. His phone rests on the dashboard.

“Tell me exactly what happened,” I say.

He nods.

“She called from a public phone.”

“How did she get the number?”

“She memorized it.”

The answer does not surprise me. Not anymore.

Rakiya’s paintings had already taught us that she sees what others miss and stores what others discard.

“She only spoke for thirty-eight seconds,” he says.

“What did she say?”

“Not much.”

He glances briefly at me. “Because she knew somebody might be listening.”

The vehicle slows at an intersection. The traffic light spills red across the windscreen.

“She said she was being moved tomorrow morning.”

My stomach tightens. Moved.

The word sounds too clean for what it means.

“Moved where?”

“She doesn’t know.”

“Then what did she tell you?”

For the first time that night, I see a faint smile touch his mouth. “She described the route.”

I frown.

“The route?”

“Landmarks.”

He taps the steering wheel. “A fuel station. A construction site. A roundabout. A church. A damaged billboard.” The smile widens slightly. “Things nobody would mention unless they wanted to be found.”

I stare at him. She has hidden directions inside ordinary conversation. A trail. A breadcrumb path. Hope disguised as casual observation.

I suddenly understand why Ifeanyi respects her so much. Rakiya is far stronger than she appears.

“How did she sound?” I ask.

The question leaves my mouth before I can stop it.

Ifeanyi’s face settles. “Frightened.”

I look away.

“But controlled,” he adds. “She did not waste words.”

That sounds like her paintings. Quiet terror. Disciplined meaning. A message hidden where only the desperate and the attentive can read it.

By the time we reach the police station, another vehicle is already waiting.

A tall police officer stands beside it. Plain clothes. No uniform. I recognize him. DSP Ekanem. He looks more like an accountant than a police officer, which somehow reassures me. 

Ifeanyi steps out first. I follow.

“This is Superintendent Ekanem,” he says.

The officer nods to me.

“Ma.”

He either cannot remember me, or the matter is too urgent to recall faces. I do not mind.

“Good evening,” I say.

He opens a file. “I understand we are not making arrests.”

“No,” Ifeanyi replies. “Just identification.”

Ekanem nods. “We stop the vehicle. We verify identities. We take statements. We create records.”

“And the girl?” I ask.

Ifeanyi answers before the officer can.

“We separate her.”

The superintendent closes the file. “Temporarily.”

The emphasis is deliberate.

Everything must remain legal. Professional. Defensible. No mistakes. No shortcuts. No emotional rescue that can be twisted against Rakiya later.

I think of Sammy in custody. I think of how quickly machinery can be turned against the innocent when powerful people know which levers to pull.

Tonight, the same machinery must be used carefully. Or it may become another trap.



The operation begins shortly after dawn. We wait near a service road overlooking the route Rakiya described. The sky lightens slowly, changing from black to dull grey, then to a pale Abuja morning. Traffic increases steadily as the city wakes. Commuters fill the roads. Commercial vehicles move between districts. Vendors begin to appear near corners. Security men open gates. The ordinary day arrives without knowing what is hidden inside it.

The waiting feels endless. Every black SUV makes my pulse jump. Every vehicle that turns off the route makes me wonder if we have missed her. I sit in the back seat with my hands clasped around my phone, praying without forming words.

Lord, let her be in the right vehicle. Let them not move her another way. Let her remember that we are looking. Let us not fail her.

Then Ekanem’s phone rings. The officer listens. Looks toward Ifeanyi. And nods.

“They have found it.”

My pulse quickens. Several kilometres ahead, another police team has already identified the vehicle.

A black SUV. One female passenger. Driver. One male escort. Exactly as described.

For nearly twenty minutes, nobody speaks. The silence is unbearable.

Ifeanyi stands outside the vehicle, one hand in his pocket, the other holding his phone. His expression reveals nothing, but I notice the tightness around his jaw. Ekanem speaks quietly with someone on his line. The morning traffic continues as though a young woman’s future is not balancing inside one carefully handled stop.

Then the call comes. Ekanem listens. Nods once. Then turns to us.

“Proceed to the station.”

I exhale.

The first part has worked.

The second part feels far more important.



By the time we arrive, the driver and the handler are already being interviewed separately. Nothing dramatic. No handcuffs. No shouting. Just questions. The sort of routine scrutiny most people never think about. Yet it is enough. Enough to create distance. Enough to create opportunity. Enough to make the handler’s quiet control break into administrative pieces.

A female officer escorts Rakiya into a private interview room. I see her through the glass partition. She looks exhausted. Anxious. Smaller than I remembered from the exhibition.

At the gallery, fear had made her body careful, but lights and guests and paintings had framed her with an artist’s strange dignity. Here, under the flat light of a police station, she looks young. Too young to have learned this much silence.

But she is finally alone. For the first time. Truly alone.

The female officer opens the door. Ifeanyi enters first. I follow.

The moment Rakiya sees us, her composure shatters. Not dramatically. Not with tears. With relief that comes when a person has spent too long carrying fear alone and suddenly discovers that someone else has arrived to hold part of its weight.

Her lips tremble once. Then she steadies them. For several seconds, nobody speaks.

The room holds all of us carefully. Outside, voices continue in the corridor. Papers move. Phones ring. The driver answers questions somewhere behind another door. The handler’s name, whatever version of it he is using today, begins to enter official record.

Inside, Rakiya looks from Ifeanyi to me. Her eyes stop on my face. Recognition flickers again. The help she had dared to ask for in a room full of people who could not see her.

Then she whispers, “You found me.”

Ifeanyi shakes his head gently. “No.” His voice softens. “You found us.”

The words undo something in her. She lowers her face.

This time, the tears come quietly. Not as collapse. As release.

I move closer, but slowly, careful not to crowd her. “Rakiya,” I say.

Her name feels different now. No longer only a clue. No longer only the imagined artist behind the letter R. A trembling witness.

She looks up at me. “They were moving me,” she says. Her voice is barely above a whisper.

“We know.”

“I didn’t know where.”

“You gave the route,” Ifeanyi says.

She looks at him. “I saw the number.”

“I know.”

“I kept saying it in my head.”

“You remembered.”

“I was afraid I would forget.”

“But you didn’t.”

Her eyes close briefly. “No.”

The female officer glances at Ifeanyi, then at me, as if waiting for the proper next step. Ekanem appears beyond the glass, speaking to another officer. His face remains professional, but his attention is on this room.

Everything still has to be handled carefully. Statements. Custody. Protection. Records.

Questions that can help without reopening wounds too roughly. But in this moment, before the machinery begins again, there is only one truth I can hold. Rakiya is here. Not free yet. No longer unseen. I sit opposite her.

Ifeanyi remains standing beside the table, his hands resting lightly on the back of a chair.

Rakiya looks at both of us. Her fear has not disappeared. It has only loosened enough to let words pass through.

And judging by the fear I see still living in her eyes, I know that story is about to change everything we thought we knew.









































































TWENTY-EIGHT





The interview room is strangely quiet. Outside, officers move through corridors carrying files and exchanging routine updates. Telephones ring occasionally. Doors open and close. Somewhere in the building, a printer hums steadily.

Yet inside the room, time seems to slow.

Rakiya sits opposite us, her hands wrapped around a paper cup of tea that has long gone cold. The atmosphere is still too heavy for comfort.

For several minutes, nobody interrupts her. She speaks slowly. Carefully. As though each word has to pass through a narrow place before it can reach us.

I learn that her disappearance did not happen the way newspapers usually describe such things. There had been no dramatic kidnapping. No masked men.

The process had been far more sophisticated. And far more frightening. It had begun with opportunity. An art competition. A sponsorship promise. An invitation to exhibit her work. Mentorship. Every offer had appeared legitimate. Every person had appeared professional. Until it was too late.

“They never force you at the beginning,” she says quietly. Her eyes remain fixed on the table. “They make you trust them first.” The room falls silent.

I notice Superintendent Ekanem stop writing. Even he is listening now.

“They said my paintings were special.” Her voice trembles slightly. “They said they wanted to help me.”

Ifeanyi leans forward. “And then?”

She smiles bitterly. “The help became control.”

The answer lingers in the room.

“They controlled where I stayed. Who I met. Where I travelled. Who purchased my paintings. Who spoke to me.”

She swallows. “Even the money.”

My heart tightens.

The paintings that moved audiences. The paintings that inspired collectors. The paintings that launched exhibitions. They had never truly belonged to her. She had become a prisoner of her own gift.

“They kept moving me,” she says. “Abuja, Lagos, Port-Harcourt.” She pauses. “As long as people believed I was difficult to find, nobody asked questions.”

The simplicity of the statement disturbs me. Because it is true. Missing people often disappear not because nobody searches. But because nobody searches in the right places.

Ifeanyi’s expression becomes unreadable. “Did you ever meet the people funding the operation?”

Rakiya nods.

“Sometimes.”

“Did you know their names?”

“Not always.”

“Did you know who reported to whom?”

Another nod.

The officer glances at Ifeanyi.

We are finally approaching the centre of the maze. The people at the top. The ones who never appear in photographs. Never sign the obvious documents. Never attend the meetings where frightened girls are told what to say. The invisible architects.

Ifeanyi folds his hands. His voice remains calm. Professional. Measured. Yet I sense tension beneath it. The question matters. Perhaps more than any other.

“Rakiya…”

She looks up.

“Do you know who the boss is?”

For the first time since the interview began, she hesitates. Not out of fear. Out of uncertainty. As though she is searching through a room in her memory where several doors remain locked. 

Then she nods slowly. “I saw him twice.”

The room becomes completely silent. Twice.

Not enough to know everything.

Ifeanyi exchanges a glance with Ekanem.

“Do you know his name?”

“No.”

The answer deflates the room slightly.

Then she adds, “But I know his face.”

Nobody moves. I watch Ifeanyi carefully. His attention sharpens immediately.

“You’re certain?”

She nods. “Very certain.”

“What did he look like?”

Instead of answering, she looks around the room. “Do you have paper?”

Ifeanyi frowns. “Paper?”

“And a pen.”

Superintendent Ekanem immediately slides a writing pad across the table. A pen follows.

Rakiya accepts both. Then something extraordinary happens. The frightened young woman disappears. The artist emerges. Her posture changes. Her concentration deepens. The same hands that painted captivity into beauty now begin moving across the paper. Confidently. Deliberately. Line after line. Curve after curve.

Nobody speaks.

The scratching of the pen becomes the only sound in the room. Five minutes pass. Then ten. The drawing gradually takes shape. A forehead. A nose. Cheekbones. Eyes. A mouth. An aging face. The likeness becomes increasingly detailed.

Increasingly familiar. I notice Ifeanyi stop breathing. At first, I think I imagine it. Then I see his expression. The colour has drained from his face. His eyes remain fixed on the paper. Unblinking. Disbelieving.

“No…”

The word escapes him involuntarily.

Rakiya continues drawing. Oblivious to what is happening to him.

The room feels suddenly colder.

Ekanem notices it too. “What is it?”

Ifeanyi does not answer.

The sketch is almost complete.

I look from the drawing to Ifeanyi. Back to the drawing. Then back to him again. And suddenly I understand. Because I have seen that face before. Not in a criminal file. Not in a police report. Not in an exhibition catalogue. In family photographs. Inside Rev. Obiajulu’s house.  In one of their family photographs, standing beside Ikenna.

The pen finally stops moving. Rakiya pushes the paper forward.

“That’s him.”

Nobody speaks. The silence is suffocating.

Ekanem picks up the sketch. Studies it. Looks at Ifeanyi. Then looks back at the drawing. Confusion slowly gives way to recognition.

My eyes drift toward Ifeanyi. He has not moved. He has not blinked. He has not spoken. He simply stares at the portrait as though reality itself has fractured in front of him.

Finally, his voice emerges. Barely above a whisper. “My God…” The words sound less like a statement and more like a prayer. Or perhaps a lament. Then he looks up. Not at me. Not at the officer. At the sketch. At the face he has known all his life.

And in a voice hollowed by disbelief, he says, “That’s my uncle.”

The room freezes.

“Dike Obiajulu.”

The name lands like thunder.

For a moment, I do not hear the hum of the printer outside. I do not hear the corridor voices. I do not hear the phones. I hear only the name. Dike Obiajulu.

The uncle whose house had once shaped part of Ikenna’s journey. The family connection that had always hovered in the background like an old wound. The man whose name belonged to private histories, family breaches, and memories no one had fully explained to me.

Now his face sits on the table between us, drawn by a girl who has spent months inside a system that knows how to turn talent into captivity.

Ifeanyi’s hands close slowly into fists. Not in rage. Not yet. In shock. In restraint. In the effort not to let the room see how deeply the name has cut him.

Rakiya looks from him to me, suddenly afraid that she has done something wrong.

“I saw him,” she says quickly. “I’m sure. He came twice. They all acted differently when he came.”

Ifeanyi closes his eyes. Only for a second. When he opens them, something in his face has changed. The polished curator is gone. The careful strategist is still there, but behind him stands a son of a wounded family staring at the return of an old darkness.

Ekanem places the sketch flat on the table.

“We will need to verify this.”

Ifeanyi nods once. “Yes.” His voice is controlled now. Too controlled.

Rakiya lowers her eyes. “I didn’t know he was your family.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says quietly. “You did nothing wrong.”

But the assurance seems to cost him.

I sit very still.

Every mystery we have chased suddenly rearranges itself. The art network. The hidden artist. The paintings. The Program. What had felt like separate doors begins to look like one corridor. A corridor leading back into the Obiajulu family itself.

And in that moment, every mystery we have spent months chasing becomes something far worse. Personal.



TWENTY-NINE





Neither of us speaks during the drive back to the hotel. Ifeanyi is too shocked, and maybe ashamed to look me in the face. 

Abuja’s streets glide past the windows in a blur of headlights and shadows. The city seems unchanged. Yet everything feels different. Hours earlier, we were searching for a trafficker. Now we are confronting a member of Ifeanyi’s family. A member of Rev. Obiajulu’s family.

The realization refuses to settle comfortably in my mind. 

Dike Obiajulu. The name keeps returning with the face Rakiya drew. The same face I had seen in family photographs inside Rev. Obiajulu’s house. The same face that had belonged to old stories, old disagreements, and the kind of family silence people preserve because the truth is too expensive to say aloud.

I glance once at Ifeanyi. He sits beside me, still as stone, his eyes fixed ahead. Not speaking. Unwilling yet to offer any explanations. 

The quiet around him is not ordinary silence. It feels like a man standing before a collapsed building and recognizing the first crack he once ignored.

When we arrive at the hotel, he does not suggest the restaurant. Perhaps it is too crowded. The lobby is too noisy. Or maybe he simply cannot bear polished voices tonight.

Instead, he says, “Let’s sit outside.”

We find a quiet corner overlooking the illuminated swimming pool. The water reflects lights from the surrounding buildings, creating ripples of gold and blue across the surface. Palm fronds shift gently in the night breeze. 

For several minutes, neither of us speaks. I sense he is wrestling with something. Not the revelation.  Finally, he breaks the silence.

“You remember asking me why I left Onitsha?”

I nod. I had asked and he had avoided answering. Tonight, there is nowhere left to hide.

“I lied.”

The words surprise me. “What do you mean?”

He stares at the water.

“I told everyone I left because I wanted independence.” His laugh carries no humour. “That was not the reason.”

The breeze stirs gently through the palms. I remain silent.

He continues. “When I was younger, Dike was everything I wanted to become.”

I frown. 

The statement feels strange after what we have just learned.

“He was successful,” Ifeanyi says. “Influential. Respected. He always seemed to know important people.”

His eyes not leaving the water. “While other men struggled, he appeared to move through life effortlessly.”

I understand then. Young men often admire power long before they understand its source.

“I thought he was brilliant.” The bitterness in his voice is unmistakable. “I thought he was connected.” He pauses. “What I did not know was how those connections were made.”

The silence returns briefly. 

Then he speaks again. “I left my family and moved into his house.”

The confession feels strangely intimate. Like a door opening into a room he has kept locked for years.

“He said he would mentor me. Help me establish myself. Introduce me to people.”

Everything about the arrangement had sounded reasonable. Normal. Even generous.

“He did help me,” Ifeanyi says. The words emerge reluctantly.

“I learned business from him. Negotiation. Networking. How influential people think.” He pauses. “Looking back, I suppose that is what made it dangerous.”

Because evil rarely introduces itself as evil. It often arrives disguised as opportunity. As mentorship. As favour. As success.

Ifeanyi folds his arms. “I started noticing strange things.”

My pulse quickens. He turns away from the pool briefly.

“What kind of things?”

“Visitors arriving at odd hours. Meetings that stopped when I entered rooms. Young people constantly being discussed as assets.”

The word unsettles me. Assets. Not people. Assets.

“I ignored it,” he says.

His voice softens. “At first.”

The guilt is becoming visible now. Not spoken. But visible. Years of carrying it have etched themselves into him.

Then he says the words that change everything. “I brought Ikenna there.”

The confession hangs heavily between us. For a moment, I forget to breathe.

“What?”

His eyes finally meet mine. The pain inside them startles me.

“I introduced him to Dike.”

The words emerge almost as a whisper.

“I was the one who brought him into that house.”

The sounds of the city fade into the background. Everything suddenly makes sense. The guilt. The obsession. The determination. The need to uncover the truth. It has never been solely about justice. It has been about responsibility.

“I didn’t know,” he says quickly. “As God is my witness, I didn’t know.”

His voice cracks for the first time. “If I had known…”

The sentence dies unfinished. Neither of us needs to hear the rest… Ikenna. The young man who would later become Jehoshaphat. The young man whose life would change forever. The young man whose death still haunts everyone connected to him. And Ifeanyi had unknowingly opened the door.

“I have carried that for years,” he says.

The confession sounds exhausted.

“As though every terrible thing that happened afterward began with me.”

I want to tell him he is wrong. But grief does not obey logic. Guilt rarely does either. The pool lights shimmer across the water.

For a long moment, he simply watches them. Then he speaks again.

“There was another reason I left.” The tension in his voice returns immediately.

“What happened?”

His jaw tightens. The answer takes several seconds.

Then he says, “He invited me to join him.”

I frown.

“Join him how?”

His eyes close briefly. When they reopen, they seem older.

“He said I was smart. Disciplined. Trustworthy. He said there were opportunities.”

The night suddenly feels colder.

“What kind of opportunities?”

The silence that follows is enough. I already know. Yet I need to hear it.

Finally, he says, “Children.”

The word barely escapes his lips.

My stomach turns.

“He wanted me to work with him.”

His voice sounds distant now, as though he is reliving the conversation. “Recruitment. Transportation. Coordination.”

Each word lands like a stone. I feel physically sick.

“He did not call it abduction,” Ifeanyi says. “People like him never do.” His smile is hollow.

“There are always better words. Placement. Sponsorship. Talent development. Protection.”

His gaze hardens. “But when you strip away the language, the reality remains the same.”

For several seconds, neither of us speaks. The enormity of it settles between us. Then he looks at me. Directly. Without hiding.

“That was the day I left.”

The conviction in his voice surprises me.

“He offered money. Influence. Connections. A future.”

He shakes his head. “But something inside me refused.”

I remember Sammy’s words. Purpose. Conscience. The quiet voice that survives even when everything else becomes corrupted.

“I walked away,” Ifeanyi says.

The confession sounds simple. Yet I know it was not simple at all. Walking away from power never is.

“I lost opportunities. I lost relationships. I lost protection.”

His eyes drift toward the city skyline. “But at least I kept myself.”

The words linger.

A rare victory in a story filled with regrets. For a long time, we sit quietly. The water shimmers. The city lights flicker.

And for the first time since I met him, I understand the burden Ifeanyi carries. He is not hunting Dike because he hates him. He is hunting Dike because he once loved him. Because he admired him. Because he trusted him.

Because he had believed the brightness around Dike was success, not the shine of things stolen from other people’s lives. And because some betrayals cut deepest when they come from family.

“Ifeanyi,” I say softly.

He does not look at me.

“You were young.”

“I was not a child.”

“But you were deceived.”

“I was proud,” he says. “Ambitious. Hungry to prove that I was not the useless son everyone thought I was.”

His voice lowers. “Dike saw that hunger.”

The words strike me. Predators study the wound before offering the promise. It is the same pattern again. Different person. Different room. Different generation. Same appetite.

“He saw your wound,” I say.

Ifeanyi turns slightly. “And he used it.”

For the first time, he does not argue. He only looks back at the water.

“I should have seen him clearly.”

“Maybe.”

The honesty surprises even me.

“But seeing clearly is not always the same as being innocent of blindness.”

He studies me then. Something in his expression softens, but the grief remains.

“I brought Ikenna there,” he repeats.

“I know.”

“That house put him within reach.”

“Maybe it did.”

He winces.

I continue before he can retreat into punishment. “But Dike still chose what he chose. Ikenna still made his own decisions. The fraternity still built its altar. The GODs still demanded what they demanded. You are part of the story, Ifeanyi, but you are not the whole evil inside it.”

He breathes out slowly. The words do not free him. Not yet. But they enter somewhere. I see that.

He rests his elbows on his knees and clasps his hands. “I spent years wondering whether I could have saved Ikenna.”

The sentence comes quietly. Almost without strength.

“I wondered if I had stayed away from Dike, if I had never taken Ikenna there, if I had warned him earlier, if I had been a better brother…” His voice breaks around the last word.

A better brother. The phrase settles between us with Ikenna’s memory. I think of the chain at my neck. The chain Ifeanyi had once given Ikenna as a token of a vow. The chain that became a broken promise.

I touch it without thinking. Ifeanyi notices. His eyes rest on the pendant for a moment, then return to the water.

“Now I know I cannot change what happened,” he says.

His expression hardens slowly. “But I can stop it from happening again.”

And for the first time since Rakiya’s sketch was placed on the table at the police station, I see something stronger than guilt in his eyes. Purpose.

Not the restless kind that runs ahead of wisdom. Not the violent kind that mistakes revenge for justice. Something quieter. Older. More dangerous because it has been purified by regret.

I sit beside him and look at the pool. The water keeps trembling under the lights, as if even beauty cannot remain still after truth has entered it.

Dike Obiajulu is no longer a distant name. He is blood. History. Betrayal. A corridor back into the wounds of the Obiajulu family.

And as Abuja glitters around us, pretending peace, I understand that the search for Rakiya has opened a door none of us can close again.































































THIRTY





Our second meeting with ASP Ekanem takes place at a discreet hotel, far removed from the public glamour of the Hilton. Ifeanyi insists on it. According to him, we have come too far to become careless now. We have already left enough footprints for an observant predator to notice that something is shifting beneath the surface.

I understand what he means. Still, a part of me believes that unless the crook can outwit Ifeanyi’s own sharp instincts, he may gather the scattered pieces and still fail to make sense of them. But I do not argue. In matters like this, Ifeanyi’s caution is not fear. It is discipline.

The hotel is quieter, tucked away from the main road, respectable without being dramatic, busy enough to hide a meeting but not so busy that movement becomes uncontrollable. A television mounted on the wall plays a muted news channel. Two men sit at the far end pretending to discuss business over a half-empty bottle of water.

We sit in a private corner of the restaurant. Ifeanyi takes the seat facing the entrance. ASP Ekanem notices. So do I.

He is a compact man with calm eyes and the kind of face that does not waste expression. He wears plain clothes, but nothing about him feels casual. Even when he leans back, he appears alert. His phone lies face down on the table. A small notebook rests beside it.

He greets me politely, then turns most of his attention to Ifeanyi. That tells me the nature of the meeting.  I am present. But the men are about to discuss danger in a language older than comfort.

“We cannot go after Dike through Rakiya’s testimony,” Ifeanyi says.

He does not begin with greetings. He begins with the line he has already drawn in his mind. ASP Ekanem watches him.

“Why?”

“Because if Dike knows the girl has spoken, Sammy dies.”

My breath catches. The sentence lands on the table with terrible simplicity.

ASP Ekanem’s face does not change. “You are certain?”

“Not certain,” Ifeanyi says. “Certain is too weak. I know him.”

I look at him. There is no theatre in his voice. Only memory.

“Dike is a vicious man,” he continues. “He is capable of unimaginable evil, but what makes him more dangerous is not cruelty alone. It is calculation. He does not merely react. He preserves options.”

ASP Ekanem opens his notebook. “You said he trusts you.”

“Yes.”

“Why?”

“Because I was useful to him once.”

The answer comes too quickly. Then silence follows. Ifeanyi looks briefly away. I understand enough not to ask. 

ASP Ekanem taps his pen lightly against the notebook. “Rakiya identified him as the boss.”

“Yes,” Ifeanyi says. “But if we act as if we know that, he will know the source.”

“And he will clean house.”

“Exactly.”

I frown. “Clean house?”

Ifeanyi looks at me. “Move people. Destroy links. Silence witnesses. Scatter documents. Transfer the girl if he still has access to her. And if Sammy appears to be the reason any of this began, Sammy becomes expendable.”

I swallow.

The room seems colder.

“Then how do we touch him?” Ekanem asks.

“We do not touch him first through the child-abduction angle.” Ifeanyi leans forward. “We go through the attempt on Sammy’s life.”

ASP Ekanem’s eyes narrow. “Explain.”

“Sammy has already been framed. The case around him is poisoned. But Dike’s interest in Sammy is separate. If we can get Dike to expose knowledge of Sammy’s situation, or speak in a way that connects him to action against Sammy, then we open a lawful path that does not immediately reveal Rakiya as our source.”

Ekanem considers it. “You want to keep the girl out of the first strike.”

“I want to keep her alive.”

The officer’s pen stops. For the first time, I see respect flicker in his eyes.

“And Sammy?” he asks.

“Same.”

“Then what is your plan?”

“I will go to Dike.”

My whole body stiffens. “You?”

Ifeanyi does not look at me. “Yes.”

ASP Ekanem leans back. “That is dangerous.”

“I know.”

“Get bugged.”

“No.”

The answer comes instantly. 

Ekanem’s expression hardens. “Ifeanyi, don’t be foolish. If you are walking into that man’s house, we need a recording.”

“If he finds a device on me, I will not make it out alive.”

“You think he will search you?”

“I think Dike’s suspicion has survived longer than many people’s intelligence. He may not search me like a criminal at the gate, but he will look. He will test. He will arrange the room. He will watch what I touch. He will listen to where my questions come from.”

Ekanem says nothing.

Ifeanyi continues. “And if he senses police around me, the door closes. Worse, he may choose the Samson option.”

“What is that?” I ask.

Both men turn to me.

Ifeanyi’s face darkens. “It is his last option.”

“What does it mean?”

“He used the phrase when he was imprisoned in Paris,” Ifeanyi says. “He threatened members of the Order that if they did not make enough effort to get him out, he would bring everyone down with himself.”

ASP Ekanem nods slowly. “A scorched-earth move.”

Ifeanyi looks at me. “Destroy everything along with himself rather than accept defeat.”

The explanation chills me.

I think of Samson pulling down the pillars in the old Bible story, dying with his enemies. But in Dike’s mouth, it has no nobility. It is not sacrifice. It is revenge wearing biblical language.

“You think he could have Sammy killed if he knows we are coming through Rakiya?” I ask.

“Not could,” Ifeanyi says, cutting in. “He will.”

The certainty silences me. He looks back at Ekanem.

“That is why the first approach must come from me, not from police, and not through Rakiya.”

“Why would he talk to you?”

“Because he trusts one thing about me.”

“What?”

“My ambition.”

The answer seems to cost him. Ekanem watches him more carefully.

Ifeanyi’s voice remains controlled. “Dike believes every man has a price, but he does not respect cheap greed. He respects appetite with style. If I approach him as a nephew worried about exposure, or as a curator who has stumbled into something valuable and dangerous, he will listen long enough to measure whether there is profit.”

I feel uneasy.

“You want him to believe you are interested in the business?”

“No,” Ifeanyi says. “Not directly. That would be too crude. I want him to believe I am close enough to something that may affect him.”

“Rakiya’s art,” Ekanem says.

“Yes.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes sharpen. “Dike probably knows I am involved with one of the biggest art exhibitions on the continent. He knows the art world touches money, reputation, foreign collectors, laundering possibilities, hidden ownership, and soft power. If a gifted girl’s work has entered that world through his network, he will want to know what I know.”

“And if he knows nothing?” I ask.

“Then his ignorance will show.”

“How?”

“In what he fails to fear.”

That sentence stays with me.

Ekanem taps his pen once. “What exactly will you say?”

“I will not mention child kidnapping. I will not mention Rakiya as a rescued victim. I will not say she identified him. I will not speak of Sammy unless he brings him up first.”

“And if he doesn’t?”

“I will place bait carefully.”

“What bait?”

“The paintings. A young northern girl. Left-handed. Raw spiritual work. A possible unsigned artist whose work has begun attracting attention. I will make it sound like the art may become public.”

Ekanem nods slowly. “You want him to worry that the girl’s visibility could expose the chain behind her.”

“Yes.”

“And you want him to talk.”

“I want him to react.”

“Reaction is not evidence.”

“No. But it tells us where to dig.”

ASP Ekanem leans forward. “Without a bug, you are asking us to trust your recollection.”

“I am asking you to use my meeting as intelligence, not prosecution.”

The officer’s eyes hold his. For several seconds, neither man speaks.

I sit between them, feeling the weight of two worlds: Ifeanyi’s world of influence and psychology; Ekanem’s world of procedure and lawful proof.

Both are necessary. Neither is safe.

Finally, Ekanem says, “I don’t like it.”

“I don’t either.”

“That does not comfort me.”

“It shouldn’t.”

Ekanem looks at me. “And you?”

The question startles me. “I don’t want him to go.”

Ifeanyi glances at me. I ignore him.

“But I don’t know another way.” 

The officer seems satisfied by the honesty.

Ifeanyi says, “ASP Ekanem, let us work with this plan. I will give you your trophy.”

Ekanem’s eyes narrow. “Trophy?”

“Enough to make your superiors listen without making them ask how you got there too early.”

The officer closes his notebook. “You are too confident.”

“No,” Ifeanyi replies. “I am afraid of the light.”

That sentence shifts something in the room.

Ekanem studies him. “What does that mean?”

Ifeanyi’s jaw tightens. “It means darkness is not Dike’s weakness. He is comfortable there. Expose him wrongly, and he becomes smoke. Expose him too early, and he becomes fire. But expose him under the right light, and he must choose whether to stand as himself or reveal the monster behind the costume.”

For the first time, Ekanem almost smiles. “That sounds like art language.”

“It is also investigation.”

The officer stands. “I will make discreet preparations. No formal movement until I hear from you.”

“Agreed.”

“No heroics.”

Ifeanyi smiles faintly.

“I am too selfish for heroics.”

“I doubt that.”

Ekanem turns to me. “Miss Elizabeth, do not discuss this with anyone outside your trusted circle. Not friends. Not students. Not family members who do not need to know.”

I nod. “Yes, sir.”

“And if anything changes with Sammy’s custody, call me through the number I gave you.”

“I will.”

He leaves us there. The restaurant seems larger after he walks away.

Ifeanyi remains seated, his eyes fixed on the table.

For the first time since I have met him, he looks tired in a way expensive clothes cannot hide.

“Thank you,” I say quietly.

He does not look up. “Don’t thank me.”

“Ifeanyi—”

“I am doing this for my brother.”

The words close the space between gratitude and grief. 

I nod slowly.

“For Ikenna.”

He finally looks at me.

“Yes.”

There is a silence. Then I remember.

“I kept forgetting to show you something.”

His brows gather.

I feel I should say something to lighten the grief, or guilt written all over his face. But words fail me. Nothing I can say feels large enough for the wound opening in him.

My head lowers for a moment. My hands tremble in my lap. Slowly, almost uncertainly, I lift them toward him and place them over his palms.

He does not pull away.

For a few seconds, he simply stares at our joined hands, as though he has forgotten what comfort feels like. Then his fingers loosen. The tension in his shoulders gives way first, then the hard line of his jaw. Something deep and buried moves through him – a silent tremor, like grief finally breaking through stone.

His breath catches.

Then he bends forward and breaks down in tears. I do not speak. I only hold his hands and let him release what he has carried alone for too long. 







THIRTY-ONE





Hilton has become quiet in that deceptive way hotels become quiet at night. The curtains hang perfectly still. Abuja glitters beyond the glass window in distant, expensive lights. Somewhere below, cars move in and out of the hotel entrance, carrying people with plans, appointments, secrets, and destinations.

But the room is too large for one frightened woman. Inside my room, time refuses to move. Ifeanyi has gone to meet Dike. Those words alone are enough to make my hands cold.

He left with the composure of a man who understands danger too well to dramatize it. No grand farewell. No heroic declaration. No unnecessary assurance. Only instructions.

If I hear from him, I should answer immediately. If I hear from unknown numbers, I should not speak carelessly. If anyone asks for him, I should know nothing. If there is trouble, I should call ASP Ekanem. Then he left.

And I remain in the room with prayer, fear, and imagination. That is the cruelest part. Imagination.

It creates rooms I have never seen. Dike’s house. Dike’s men. His eyes watching Ifeanyi. A search at the gate.  A wrong question. A wrong pause. A phone taken. A door locked.

I press my phone harder in my palm and call Bitty.

He answers almost immediately. “Lizzy?”

His voice carries alarm before I speak. “Are you okay?”

“I’m fine.”

“You don’t sound fine.”

“I need you to listen carefully.”

Silence comes over the line.

“What happened?”

“Ifeanyi has gone to meet Dike.”

For a moment, Bitty says nothing. Then his voice returns lower. “Dike Obiajulu?”

“Yes.”

“Why would he do that?”

“Rakiya identified him as the boss behind her abduction.”

The silence that follows is not confusion. It is dread.

“Lizzy,” Bitty says slowly, “please tell me this is not some open confrontation.”

“No. It’s discreet.”

“Discreet how?”

“He is not going as police. He is not mentioning Rakiya’s testimony. He is not linking this to Sammy directly. The plan is to make Dike react around the art angle, the girl, the paintings — anything that gives ASP Ekanem a trail to work with.”

“Is Ifeanyi bugged?”

“No.”

“Why not?”

“He refused. He said if Dike finds anything on him, he may not come out alive.”

Bitty exhales sharply.

“He is right.”

That frightens me more than if he had argued.

“You think so?”

“I know Dike by reputation. Men like that don’t survive long because they are careless. Lizzy, this is dangerous.”

“I know.”

“No, listen to me. Dike is not Steel. He is not one of those campus boys flexing power they borrowed from darkness. Dike is older, smarter, and deeper in the system. If Ifeanyi miscalculates—”

“Don’t.” My voice breaks. “Please don’t finish that sentence.”

Bitty becomes quiet. I move away from the window and begin pacing.

“It is only for you to hear,” I say. “Do not tell anyone. Not even casually. Not even as a prayer point to a group. I only called because I want you to join me in prayer.”

“I will.”

“Please.”

“I said I will.” His voice softens. “Where are you now?”

“In my room.”

“Lock the door.”

“It is locked.”

“Double-check.”

“I already did.”

“Check again.”

I go to the door and check the lock. Then the chain. Then I return to the middle of the room.

“It’s locked.”

“Good. Keep your phone charged.”

“It is.”

“Send me Ifeanyi’s number.”

“I don’t know if I should.”

“Lizzy.”

“No. This has to stay tight.”

He pauses, then accepts it. 

“I understand,” he says finally. “You are protecting the operation.”

“I am trying to protect everyone.”

“Including yourself?”

I do not answer.

“Lizzy.”

“I heard you.”

“No, you didn’t. You are in the middle of Abuja with people who can make others disappear behind official language. You have to protect yourself too.”

My eyes fill suddenly.

“I’m tired, Bitty.”

“I know.”

“I am tired of being afraid.”

“I know.”

“I am tired of names that sound like danger.”

“I know.”

“I’m tired of carrying things I don’t understand.”

His voice becomes very gentle. “Then don’t carry them alone tonight. Put the phone on speaker. Let us pray.”

I place the phone on the bed and kneel beside it.

Bitty prays for me and the mission. Not loudly. Not like a man trying to impress heaven. He prays like someone who knows what darkness is capable of and has learned not to negotiate with it. He asks God to cover Ifeanyi, confuse every evil counsel, restrain Dike’s violence, protect Rakiya wherever she is, preserve Sammy in custody, expose the hidden works of darkness, and give me strength to wait without collapsing inside fear.

By the time he finishes, I am crying quietly.

“Amen,” I whisper.

“Lizzy?”

“Yes.”

“Keep praying. Don’t let fear be the only voice in the room.”

The line ends a few minutes later.

Then the waiting begins again.

I pace. From the bed to the window. From the window to the door. From the door to the small table where Ikenna’s memoir lies.

Every object in the room seems to carry a part of the story: the emerald dress hanging in the wardrobe, the Cross pendant resting against my chest, my Bible open on the bed, the phone waiting for news, the curtains concealing a city too calm for what is unfolding inside it.

I begin to speak Scripture aloud. At first, softly.

“The Lord is my light and my salvation; whom shall I fear?”

My voice trembles. “The Lord is the strength of my life; of whom shall I be afraid?”

I walk to the window.

“When the wicked, even mine enemies and my foes, came upon me to eat up my flesh, they stumbled and fell.”

I turn back.

“No weapon formed against us shall prosper.”

The words sound small against the size of my fear.

So I speak louder. “He that dwelleth in the secret place of the Most High shall abide under the shadow of the Almighty.”

I press my palm to my chest. “Lord, hide him. Hide Ifeanyi. Hide Rakiya. Hide Sammy. Let Dike’s eyes be darkened to anything that will expose this plan.”

I pace again.

“Deliver me from mine enemies, O my God. Defend me from them that rise up against me.”

The room does not change. No angel appears. No message comes. No call.

Only the hum of the air-conditioning and the faint sound of traffic far below. I check my phone again. Nothing.

My legs begin to ache, but I cannot sit. If I sit, fear will sit with me. So I continue pacing.

Then comes the knock. Soft. Three times.

I freeze.

My phone is in my hand instantly.

Another knock. Not loud. Not urgent. But certain.

I move quietly to the door and look through the peephole.

Ifeanyi stands outside.

My breath leaves me. He is alive.

I unfasten the chain with trembling fingers and open the door.

“Ifeanyi—” The word dies in my mouth.

Behind him stands Rakiya.

For one suspended moment, the world stops.

She looks smaller than I remember. Thinner.

Her face carries the pale exhaustion of someone who has been moved through fear and brought back through mercy. A scarf covers part of her hair. Her clothes are not the ones she wore in Ogbomosho. One hand holds a small sketchbook tightly against her chest.

Her left hand.

I stare at her, unable to speak.

Rakiya looks at me. Then her face crumples.

“Lizzy.”

The sound of my name breaks the spell. 

I cover my mouth with both hands, but the cry still escapes. “Oh my God.”

She steps forward. I step toward her. Then we are holding each other. Not politely. Not carefully. We hold each other like two people rescued from separate drowning places.

Her body shakes against mine. Mine shakes too. I feel her bones beneath my arms, the tremor in her breath, the desperate grip of her fingers at my back. She smells of dust, fear, car leather, and faint paint.

“You’re alive,” I whisper.

She begins to sob. “I thought I would not see you again.”

“Jesus,” I cry softly. “Thank You, Jesus.”

Ifeanyi stands at the doorway, watching us for only a moment before looking away. His face is controlled, but his eyes are not. Something has happened. Something he has not yet said.

He speaks quickly. “Let her have anything she needs. Food. Bath. Fresh clothes. Rest. Do not ask too many questions yet.”

I release Rakiya enough to look at him. “You’re leaving?”

“I have to run and finish this up.”

“What does that mean?”

“It means tonight is not over.”

Fear returns.

“Ifeanyi—”

“No time.” His voice is calm but urgent. “Lock the door after me. Do not open for anyone unless I call first and you hear my voice. If Ekanem calls, answer. If I send Madu, I will call before he comes.”

Rakiya grips my hand.

I look at Ifeanyi’s face more closely. There is strain around his eyes. A small mark near his jaw. His shirt sleeve is slightly rumpled.

“You’re hurt.”

“It is nothing.”

“That is what men say when it is something.”

For a second, despite the urgency, something like a smile crosses his mouth. “I am fine.”

“You are not.”

“Lizzy.” He says my name gently, but firmly. “I have to go.”

There is no room to argue. I step closer. 

“Okay.” My voice shakes. “I pray for God’s protection over you in Jesus’ name.”

He lowers his head slightly. Not dramatically. Not mockingly. As if he receives it. “Amen.”

Our eyes meet.

For a brief second, the corridor, the danger, the plan, and the night narrow into that word. Amen. Then he turns and walks away.

I watch him until he disappears toward the elevator. Then I close the door. Lock it. Latch the chain. Check it twice. Only then do I turn back to Rakiya.

She stands near the centre of the room, looking around like a child who has been brought into safety but does not yet trust walls. Her fingers still clutch the sketchbook. Her eyes move from the bed to the curtains, from the table to the Bible, from my face to the door.

“You are safe here,” I say softly.

She nods, but fear remains in her eyes.

“Come.”

I lead her to the bed.

She sits carefully, as though afraid of dirtying it.

I kneel before her. “Are you hurt?”

She shakes her head. 

“Did they touch you?”

Her eyes fill. “Not like that.”

Relief and anger move through me together.

“Did they beat you?”

“No. They watched me.”

The answer chills me.

“What do you mean?”

She looks at the sketchbook in her lap.

“They wanted me to paint.”

I close my eyes briefly. Of course. Not her body first. Her gift. The merchants always knew where the treasure was.

“Have you eaten?”

She shakes her head. “I was afraid.”

“Afraid to eat?”

“I thought maybe…”

She does not finish. I understand enough.

I stand and go to the table. There are snacks, bottled water, fruit, and a meal I barely touched earlier. I bring the water first.

“Small sips.”

She drinks carefully. Then more quickly.

“Slowly,” I say.

She obeys.

I sit beside her.

“Do you want to bathe first or eat first?”

She looks confused by the kindness of options.

“Can I wash my face?”

“Of course.”

I take her to the bathroom, give her a towel, soap, and one of the loose T-shirts I packed. When she comes out, her face is clean but tired, the scarf removed, her hair loose around her shoulders. She looks more like the girl from Ogbomosho now, though something guarded has entered her.

She eats slowly at first. Then hunger overcomes fear. I watch her with an ache in my chest. After a few minutes, she looks at me.

“Where is Uncle Sam?”

The question strikes me. I set my hand gently on hers.

“He is still in custody.”

Her face changes. “Because of me.”

“No.”

“Yes. They said he took me.”

“He rescued you.”

“But they said—”

“Listen to me.” My voice becomes firmer.

“You are not the reason evil people lied. You are not the reason Sammy is in custody. You are one of the reasons we will prove the truth.”

Tears roll down her cheeks. “I should have stayed.”

“With Sammy?”

She nods. “I was afraid. When they came near the house after he was taken, I hid. Then one woman came. She said she knew how to keep me safe. She said Uncle Sam would be freed if I followed her.”

My body goes still. “What woman?”

“I don’t know her name.”

“Can you describe her?”

Rakiya nods, then looks at the sketchbook. “I drew her.”

She opens it.

My pulse quickens.

On the page is a face sketched in hurried lines: a woman with a narrow chin, full lips, and a headscarf wrapped low over her forehead. The drawing is rough but vivid. Rakiya has caught the eyes especially — calm, watchful, unkind beneath a smile.

I stare at the image.

“Did Ifeanyi see this?”

“Yes.”

“That is how he found you?”

She nods.

“He asked me to show him everyone.”

I look again at the sketch. The gift that made her valuable has also begun to testify. I place the book carefully on the table.

“Rakiya, I need you to rest.”

“I don’t want to sleep.”

“I understand.”

“If I sleep, they may come.”

“They will not enter this room.”

“You don’t know that.”

She is right. I do not. So I do not lie.

“Then we will stay awake for a while,” I say. “Together.”

She looks at me, searching my face.

“Will you leave?”

“No.”

“Promise?”

“I promise.”

She leans against me slowly, like someone testing whether a wall will hold.

I put my arm around her.

For a while, neither of us speaks. My phone lies on the bed, waiting for Ifeanyi’s call. Rakiya’s breathing gradually slows. Her head rests against my shoulder. I hold her and look at the door.

Ifeanyi has brought her back. But he has gone out again. Dike is still in the night. Sammy is still in custody. The woman in Rakiya’s sketch has a face. The operation is not over.

I whisper another Scripture under my breath. “The Lord shall preserve thy going out and thy coming in…” My voice trembles. “…from this time forth, and even for evermore.”

Rakiya stirs slightly but does not lift her head. I hold her tighter.

The prayer continues inside me long after the words end.







































































THIRTY-TWO





Rakiya sleeps with her fingers wrapped around the edge of my skirt. Even in sleep, she does not fully let go.

I sit beside her on the bed, one hand resting lightly over hers, afraid that if I move too much she will wake and remember fear before safety. The room is dim now. I have turned off the main lights and left only the bedside lamp on. Its soft gold spreads across the sheets, the half-open sketchbook, the bottled water, the food tray she barely finished, and the small towel she used after washing her face.

She looks younger asleep. Younger than the girl whose paintings carry fire, tombs, chains, crowns, strange skies, and children walking out of darkness. Younger than the girl who had died in a hospital and returned asking for food. Younger than the gift people have begun to hunt.

My phone vibrates. I snatch it from the bed. Ifeanyi. My heart leaps into my throat before I answer.

“Hello?”

“I am downstairs.” His voice is low. Controlled.

“Are you safe?”

“Yes.”

“Is she asleep?”

“Yes.”

“Good. Open for me in three minutes. I’m coming up alone.”

The line ends.

I stare at the phone.

Then I gently loosen Rakiya’s fingers from my skirt. She stirs, frowns slightly, then settles again. I cover her properly and go to the door.

When Ifeanyi knocks, I check through the peephole first. He stands alone.

I open quickly and let him in.

He enters without drama, but the room changes with him. He carries the night on his clothes. Not dust exactly. Not blood. Not anything one can point to and name. But a tension clings to him, as if he has walked through danger and brought back its scent.

His shirt is rumpled at the sleeves. The small mark near his jaw has darkened slightly. His eyes move first to Rakiya. Only after confirming she is asleep does he exhale.

“Thank God,” I whisper.

He nods. “Yes.”

For a moment, neither of us speaks.

He stands near the small sitting area, looking at the sleeping girl as though he has not fully accepted that she is real and safe. Then he sinks into the nearest chair, leans forward, and rubs both hands over his face.

That frightens me more than his silence. Ifeanyi Obiajulu does not collapse into chairs. He arranges himself into them. Tonight, he simply sits.

“What happened?” I ask.

He lowers his hands. “You should sit.”

“I don’t want to sit.”

“Lizzy.”

“Ifeanyi, what happened?”

He looks at Rakiya again. Then he nods once, as though deciding how much the room can bear.

“Dike’s weakness is light.”

I frown. “What does that mean?”

“It means he can survive darkness. He understands darkness. He negotiates with it, hides inside it, commands through it. But once he suspects that something has entered public light, he becomes restless.”

He leans back.

“My father once told us something strange about him. When they were children, Dike hated light. Not normal dislike. Not ordinary sensitivity. He would avoid bright places, cry in the sun, hide in shadowed corners. Their parents thought it was medical at first, but nothing explained it. Especially because he and my father were twins.”

His voice lowers. “My father said it was as if Dike came into the world already at war with light.”

A chill passes through me. I look at Rakiya. She sleeps on, unaware of the darkness that has been spoken over her name.

“I did not go to Dike as an accuser,” Ifeanyi continues. “That would have ended everything. I went as a nephew with information.” He pauses. “And as a man he still believes can be tempted by profit.”

I sit slowly then.

“What did you tell him?”

“I began with the exhibition. Art. Collectors. Abuja. Foreign interest. I let him know the event was attracting more attention than expected. Then I mentioned that a work had surfaced with a possible problem attached to it.”

“Rakiya’s work?”

“Not directly. I described the kind of work. Young northern girl. Raw spiritual themes. No formal training. Signed only with an R. I made it sound as if the art world was beginning to talk.”

His eyes harden.

“That got his attention.”

“How did he react?”

“At first, he pretended not to understand.”

Of course. Men like Dike do not meet danger at the door. They first ask it to introduce itself.

“He said many young artists are strange,” Ifeanyi continues. “He said collectors like mystery. He asked why I was bringing such talk to him.”

“What did you say?”

“I showed him Rakiya’s sketch.”

My eyes move to the sketchbook on the table.

“The woman?”

“Yes.”

Rakiya had drawn her with frightening clarity: narrow chin, full lips, a headscarf pulled low, eyes too calm to be kind.

“I placed the picture before him and told him someone had tipped off the police. I said a car conveying the girl had been intercepted, and the woman’s image was now in evidence.”

My breath stops.

“You told him the police had her?”

“No. I told him the police had intercepted the car.”

“That is dangerous.”

“It was meant to be.”

His gaze remains steady. “Truth would not move him. Fear would.”

I swallow.

“What did he do?”

“For the first time, he lost rhythm.”

The phrase stays with me.

“He asked me where I got the picture. I told him a contact in the police called because the girl had mentioned my family name and he wanted to know if we were related. I told him I paid the contact to retrieve the evidence before it moved upward.”

“You lied to him.”

“Yes.”

“And he believed you?”

“He wanted to.”

That is worse.

“Dike does not believe people because they sound honest,” Ifeanyi says. “He believes what agrees with his fear. The moment I suggested police evidence, his mind began running ahead of me.”

I can almost see it. Dike, shadowed in some expensive room, his calm cracking not because of conscience but because exposure has entered the conversation.

“He asked whether I was sure the police had the girl,” Ifeanyi continues.

“What did you say?”

“I did not answer directly. I told him what mattered was not where the girl was, but who else could connect her work to him before morning.”

“And?”

“He became angry.”

Ifeanyi looks down at his hands. “Not loud at first. Dike rarely begins loudly. He asked what I thought we should do.”

“You made him ask you?”

“I made him think asking me was safer than deciding alone.”

I stare at him. For the first time, I understand the danger of Ifeanyi’s gift. He does not merely speak. He positions.

“What did you suggest?”

“I used something he had once suggested to me.” His jaw tightens.

“Years ago, Dike spoke loosely about children who could be ‘moved’ for private buyers and foreign arrangements. He called it opportunity. Training. Adoption. Talent transfer. Whatever respectable language men like him use when they do not want to say trafficking.”

My stomach turns.

“I told him if the girl was still recoverable before the police understood her value, I could move her out quietly. I said I was leaving Abuja the next day and could use my art contacts to place her beyond local reach.”

The room seems to tilt.

“You made him think you would help him traffic her?”

“I made him think I was willing to remove his problem for profit.”

His voice becomes flat. “I hated every word.”

I believe him. The disgust in his face is not performed. It sits beneath his skin like poison.

“But he trusted it?” I ask.

“He trusted the version of me he wanted to believe still existed.”

Silence falls. That sentence carries more history than he explains.

“What happened then?”

“He made a call.”

“To whom?”

“I don’t know. He walked away to make it. I could not follow without becoming suspicious. But he returned calmer. Too calm. That told me the girl was not far from his reach.”

My fingers tighten around the edge of the chair.

“He knew where she was.”

“Yes.”

Ifeanyi leans forward. “He did not give me an address immediately. He tested me first. Asked why I cared. Asked how much the work might be worth. Asked whether the girl could produce more. Asked whether foreign collectors liked tragic backstories.”

I close my eyes.

Rakiya is sleeping five steps away, and men have discussed her pain as market value.

“What did you say?”

“I said tragedy sells only when it remains controlled.”

The words are ugly. His expression says he knows it.

“And that if the police already had pieces of the story, the girl had to disappear from the local chain before sunrise.”

I open my eyes.

“That pushed him?”

“Yes.”

“Why?”

“Because Dike will do anything to avoid police exposure. Not because he fears prison in the ordinary sense. He has survived imprisonment before. But he knows his crimes are layered. If one case opens properly, many buried things may begin to breathe.”

“The Samson option,” I whisper.

He looks at me. “Yes. I needed him frightened enough to move, but not desperate enough to destroy everything.”

“How did you find her?”

“He sent me to a woman.”

“The woman in the sketch?”

“No. A link to her.” He pauses. “She was being held in a serviced apartment under the care of two women and one man. Not chained. Not visibly abused. That is the cleverness. Anyone who entered casually would think she was a difficult young artist being managed by handlers.”

“Handlers?”

“They called themselves handlers.”

The word fills me with anger.

“They had her materials. Paints. Canvases. Clothes. Food. A clean room. Nothing that would look like captivity to someone who did not know what captivity feels like.”

“But she could not leave.”

“No.”

“How did you get her out?”

“I arrived with Madu and one of Ekanem’s discreet men nearby.”

I stiffen.

“You involved the police?”

“Privately, as planned. Not in uniform. Not in numbers. I needed lawful eyes close, but invisible.”

He continues before I can ask more.

“I told the handlers Dike had authorized immediate transfer. I used enough impatience to sound like someone under pressure from above. The woman resisted at first. She said the girl was not stable enough to move.”

“And Rakiya?”

“She saw me and became afraid.”

His voice softens for the first time. “She thought I was one of them.”

My chest hurts.

“I showed her the sketch she had made of the woman who led her away. Then I told her you were waiting.”

I cover my mouth.

“That was when she moved.”

The tears come quickly. “She believed my name?” I bow my head.

For all my fear, all my confusion, all my uncertainty, my name has become a rope to a frightened child.

“She asked about Sammy,” Ifeanyi says.

“I know. She asked me too.”

“I told her he was alive.”

“Thank you.”

“She held her sketchbook like it was the only thing she owned.”

I look at the book on the table. It is more than a sketchbook. It is record. Witness. Weapon.

“When we brought her out,” he continues, “the woman tried to call someone. Madu took the phone from her before the call connected.”

“Was that safe?”

“No. But necessary.”

“What happened to them?”

“Ekanem’s man stayed behind long enough to identify them and collect what he could without turning the place into a public operation. They will be watched.”

“Not arrested?”

“Not yet.”

The answer frustrates me. “Why not?”

“Because if we arrest the small hands too early, the head disappears.”

I hate that he is right.

“What of Dike?” I ask.

“I returned to him briefly.”

My heart jumps.

“You went back?”

“I had to close the loop.”

“Ifeanyi!”

“If I vanished immediately after taking her, he would know I had played him. I told him the girl had been moved and that I would update him only after I travelled.”

“And he believed you?”

“He accepted it.”

“That is not the same thing.”

“No.”

His eyes darken.

“Dike suspects everyone eventually. But tonight, suspicion is competing with relief. That gives us a little time.”

“How much time?”

“Not much.”

The room becomes silent except for Rakiya’s soft breathing.

Ifeanyi looks toward her.

“She must not leave your sight.”

“She won’t.”

“Tomorrow, we need her formally protected without exposing the full testimony yet.”

“How?”

“Ekanem will advise.”

“And Sammy?”

“If Rakiya can later testify properly, Sammy’s case begins to crack. But we must not rush her.”

“She is terrified.”

“I know.”

He stands and walks toward the window, keeping his back partly to me. For a moment, he looks like a man holding himself together by force.

“You did it,” I say quietly.

“No.”

He does not turn.

“God did.”

The answer surprises me. It does not sound like religious politeness. It sounds like admission.

“Ifeanyi.”

He turns slightly.

“Thank you.”

His face tightens. “Don’t thank me yet.”

“You brought her back.”

“I also walked into Dike’s trust and used the version of myself he still believes in.”

“And that bothers you.”

“It should.”

His eyes meet mine.

“Lizzy, tonight I learned something unpleasant.”

“What?”

“That it was too easy for me to speak his language.”

I have no answer.

He looks away again. “Some darkness remains fluent in a man even after he thinks he has left it.”

The confession sits heavily between us. I rise and go to him, stopping at a respectful distance.

“You used what you knew to save her.”

He gives a faint, bitter smile. “That is what people say when they want the knife to feel like medicine.”

“No,” I say. “That is what I say because the girl is sleeping in that bed and not in their room.”

He looks at Rakiya.

The bitterness does not leave his face completely, but something in it softens.

“She will need more than rescue,” he says.

“I know.”

“She will need protection. Legal cover. Medical attention. A statement taken carefully. Trauma support. Someone to make sure no one converts her gift into another cage.”

“I know.”

“And you cannot do it alone.”

“I know that too.”

He looks at me, almost amused despite the exhaustion. “Good. You are learning.”

Before I can answer, Rakiya stirs. Her eyes open suddenly, wide with fear.

“Lizzy?”

I rush back to the bed.

“I’m here.”

She sits up, breathing fast.

“I thought…”

“You’re safe.”

Her gaze moves to Ifeanyi.

For a second, her body tightens.

Then recognition comes.

“You came back.”

Ifeanyi nods. “I told you I would bring you to her.”

She looks at me, then at him.

“Where is the woman?”

“She cannot reach you here,” he says.

Rakiya’s fingers search for the sketchbook.

I hand it to her.

She clutches it to her chest.

“She said Uncle Sam sold me.”

The words enter the room like a blade.

I close my eyes. Ifeanyi’s face goes hard.

“She lied.”

Rakiya looks at me.

“She said he took money. She said if I stayed, police would arrest me too.”

“No,” I say firmly. “Sammy never sold you. Sammy is in trouble because he tried to protect you.”

Tears fill her eyes.

“I knew it.” She says it like someone trying to convince the part of herself that fear had damaged. “I knew he would not.”

“You were scared,” I say. “They used that.”

She nods slowly.

Then she opens the sketchbook with trembling fingers. “There are more faces.”

Ifeanyi and I look at each other.

She turns the pages. One face. Then another. A man with heavy eyelids. A woman with a scar near her eyebrow. A driver with a thick neck. A room with a painting leaning against the wall. A gate. A logo on a folder. A signboard partly hidden behind curtains. Each sketch is rough, hurried, but alive with detail.

Ifeanyi moves closer. His exhaustion disappears.

“Rakiya,” he says carefully, “did you draw these from memory?”

She nods.

“I draw so I don’t forget.”

A strange silence fills the room. The child they wanted to exploit has been recording them all along. I touch the Cross pendant at my neck.

For the first time that night, hope comes not as comfort, but as evidence. Ifeanyi looks at me. Then at the sketches. Then back at me.

“This,” he says quietly, “is our trail.”

Rakiya holds the book tighter.













































THIRTY-THREE





With Rakiya on my side, sleep has become impossible. Ifeanyi told me that Dike has been taken in by the police for interrogation. But I still have the feeling that Rakiya is not yet out of danger. 

ASP Ekanem has moved swiftly, using Rakiya’s sketches, Ifeanyi’s intelligence, and the evidence gathered from the serviced apartment to open the first legal door against Dike Obiajulu. By every ordinary measure, something has broken in our favour.

But my spirit is not at rest.

I sit near the window of my hotel room, watching Abuja surrender its night slowly. The city lights still glow beneath the pale sky. Behind me, Rakiya sleeps with her sketchbook held close to her chest, as though even in sleep she fears that memory can be stolen.

I should feel relief. Instead, I feel warning. The full moon is in four days.

The thought returns to me again and again, pressing into my mind until prayer itself becomes urgent. Sammy had once said enough for me to understand that the GODs did not treat the full moon as decoration. In their world, timing mattered. Ritual seasons mattered. Altars mattered. A broken spiritual claim would not be left unattended.

If Dike has been exposed, if Rakiya has been recovered, if the child-abduction chain has been disturbed, then the Order will not simply fold its hands. Darkness does not surrender because one room has been lit. It searches for another altar.

Rakiya is more relaxed now. She is asleep. I pick up my phone and dial. Bitty answers on the second ring.

“Lizzy?”

His voice carries sleep and alarm together.

“Please listen carefully,” I say.

“What happened?”

“Rakiya is safe.”

He exhales sharply.

“Thank God.”

“And Dike has been taken.”

There is silence.

Then he asks, “Taken how?”

“ASP Ekanem moved with the evidence. Rakiya’s sketches helped. Ifeanyi’s visit to Dike worked.”

“Where is Ifeanyi?”

“He is safe.”

“And Sammy?”

“Still in custody.”

The silence that follows is different. He understands the danger before I say it.

“The full moon is in four days,” I whisper.

Bitty becomes very still on the other end of the line.

“You remembered.”

“Yes.”

“Then we don’t have time.”

My hand tightens around the phone.

“That is why I called you. I need you to pray with me.”

“I will.”

“Bitty, I am afraid they will try one last thing.”

“They will.”

His certainty chills me.

“They may try to reclaim power through Sammy.”

“Yes.”

I close my eyes. “Please don’t say it like that.”

“I won’t soften it and make you careless.”

“I am not careless.”

“I know. But fear can make people either careless or frozen.”

I stand and begin pacing slowly.

“Sammy must not remain exposed.”

“No, he must not.”

“Police custody isolates him.”

“Yes.”

“And if one person inside that station is compromised…”

“They don’t need to break into the station,” Bitty says quietly. “They only need access.”

The words enter the room like cold wind. I look at Rakiya. She stirs slightly but does not wake.

“I need you to call Ifeanyi,” I say. “He understands power, movement, law, and pressure. But he may not understand the ritual urgency the way you do.”

“I will call him.”

“Tell him the full moon matters.”

“I will.”

“Tell him Sammy must be moved or protected.”

“I will.”

“And Bitty…”

“Yes?”

“Do not tell anyone else. This is only for you to hear because I need you to join me in prayer.”

“I understand.”

“No group prayer message. No coded discussion. No one who doesn’t need to know.”

“I understand, Lizzy.” His voice softens.

“Put the phone on speaker.”

I do.

Then I kneel beside the bed.

Bitty prays again for Sammy’s preservation, for his deliverance and for confusion in the camp of the GODs, for every compromised hand around Sammy to be exposed, for Ifeanyi’s wisdom, for Ekanem’s protection, for Rakiya’s healing, and for the Cross of Christ to speak louder than every covenant of the Order.

By the time he finishes, tears are running down my face.

“Amen,” I whisper.

“Keep praying,” he says. “Do not let fear be the only voice in that room.”

After the call ends, I remain on my knees. I instinctively open the Bible and start to read, since I cannot sleep. 

Then my phone vibrates. Ifeanyi.

I answer immediately.

“Lizzy.”

His voice is sharp, fully awake. “Bitty just called me.”

“I asked him to.”

“The full moon is in four days.”

“Yes.”

“He says Sammy must not remain where he is.”

“Yes.”

“What exactly is he afraid they will do?”

I close my eyes.

There it is. The question.

Ifeanyi understands Dike’s psychology. He understands political cover, legal strategy, police pressure, art-market laundering, and the arrangement of power. But he does not understand this part. Not yet.

“He called it the last joker of the GODs,” Ifeanyi continues, “What does that mean?”

I swallow.

“They may try to offer Sammy as the devil’s alternative.”

Silence.

Then Ifeanyi’s voice comes lower. “Explain that to me.”

I sit on the edge of the bed.

Rakiya’s eyes open slowly behind me, but she does not speak.

“It is an inversion,” I say. “A counterfeit pattern.”

“Of what?”

“Abraham and Isaac.”

Another silence follows.

I imagine Ifeanyi standing somewhere in the hotel corridor or in his room, one hand on his phone, impatience fighting with attention.

“Go on,” he says.

“In the Book of Genesis, God commanded Abraham to offer Isaac, his son of promise. To many people, the story sounds cruel when read only on the surface. But God was not hungry for Isaac’s blood. He was proving Abraham’s faith and establishing a spiritual pattern.”

I look at my open Bible.

“Abraham surrendered what was most precious to him. But when the knife was raised, God stopped him. God provided a ram.”

“A substitute,” Ifeanyi says.

“Yes. Isaac was spared. God received Abraham’s faith, not Isaac’s blood. The altar became a place of divine provision, not human destruction.”

Ifeanyi says nothing.

I continue. “That pattern points forward to Christ. God Himself would later give His own Son, not as murder, not as ritual cruelty, but as willing redemption. Christ laid down His life to redeem mankind from sin and death. The true sacrifice was love. The true altar was obedience. The true outcome was life.”

“And the devil’s alternative?”

“It twists the pattern.”

My voice grows steadier as I speak. “Darkness cannot create. It imitates and corrupts. So where God establishes faith, Satan establishes fear. Where God provides a substitute to preserve life, darkness demands a victim to invoke death. Where Christ offers Himself willingly to redeem, the Order forces a marked person onto an altar to enslave others.”

Ifeanyi’s breathing changes slightly. “Sammy is the marked person.”

“Yes.”

“The Key.”

“Yes.”

“The breach he created by laying the Cross upright.” 

“Yes.”

I stand again, unable to remain seated.

“Sammy laid the Cross upright on Ikenna’s grave. He disrupted the inversion. If they cannot make him reverse it, they may try to use him as a counterfeit offering – not to redeem, but to restore their broken power.”

Ifeanyi is silent for several seconds. When he speaks, his voice has lost its impatience. “So they will not necessarily need to kill him publicly.”

“No.”

“They need access.”

“Yes.”

“A controlled transfer, a false release, a compromised officer, a staged incident…”

My chest tightens. “Exactly.”

“Or they keep him isolated until the ritual window.”

I close my eyes. “Yes.”

“Then custody is not protection.”

“No.”

“It is containment.”

The word strikes me. Containment. That is what frightens me. A legal room becoming a ritual waiting room.

“Ifeanyi,” I say, “I know how this sounds.”

“No,” he cuts in. “Do not apologize. After Rakiya, I am finished dismissing things because they sound strange.”

Relief weakens me.

“What do we do?”

“I am calling Ekanem.”

“Can he move Sammy?”

“He may not have authority alone. But he can create pressure. Medical review. Transfer request. Protective custody. Reclassification of threat. Anything that changes access.”

“And if they refuse?”

“Then refusal becomes part of the evidence.”

That is Ifeanyi again. Turning danger into leverage.

“Ifeanyi…”

“Yes?”

“Please be careful.”

“I will.”

“Do not underestimate the spiritual side.”

“I don’t.” He pauses. “But you too must not underestimate the human side. Spirits use men. Men sign papers. Men open doors. Men look away. We must close the human doors while you pray against the unseen ones.”

That sentence steadies me. “Okay.”

“Keep Rakiya with you. Do not let anyone know she is there.”

“She is awake.”

Rakiya sits up slowly, clutching the blanket.

“What did she hear?” Ifeanyi asks.

I look at her. Her face is pale.

“Enough.”

“Ask her if she has seen anything concerning Sammy.”

I turn to Rakiya. She is already staring at me with frightened eyes.

“I saw him,” she whispers.

My heart begins to pound. “In a dream?”

She nods.

“He was standing in a dark place. People were around him, but their faces were covered. They wanted him to kneel.”

I repeat her words to Ifeanyi. He listens quietly.

“What else?” he asks.

I ask her.

Rakiya swallows. “He refused.”

Tears fill my eyes.

“And behind him,” she continues, “there was a Cross. Not upside down. Standing.”

I can barely breathe.

“And there was a ram.”

“A ram?” I whisper.

She nods. “It stood between him and the people.”

I repeat it to Ifeanyi.

For a long moment, he says nothing. Then he exhales slowly.
