“When this is over,” he says, “you people will explain to me how visions became operational intelligence.”

Despite the fear, a fragile smile touches my lips.

“Move Sammy.”

“I am calling Ekanem now.”

The line ends.

I remain beside Rakiya, holding the phone.

The morning has fully entered the room, spreading light across the carpet, the Bible, the sketchbook, and the chair where I have paced through the night.

It’s four days to the full moon. Dike is in custody. Rakiya is rescued. The GODs are preparing their last joker. But now Ifeanyi understands. Not perfectly. Not as Bitty does. But enough to act. I look at Rakiya. Then at the Bible. Then, I touch the Cross on my chest.

“No,” I whisper. But with a certainty that surprises me. “No.”

The ram has already been provided. The true Lamb has already been slain.

Sammy Briggs will not be offered to darkness while the Cross still stands upright over Ikenna’s grave.



























































THIRTY-FOUR





ASP Ekanem has already begun discreet inquiries into Sammy’s prolonged detention, but he makes it clear that the process will take time. Files have to be requested. Officers have to be questioned. Orders have to be traced without alerting the wrong people too early. 

Now that the art exhibition has ended successfully and the pressure on Ifeanyi’s schedule has eased, he decides we can no longer wait from a distance. We have to go to Lagos ourselves, see Sammy in custody, and press for his release before time slips any further out of our hands.

 The urgency has become larger than all of us. Rakiya sits beside me in the aircraft, quiet and withdrawn, her sketchbook held against her chest. She has not said much since the morning vision, but her silence is not emptiness. It feels occupied, as though something inside her is still watching the dark room where Sammy had refused to kneel.

Ifeanyi sits across the aisle, phone in hand, sending messages until the cabin crew instructs him to switch the device to flight mode. Even then, his fingers remain around the phone, restless.

The man who seemed so composed in galleries, restaurants, and hotel lobbies is beginning to come apart under the pressure of time. I cannot blame him. The full moon is no longer an idea. It has become a clock. When we land in Lagos, the heat receives us first. Then noise. Then movement.

The airport is crowded with travellers pulling boxes, drivers holding placards, families embracing, and men shouting into phones as if volume can solve logistics. Ifeanyi moves through it with controlled impatience, one hand slightly behind him as if he expects us to keep close without needing to be told.

We do.

Rakiya’s fingers stay locked around mine. At the parking area, our pickup vehicle is already waiting.

“Straight to the station,” Ifeanyi says. 

No greeting. No explanation. Driver nods and moves.

The drive feels longer than it should. Lagos traffic gathers around us in stubborn layers: buses leaning into lanes, motorcycles slipping between vehicles, hawkers weaving through gridlock with bottled water, plantain chips, chargers, groundnuts, and newspapers. The city does not care that a man’s life may be counting down toward ritual darkness.

Ifeanyi makes calls. One after another. Some he speaks through calmly. Some he ends too quickly. Some he listens to without blinking, his jaw tightening each second. By the time we reach the station, something in me already knows. 

Sammy is no longer there.

An officer at the front desk claims he does not know. Another says the matter has been transferred. Transferred where? He cannot say. Who authorized it? He does not know. When? He was not on duty. Where is the record? The officer begins to look irritated.

That is when Ifeanyi loses his calm. Not by shouting. That would be easier. His voice simply drops. And the entire room feels it.

“Officer,” he says, “a suspect connected to an active petition, an attempted murder threat, and a missing-person recovery process has been moved from this station, and you are telling me no one here knows where he was taken?”

The officer shifts.

“Sir, I said I was not on duty.”

“I heard what you said. Now hear what I am asking. Who signed the movement order?”

“I don’t have access to—”

“Then bring the person who does.”

The officer hesitates.

Ifeanyi brings out his phone.

“Or I begin making calls from here, and by the time I finish, this station will explain to Abuja why a detainee vanished under your roof.”

That changes the atmosphere. Another officer is called. Then another. A file appears. Then disappears. Then reappears with fewer pages than it should have.

I stand beside Rakiya, feeling her hand grow cold in mine. Finally, one tired-looking senior officer admits that Sammy has been moved.

“Moved where?” Ifeanyi asks.

“Ogun State.”

“Where in Ogun?”

The man hesitates.

Ifeanyi leans forward. “Do not test me.”

The officer lowers his voice. “A correctional holding facility. Temporary remand.”

“For what reason?”

“Order from above.”

“Whose above?”

Silence. That silence is answer enough.

Ifeanyi steps away and calls ASP Ekanem. The call lasts several minutes.

I cannot hear everything, but I see enough in Ifeanyi’s face to know that the news is not good. When he returns, his anger has been forced back under discipline. That almost frightens me more.

“Ekanem says it is beyond his immediate jurisdiction,” he says. “He is preparing papers for Dike’s indictment, but that will take time. He will make enquiries through his contacts in Ogun.”

“We don’t have time,” I say.

“I know.”

His voice is tight.

“Sammy is in prison?”

“Remand facility.”

“That is worse.”

He looks at me. “Why?”

“Because now he is farther from the few people who know the danger.”

I turn away, pressing my hand to my mouth. The full moon. The transfer. The silence around the order. The missing pages. The refusal to name who authorized it.

It is already happening.

“They are going to sacrifice him,” I say.

Rakiya looks up sharply.

Ifeanyi’s eyes harden. “Lizzy—”

“On the full moon,” I whisper.

His face changes. Not disbelief. He has begun to understand enough to fear the pattern.

Before he can speak, Rakiya breaks the silence.

“His soul is calling out for his father.”

We both turn to her.

Her voice is small, but clear.

“What did you say?” Ifeanyi asks.

Rakiya holds her sketchbook tighter. “Uncle Sam. His soul is calling out for his father.”

My chest tightens.

Sammy’s dream returns to me at once. His foster father had laid hands on him and blessed him. His desperate request in the police station rises again in my memory. Find my father, before death finds me.

Ifeanyi rubs his forehead. “We don’t have time.”

Rakiya looks at him.

“And your dad is crying out for you too, sir.”

The words strike him visibly. He goes still.

“What?”

“Your dad is a Bishop?” Rakiya asks. “He is crying out for you.”

Ifeanyi’s expression closes.

“What has that got to do with all of this?” His voice is sharper than before.

Not at Rakiya. At the wound she has touched.

“Wait,” I say.

He turns to me. “Lizzy, we need to focus.”

“I am focusing.”

“No, you are following another spiritual branch when Sammy is being moved through a system we do not control.”

“Wait, Ifeanyi.”

Something opens in my mind. I turn to Rakiya.

“The Scripture we read this morning at our devotion, the Prophet Malachi spoke about the Last days… that the Lord will send the prophet Elijah to us, And he will turn the hearts of the fathers to the children, and the hearts of the children to their fathers, Otherwise He will come and strike the land with a curse.” 

Then I lift up my eyes to Ifeanyi. “That day I visited Sammy in Ogbomosho,” I say slowly, “He mentioned that the Lord had ministered to him by a vision a prophecy about the reconciliation of fathers to children and children to fathers.”

Ifeanyi looks away.

I continue. “That reconciliation would restrain the curse.”

The sentence enters the space between us. I see him understand before he wants to. Sammy crying out for his father. Ifeanyi’s father crying out for him. Two sons. Two fathers. Two broken lines. 

“And a curse looking for legal ground,” I whisper, “This is not a distraction,” I say. “It is instruction.”

He says nothing.

“Sammy needs his father’s blessing. But you also need your father’s reconciliation.”

His jaw tightens.

“Do not turn this toward me.”

“I am not turning it toward you. Rakiya did not know enough to manipulate you. Can’t you see that Ifeanyi.”

He looks at her. She lowers her eyes immediately.

“Ifeanyi,” I say softly, “tell me the truth. Haven’t you been longing to see the Bishop lately?”

His face changes. It is brief. But unmistakable. A blush of shame and tenderness crosses his expression before pride tries to cover it.

He looks away. That is answer enough.

“If this is the Lord’s instruction,” I say, “don’t let your ego frustrate God’s mercy.”

He exhales sharply. “My ego?”

“Yes.”

His eyes return to mine. I should be afraid of the anger there. But I am too afraid for Sammy to fear Ifeanyi now.

“You said spirits use men,” I remind him. “Men sign papers. Men open doors. Men look away. Then let God also use men. Let Him use fathers. Let Him use sons. Let Him use reconciliation as a door the enemy cannot pass through.”

He stares at me for a long moment.

The noise of the station continues around us: phones ringing, officers talking, a woman crying near a bench, someone arguing about bail. But inside our small circle, everything narrows to one decision.

Onitsha. Ifeanyi’s father.. whom he calls The Bishop. The wound he has avoided for years. Finally, Ifeanyi looks down.

“Okay.” The word comes out low. Almost unwilling. But it comes.

Relief moves through me so quickly I nearly cry. Then another fear rises. Rakiya.

I do not want to leave her behind. Not in Lagos. Not in a hotel. Not with strangers. Before I can speak.

Ifeanyi is already bringing out his phone. “She is coming with us,” he says.

I look up.

“What?”

He does not look at me.

“Enough of this drama. I am booking three tickets to Onitsha.”

Rakiya’s fingers tighten around mine. I almost laugh through my tears.

“Thank you.”

“Do not start thanking me again.” But his voice has softened.

He walks a short distance away and begins making calls.

I turn to Rakiya and draw her into my arms. She comes willingly, small and trembling.

“Sammy will be alright,” I whisper.

Her shoulders shake. “Will he?”

I hold her tighter.

“Yes.”

I do not know how yet. I do not know where the facility is. I do not know who signed the movement order. But I know this much: God is not leading us backward. He is leading us to the root. To fathers. To blessings. To broken authority restored before darkness can claim what rebellion has fractured.

“Your Sammy will be alright,” I say again.

Rakiya cries quietly against me.

Across the station, Ifeanyi stands with his phone to his ear, speaking in clipped instructions about flights, timing, security, and transport.

But his face has changed. The strategy remains. The urgency remains. The fear remains. Yet beneath it all, another battle has begun. Not against Dike. Not against the GODs. Against the pride of a son who must return to his father before another son can be rescued from death.





















































THIRTY-FIVE





Ifeanyi has not recovered from the impact of Rakiya’s revelation. Your dad is crying out for you too, sir. 

The words still hang over him. He has not dismissed them, because somewhere beneath his pride, his stubbornness, and the polished armour he has worn for years, he knows they are true. Rev. Obiajulu has never stopped loving him. No matter how far Ifeanyi ran, no matter how long he stayed away, his father’s heart had remained open.

And that is what makes Rakiya’s words impossible to ignore.

She knows nothing about the long silence between father and son. She knows nothing about the old wound, the rebellion, the unanswered calls, or the guilt Ifeanyi has carried over Ikenna. She could not have invented a truth she was never told.

So, at last, the prodigal son is ready to return. Not triumphantly. Not with explanations polished for dignity.

But with the humility of a man finally willing to face his deepest fear – bowing before the father who has loved him through absence, pride, and pain. That is how much the mission to rescue Sammy from the jaws of death now means to Ifeanyi. This is the cost of Sammy’s deliverance – to save another son, he must first return as one.

My hands are still trembling from everything that happened at the station – the news that Sammy had been moved. With trembling hands, I place a call to Nkeonyere. 

He moves quickly, booking tickets, arranging transport, making calls, speaking with the efficiency of a man who can turn crisis into movement. But something in his face has changed. The old composure remains, yet it now covers a deeper disturbance. He is no longer merely going to Onitsha because Sammy’s rescue requires it. He is going home.

“Nkeonyere,” I say when she answers.

“Lizzy?”

“Can you come to Onitsha?”

She becomes alert immediately.

“Why? What is happening?”

“We are coming home.”

There is a pause.

“We?”

“Yes.”

“You and who?”

I look across the airport lounge at Ifeanyi, standing near the glass wall with his phone to his ear. Rakiya sits beside me, clutching her sketchbook, watching him the way children watch adults who hold the next instruction to their safety.

“Ifeanyi,” I say.

Nkeonyere laughs once. Not because it is funny. Because she does not believe me. “Ifeanyi? Lizzy, you are lying.”

“I’m not.”

“Wait. My brother Ifeanyi?”

“Yes.”

“The same Ifeanyi?”

“He is booking a flight right now to the nearest airport to Onitsha.”

Silence follows.

Then, very quietly, she asks, “What happened?”

“It’s a long story.”

“Did you succeed in finding your friend’s father?”

I close my eyes.

“Nkay, please come to Onitsha. I will gist you everything when we get there.”

“You know I hate suspense.”

“I know.”

“Is Ifeanyi okay?”

I look at him again. His jaw is tight. His free hand rests against the glass, fingers spread, as though he needs to steady himself against something more solid than thought.

“No,” I say softly. “But I think he is ready.”

Nkeonyere understands enough not to ask further. “I’m coming.”

The journey to Onitsha feels shorter than it is. Perhaps because fear travels faster than aircraft, every mile seems to carry us toward more than one destination.

Sammy is still somewhere beyond our reach, hidden in a remand facility in Ogun State, caught between law, conspiracy, and ritual time. Dike has been shaken but not yet defeated. ASP Ekanem is working his end. Bitty is praying, I believe. Rakiya is beside me, sometimes silent, sometimes sketching, sometimes staring at nothing.

And Ifeanyi sits ahead of us in the vehicle from the airport to Okosi Street, saying almost nothing. The closer we come to the Obiajulu house, the more the air seems to tighten around him.

By the time we enter the street that I have become familiar with, evening has begun to soften the sky. Okosi Street looks as it did when Nkeonyere first showed it to me – fenced bungalows, old gates, small shops, children playing near the gutter, women calling across compounds, the ordinary life of a neighbourhood unaware that one of its sons is returning to the wound he has avoided for years.

The gate to Number 15 stands partly open. Mrs. Obiajulu comes out first. She sees Ifeanyi and stops. For a moment, she does not move. Then her hand goes to her chest.

“Ifeanyi?”

He steps out of the vehicle. “Mummy.”

The word breaks her.

She does not wait for luggage, explanation, or dignity. She crosses the small compound with surprising speed and holds him. Ifeanyi stands stiffly for one second, like a man unused to receiving love without negotiation.

Then his arms go around her.

I look away.

Some moments are too private even when they happen in the open.

Rakiya slips her hand into mine.

We wait.

Mrs. Obiajulu cries quietly against her son’s shoulder, touching his face, his arms, his chest, as if to confirm that he has not come as a voice on the phone but as flesh returned to her gate.

A lost son comes back home.

“Come inside,” she says, wiping her face. “Your father is inside.”

At that, Ifeanyi’s body changes. Only slightly. But I notice it.

The first embrace has been mother’s mercy. The next room holds father’s reckoning.

Rev. Obiajulu is in the sitting room when we enter. He rises slowly. Not with the shock of a man who never imagined the day. More like a father who has waited so long for a door to open that when it finally does, he fears moving too quickly might frighten the moment away.

Ifeanyi stands just inside the room. For a few seconds, father and son look at each other. No one speaks.

The air holds years. Arguments. Silences. Accusations. Letters unanswered. Calls avoided. Ikenna’s death. Dike’s shadow. A father’s regret. A son’s pride.

Then Ifeanyi moves. He crosses the room, stops before his father, and kneels.

The sound that comes from Mrs. Obiajulu is almost a cry.

Rev. Obiajulu’s face changes completely.

Ifeanyi bows his head. “Daddy.” The word trembles. “I have sinned against God and against you.”

Rev. Obiajulu remains standing, his hands hanging at his sides as though he does not yet know whether to lift them.

Ifeanyi continues, his voice breaking now. “Please forgive me for my rebellion. For the years I refused to come home. For the anger. For the pride. For everything I said. Everything I became.” 

His shoulders shake. “And for Ikenna.”

The room becomes still.

“For causing the death of your son.”

Rev. Obiajulu closes his eyes. The pain in his face is terrible. I want to tell Ifeanyi not to say it that way. Not to carry all of Dike’s evil, Ikenna’s choices, and the family’s brokenness onto his own back. But this is not my confession. It is his.

“I should have protected him,” Ifeanyi whispers. “I should have stayed. I should have fought harder. I should have seen what Dike was doing before it swallowed him.”

Rev. Obiajulu steps forward.

His voice is low when he speaks. “Ifeanyi.”

His son does not lift his head.

“Ifeanyi, look at me.”

Slowly, Ifeanyi looks up.

Rev. Obiajulu’s eyes are wet. “You are my son too.”

That sentence opens the room.

“My first son,” he continues. “And I never stopped loving you. Never.”

Ifeanyi’s face collapses.

“Daddy…”

“Welcome home, son.” Rev. Obiajulu bends and places both hands on his shoulders. “Come. Get up.”

But Ifeanyi cannot rise. The weeping overtakes him. Not controlled. Not curated. Not hidden behind expensive linen, influence, or intelligence. He weeps like a son.

Mrs. Obiajulu covers her mouth and cries. Rakiya presses against my side, frightened and moved at once. I stand still, tears running freely down my face.

Rev. Obiajulu kneels before him. 

A father kneeling to reach a son who cannot yet stand.

“I know I am not worthy of Him,” Ifeanyi says through tears. “Please… please ask Jesus to forgive me and accept me as His.”

Rev. Obiajulu’s hand moves to the back of his son’s head.

“He has already forgiven you at the Cross.”

Ifeanyi looks at him, broken.

“He paid the price in full for your sins,” his father says. “Not half. Not when you became better. Not after you returned home. In full.”

Ifeanyi sobs. “Will He receive me?”

“He has been calling you.”

“I ran.”

“And He followed.”

“I am ashamed.”

“Then bring the shame to Him.” Rev. Obiajulu’s voice strengthens. “My son. Let us pray.”

Ifeanyi bows again.

This time, not under guilt alone. Under surrender.

Rev. Obiajulu places his hands on him and prays.

There is nothing theatrical in the prayer. No shouting. No religious performance. Only the deep authority of a father and priest asking the mercy of Christ to receive what pride has released at last.

He thanks God for the Cross. For the blood of Jesus. For mercy stronger than rebellion. For a son returned. The words come haltingly at first. Then clearer – For guilt lifted. For Dike’s influence broken. For the old wound in the Obiajulu house to begin healing where it first bled. Then he leads Ifeanyi to confess Christ.

“Lord Jesus,” Ifeanyi says, “I receive You as my Lord and Saviour. Forgive my sins. Wash me. Deliver me. Take my life. I belong to You.”

At the last sentence, Mrs. Obiajulu cries aloud.

The door opens. Nkeonyere enters. She stops at the threshold, stunned.

Her eyes move from her father kneeling with his hands on Ifeanyi, to her brother bent before him, to her mother weeping, to me crying silently near the sofa.

For a moment, she cannot speak. Then tears spring to her eyes. She covers her mouth with both hands, as if trying to keep the sound inside her.

The prayer ends. Rev. Obiajulu lifts Ifeanyi gently.

Nkeonyere crosses the room. “Ifeanyi.”

He turns. His face is wet, unguarded, almost boyish.

She rushes into his arms. They hold each other tightly. Not like siblings greeting after distance. Like survivors of the same house finding each other after war.

“I’m sorry,” he whispers.

She shakes her head against his shoulder. “Shut up.”

Through my tears, I almost laugh.

“Just shut up and hug me.”

He does.

For a while, no one interrupts them.

Even Rakiya, who understands little of the family history, seems to know that something sacred is happening.

Then Nkeonyere pulls back, wiping her face with both hands.

“I came in and saw one man outside asking for Number 15.”

The room shifts back toward the ordinary so suddenly that Mrs. Obiajulu looks confused.

“Number 15?”

“Yes. A white man. He asked if this is Number 15, Okosi Street.”

Rev. Obiajulu frowns. “This is Number 15. Have you forgotten our house number?”

“I know this is Number 15, Daddy. That is why I’m telling you. He said he is looking for Reverend Obiajulu.”

“A white man?” I ask. The words escape me before I can stop them. At the same time, Ifeanyi says, “A white man?”

We look at each other. The same thought crosses both our faces.

Sammy.

We run to the door.

Behind us, everyone begins speaking at once.

At the gate stands a tall white man, older, his hair silver at the temples, his face lined by travel, worry, and prayer. Beside him stands a woman, also white, holding a handbag tightly in both hands. Their clothes bear the slight disorder of people who have come a long way without caring how they appear at arrival.

The man looks at us with anxious politeness.

“Good evening,” he says. “I am looking for Reverend Joseph Obiajulu.”

Rev. Obiajulu comes behind us.

“I am Joseph Obiajulu.”

The man’s face changes. Relief. Fear. Hope.

“My name is Reverend Andrew Briggs,” he says. “This is my wife, Margaret.”

My knees almost weaken.

Ifeanyi’s hand reaches out and lightly holds my elbow, steadying me without looking away from the couple.

Rev. Obiajulu’s eyes widen. “Briggs?”

“Yes.” The man swallows. “My wife and I have been searching for our son.”

His wife’s eyes fill immediately.

“Chukwuebuka,” she says.

The name enters the compound like a bell.

Chukwuebuka. Sammy’s Igbo name. God is great.

Rev. Obiajulu steps closer.

“Please come in.”

They enter the compound slowly, as though afraid that hope might be withdrawn if they move too quickly.

Inside the sitting room, Reverend Briggs speaks with trembling dignity.

“My wife and I have prayed for Chukwuebuka for years,” Reverend Briggs says. “We never stopped. Last week, one of the ministers in my church asked if I knew any Reverend Obiajulu in Nigeria. He said an inquiry had come from Nigeria about a priest whose foster son was trying to reconnect with him. According to the message, the boy was in the custody of Reverend Obiajulu.”

He paused, his voice tightening with emotion.

“At first, I did not understand. I did not know any Reverend Obiajulu. I did not know where my son was. But when he mentioned a foster son searching for his father, my spirit would not let the matter rest. My wife and I began making calls through the church network, and every inquiry brought us closer to this address.”

He looks from Rev. Obiajulu to Ifeanyi, then to me.

Rev. Obiajulu lifts both hands slowly. “God be magnified.”

Mrs. Briggs begins to cry. “Is Ebuka here?” she asks.

The room stills. Ifeanyi and I look at each other. A silent exchange. How much do we say? How fast?

How do we tell parents who have prayed for years that the son they have found is alive, but hidden inside danger?

Rev. Briggs sees the hesitation.

“Is he alive?”

“Yes,” I say quickly.

Both parents turn to me.

“He is alive.”

Mrs. Briggs presses a hand to her mouth.

“Thank You, Jesus.”

“But he is not here,” Ifeanyi says carefully. “He is in Lagos.”

That is not the full truth. But it is the first mercy.

Rev. Briggs’ face tightens.

“Is he all right?”

“He is alive,” Ifeanyi says.

The repetition is not enough, but it is all we can safely give at once.

Rev. Obiajulu looks at his son. “What happened?”

Ifeanyi takes a breath. “Daddy, we need to leave for Lagos immediately.”

“Now?”

“Yes.”

Rev. Briggs stands at once. “I will come.”

Mrs. Briggs rises too. “So will I.”

Ifeanyi hesitates.

“No,” Mrs. Briggs says before he can object. “I have waited too many years to be told to wait again.” Her voice carries such quiet force that no one argues.

Rev. Briggs turns to Rev. Obiajulu. “Is my son in trouble?”

The room becomes heavy.

Rev. Obiajulu answers before anyone else. “Yes.”

The word lands hard.

“But God has brought you at the right time.”

I close my eyes. Right time. Three days to the full moon. Sammy moved. His soul calling for his father. Ifeanyi reconciled. The father found. The pieces are no longer scattered. They are converging.

Ifeanyi brings out his phone. “I will book the earliest flight to Lagos.”

As he steps aside to make arrangements, I stand near the window, overwhelmed by the speed of God’s mercy. A few hours ago, Ifeanyi resisted returning to his father. Now he has knelt, repented, received Christ, and been restored.

A few minutes after that, Sammy’s foster father walks into the compound as if summoned by the same mercy.

I look at Reverend Briggs holding his wife’s hand. His eyes are wet, but steady. A father has come for his son.

I bow my head. God, please go ahead of us. Break every chain holding Sammy. Let the father’s blessing reach him before the darkness does. 

Rakiya comes quietly to my side and holds my hand. 

“He will be all right,” she whispers.

This time, I believe her more than myself.

“Yes,” I say.

“Sammy will be all right.”

Outside, the evening deepens over Okosi Street.

Inside Number 15, fathers, sons, sisters, mothers, witnesses, and a rescued child gather under one roof, not yet knowing how the next battle will unfold.

But one truth has become impossible to deny. God has begun answering before we finish asking.





































































THIRTY-SIX





Standing behind a rusted gate, with walls faded by heat, dust, rain, and years of official indifference, the police station looks smaller than the fear it carries. A torn flag moves lazily on a pole in the compound. Two seized motorcycles lean against a wall. A tired-looking officer sits under a tree with a register on his lap, and somewhere behind the building, voices rise and fall in the rough rhythm of people pleading, arguing, and waiting for authority to decide whether truth is useful.

Rev. Andrew Briggs stands still beside the vehicle for a moment before we enter. His wife holds his hand. I watch his face.

He is not a young man, but hope has given him a dangerous strength. Since he arrived in Onitsha, he has moved with the strained dignity of a father who had prayed too long to be casual. Every step toward Lagos has tightened him. Every unanswered call has aged him. Every silence around Sammy’s transfer has entered his body like a wound.

Now we are here. The station where Sammy was last confirmed. The place where a paper once held his name. The place where truth has either been hidden, sold, or moved.

Rev. Briggs adjusts his collar, though there is no need. His shirt is creased from travel, his eyes red from prayer and lack of sleep, but there is something immovable in him as he walks into the building.

Ifeanyi walks beside him. Rev. Obiajulu follows closely.

Mrs. Briggs remains near me, trembling silently. Rakiya holds my hand. Her grip is cold.

The DPO’s office smells of cheap air freshener. He exudes the arrogance of a man too comfortable in a chair that gives him power over frightened people.

The DPO sits behind a large desk covered with files, a landline telephone, a half-empty bottle of water, and a nameplate polished more carefully than the room. He does not rise when we enter. He does not offer seats. He looks at us as if we are an inconvenience that has dressed too well for the occasion.

“Yes?” he says.

Rev. Briggs steps forward. “My name is Reverend Andrew Briggs. I am looking for my son.”

The DPO leans back. “This is not missing persons office.”

The rudeness enters the room like a slap. Rev. Briggs absorbs it.

“My son was brought here. His name is Samuel Briggs. He may also be recorded as Samuel Chukwuebuka Briggs.”

The DPO’s eyes flicker. Just once. But I see it. So does Ifeanyi.

“We don’t have anybody like that here,” the DPO says.

Rev. Briggs’ wife makes a small sound beside me.

The Reverend does not move. “Sir,” he says, with the effort of a man choosing honour over anger, “please check your records. We were informed he was held here before being moved.”

“I said we don’t have him.”

“He is my son.”

“Then go and look for him where you kept him.”

The room tightens. 

Rev. Briggs takes one step closer to the desk. “I did not come to quarrel with you. I came as a father. I have searched for this boy for years. My wife and I have prayed for him. If he was here, tell me where he was taken.”

The DPO’s face hardens. “Are you deaf?”

Ifeanyi’s jaw flexes.

Rev. Obiajulu puts a quiet hand on his arm.

I step forward before I can stop myself. “Sir, Sammy was here. I came here to visit him last week, and—”

“Hey, young lady.” The DPO’s voice cuts into mine. “Don’t say what you don’t know.”

“I saw him.”

“You saw who?”

“Sammy.”

“You saw anybody you wanted to see in your dream. This is a police station.”

“He was brought here.”

The DPO reaches for the intercom and presses a button. His eyes remain on me as he speaks. “Get Alabi. Come and remove these people from my office.”

Rev. Briggs lifts his hand slightly. “Officer, please.”

The DPO drops the receiver. “No. You people have wasted enough of my time. Go back to wherever you came from and look for your son there.”

Mrs. Briggs begins to cry. Not loudly. Just a quiet breaking.

Rev. Briggs turns when he hears her. For a second, his fatherly strength falters. He looks at his wife, then at me, then at Rev. Obiajulu. The pain on his face is almost unbearable.

He has travelled across distance, faith, and years of longing. And a man behind a desk has reduced his son to denial. 

Two officers enter the room. They are broad-shouldered, stone-faced, and ready for confrontation. One stands near the door. The other moves toward us.

“Oga said make una come out.”

Rev. Briggs does not move.

“Sir,” the officer says more firmly, “please let’s go.”

The Reverend lifts his face toward the ceiling. The room becomes quiet.

Even the DPO pauses, perhaps curious to see whether the old white priest intends to curse or beg.

Rev. Briggs closes his eyes. “God Almighty,” he says, his voice low but carrying, “only You know the truth. Please protect my son, Ebuka. Wherever he is, let Your mercy find him before wickedness does.” Mrs. Briggs sobs.

Rakiya’s fingers tighten around mine.

Then Rev. Briggs lowers his face and looks directly at the DPO. There is no hatred in his eyes. That makes his words heavier. “Faith is more powerful than government,” he says. “And nothing is more powerful than God.”

For a moment, the office holds its breath.

Then the DPO laughs. Not because it is funny. Because arrogance often laughs at what it fears but refuses to respect.

“Please take that useless quote back to your country,” he says. “This is Nigeria. This is not part of God’s jurisdiction. I am in charge here.”

The words strike the room with the smell of blasphemy.

Rev. Obiajulu shuts his eyes briefly.

Ifeanyi’s face goes cold.

The DPO waves toward the officers. “Take these people out of my office.”

Rev. Briggs resists when one officer touches his arm. Not violently. “Don’t touch me,” he says.

“Sir, please,” I whisper. “Let’s go.”

“Elizabeth,” he says, and his voice breaks, “my son is here somewhere.”

“I know.”

“They know.”

“I know.”

“They are hiding him.”

“I know.”

The officers begin moving us toward the door.

Mrs. Briggs weeps openly now. Rev. Obiajulu holds her gently. Ifeanyi walks last, his eyes fixed on the DPO with a promise so cold I fear it.

We step out into the compound. The sunlight hits us hard. For a few seconds, nobody speaks.

Rev. Briggs stands in the open yard, breathing as if the air itself has become heavy. He looks toward the cell block. Then toward the gate. Then back at the building.

“Ebuka,” he whispers. It is not a call. It is a wound.

Then we hear the siren. Low at first. Then louder.

A dark blue police pickup swings into the compound and stops abruptly, its tyres biting the dusty ground as though the vehicle itself is angry. Dust rises around it.

Every head turns.

Before anyone can understand what is happening, another pickup enters behind it, siren flashing. Then a Peugeot 504 saloon car rolls in with terrible calm and stops at the centre of the compound. Doors open. Men step down. Not lazily. Not as local officers returning from patrol. These men move with purpose.

ASP Ekanem emerges from the Peugeot.

He is dressed in plain clothes, but the entire station seems to recognize authority before anyone names it. He does not look left. He does not greet us. He does not waste one second on explanation.

He walks straight into the building. Fast and hard. Not loud, not showy, but inevitable. His men spread behind him. One covers the entrance. Another moves toward the charge room. Two go around the side of the building. The officers who pushed us out suddenly lose their confidence.

Ifeanyi steps beside me.

“He came himself,” he says.

His voice carries relief and danger together. Then a sound cuts through the compound.

“Daddy!”

Everything in Rev. Briggs stops.

Again. “Daddy!”

This time the voice breaks through the yard, muffled but unmistakable.

Mrs. Briggs screams. “Ebuka!”

I turn toward the first pickup that drove in.

The rear of the vehicle is built like a cubicle, with a small square vent protected by crossed metal bars. The police van is used to convey detainees, and makes human beings look like cargo.

A face appears behind the bars. Thin. Tired. Alive. It’s Sammy. His hand pushes through the vent as far as it can. 

“Daddy!”

Rev. Briggs staggers. For one terrible second, I think his knees will fail.

“Where?” he cries. “Where is Ebuka?”

“There!” I point, tears blinding me. “Sir, he is in the van!”

Mrs. Briggs runs first. Not with the careful movement of a woman her age. Motherhood has taken the years off her body.

Rev. Briggs follows, almost stumbling, calling the name again and again. “Ebuka! Ebuka!”

Sammy presses his face to the bars. He is crying. Not silently. With no dignity. He weeps like a boy who has finally heard the voice he thought he would never hear again.

“Daddy, I’m sorry!”

Rev. Briggs reaches the van and places both hands against the metal.

“My son,” he says, his voice breaking completely. “My son.”

“I’m sorry.”

“No. No. No.”

Rev. Briggs presses his forehead against the bars.

“You are alive. Thank You, Jesus. You are alive.”

Mrs. Briggs touches Sammy’s fingers through the vent, sobbing and calling his name.

“Ebuka. My baby. My son.”

Sammy collapses against the inside of the van, his hand still reaching through the bars.

The compound has gone silent around them. Even officers watch. Some things overpower uniform. A father finding his son is one of them.

Then the station doors burst open.

ASP Ekanem steps out first. Behind him comes the DPO. In handcuffs. The sight shifts the atmosphere again.

The man who mocked faith minutes earlier now walks with his hands bound in front of him, his face stripped of command. His eyes dart around the compound, searching for sympathy among men who suddenly find the dust more interesting.

Ekanem does not look triumphant. That makes him more formidable. He looks precise. Like a blade doing its work. 

He turns to one of his men. “Open the van. Bring the boy out.”

Then he glances at the handcuffed DPO. “Put him inside there. We are going to Force Headquarters.”

The order strikes the yard. The same van that held Sammy will now carry the man who tried to erase him. No one moves for half a second.

Then Ekanem’s voice sharpens. “Now.”

Men rush. The van door is opened.

Sammy steps down weakly. He almost falls, but Rev. Briggs catches him. Father and son collapse into each other.

“Daddy,” Sammy cries.

“I am here.”

“I thought you hated me.”

“No.”

“I ran.”

“I know.”

“I believed them.”

“I know.”

“I’m sorry.”

Rev. Briggs holds him tighter.

“My son, I never stopped looking for you.”

The sentence breaks Sammy completely. He weeps against his father’s shoulder.

Mrs. Briggs wraps her arms around both of them, crying and thanking Jesus at the same time. Rev. Obiajulu stands a short distance away, wiping his eyes. Ifeanyi looks away, but not before I see his face tremble.

Rakiya stands beside me, crying quietly.

“Uncle Sam,” she whispers.

Sammy hears her. He lifts his head. “Rakiya?”

She nods, covering her mouth.

“You’re safe?”

She runs to him.

Rev. Briggs loosens his hold just enough for Sammy to reach her with one arm.

“I’m sorry,” Rakiya cries. “They lied to me.”

“It’s okay,” Sammy says, though he can barely stand. “You’re safe. That’s what matters.”

ASP Ekanem approaches, breaking the moment only because time still matters.

“Reverend Briggs?”

Rev. Briggs turns, still holding Sammy by the shoulders.

“Yes. I am his father.”

Ekanem’s face softens slightly.

“We will process his release immediately.”

“Thank you, officer.”

“You can thank God. We only arrived in time.”

“God bless you, my son.”

“Amen.”

Then Ekanem turns to another officer, a senior man who arrived with him and now stands where the former DPO had stood minutes earlier.

“Take over this process. His release paperwork begins now. Medical check. Statement in the presence of counsel or family representative. No further unauthorized movement. Do you understand?”

“Yes, sir.”

“No one touches this boy without clearance.”

“Yes, sir.”

Ekanem glances toward the handcuffed DPO being pushed toward the van.

“And secure all transfer records from this station before they grow legs.”

“Yes, sir.”

He turns to Ifeanyi.

“We will talk later.”

Ifeanyi nods. “Alright, Supro. Thank you.”

Ekanem’s mouth twitches faintly at the nickname, but he does not smile.

“Get him out of here safely.”

Then he walks toward the vehicle.

The DPO, now seated inside the van, looks out through the same barred vent where Sammy cried for his father. His eyes meet Rev. Briggs’ for one brief second.

Rev. Briggs does not gloat. He only looks at him with sorrow. Perhaps that is worse. “My President was right when he said that “Faith is more powerful than government, and nothing is more powerful than God.”

The convoy begins to move. The Peugeot first. Then the pickup carrying the DPO. Then the second vehicle.

Sirens rise again, cutting through the compound and spilling into the road beyond the gate. Dust follows them out. When the sound fades, the station seems smaller than before. Less powerful.

He places both hands on his son’s face, as if still needing to confirm that the miracle has flesh. “Ebuka,” he whispers.

Sammy cannot answer. He only weeps.

I stand watching them, my own tears falling freely. The full moon is still coming. The GODs are not yet defeated. Dike’s arrest has only opened the next battle.

But in that police compound, before officers, witnesses, and dust, something ancient has been repaired. A father has found his son. A son has returned to his father’s arms. And the curse that needed broken fatherhood for legal ground has met a blessing it did not expect.

Rev. Briggs holds Sammy close and lifts his eyes to heaven. “Thank You, Jesus,” he whispers.

This time, nobody in the station mocks.



































































THIRTY-SEVEN





We are in the hotel lobby when he receives the call. He listens quietly, his face giving nothing away, then ends the call and stands still for a few seconds. Around us, Lagos continues with its restless hotel movement – guests checking out, porters rolling luggage, a child crying near the elevator because his mother took his toy car away temporarily.

But Ifeanyi’s silence pushes everything else away.

“What is it?” I ask.

“They moved my uncle.”

“To where?”

“Kirikiri Maximum.”

The name brings a strange heaviness.

I have heard of Kirikiri before, of course. Everyone has. Even people who have never seen its walls know the name. It belongs to stories of hard men, condemned men, forgotten men, men whose names once frightened others but now answer to numbers, whistles, locked doors, and iron routine.

Ifeanyi slips the phone into his pocket.

“I want to go and see him.”

I look at him. “Why?”

“I need to look at him behind bars.”

“That is not an answer.”

“It is the only one I have.”

His face is calm, but not peaceful.

I understand then that Dike’s arrest has not finished something in him. Not yet. Ifeanyi betrayed Dike to save Rakiya, to help Sammy, and to break open the child-trafficking chain, but betrayal has a cost even when it is righteous.

Especially when the betrayed man is family.

“I’ll go with you,” I say.

He turns sharply. “No.”

“I’m going.”

“No, Lizzy.”

“I want to see this beast of a man.”

His eyes narrow. “This is not a spectacle.”

“I know.”

“You do not need to be in that room.”

“Yes, I do.”

“Why?”

“Because Ikenna cannot be there.”

That silences him. For a moment, something painful moves across his face.

I continue more softly. “Because Rakiya is still afraid of his shadow. Because Sammy nearly died under a chain of events Dike helped set in motion. Because your father’s house carried his poison for years. Because I have carried Jshap’s memoir, worn his chain, followed his testimony, and watched the Cross stand upright over his grave.”

I hold Ifeanyi’s gaze.

“If you are going to face Dike as the nephew who betrayed him, then let me stand there as the witness he failed to erase.”

He looks away.

The argument ends before he admits it.

“Fine,” he says.

But his voice carries warning.

“You will say very little.”

“I know.”

“You will not let him draw you into anything.”

“I know.”

“And if I tell you we are leaving, we leave.”

I nod. “Okay.”

He does not look convinced. Neither am I.



Kirikiri does not look like a place built merely to confine men. It looks like a place built to strip them of imagination. The walls rise high and stern against the Lagos sky, strained by the breath of too many trapped lives. The entrance carries the heavy authority of steel, uniform, procedure, and suspicion. The air around the prison feels different from the city outside it. Lagos noise still exists beyond the gates – horns, vendors, motorcycles, impatience – but inside, sound moves with caution. The prison silences the noise.

We pass through checks, waiting points, signatures, questions, and the dull scrutiny of officers who have seen too many visitors arrive with fear disguised as dignity. Ifeanyi handles the process with quiet efficiency. He has arranged the visit through legal channels, but even his influence has to bow before the rituals of incarceration.

Names are written. Phones are surrendered. Identification is inspected. A gate opens. Then closes behind us.

We are led through a passage where the smell of disinfectant cannot fully cover sweat, damp walls, old iron, and human despair. Somewhere far off, a man shouts and is answered by silence. A warder walks ahead of us with keys at his waist, their clink marking each step like a small warning.

I touch the Cross pendant at my neck. Ifeanyi has noticed this too often. He says nothing.

The visiting room is not large. A table. Three chairs on one side. One chair opposite. A wall fan turns slowly above us. A barred window high enough to admit light but not view. The light that enters is thin and pale, as though even the sun has been searched before being allowed inside.

We wait. Ifeanyi stands rather than sit. His hands are at his sides, but I see the tension in his fingers. 

Then the door opens. Dike enters between two warders. For the first time, I see him fully. Not as a name. Not as a shadow in Sammy’s story. Not as the boss Rakiya identified. Not as Rev. Obiajulu’s twin brother gone wrong. But as flesh.

He is older than I expect, yet age has not softened him. Prison clothes sit badly on him, not because they do not fit, but because his body resists the humiliation of uniformity. His face is lean, his eyes deep-set and sharp, his mouth pressed into a line that suggests disgust has become its natural shape. His hair is greying, but even that seems less like weakness and more like ash left after something burned too long.

He pauses when he sees Ifeanyi. Then he sees me. The pause lengthens. He expected his nephew alone. He expected fear, perhaps. Or guilt. Or negotiation.

He did not expect a young woman sitting quietly beside him, wearing a Cross on her chest and looking at him as if she has come not to beg, but to witness.

Dike’s eyes narrow.

“Who is she?” His voice is smooth, but the smoothness carries a blade.

Ifeanyi sits slowly. “She is with me.”

Dike looks at him. “You were supposed to come alone.”

“To get a brief on your estate, I suppose?”

A faint movement touches Dike’s mouth. Not a smile. A memory of one.

“You are still clever.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says. “I am late.”

Dike studies him. Then his eyes return to me.

“Who is she?” he repeats.

“It’s okay,” Ifeanyi says. “We can talk. She is with me. You can trust her.”

Dike leans back. Then he laughs. Softly.

“Ah.”

The laugh grows colder.

“Trust.”

The word seems to amuse him and offend him at once. He places both hands on the table.

“Did you betray my trust, Ifeanyi?”

The question changes the air.

Ifeanyi does not answer immediately.

Dike’s eyes sharpen.

“You came to my house. You sat before me. You spoke like blood. You carried my fear in your hand and called it concern.”

His voice lowers. “You sold me out?”

Ifeanyi looks at him. “Yes.”

No explanation. No softening. No apology. The word strikes Dike harder than I expect. For one moment, something human tears through his face. Pain. Not sorrow for evil. The pain of a man who believed one particular darkness still belonged to him.

Ifeanyi had been his last private confidence. The nephew who knew enough. The polished one. The useful one. The boy he had once drawn close after the father’s house broke apart. The one he believed still understood the language of ambition, secrecy, and power.

And now that boy sits before him and says yes. I sold you out.

Dike’s face hardens again, but the wound has shown. “You?” he says quietly.

That single word carries more hurt than shouting would have.

Ifeanyi’s voice remains steady. “Yes. Me.”

Dike stares at him.

“I trusted you.”

“You corrupted trust long before I betrayed it.”

Dike’s eyes flash. “I made you useful.”

“You made me ashamed.”

The words land between them.

Dike leans forward.

“You think these people will save you? The Bishop? Police? This girl with church eyes? You think they know you?”

Ifeanyi’s jaw tightens. “You do not get to speak of knowing me.”

“I know what you are.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says. “You know what you encouraged.”

Dike smiles then. A cruel, thin smile.

“And your father? Has he welcomed the prodigal now? Did he cry? Did he lay hands on you? Did you confess and become clean?”

I feel Ifeanyi go still beside me. 

Dike sees it and presses. “Blood does not wash off with prayers, my boy.”

The warder near the door shifts slightly.

Ifeanyi’s hand tightens on the table. For a moment, I fear he will rise. Then he breathes once. Slowly.

“The blood of Jesus washes deeper than yours ever stained.”

Dike’s smile vanishes. That is the first time I see fear in his eyes. Not fear of prison. Not fear of police. Fear of a language he cannot command.

Ifeanyi leans forward. “You sold my brother to your master, the devil.”

Dike’s face changes. “Careful.”

“No. You be careful.”

Ifeanyi’s voice does not rise, but it cuts.

“You took what my father’s house wounded and made it poison. You found Ikenna’s grief, his anger, his hunger to prove himself, and you fed it to darkness. You did not love him. You used him.”

Dike’s eyes move to me again. Perhaps because my presence makes Ikenna harder to bury. Perhaps because he sees the chain.

The Cross pendant rests against my dress, small but bright in the poor prison light.

His eyes fix on it. Something passes through his face. Recognition. Disgust, or defeat.

He looks from the chain to my face.

“Where did you get that?”

Ifeanyi answers before I can. “From Ikenna.”

Dike’s gaze sharpens.

I feel suddenly exposed, but I do not look away.

“Ikenna gave it to her,” Ifeanyi says. “After you and your master failed to keep him.”

Dike scowls. The humiliation enters him visibly now. Not only has Ifeanyi betrayed him. Not only have the police taken him. Not only has Rakiya escaped.

Now Ikenna’s lover sits before him wearing the Cross he could not invert, carrying the testimony he could not suppress, standing as evidence that the dead man’s love, faith, and final choices have reached beyond the grave.

Dike’s voice lowers. “So this is the girl.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes are cold. “Yes.”

“Ikenna’s weakness.”

“No,” I say before Ifeanyi can respond.

Both men turn to me.

My voice surprises even me.

“I was not his weakness.”

Dike studies me, almost entertained.

“You were what, then?”

I touch the Cross pendant.

“I was one of the witnesses that Christ took him back from you.”

The room becomes still.

Dike’s eyes darken.

I feel Ifeanyi watching me.

I continue, not loudly. “You built a fraternity around an inverted Cross. You made boys violent and called it power. You taught them rebellion and called it freedom. You turned grief into recruitment and guilt into chains. But Ikenna died in Christ. Sammy laid the Cross upright on his grave. Rakiya was rescued. Your secrets are opening.”

Dike’s mouth tightens.

“And you,” I say, feeling the fear inside me but refusing to bow to it, “are sitting here.”

That does it. His face twists. Not with rage alone. With humiliation. Because I am not a policeman. Not a rival. Not a man in his order. I am a young woman he would have considered irrelevant. And yet my presence forces him to see the thing he hates most: that God has used the weak, the grieving, the rescued, the praying, and the once-afraid to expose him.

He turns to Ifeanyi. “You brought her here to insult me.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says.

He rises slowly.

“I brought her here so you would understand who really brought you down.”

Dike’s eyes narrow. “I thought you said you betrayed me.”

“I did.” Ifeanyi looks at me briefly. “But I did not begin your fall.”

He turns back to Dike. “She did.”

Dike stares at me.

“She carried Ikenna’s memoir. She followed the trail. She believed Sammy. She protected Rakiya. She stood where men like you expected fear to silence her.”

His voice lowers. “She is Ikenna’s lover.”

The word enters the room with painful weight. Lover. Not a careless label now. A final humiliation.

Dike’s scowl deepens.

I feel my face warm, but I do not shrink. For once, I allow the title to stand. Not because I need romance from a dead man. But because the love Dike mocks has become part of the testimony that defeats him.

Dike looks at me as though he wishes hatred can reach across the table. “So the dead boy still has witnesses.”

“Yes,” Ifeanyi says. “And now you have walls.”

For a moment, nobody speaks. The warder shifts near the door. Somewhere beyond the room, a gate clangs shut.

Dike leans back slowly. “You think this is over.”

“No,” Ifeanyi says. “But your part in it has changed.”

Dike smiles faintly again, gathering the remains of his pride. “Prison is a room. Not an ending.”

“I know.”

“Men like me do not rot quietly.”

“I know that too.”

Dike’s eyes gleam. “Then you are still afraid.”

Ifeanyi looks at him for a long moment.

“Yes.”

The honesty seems to surprise Dike. Then Ifeanyi adds, “But not of you.”

He turns toward me.

“Let’s go, Lizzy.”

We rise.

I pick up my handbag, my legs steadier than I expected.

At the door, Ifeanyi stops. He does not turn immediately. For a second, I think he will leave without saying more.

Then he looks back at Dike. All the years are in his face now. The childhood memory. The corruption. The brother lost. The father wounded. The trust betrayed. The guilt confessed. And yes, Christ received. And the uncle who became less than family by choosing darkness over blood.

“Go rot with satan your master,” he says. His voice is quiet. Not theatrical. Final. “You do not belong among humans.”

Dike’s face changes again. This time, no mask returns quickly enough to hide his pain, his humiliation, his rage. And beneath them all, something close to fear. We step out before he can answer.

The door closes behind us. The corridor smells of iron and heat. For several steps, neither of us speaks. Then I hear Ifeanyi exhale. Not relief, but release. I look at him.

His eyes are wet, but he does not cry.

“Ifeanyi.”

He shakes his head.

“Not now.”

He nods.

We walk together through the corridors of Kirikiri, past barred doors, warders, locked rooms, and men whose stories remain behind walls.

Outside, the sun strikes my face with almost painful brightness. I breathe in. My eyes have finally seen Dike. He is still alive. Still dangerous. Still capable of reaching beyond prison if the systems around him allow it.

But something has ended in that room. Not the war. The illusion. Dike has seen that Ifeanyi is no longer his. He has seen Ikenna’s lover wearing the Cross. He has seen the witness he underestimated. And for a man who loves darkness, that is a form of judgment. Light has entered his cell. And he cannot command it to leave.













































THIRTY-EIGHT





The visitor’s room still seems to follow us after we leave it. Even outside, beyond the reinforced doors and the echoing corridors of Kirikiri, Dike Obiajulu’s voice remains with me — low, bitter, wounded, and yet strangely certain.

Until the last day, I protected him. But Ikenna died because he became careless.

The words do not leave me.

They walk with me through the prison corridor, past the warders whose faces have become trained into professional indifference, past the barred windows throwing narrow strips of light on the concrete floor, past the heavy gate that opens reluctantly and shuts behind us with the final sound of iron returning to iron.

The afternoon sun strikes my face as we step into the prison compound.

For a moment, I blink.

The world outside feels too bright after the dimness of the visitor’s room. The yard is dusty, tense, and watchful. A few officers stand near a parked van, speaking in low voices. Somewhere behind the prison blocks, a metal door clangs shut. The air smells of heat, rust, sweat, and the faint dryness of old concrete.

Ifeanyi walks ahead of me without speaking. His shoulders are stiff. His face is set.

He has said what he came to say to Dike, but I know the encounter has not left him clean. No man walks away easily from the ruins of blood, betrayal, and family. Dike may be behind prison walls, but his words still know how to reach for the wounds in Ifeanyi’s chest.

We enter the car. The door shuts beside me. For a few seconds, neither of us moves.

Ifeanyi places both hands on the steering wheel but does not start the engine. His jaw is tight. His eyes remain fixed ahead, not on the prison gate, not on the officers passing by, but somewhere farther inward.

I lean back against the seat and close my eyes.

Then it happens. The prison compound fades. Another night opens inside me. Ugo’s car. The stale smell of leather and fear.

The darkness outside the windows. My own heart beating too fast.

The first time I meet Jshap.

Not as legend. Not as Ikenna Obiajulu. Not even as the man I would come to love. But as a figure stepping out of danger with a calmness that frightens the boys who come to harm him.

My eyes open. A cold awareness moves through me.

“Ifeanyi,” I whisper.

He turns slightly.

“What?”

I stare through the windscreen, but I am no longer seeing the prison yard.

“I think I understand what Dike meant.”

His fingers tighten on the steering wheel.

“What did he mean?”

“When he said he protected him until the last day.”

Ifeanyi says nothing.

I turn to him. “The night Ikenna was shot… I never understood everything that happened. Not fully. I knew Ugo came with the boys. I knew Jshap refused him. I knew there was something larger than a mere attack. But now…”

My voice catches. I swallow and continue. “Now I understand the shape of it.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes sharpen.

“Tell me.”

I look down at my hands. They are trembling slightly. “Ugo wanted Jshap to crucify me.”

The words enter the car and seem to darken it.

Ifeanyi stares at me. “What?”

“He wanted him to use me to satisfy the requirements of the Law of the Hangman.”

Ifeanyi’s expression hardens first with anger, then confusion. “What requirement is that?”

I take a breath. “Bitty told me that a Hangman must shed his five fingers during his tenure before a new Hangman can take over the mantle after him.”

Ifeanyi looks lost. Not because he is slow. Because the evil is too strange to enter the mind all at once.

I explain carefully. “When a Hangman is ordained by the GODs, he is given a hammer and five six-inch bronze nails in a quiver worn on the arm. They call them the fingers of the Hangman.”

Ifeanyi turns fully toward me now. His face changes as the meaning begins to form.

“So shedding the fingers means the Hangman must use up the nails before another Hangman takes the office after him?”

“Yes.”

My voice lowers. “The hit led by the Hangman is called a Crucifixion Parade.”

The silence that follows is heavy.

Outside the car, life continues in the prison compound. An officer walks past. A warder laughs at something another man says. A vehicle reverses near the gate. But inside the car, time seems to hold its breath.

I look away from Ifeanyi. “Ugo had compelled him to…” I stop. The sentence refuses to complete itself.

Some memories do not need full description to remain terrible.

“But he refused,” I say at last.

Ifeanyi’s breathing changes.

I continue, slowly. “And then, surprisingly, he exposes himself. He reveals his identity to the intruders as the elusive Hangman… the one the younger disciples have heard about but do not know.”

Ifeanyi closes his eyes briefly.

“When the boys that came with Ugo knew,” I say, “they were frightened.”

“Then Ugo knew Ikenna,” Ifeanyi says quietly.

“Yes.”

“And he probably did not disclose to them who they were going to attack.”

“Yes.”

Ifeanyi exhales, sharp and pained.

I press my fingers together, trying to keep my voice steady.

“Now I know why Legion reacted the way he did that night. He asked Ugo to abort the hit, but Ugo had already sold his conscience.” I pause. “I think Legion was sent by Dike to stop Ugo. But Legion acted too late.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes return to the prison gate. His face is thoughtful now, piecing together old secrets with fresh pain.

“It is clear,” he says. “Dike sent him to kill Ugo before Ugo found the former Hangman.”

The words settle between us.

“He tried to protect him,” I say.

Ifeanyi gives a bitter, wounded smile. “Dike protecting someone. Imagine that.”

“But he did,” I say softly. “In his own dark way.”

Ifeanyi says nothing.

“Legion may have reported back to him that Jshap gave up his cover,” I continue. “Maybe he did it to absolve himself from blame for failing to protect him.”

Ifeanyi’s face tightens. “So Dike knew.”

“Yes.”

“He knew Ikenna died because he revealed himself.”

“Yes.” I look toward the prison building.

Somewhere behind those walls, Dike sits with his pride, his crimes, his ruined schemes, and the one truth he cannot twist completely.

“He is right about one thing,” I say.

Ifeanyi turns from the windscreen and looks at me. “About what?”

For a moment, I do not answer.

The prison walls remain in the side mirror, shrinking slowly behind us, but Dike’s voice is still near. It has followed us out of the visitor’s room, through the iron corridors, past the warders, into this car. I can still see the cold satisfaction in his eyes when he speaks of Ikenna’s death as if even tragedy belongs to him.

I press my fingers together on my lap. “In Jshap’s memoir,” I begin slowly, “he wrote about a meeting he had with Dike.”

Ifeanyi’s gaze sharpens.

“The day he received Jesus as his Lord and Saviour,” I continue. “After he was released from prison.”

The memory of the memoir opens before me like a page turned by wind. I can almost see Jshap seated before Dike, no longer the feared Hangman, no longer the hidden instrument of the GODs, but a man freshly broken by grace and ashamed of the darkness he once helped build.

“He confessed his regrets about starting the fraternity,” I say. “He told Dike he was quitting.”

Ifeanyi’s face tightens.

I look toward the road. “Dike told him it was too late.”

The sentence settles heavily between us.

“And Jshap told him that even if it would cost him his life to destroy that testament, he swore he would do it.”

Ifeanyi says nothing. But I see the words enter him.

I swallow against the ache rising in my throat. “Then Dike boasted.”

Ifeanyi’s hand tightens around the steering wheel. “What did he say?”

I turn to him. “He told him, ‘It’ll certainly cost him his life, yet there will always be disciples of the Inverted Cross.’”

For a few seconds, Ifeanyi does not move.

The engine hums beneath us. Outside, the road stretches ahead, bright and indifferent. But inside the car, the past has become present again.

“That was what he meant,” I say quietly. “Dike knew Ikenna was willing to die to destroy what he helped create. And he knew the fraternity would not surrender easily. He believed darkness would outlive him.”

Ifeanyi exhales slowly, the sound rough with pain. “But Ikenna still chose it,” he murmurs.

“Yes,” I say. “He chose it.”

And for the first time, the full weight of Jshap’s death no longer feels like an unanswered tragedy. It feels like a vow fulfilled.

“Yeah, he is right,” Ifeanyi turns to me. “There will always be disciples of the Inverted Cross.” The sentence hurts as he says it.

“Yes,” I say. “But Ikenna has made a way for those who have experienced the inversion to find light and live again. I speak more confidently now. “That is why his death could not end as defeat.”

Ifeanyi’s eyes soften.

The words are simple. But they carry surrender.

The engine comes alive. For a moment, we remain parked there, facing the prison gate. Behind us is Dike, confined, humiliated, still dangerous, but stripped of the illusion that darkness has the final interpretation of Ikenna’s death.

Before us is the road. Ifeanyi shifts the gear and drives slowly toward the gate. The warder waves us through. As the prison compound falls behind us, I look through the side mirror and watch the walls shrink in the distance. A strange peace settles over me. Something deeper. I now understand why Ikenna’s story refuses to die.

Because it is no longer only about the boy who wanted to become a doctor and was swallowed by the fraternity. It is about the Cross standing upright where darkness tried to invert it. It is about Sammy, who once served fear, now learning to serve young people. It is about Ifeanyi, who carries blame for years, now returning to the Father. It is about me, who thought I loved a man and later discovered that love had made me witness to a mystery larger than grief.

Ikenna died. But his death opened a road. And on that road, the lost are still finding their way home.











THIRTY-NINE





We are seated in the back of the vehicle on our way to Destiny Institute, the same study centre where his life slowly began to return to him after Ogbomosho broke him open. Outside the window, the town moves in its familiar rhythm – shops opening for the day’s business, motorcycles weaving through traffic, students in uniforms walking along the road, women arranging trays of food under umbrellas, dust rising beneath the feet of a city that never fully rests.

I turn to him and hand over copies of his travel documents he had given me to look at. “I’m so happy for you Sammy”

He smiles faintly. “My status as his son was never revoked.”

The words carry more than legal meaning. They carry restoration.

“He said once the processing is completed, it should come out soon. I’ll travel with him.”

For a moment, I cannot answer. I think of the police van. The barred vent. Sammy crying, Daddy. Rev. Briggs holding his face with trembling hands. The years of separation. The years the fraternity took from him and the Cross on Ikenna’s grave.

And now this. A son going home.

“Sammy,” I whisper, “that is beautiful.”

He looks through the window. “Yes.”

But there is sadness in his smile.

“Leaving them will be hard.”

I know whom he means before he explains. The students. Destiny Institute.

The noisy, wounded, brilliant, troublesome children who became part of his healing as much as he became part of theirs.

“They need to hear from you,” I say.

“That is why we are going.”

Then he turns to me. “And they need to hear from you.”

“Me?”

“Yes.”

“You are their teacher.”

“And you are the person God used to save their teacher’s life.”

We both laugh it off. I look away.

“I did not save your life.”

“You stood when others would have walked away.”

His voice softens. “You must speak to them, Lizzy. Not as a lecturer. Not as a preacher trying to impress them. Speak to them as someone who has seen what can happen when a young person either surrenders to darkness or chooses purpose.”

I say nothing.

He continues. “Tell them about life. Purpose. Faith. The Kingdom. Tell them what you have learned.”

I look at him. “That sounds like a valedictory address.”

He smiles. “Maybe it is.”



Destiny Institute does not know how to receive Sammy quietly. The moment he enters the compound, a shout rises from somewhere near the staircase.

“Mr. Briggs!”

Then another. “Sir!”

“Tunde! Mr. Briggs is here!”

Students pour out of classrooms with the speed of children running toward rain after drought. Some stop halfway, suddenly shy. Others run straight to him. Tunde arrives first, trying to look composed and failing completely. Eno stands near the veranda, both hands over her mouth, tears already filling her eyes. Genevieve comes out with a notebook still in her hand. Oluchi follows, dragging Bisi with her. Patrick stands at the back, blinking too much for someone pretending not to cry.

For several seconds, Sammy says nothing. He simply stands there and watches them. A man who once commanded fear now stands trembling before love.

Tunde breaks first. “Sir, you scared us.”

The sentence is almost an accusation.

Sammy smiles. “I scared myself too.”

A weak laugh moves through the students, then collapses into emotion.

Eno comes forward slowly. “Teacher.”

Sammy opens his arms.

She enters them like a daughter returning to shelter.

That breaks the rest. They surround him. Some hug him. Some shake his hand. Some stand nearby and cry without touching him.

Mrs. Animashaun and Mr. Adewale watch from the office corridor, both visibly moved. For once, no one cares about discipline, assembly order, or classroom timing.

After a while, Sammy gently steps back.

“Please gather everyone,” he says.

His voice is soft, but they obey immediately. That is the fruit of trust. Not fear.

Within minutes, the students fill the largest classroom. Desks are pushed aside. Some sit. Some stand by the walls. Others crowd near the windows. The air is thick with heat, chalk dust, emotion, and the faint smell of the compound’s dry earth. On the blackboard, someone has written in hurried chalk:

WELCOME BACK, MR. BRIGGS

Underneath it, in smaller writing – probably Tunde’s – someone has added:

NO MORE DISAPPEARING WITHOUT PERMISSION

For the first time that day, Sammy laughs. The class laughs with him, relieved to hear the sound. He stands before them, thinner than before, still recovering, but with a quiet strength that makes the room attentive.

“My children,” he begins.

That alone silences them.

“I have come today to thank you.”

Tunde frowns. “Sir, we are supposed to thank you.”

“You will have your turn.”

A few students smile.

Sammy looks around the room, taking in each face.

“I want to tell you three lessons life has taught me. Not from textbooks. From experience. From experiment. And from example.”

He pauses. “Experience is what life teaches you after something has happened. It can be painful, but if you are humble, pain becomes a teacher. Experiment is what you do when you try, fail, learn, adjust, and try again. Many people want success without experiment. They want greatness without process. That is why they fall into the hands of people who sell shortcuts.”

His eyes move briefly to Tunde.

“Example is the life you watch and learn from. Choose your examples carefully. Some people are examples of what to follow. Others are examples of what to avoid.”

The students listen without interruption.

“I became an example of both.”

The room tightens.

“I have lived wrongly. I have followed darkness. I have hurt people. I have wasted years. I have carried guilt. I have almost lost my life. But I stand before you today because the mercy of God can enter a ruined life and rebuild it.”

Eno begins to cry quietly.

Sammy’s voice softens. “When I first came to this institute, many of you thought you were here because you had failed. Failed WAEC. Failed NECO. Failed admission. Failed your parents. Failed yourselves.”

He shakes his head. “You were wrong.”

A silence falls.

“You were here because God still wanted to reach you before the world gave you a wrong name.”

Tunde lowers his eyes.

“Some of you hide fear behind jokes. Some hide shame behind stubbornness. Some hide pain behind arrogance. Some hide talent behind laziness because if you never try, no one can say you failed.”

He pauses. “But I came to tell you that your life is too valuable to be hidden behind excuses.”

The room holds its breath.

“You are not ordinary. But being gifted does not make you safe. In fact, gifted young people are often the first targets of dangerous systems. People will come to you with promises. Fame. Money. Love. Belonging. Protection. Recognition. They will offer you shortcuts and call them opportunities. They will study your hunger before they sell you your own prison.”

His words land deeply.

“Do not mortgage your future because someone clapped for your talent. Do not sell your soul because someone called you special. Do not enter darkness because it offers you a chair. The devil has chairs too.”

A murmur moves through the students.

Sammy lifts his hand. “The Kingdom of God is not only about going to heaven when you die. It is about allowing God to rule your life while you live. It means your talent belongs to Him. Your intelligence belongs to Him. Your voice, your art, your leadership, your body, your decisions, your future — everything must come under the government of Christ.”

He steps back slightly.

“And because I am leaving soon, I want to introduce someone to you. Someone God used greatly in my life. Someone who stood with me when standing was dangerous. Someone who believed that I was not finished when many things about my life looked finished.”

He turns to me. “Lizzy.”

My heart begins to pound.

The students turn. Some smile. Some look curious. Some sit straighter.

I walk slowly to the front. For a moment, I look at the faces before me and feel the weight of Sammy’s trust. 

These are not merely students. They are seeds. Some bruised. Some stubborn. Some frightened. Some gifted. Some already being watched by systems that know how to harvest young dreams.

I draw in a breath.

“Good afternoon,” I say.

“Good afternoon, Miss Lizzy.” Their response is uneven, but warm.

I smile.

“I am not your teacher, so I will not pretend I know how to control this class.”

Tunde raises his hand. “That is good, ma. We are difficult.”

The room laughs.

I point at him. “I have heard about you.”

“From sir?”

“From everybody.”

More laughter. The laughter helps me settle.

Then I become serious. “Sammy asked me to speak to you about life, purpose, faith, and the Kingdom of God. Those are big words. But I want to bring them close to where you are.”

I look around the room.

“Life will ask you one question many times: Who owns you?”

The room quiets.

“Not who gave birth to you. Not who pays your school fees. Not who likes your picture. Not who follows you online. Who owns your heart? Who controls your choices? Who do you obey when nobody is watching? Who do you become when pressure comes?”

I let the questions settle.

“Young people often think freedom means nobody can tell them what to do. But that is not freedom. That is exposure. True freedom is when your life is governed by truth so strongly that lies cannot easily buy you.”

Genevieve nods slowly.

“Purpose is not just what you want to become,” I continue. “Purpose is what God wants to express through your life. One person may sing. Another may draw. Another may teach. Another may organize. Another may speak. Another may invent. Another may lead quietly without applause. But every gift must answer one question: Will this serve God, or will it serve my ego?”

Some faces become thoughtful.

“Talent without surrender becomes dangerous. Intelligence without character becomes manipulation. Beauty without wisdom becomes a trap. Confidence without humility becomes arrogance. Ambition without God becomes hunger that never gets full.”

I see Tunde look up.

“Some of you want to be seen. That is not wrong. God sees you. But be careful of people who see you only because they want to use you. Be careful of platforms that promise to make you great but ask you to compromise truth. Be careful of love that requires secrecy. Be careful of money that requires silence. Be careful of friends who laugh when you are destroying yourself.”

Eno wipes her face.

I soften my voice. “Faith does not mean life becomes easy. Faith means that even when life becomes difficult, you do not face it alone. Faith means you trust God’s character when you do not understand His process. Faith means you can fall and rise. You can fail and learn. You can be corrected without being condemned.”

I look at Sammy.

“Faith means a man can be rescued from darkness and still become a voice of hope.”

The students turn toward him.

He bows his head.

“And the Kingdom,” I continue, “is God’s answer to every false kingdom trying to rule your generation. The kingdom of fear. The kingdom of cultism. The kingdom of addiction. The kingdom of shame. The kingdom of fame at any cost. The kingdom of violence. The kingdom of corruption. The kingdom of self.”

My voice strengthens.

“The Kingdom of God says your life is not for sale.”

The room becomes completely silent.

“Your body is not for sale. Your conscience is not for sale. Your gift is not for sale. Your future is not for sale. Your soul is not for sale.”

I step closer.

“Do not let anyone convince you that you must become dirty to become great. Do not let Nigeria’s broken systems make you believe righteousness is foolishness. Do not let delay make you desperate. Do not let failure make you available for darkness. Do not let loneliness make you accept counterfeit love.”

I look at them one by one.

“If you remember nothing else I say today, remember this: God does not waste surrendered lives.”

Eno begins crying openly now. Tunde’s eyes are wet. Patrick looks down at his shoes. Oluchi holds Genevieve’s hand.

“Some of you are carrying shame. Some are carrying fear. Some are carrying family pressure. Some are carrying failure. Some are carrying gifts you do not know how to manage. Bring them to Jesus. Not because church people are perfect. Not because life will suddenly become painless. But because Christ is the only King who does not exploit what He rules.”

The words come from somewhere deep inside me.

“He does not rule you to enslave you. He rules you to make you whole.” I pause. “Sammy is leaving soon to join his family in the United States.”

A wave of emotion moves through the room.

Tunde sits up. “Sir?”

Sammy lifts a hand gently. “I will speak with you about it.”

I continue.

“He is not abandoning you. He is completing one part of his journey and beginning another. But what God started in this institute must not die because Sammy travels.”

The students become very still.

“You must carry it.”

“Us?” Bisi asks.

“Yes. You.”

I point gently at the group.

“Tunde, draw with discipline. Let your art heal more than it wounds. Eno, keep living like the miracle you are. Genevieve, let your seriousness become leadership, not pride. Oluchi, keep protecting people with loyalty, but add wisdom. Patrick, stop pretending you do not care when you care deeply. Jumoke, let your voice carry humility, not just performance.”

A few students laugh through tears.

“All of you — study. Pray. Create. Serve. Watch over one another. Refuse gossip. Refuse shame. Refuse shortcuts. If one of you is falling, do not laugh. Reach out. If one of you is succeeding, do not envy. Celebrate. If one of you is gifted, help them grow. If one of you is weak, cover them with love and truth.”

I take a breath. “Soon, there will be an exhibition.”

Their faces lift.

“Rakiya – whom many of you knew briefly as the girl who painted – has reconnected with her family. She has chosen the name Rachel, in memory of the mysterious helper God used to lead Sammy to her when her life was in danger.”

A murmur moves through the room.

“Her first exhibition will be supported by Bitty and Ifeanyi. Sammy has given it the theme, Rhapsodies of the Coming Regent.”

The title seems to move through them like music.

“These paintings are not ordinary works. They are prophetic expressions – visions of the Kingdom of Christ, His sacrifice, His resurrection, His coming judgment, and His eternal reign. Her art declares that Jesus is the Son of God, that He died as atonement for sin, that He rose again, that He is alive, and that He will return as Judge of the living and the dead.”

Their faces glow with wonder.

“The event will bring people from the art world, collectors, enthusiasts, guests from different places all over the world, and people who may not yet understand the message behind what they are coming to see.”

I smile. “And you will be there too.”

The class stirs.

“Us?”

“Yes. Sammy’s students will make a presentation as part of the event.”

Tunde stands halfway. “Miss Lizzy, are you serious?”

“Very serious.”

The room erupts. For the first time that day, joy breaks through grief.
