Students whisper, laugh, ask questions, cover their mouths, and look at one another as if the world has suddenly widened beyond the classroom walls.

I raise my hand. They quiet slowly.

“Do not treat it as entertainment only. Treat it as assignment. You will stand before people who may have money, status, education, and influence. But you will carry something greater if your hearts are surrendered.”

I look around.

“You will carry witness.”

The word settles.

“Your presentation must show what God can do with young lives that refuse to be wasted. Let the art world see students who were once discouraged now standing with purpose. Let them see discipline. Let them see excellence. Let them see joy. Let them see the Kingdom.”

Sammy steps beside me.

His eyes are wet.

“I am proud of you,” he says.

No one jokes. No one makes noise. They receive it like food.

“I may travel,” he continues, “but I will not forget you. You are part of my healing. You are part of my testimony. And when I stand at Rachel’s exhibition to give the valedictory speech she requested, I will speak as a man who learned from all of you that God can rebuild a life by giving it other lives to serve.”

Eno covers her face. Tunde wipes his eyes quickly and pretends it is sweat.

Sammy smiles. “So, Destiny Institute, I ask you: will you continue?”

“Yes, sir,” a few answer.

“That is too weak.”

The old classroom energy stirs.

He lifts his voice. “Will you continue?”

“Yes, sir!”

“Will you study?”

“Yes, sir!”

“Will you grow your gifts?”

“Yes, sir!”

“Will you refuse darkness?”

“Yes, sir!”

“Will you stand for Christ?”

“Yes, sir!”

“Will you represent the Kingdom with excellence?”

The room thunders. “Yes, sir!”

I stand there, watching them, overwhelmed.

A few months earlier, these students had been scattered, noisy, wounded, ashamed, unserious, and unsure. Now they stand as a small army of unfinished but awakened lives. Not perfect. Not fully formed. But awake.

And sometimes awakening is the beginning of revolution. After the meeting, students surround Sammy again. Some ask about the United States. Some ask about Rachel. Some want to know if Bitty will come. Tunde asks if international art collectors like cartoons. Genevieve scolds him before I can answer.

I laugh.

For the first time in a long while, the laughter does not feel like escape. It feels like promise.

Later, as the students disperse, Sammy stands beside me near the blackboard.

“You spoke well,” he says.

“So did you.”

“They listened to you.”

“They love you.”

He looks toward the door where Tunde is trying to organize a group discussion and failing loudly.

“I will miss them.”

“I know.” I look at him. “But they will be fine.”

“Will they?”

I nod slowly. “They have heard truth. And truth is not weak.”

Outside, the afternoon sun rests on the compound. In the distance, students’ voices rise again – noisy, alive, hopeful.

The same noise that once sounded like disorder now sounds like future.

And as I stand in Sammy’s classroom, I understand that the Kingdom does not always arrive with trumpets. Sometimes it enters through a tired study centre, a chalkboard, a group of wounded students, a repentant teacher, and a message spoken to young hearts before the merchants of darkness can buy them.

The future is not safe. But it is no longer asleep. 

















































EPILOGUE





“Excuse me sir, you buzzed for a steward,” the airhostess tells me. 

“I’m afraid I didn’t.” 

She looks up at the panel. “Sir, your buzzer is engaged.” 

“Well, I didn’t press it,” I reply. 

She put it off, gives me a friendly smile and turns to walk away. 

“Excuse me,” I beckon her, “Since you’re here, can you help me with a glass of lemonade?” 

“Sure. I’ll be right back,” she replies. She returns about three minutes later and serves me a cup of chilled lemonade and peanuts. 

“Thank you, Mrs. Prikit.” I got her name from the tag on her chest. 

“You’re welcome, but that’s Miss Joanna,” she corrects me. 

“Oh I’m sorry. How long is the transit going to last in Kenya?” 

“It depends. Usually it doesn’t exceed two hours. You don’t look like a fresher on this route,” she adds invitingly. 

“How do you identify a fresher?” 

“I don’t know, you just don’t look like one,” she says politely. 

“Well, this is my first time on the Kenya Airways, I couldn’t get a booking on my regular airline.” 

“So, are you going to switch over to our airline after this trip?” 

“If you turn out to be as friendly as their airhostesses are,” I reply jokingly. 

“So how am I doing for P.R.?” 

“Not too good. You just falsely accused me of a fault.”  

“It’s not your fault, actually…” she says in a hushed tone, “I’m a divorcee.” 

“I wasn’t referring to that.” I lift my eyes to the panel above my head. 

“Oh that,” she giggles, “Forget it.” We laugh over it and she returns to her duty, while I return to the pages of the Scripture I was reading. 

Two hours on transit! Damn! It’s like two decades. Anyhow it’s better than having to wait for the next flight on my regular airliner, which is for next tomorrow, I can’t wait that long to set my eyes on Lizzy again. 

Lizzy and I have not known each other as much as I courted Lilian, but she proved a major source of encouragement during the days of persecution from my family over my newfound faith in the Lord Jesus. My father disowned me and I was banished for when he heard about my conversion to the Christian faith. But during one of my trips to stand witness in Dike’s trial, I learnt that he was sick to the point of death, so I sent Lizzy and Sammy to go and preach Christ to him and pray for him since his charms had disappointed him and the so-called priests couldn’t help him either. 

He eventually accepted Jesus, secretly, after he was miraculously healed by the power in the name of Jesus and sent for me to return to the kingdom. He publicly renounced his earlier position against me and being sympathetic to the faith, he made the declaration granting his heir and the subjects of the ancient kingdom of Gunkun the freedom of worship. He was severely censured by his palace chiefs who threatened to oust him. But until my father died two months ago, they couldn’t carry out their threats because the sovereign Lord was on his side. 

Because of my hope for a better inheritance in Christ, and that I will reign with Him at His second coming, I willingly gave up my right as the heir-apparent to the throne when my father died and the chiefs-in-council insisted that I perform the barbaric rituals, which involved that I present six live bulls for sacrifice to the gods of our forefathers after which I will be escorted by the chief priest of the idol to make atonement for the land by the sprinkling of the blood of the offering in the shrine. I was warned that the gods would reject my reign and bring a plague upon the land if I ascend to the throne without performing the rituals. 

God clearly expresses His displeasure against such idol practices and demon worship in the Scriptures, although it had been the custom of my people, the land had perpetually been polluted by such practices as the Sovereign Lord declares.

The Bible also teaches us that Christ who is exalted above the heavens, holy and blameless offered Himself once for all as a perfect and eternal atonement for the sins of all mankind, and that all who believe in His name are saved from the penalty of their sins, which the continual animal sacrifices could not atone for, although outwardly, it suggests to us that we are cleansed, it is not able to cleanse our conscience from acts that lead to death, which prevents us from serving the living God in true holiness. 

These facts I put in plain words to the palace chiefs, but, for fear of being destroyed by the dead gods they worship, they rejected my preaching and branded me an enemy of the kingdom and of the gods of their fathers. They also accused me of being responsible for the death of their king, my father, and ordered young men to flog me and banish me in the forest to be devoured by ferocious beasts. But the Lord delivered me.

“This time I know you called me.” Joanna is back again and she’s still wearing that charming smile.

When I look up to the panel, I notice that the buzzer is beeping. And the fellow sitting next to me is fast asleep. I put it off. “Ignore it when next it happens okay.” 

“You didn’t buzz?” she asks raising her eyebrows. 

“I didn’t,” I reply. 

She shrugs her shoulders and turns to leave. 

“Excuse me.” 

She turns around smiling, “What is it you want this time?” 

“Can I ask you something personal Miss Joanna?” 

She consents by nodding her head. 

“You are a very beautiful lady. Did you say you’re divorced?” 

Her face lights up and she licks her lips expectantly. 

“Tell me the truth.

She looks to right and to the left conscious that anyone may hear us, and bows on her knees.

I continue, “It’s not the first time it’s happening… in fact, I perceive the man you’re presently living with whom you call your husband is about to kick you out of the house because he’s dissatisfied with the relationship.” 

She looks to her left and to the right again and hangs her head. “How did you know about this?” she asks me in a low tone. Her eyes drop to the Bible open before me. “I perceive you’re a prophet,” she quavers.

“I just realized you’re the reason God directed me to board this flight.”

She takes the tray and as she’s about to scurry away from our conversation, the cup falls from the tray. She bends down to pick it.

“It’s okay Joanna, God spoke to me about you. He wants to help you.”

“What did God tell you?” she says in a hushed tone as she’s kneeling, pretending to be looking for the cup.

“Your bitter feeling about your first date is carried over and you have not forgiven him for how he treated you. You’re angry with yourself because you think you’re such a nice person, but people just don’t get to like you.”

She forgets about looking for the cup and jolts up in amazement. Then she looks to her right then to her left as if she’s conscious that somebody may be listening and whispers in a frightened tone, “Can we talk about this when we get to Kenya?”

“My pleasure Miss Joanna,” I beam at her.  

She raises her eyebrows at the panel. I follow her gaze and find that the buzzer is blinking again. I make a grimace and we smile at each other. 

“Now I know why the buzzer keeps going on. I’ll ignore it when it comes on again,” she says and thanks me again. Then she returns to her duty. 

I bow my head and pray silently to God for her. 

My impromptu trip will come as a surprise to Lizzy because she’s not expecting me, not so soon. It was after my three-hour telephone conversation with her yesterday that I took the hasty decision to visit Nigeria. 

Initially our telephone chat started with the boring details of the court proceedings, she informed me that Dike’s trial was struck out yesterday morning. He was found dead in his room in the early hours of the day. He took his own life. Afterwards, Lizzy briefed me about preparations for Rachel’s upcoming art exhibition, Rhapsodies of the Coming Regent. When eventually there was nothing else to discuss, and neither of us was willing to hang up, we switched on to nattering about our families, then to her future ambition after school, and finally we arrived at a tête-à-tête about the subject of inexpressible mutual interest – marriage. She eventually inspired me into asking her to. And when I did, she simply giggled and asked me to get her a beautiful bridal gown when next I’m coming to Nigeria. What a style! 

There is a beep from the loudspeakers instructing all passengers to put on their seatbelts in preparation for landing as we approach the Murtala Mohammed international airport. 

Opening the window shutter I catch a distant glimpse of the built-up city and parches of vegetation from the oval window as the plane leaves the thick cloud covering. I’m overwhelmed with the enthusiasm of being with my bride, Lizzy, with whom I’ll spend my whole life. I don’t know what my hope would have been if Jesus hadn’t found me, or rather if I didn’t find my best friend, Jehoshaphat Obiajulu who led me to Lizzy, my friend, my love. 

I flush with rapture as I reflect, with the descent of the aircraft, on the portion of the Bible that I’ve been reading – at the unspeakable joy that will be on that day, like our wedding – when Jesus shall return to the earth, according to His promise, to take us, his bride, to be with Him and reign with Him, forever and ever.  





THE END
